Jerusalem Talmud Nazir



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All substitute names<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a> reads: “A man or a woman who clearly intend to vow the vow of a <i>nazir</i>, to become a <i>nazir</i> for the Eternal.” This makes it clear that making a vow of <i>nazir</i>, like any other vow, is implicitly an invocation of God’s name. The speaking of any vow therefore is an invocation of God’s name and this should be avoided; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1, Note 1</a>. In this the vow of <i>nazir</i> is not different from any other vow.</i> for <i>nazir</i> vows are like <i>nazir</i> vows. If somebody says “I shall be” he is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">. But only if stated in the presence of a <i>nazir</i>, when it can be interpreted as “I shall be like him”.</i>, “I shall be beautiful”, he is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">. But only if stated in the presence of a <i>nazir</i>, when it can be interpreted as “I shall be like him”.</i><i>; naziq</i>,<i>naziaḥ</i>,<i>paziaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">. Names invented to avoid spelling out “<i>nazir</i>”; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.2.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 1:2</a>. Some of these words have meaning in Arabic: نزق “to be quick (or irritable)”, نزح “to be far away”.</i>, he is a <i>nazir</i>. “I shall be like this one”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">. But only if stated in the presence of a <i>nazir</i>, when it can be interpreted as “I shall be like him”.</i>, “I shall tend my hair,” “I shall groom my hair”. “I shall be obligated to grow my hair”, he is a <i>nazir</i>. “I have to bring birds”, Rebbi Meїr says, he is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The required sacrifice for a <i>nazir</i> who became impure, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:10" href="/Numbers.6.10"><i>Num</i>. 6:10</a>.</i>, but the Sages say, he is not a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It is not reasonable to assume that a person vows to be a <i>nazir</i> with the expectation to break the rules, even if unintentionally.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “All substitute names<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a> reads: “A man or a woman who clearly intend to vow the vow of a <i>nazir</i>, to become a <i>nazir</i> for the Eternal.” This makes it clear that making a vow of <i>nazir</i>, like any other vow, is implicitly an invocation of God’s name. The speaking of any vow therefore is an invocation of God’s name and this should be avoided; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1, Note 1</a>. In this the vow of <i>nazir</i> is not different from any other vow.</i> for <i>nazir</i> vows are like <i>nazir</i> vows,” etc. “All substitute names for vows are like vows,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">From the list mentioned in the Mishnah.</i> “Any person who vows,” why does the verse say “a vow”? From here that substitute names for vows are like vows. “Or he swears,” why does the verse say “an oath”? From here that substitute names for oaths are like oaths. One reads that<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The text is quoted from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.2-3"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1, Notes 12–22</a>.</i> at the start of the first Chapter of Nedarim, up to: Rebbi Ismael stated: “any person who vows a vow of <i>nazir</i>”. From there that a person can obligate himself as <i>nazir</i> while he currently is a <i>nazir</i>.
It was stated: “All substitute names for <i>nazir</i> vows are like <i>nazir</i> vows, and one whips because of them.” Even though Rebbi Joḥanan said, one does not whip for prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.5"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1, Note 36</a>.</i>, he agrees in this case that he is whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody makes a vow using unapproved language and does not keep it, he cannot be accused of transgressing a biblical prohibition. But if he refers, if only obliquely, to the biblical laws of the <i>nazir</i>, such an infringement is punishable by law.</i>. Even though Rebbi Simeon said, he does not bring a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.4.1">Mishnah 5:7</a>, R. Simeon holds that for a questionable vow of <i>nazir</i> one cannot bring a sacrifice since the Temple does not accept questionable sacrifices.</i>, he agrees in this case that he be whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vow is not questionable.</i>. Even though Rebbi Jehudah said, a questionable <i>nazir</i> vow is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:6:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.6.2-8">Halakhah 4:6</a>; based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a>.</i>, he agrees in this case that he be whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vow is not questionable.</i>. Where do we hold? If he has the intention of becoming a <i>nazir</i>, even if he only said, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if I mention bread, he is a <i>nazir</i>. Similarly, if he had no intention of becoming a <i>nazir</i>, even if he mentioned <i>nazir</i>, he is no <i>nazir</i>; for example if he was reading the Torah and mentioned <i>nazir</i>,<i>naziq</i>. But we hold about one who says, I declared my vow of <i>nazir</i> by any of these expressions. If one of them is a valid expression of a vow of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">From the list mentioned in the Mishnah.</i>, it will fall on him, otherwise, will the vow of <i>nazir</i> not fall on him? One tells him: keep the discipline<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:28" href="/Deuteronomy.12.28"><i>Deut</i>. 12:28</a>; if he had the intention of becoming a <i>nazir</i>, even if he did not use exactly the prescribed language. The same expression is used in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 81b" href="/Menachot.81b">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 81b</a>, regarding a person who makes an irregular vow of a sacrifice.</i>.
“I shall be”. Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: When he saw <i>nezirim</i> pass by<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">There is no vow of <i>nazir</i> without an implicit or explicit reference to the institution of <i>nazir</i>. The reference may be to a <i>nazir</i> passing by.</i>. If he said “beautiful”, what is the rule? Does he ridicule them or [does he mean] “I shall be like them”? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 2b" href="/Nazir.2b">Babli, 2b</a>, Samuel takes the position attributed to R. Yose ben Ḥanina in the next paragraph.</i>: Certainly, I shall be like them.
“Or I shall be beautiful”. Where do we hold? If he said “beautiful”, is that not the previous case? When he grabs his hair, is that not what we have stated: “I shall be like this one”, following Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina who holds that “I shall be like this one” refers to the case that he is grabbing his hair<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.8">Note 23</a>.</i>. But we must hold that he says, “there is nothing more beautiful than this”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">. In that case, we assume that he himself wants to be as beautiful and let his hair grow.</i>.
“<i>Naziq</i>,<i>naziaḥ</i>,<i>paziq</i>”. Rebbi Joḥanan said, these are expressions chosen by earlier generations and nobody has the right to add to them. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: <i>raziaḥ</i>,<i>haziaḥ</i>? Rebbi Shila said, also to expressions chosen by earlier secondary ones nobody has the right to add. <sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.2.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:2, Notes 115–120</a> (<b>נ</b>).</i> But did not Bar Qappara state <i>ḥeres</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, that is a name relating to the High One: “If He commands the sun: would it not shine?”. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, these are Gentile words, like those Nabateans who say <i>khaspa</i> for <i>ḥaspa</i>. Rebbi Yose said, it is reasonable in other places, but in a place where the <i>nazir</i> is called <i>naziq</i>, do I say that a <i>nazir</i> of people with speech defects should not be a <i>nazir</i>?
It was stated: “The House of Shammai say, both substitute names and substitutes of substitutes are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta <i>Nazir</i> 1:1</a> (cf. also 1:2); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 10b" href="/Nedarim.10b">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 10b</a>. The expression “forbidden” refers to “vows of prohibition” rather than to vows of <i>nazir</i>.</i>. But the House of Hillel say, substitute names are forbidden, substitutes of substitutes are permitted.” What are substitutes of substitutes? Rebbi Abba bar Zavda said, <i>menazaqa</i>,<i>menaziqna</i>,<i>mefaḥazna</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pi‘el</i> forms of the accepted roots נזק, פזח.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, these are not substitutes of substitutes, they are really substitute names, for is somebody who said <i>menadarna</i> not a <i>nazir</i>? But one who says <i>menadarna</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since Galilean rabbinic Hebrew mostly replaces <i>qal</i> by <i>pi‘el</i> (such as מְהַלֵּךְ for הוֹלֵך), an unusual <i>pi‘el</i> form for נדר “to make a vow” (and equally, for נזר “to vow to be a <i>nazir</i>”) is valid speech.<br>The form מנזדנא used here makes no sense; most probably one has to read מְנַזַּרְנָא as in the preceding sentence.</i> is like one who said <i>mefaḥazna</i>. Following Rebbi Yose, these are substitutes of substitutes, as we have stated: “I have to bring birds’, Rebbi Meïr says, he is a <i>nazir</i>, but the Sages say, he is not a <i>nazir</i>. “Rebbi Joḥanan said, because of substitutes of substitutes: <sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 4:30" href="/Daniel.4.30">Dan. 4:30</a>. This establishes a proverbial connection between long hair and a mention of birds.</i>“Until his hair became mighty as an eagle’s and his fingernails like those of birds.”
“I shall be like this one”; Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, if he grabs [a <i>nazir</i>’s] hair and says, “I shall be like this one.”<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, Samuel admits the possibility that a <i>nazir</i> was standing nearby and the person making the vow pointed to him.</i> “I shall tend my hair, I shall groom my hair”. If he says, I shall be of those who have to tend or grow their hair<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since in popular speech a <i>nazir</i> is characterized by his long hair, saying that one wants to be “of those who grow their hair” is a clear vow of <i>nazir</i>.</i>. “I shall tend my hair and I shall groom my hair”, if he says I shall neither tend nor grow my hair for less than thirty [days], he means thirty<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.1">Mishnah 1:3</a> states that an unspecified period of <i>nezirut</i> is 30 days. Therefore, mentioning 30 days in a statement regarding hair is a statement of <i>nezirut</i>.</i>. “It shall be my obligation neither to tend nor to grow my hair,” if he says, it shall be my obligation neither to tend nor to grow my hair more than thirty [days], he means thirty. “I shall be obligated to grow my hair”, means “I shall tend my hair, I shall groom my hair”. Or “It shall be my obligation neither to tend nor to groom my hair, but to let it grow wildly<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For the standard 30 days.</i>.”
“ ‘I have to bring birds’, Rebbi Meïr says, he is a <i>nazir</i>, but the Sages say, he is not a <i>nazir</i>.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, because of substitutes of substitutes: “Until his hair became mighty as an eagle’s and his fingernails like those of birds.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 4:30" href="/Daniel.4.30">Dan. 4:30</a>. This establishes a proverbial connection between long hair and a mention of birds.</i>” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, because an impure <i>nazir</i> brings birds<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:10" href="/Numbers.6.10"><i>Num</i>. 6:10</a>. But no pure person would entertain the idea of becoming a <i>nazir</i> if he expects to become impure since that could extend his period of <i>nezirut</i> indefinitely.</i>. Does he bring birds<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Many people use צִפּוֹר only for wild birds.</i>? He brings turtledoves or young pigeons. There are some Tannaïm who state that all pure birds are called צפור, and there are some Tannaïm who state that all birds, whether pure or impure, are called צפור. He who says that all pure birds are called צפור, “you may eat any pure bird<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:11" href="/Deuteronomy.14.11"><i>Deut</i>. 14:11</a>. In Scripture, צפור is feminine.</i>.” He who says that all birds, whether pure or impure, are called צפור, “say to any winged bird<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 39:14" href="/Ezekiel.39.14"><i>Ez</i>. 39:14</a>.</i>. “What is the rabbi’s reason? He is like somebody offering birds for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The expression הֲרֵי עָלַי “I have to bring” is a regular form of a vow, which in Temple times implied a gift to the Temple. A single bird can be offered as a voluntary sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:14-17" href="/Leviticus.1.14-17"><i>Lev</i>. 1:14–17</a>) but a couple can be given only as an obligatory sacrifice, i. e., a reparation or a purification sacrifice. These can never be given voluntarily, as result of a vow. Since the vow was formulated for <i>birds</i>, not <i>a bird</i>, it is concluded that the birds have to be given to the Temple for its upkeep, to be sold to persons needing them for obligatory sacrifices, with the proceeds given to the Temple treasury.</i>. What is Rebbi Meïr’s reason? He is like somebody offering a reparation sacrifice for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This is an impossibility; an obligatory sacrifice cannot be given voluntarily and it has to be offered on the altar, not sold for the Temple’s benefit. Therefore, the vow has to be interpreted as a wish to be in a situation in which one has to bring a reparation sacrifice to the Temple. The only reparation sacrifices which depend on the person’s initiative are either the possible sacrifice of the impure <i>nazir</i> or those required of the person guilty of larceny (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21-26" href="/Leviticus.21-26">21–26</a>). Since it is impossible to think that a person should want to commit larceny for religious purposes, the state of <i>nazir</i> is the only alternative.</i>. What is the difference between them? If somebody says, “I take upon myself to bring a reparation sacrifice.” In the opinion of Rebbi Meïr he is a <i>nazir</i> since one cannot bring a reparation sacrifice for the upkeep of the Temple. In the opinion of the rabbis he is a <i>nazir</i> since an impure <i>nazir</i> brings a reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since in this formulation there is no difference between the rabbis and R. Meïr, all commentaries read “he is <i>not</i> a <i>nazir</i>”; since no person would accept <i>nezirut</i> with the prospect of an indefinite duration unless he spells this out clearly at the start, the vow is invalid because it is unrealistic. If one keeps the original wording, the rabbis and R. Meїr agree not only on the result but also on the reasoning behind it.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am off grape kernels<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This uses the expression of the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num</i>. 6:4</a>: “During the period of his vow, he may eat nothing which can be made from wine-grapes, neither kernels nor skin.” Anybody who prohibits to himself anything characteristically forbidden to a <i>nazir</i> makes a vow of <i>nazir</i> (unless explicitly disawoved in the same breath) and is subject to all its rules.</i>,” or “off grape skin,” or “off hair shaving,” or “off impurity”; he is a <i>nazir</i> and all rules of <i>nezirut</i> apply to him. “I am like Samson ben Manoaḥ, like Dalilah’s husband, like the one who lifted the gates of Gaza, like the one blinded by the Philistines,” he is a Samson-<i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A Samson-<i>nazir</i> follows the rules not of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6" href="/Numbers.6"><i>Num</i>. 6</a> but of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 13:1" href="/Judges.13.1"><i>Jud</i>. 13:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 13:5" href="/Judges.13.5">5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 13:14" href="/Judges.13.14">14</a>: His vow is life-long; he is forbidden wine and any intoxicating drink, and cannot shear his hair. He does not have to avoid the impurity of the dead.</i>. What is the difference between a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">A person who made a vow to follow the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6" href="/Numbers.6"><i>Num</i>. 6</a> for the rest of his life.</i> and a Samson-<i>nazir</i>? If the hair of a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity becomes heavy, he shaves it off with a knife and brings three animals<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He celebrates the end of a <i>nazir</i> period, as prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:13-20" href="/Numbers.6.13-20"><i>Num</i>. 6:13–20</a>, except that at the end of the ceremony he cannot drink wine but immediately starts the next period. The frequency of his shaving is a matter of dispute in the Halakhah.</i>; if he becomes impure, he brings a sacrifice of impurity. If the hair of a Samson-<i>nazir</i> becomes heavy, he does not shave; if he becomes impure, he does not bring a sacrifice of impurity.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I am a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Mishnah in the Babli and most Mishnah mss.</i> off grape kernels,” etc. So is the Mishnah: “either off grape kernels,” or “off grape skin,” or “off hair shaving,” or “off impurity”<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">He is <i>nazir</i> if only one of the expressions mentioned were used. One should not translate: “I am off grape kernels and off grape skin and off hair shaving and off impurity”, implying that he only is a <i>nazir</i> if he recited the entire catalogue. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 3b" href="/Nazir.3b">Babli, 3b</a>, the latter is the opinion of R. Simeon.</i>. If he mentioned <i>nazir</i> with any one of them, following Rebbi Jehudah only if he mentioned “and”, but following Rebbi Meïr even if he did not mention “and”<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement between them was explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.6.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 9:7, Notes 85–101</a>, mainly in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.2">Notes 93–95</a>. According to R. Jehudah, if he makes a vow to become a <i>nazir</i> and then adds “and forbidden kernels, and forbidden skins, and forbidden haircuts, and forbidden impurity”, each “and” implies a new vow for an additional period of <i>nezirut</i>. According to R. Meïr, just reciting the catalogue adds a new obligation for each item listed, even if the items are not connected by “and”.</i>.
“I am a <i>nazir</i> and a <i>nazir</i>;” he is two times a <i>nazir</i>, for he could have said, “ “I am a <i>nazir</i>.” “I am a <i>nazir</i>,<i>nazir</i>,” two. “I am a <i>nazir</i>, once, and repeated,” he is four times a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since he will be twice a <i>nazir</i> if he said “I am a <i>nazir</i>, once” by the preceding argument, the repetition would apply to all that precedes it.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, “as they”, eight. “Like they,” sixteen<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the preceding statement. If one starts with a 4-fold obligation, any repetition doubles the number.</i>. Following Symmachos<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The text כמוכוס is corrupt. One has to read with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 8b" href="/Nazir.8b">Babli 8b</a>): סימכוס or סומכוס, סמכוס. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>: If he says, I am a <i>tetragon nazir</i>, he is a <i>nazir</i> four times, דיגין three times, דריגון (<i>sic!</i>) two times.<br>The problem of identifying the numerals is complicated by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 8b" href="/Nazir.8b">Babli (8b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 164b" href="/Bava_Batra.164b"><i>Baba batra</i> 164b</a>): Symmachos says, <i>hen</i> is 1, <i>digon</i> 2, <i>trigon</i> 3, <i>tetragon</i> 4, <i>pentagon</i> 5. The rabbis have stated: A circular house, or one <i>digon, trigon, pentagon</i>, is not subject to the laws of leprosy of houses (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:33-53" href="/Leviticus.14.33-53"><i>Lev.</i> 14:33–53</a>); a house built <i>tetragon</i> is subject to those laws. (A text similar to the last sentence is in Tosefta <i>Nega‘im</i> 6:3; Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 12:1 mentions only a round house and a <i>trigon</i> house.) In <i>Baba batra</i>, the word <i>digon</i> is further determined by the note that of the two consuls (<i>Archontes</i>), the one who is not eponymous is called <i>archon digon</i> (in the interpretation of the commentary ascribed to R. Gershom, a person “appointed <i>archon</i> for the second time”.)<br>Under the influence of the Babli, the dictionaries, starting with the 11th Cent. <i>Arukh</i>, have identified the words <i>digon, trigon, tetragon</i> used by Symmachos with the same words used in the Tosephta of <i>Nega‘im</i>. Now it is clear that in the latter Tosephta, <i>trigon</i> corresponds to τρίγωνος “triangular”, <i>tetragon</i> is τετράγωνος “quadrilateral, square”, and <i>pentagon</i> to πεντάγωνος “pentagonal”. In spherical geometry, there exists a notion of <i>digon</i>, but this cannot be traced back earlier than the 17th Cent.; it is a notion foreign to the spirit of Greek mathematics. S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta kiFshutah Nazir</i> p. 504) has recognized correctly that a word δίγωνον does not exist in classical or Byzantine Greek. Therefore, the talmudic <i>digon</i> cannot be translated “bi-angular”. The word is more likely to be δίγονος “double, twin”, which certainly makes sense in the statements about <i>nazir</i> and <i>archon</i> (<i>archon digon</i> “associate <i>archon</i>”). In parallel, the words <i>trigon</i> and <i>tetragon</i> used for the <i>nazir’s</i> vow have well documented non-geometric meanings τρίγωνος “threefold”, τετράγωνος “fourfold”.<br>There only remains the problem of explaining the word <i>digon</i> used in Tosephta <i>Nega‘im</i>. It cannot mean “biangle” since a house with only two corners automatically must have curved walls and is excluded as a “round house”. Since “house” (at least in the Babli) as a rule means “one-room house”, it could be that the Tosephta excludes multi-room dwellings from the laws of the leprous house, referring here also to δίγονος.</i>: “<i>Tetragon</i>, four; <i>trigon</i>, three; <i>digon</i>, two.”
“I am” is a handle<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.8"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1, Note 67</a>, a <i>handle</i> of a vow is an expression of a vow used in disregard of the formal rules of vows.</i> for <i>nezirut</i>, “I am obligated” is a handle for <i>qorban</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Not that he vowed a sacrifice but that it should be forbidden to him as if it were a sacrifice; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Nedarim</i>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">From here on, a parallel to the text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.10"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1, Notes 75–77</a>.</i> Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah: one catches him because of a handle for <i>qorbān</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked, if he said, I shall not eat from you, does one catch him because of a handle of an oath? Rebbi Yose said, <sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Nedarim</i>, the text between people … people is missing.</i> people usually say “an oath that I shall not eat from yours;” do they ever say “that I shall not eat from yours, an oath”? Rebbi Mattaniah said, people usually say “handle of an axe”; do they ever say “axe of a handle”?
“I did not vow as a <i>nazir</i>,” he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody used one of the languages classified as referring to <i>nazir</i> but immediately puts in a disclaimer, he is free from all rules of <i>nazir</i>.</i>. “I already had been a <i>nazir</i>,” he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This is not a disclaimer since a person who had been a <i>nazir</i> might want to be a <i>nazir</i> for a second time.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Avina, Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: If somebody says, I am like <i>‘orlah</i> juice<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Juice from the fruits of a tree less than three full years old. All parts of the fruit, including the juice, are forbidden for any usufruct; cf. <i>Introduction</i> to Tractate <i>‘Orlah</i>.</i>, he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Even though הֲרֵינִי was declared “a handle for <i>nezirut</i>,” if somebody declares that <i>‘orlah</i> juice is forbidden to him he is not a <i>nazir</i> since <i>‘orlah</i> juice is forbidden to any Jew.</i>. The colleagues say, that follows Rebbi Simeon in a disagreement. As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 3:5.</i>: “If somebody said, an oath that I shall not eat, but he ate carcass or torn meat, abominations or crawling things, he is guilty. But Rebbi Simeon declares him free from prosecution<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For transgressing his oath, but naturally he can be prosecuted for eating prohibited food.</i>.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, they disagree if it is an inclusive statement<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody makes an oath which prohibits to him both things originally permitted and those prohibited by biblical law, the rabbis hold that an oath partially valid is valid and any infringement can be prosecuted. But R. Simeon holds that the oath exists only as far as things originally permitted are concerned; for the rest it is non-existent since “he already was sworn to it at Mount Sinai”.</i>. But if it is a detailed statement<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">If the oath only contains a list of items prohibited anyway. In <i>Šebuot</i> (3:3; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 22b" href="/Nazir.22b">Babli 22b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 23b" href="/Nazir.23b">23b</a>) this is a matter of dispute and is asserted only by R. Joḥanan (supported in the Babli by Rav and Samuel) but denied by R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>, everybody agrees that no oath can be applied to prohibitions. And here, we consider an inclusive statement. Rebbi Yudan said, one is about vows, the other about oaths. Vows can be applied to prohibitions but no oaths can be applied to prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.2.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 2:2, Note 30</a>.</i>.
“Just as substitute names for <i>nazir</i> vows are like <i>nazir</i> vows, so substitute names for Samson [vows] are like Samson [vows].”<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>.</i> What are substitute names for Samson [vows]? Rebbi Avina said, Šimšok, Šimšor, Šimšoṣ.
From here<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Possibly one should read מִן הָדָא “from the following.” One starts the discussion of the <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity.</i>, “if his hair became heavy”: Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, which states that a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity can bring the required sacrifices in order to shave his hair only if it really is heavy, follows Rebbi.</i>. For Rebbi Jeremiah said in the name of Rebbi Immi: The words of Rebbi: A <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity shaves once in twelve months. The words of the Sages: A <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity sometimes shaves every thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>, this is the minimum period for a vow of <i>nazir</i> and is automatically assumed to be the period intended if nothing else is specified.</i>, sometimes once in twelve months. Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">This is an intrusion of a Babylonian text added by the corrector. His name in the Yerushalmi usually is: R. Yasa.</i>: It says so<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps one should read: מתניתא אמרה כן “The Mishnah said so.”</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:4</a>.</i>“I am a <i>nazir</i> like the hair on my head, like the dust of the earth, or like the sand of the sea. He is a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity and shaves every thirty days. Rebbi says, this one does not shave every thirty days,” but once in twelve months. “Who is one who shaves every thirty days? If he says, I am obligated for <i>nezirut</i> like the hair on my head, like the dust of the earth, or like the sand of the sea.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi agrees that an unspecified period of <i>nezirut</i> is 30 days. He requires the vow to state that the person making the vow intends a multitude of vows (each one implying a major expense for three sacrifices). But a reference to the hair on his head is for him a reference to a big tuft of hair, just as a reference to the dust of the earth is to a mound of earth. The Sages hold that the language used implies a reference to a multitude of separate things (hairs, dust particles, sand grains).</i>” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, they differ if it was not made explicit. Where do we hold? If he says, “the fullness of my hair”, everybody agrees that he shaves once every thirty days. If he says, “the number of the hairs on my head,” everybody agrees that he shaves after twelve months<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the text is corrupt and the statements should be interchanged. If he says “the fulness of my head”, he refers to the growth on his head as one entity, implying an indefinite duration of his vow but no obligation for any particular Temple ceremony. He <i>may</i> shave his hair after 12 months, provided he offers the three prescribed sacrifices. But if he refers to the “number of hairs on his head”, he refers to a very great number of separate <i>neziriot</i>, and he can fulfill his obligation only by always offering his sacrifices at the earliest possible moment, after 30 days. He <i>must</i> shave every 30 days.</i>. But we must hold that he says “like hair”. Rebbi says, “the fullness of my hair”. But the rabbis say, he means “the number of the hairs on my head.”<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This confirms the correction made in the preceding Note.</i> And here, why? Why did the man not make the vow? He only encourages himself to avoid prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">While it is clear that for the rabbis he may shave after 30 days, it is not clear whether he is obligated for more than one period. Maybe he is not a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity.</i>. In addition, from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 8b" href="/Nazir.8b">Babli 8b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>. In these sources: כהלקטי קיץ וכשבילי שמיטה “the heaps of figs and the pathways of the Sabbatical” (when everybody is permitted to enter any field at any time.)</i>“Rebbi Jehudah added: If he said, like that which is collected in the fig harvest, or like sheaves in a Sabbatical year, or like stars in the sky, he is a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity and shaves every thirty days.” Could you say that Rebbi Jehudah follows the rabbis? He holds with Rebbi, as Rebbi Jehudah added: <sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 4b" href="/Nazir.4b">Babli, 4b</a>, the statement is attributed to Rebbi. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 3:16" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).3.16">Tosephta <i>Soṭah</i> 3:16</a>: R. Jehudah the Prince (= Rebbi). In <i>Mekhilta deR. Ismael, Širah</i> 2: R. Jehudah; <i>Mekhilta deR</i>.<i>Simeon ben Ioḥai</i>, p. 75: R. Jehudah the Prince. The Yerushalmi rejects the attribution to Rebbi. Since R. Jehudah admits that a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity shaves only once a year, he must agree with Rebbi.</i>“Absalom was a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity and shaved every twelve months.” What is the reason? Because of <i>nezirut</i>. But that would mean for the rabbis that he shaved every thirty days; that is what we have stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.5.1">Mishnah 2:5</a>.</i>“I am a <i>nazir</i> the house full, or a chest full. One checks him out.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">One has to ask him what he meant before deciding his status.</i>” It was stated on that: He shaves every thirty days. But here he said, he shaved every twelve months<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was stated that for the rabbis of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.4.1">Mishnah 5</a>, he shaves every 30 days even if he vowed <i>nezirut</i> in perpetuity, it follows that R. Ze‘ira could not be correct when he stated that the rabbis agree that “the fullness of my hair” implies even for the rabbis that he only shaves every 12 months since there is no visible difference between “the fullness of my hair” and “a chest full”.</i>.
If he had counted six months when he became impure<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">A regular <i>nazir</i> has to start his <i>nezirut</i> after he underwent the purification ritual (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num</i>. 6:12</a>). But a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity has nothing to count; the rules of the regular <i>nazir</i> are no help in answering the question.</i>, does he count another six months or does he start anew counting twelve months? If he completed his term as <i>nazir</i> but did not manage to shave until two or three days had passed when he became impure<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that he has to undergo the purification rite and then start his <i>nezirut</i> again, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.4.1">Mishnah 3:4</a>.</i>. Rebbi Mani asked: Since he did not sanctify his hair, but what permits him to shave<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this sentence contains a scribal error and the text should be corrected as in the <i>editio princeps</i>: מִכֵּיוָן שֶׁלֹּא קִידֵּשׁ שְׂעָרוֹ <b>בְּדָם</b> מִי מַתִּירוֹ לְגַלֵּחַ “Since he did not sanctify his hair <i>by blood</i> (i. e., by the prescribed triple sacrifice) what permits him to shave?</i>? “I am a <i>nazir</i> after two days, already a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity.” Since he may shave, he is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.8">Note 23</a>.</i>. “I am a <i>nazir</i> after [20]<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Reading כ֗ “20” instead of ב֗ “2” (as in the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 14a" href="/Nazir.14a">Babli, 14a</a>) with the standard commentaries. Not only is “20” required by the context but also the reading “2” would require ב֗ ימים as against the collective כ֗ יום.</i> days, already a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity.” Since he may shave, is he a <i>nazir</i>, or since if he became impure he has no time to cancel, is he no <i>nazir</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, one has to assume that he set a date for the start of his <i>nezirut</i> in perpetuity. The question is whether the introductory <i>nezirut</i> represents a valid vow. If the dates are chosen so that the <i>nezirut</i> in perpetuity starts immediately after the end of the regular <i>nazir</i> period, he would be able to fulfill all conditions of that period, which include shaving his head and burning the hair under the well-being offering. But if he should become impure during that time and has to start again, he would not be able at the end of the period to shave his head, and, therefore, could not fulfill the requirements for the triple sacrifice at that time. This might invalidate the first vow. No answer is given; the problem is to be resolved in the future if sacrifices are renewed in a rebuilt Temple. Similarly, the problem remains open in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 14a" href="/Nazir.14a">Babli, 14a</a>.</i>.
“I am a <i>nazir</i> after 30 days, but already a Samson-<i>nazir</i>.” Rebbi Ḥinena said, it is reasonable that the Torah <i>nezirut</i> should preëmpt the Samson-<i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He keeps the regular <i>nezirut</i>, including its shaving, as if the Samson-<i>nezirut</i> did not exist.</i>. What is the reason? “Thus he shall proceed, following the Torah of his <i>nazir</i> vow;<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:21" href="/Numbers.6.21"><i>Num</i>. 6:21</a>.</i>” if his <i>nazir</i> vow follows the Torah. This excludes Samson-<i>nezirut</i> which is not from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">But from the prophets; it is valid as a common usage, not as a biblical precept.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the rules of the Samson-<i>nazir</i>.</i>“If he becomes impure, he does not bring a sacrifice of impurity.” He only said, “he does not bring a sacrifice of impurity.” But is he whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">For intentionally violating the commandment of purity of a <i>nazir</i>.</i>? The Mishnah follows Rebbi Jehudah, as it was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 4b" href="/Nazir.4b">Babli 4b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>. The Babli explains that the sentence about the Samson-<i>nazir</i> is formulated in parallel to the sentence about the <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity who is forbidden to become impure.</i>: A Samson-<i>nazir</i> makes himself impure for the dead, since Samson himself was making himself impure for the dead. Rebbi Simeon says, if somebody said, “as Samson”, he did not say anything, since the quality of <i>nazir</i> was not brought on by his mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 4b" href="/Nazir.4b">Babli, 4b</a>, R. Simeon negates the possibility for anybody to validly vow to be a Samson-<i>nazir</i>.</i>. What is the reason? “By the word of his <i>nazir</i>-vow”<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:21" href="/Numbers.6.21"><i>Num</i>. 6:21</a>. In the Biblical text: כְּפִי נִדְרוֹ “by the mouth of his vow”.</i>. Any whose quality of <i>nazir</i> was brought on by his mouth; this excludes Samson<i>-nezirut</i> which was not brought on by his mouth but by the Word. What is the reason? “For the lad will be God’s <i>nazir</i> from the womb.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 13:5" href="/Judges.13.5"><i>Jud</i>. 13:5</a>.</i>”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An unspecified <i>nezirut</i> is for thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">If no particular time-frame was indicated at the time the vow was made, the vow automatically extends to 30 days, which is the minimum possible for the duration of such a vow..</i>. If he said, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> once large, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> once small, even from here to the end of the world<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">All these expressions relate to surface area, not time measurements; they are irrelevant for a vow of <i>nazir</i>. From <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.5.1">Mishnah 6</a> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.3">Note 106</a>) it is clear that he said “I shall be <i>nazir</i> once from here to the end of the world.”</i>, he is a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days. I shall be a <i>nazir</i> [and]<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Babli and all Mishnah mss.; it is required by the context.</i> one day, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> [and] one hour, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> one and a half times, he is a <i>nazir</i> two times<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Since the original vow was not specified in time, it is for 30 days. If then something is added for a specific time (≤ 30 days), the second vow automatically is extended to the full 30 days.</i>. I am a <i>nazir</i> for thirty days and one hour, he is a <i>nazir</i> for 31 days since a <i>nazir</i> vow cannot be made for hours<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:13" href="/Numbers.6.13"><i>Num</i>. 6:13</a>) speaks of “fulfilling the <i>days</i> of his <i>nazir</i> vow.” Since he said “30 days and …”, he has to bring the three sacrifices after 30 days, shave, and observe a second period of thirty days. But if he said “30+<i>x</i> days”, he observes 30+<i>x</i> days and only then brings the sacrifices.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An unspecified <i>nezirut</i> is for thirty days,” etc. Bar Qappara said “ιʼεʼιʼεʼ” is thirty<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num</i>. 6:5</a>: “Until the fulfillment of the days he shall be holy”. Since the verse mentions “the fulfillment of the days”, it must refer to a fixed number; but none is indicated. The number is found by interpreting “he shall be” יהיה in the Alexandrian numbering system using letters as numbers. Since י (ι) = 10, ה (ε) = 5, the sum is 2∙10+2∙5 = 30. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 5a" href="/Nazir.5a">Babli, 5a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 22b" href="/Sanhedrin.22b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 22b</a>, this is attributed to Rav Mattanah; in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 25 it is a gloss.)</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Corresponding to the 29 times that in the Chapter about the <i>nazir</i> in the Torah is written “vow, <i>nazir</i>, to vow as <i>nazir</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 6, 6 times in v. 21, 4 times in v. 2, 3 each in vv. 5,12,16, 2 each in vv. 13,19, and once in vv. 3,4,6,8,9,20. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 5a" href="/Nazir.5a">Babli, 5a</a>, this passage is attributed to bar Pada.</i>. Are they not 30<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">If one counts the related word נֶזֶר in v. 7.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, one has to be removed for its definition<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Before rules of the <i>nazir</i> can be explained, the notion of <i>nazir</i> has to be defined.</i>. In the opinion of Bar Qappara, if he shaved on the 30th day, he did not fulfill his obligation. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">This should read: “R. Jonathan” since it refers to the statement of R. Samuel bar Nahman. (However, in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 22b" href="/Sanhedrin.22b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 22b</a>, R. Samuel bar Naḥman reports a similar statement in the name of R. Joḥanan.)</i>, if he shaved on the 30th day, he fulfilled his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation is 29 days, the 30th day automatically is the day of celebration.</i>. Some want to understand it from here: “To let his head’s hair grow wildly.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num</i>. 6:5</a>.</i>” How much is a hair growth<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote first: “How much is wild hair?” This might be the better reading.</i>? 30 days. Some want to understand it from here: “She shall cry for her father and her mother the days of a month<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:13" href="/Deuteronomy.21.13"><i>Deut</i>. 21:13</a>. The argument is based on the doctrine of uniqueness of lexemes, <i>viz.</i>, that a word used in the Torah has one and only one meaning: A meaning established in one place can be transferred to any other. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.20"><i>Berakhot</i> 1:1, Note 70</a>.</i>.” Since “days” mentioned there are 30, so also “days” mentioned here. Some want to understand it from here: <sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num</i>. 6:12</a>.</i>“The prior days shall fall, for his <i>nezirut</i> is impure.” The days which became permitted, which Moses and his court had permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Moses had forbidden Aaron and his sons to let their hair grow in mourning for Nadab and Abihu (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 10:6" href="/Leviticus.10.6"><i>Lev</i>. 10:6</a>), which otherwise they would have done for the customary period of 30 days (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 20:29" href="/Numbers.20.29"><i>Num</i>. 20:29</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 34:8" href="/Deuteronomy.34.8"><i>Deut</i>. 34:8</a>).</i>, and that is no less than 30 days. Some want to understand it from here: “Until the days are fulfilled<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num</i>. 6:5</a>.</i>”. How much are full days<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">From one full moon to the next.</i>? 30 days. Then if he shaved on the 30th day, he did not fulfill his obligation! Rebbi Isaac bar Eleazar said, “days” are written defectively יָמִם, with a letter י missing<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, there can be a day missing in the count, as there may be only 29 days from one full moon to the next.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:6</a>. He is a <i>nazir</i> for the greater number of 30 days or the days one needs to get to place X. Why is the person who is a <i>nazir</i> to the end of the world not a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity? Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.1">Note 89</a>. The same explanation is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 7a" href="/Nazir.7a">Babli, 7a</a>.</i>, we have stated: “I am a <i>nazir</i> from here to place X”, and here, he says so? There is a difference, for he said “once”.
Rav said: “I am a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days and one day<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the Mishnah (Note 90), where <i>nazir</i> was not mentioned and, therefore, the addition was something new, here <i>nazir</i> is mentioned and it is not clear whether the addition is a new vow.</i>,” he is a <i>nazir</i> twice since he could have said “31 days”. The Mishnah disagrees with Rav: “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> [and]<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Babli and all Mishnah mss.; it is required by the context.</i> one day, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> [and] one hour, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> one and a half times, he is a <i>nazir</i> two times.” What could he have said, “30, and an hour”<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of Rav is disproved from the second part of the sentence, referring to the man who says that he shall be <i>nazir</i> “and an hour”. Since the Mishnah says that if he said “for 30 days and one hour” he is a <i>nazir</i> for 31 days and the mention of “days” does not finish the sentence; each sentence has to be analyzed to determine as to which expressions are covered by <i>nazir</i>.</i>? A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this must read “Rav”, since no rabbis are mentioned in this context.</i>: Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Aqiba did not disagree about one who said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> 30 days and one day,” that he is a <i>nazir</i> only once. Where do they disagree? About one who said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> 30 days and in addition one day,” where Rebbi Ismael said, he is a <i>nazir</i> twice<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Since “in addition” introduces a second vow.</i>, but Rebbi Aqiba said, he is a <i>nazir</i> only once<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">While in speech, “and in addition” may introduce a new clause, in elementary arithmetic וְעוֹד simply means “+”. Since one deals with numbers, the usage in arithmetic is decisive.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am a <i>nazir</i> like the hair on my head, like the dust of the earth, or like the sand of the sea.” He is a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity and shaves every thirty days. Rebbi says, this one does not shave every thirty days. Who is one who shaves every thirty days? If he says, “I am obligated for <i>nezirut</i> like the hair on my head, like the dust of the earth, or like the sand of the sea<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi agrees that an unspecified period of <i>nezirut</i> is 30 days. He requires the vow to state that the person making the vow intends a multitude of vows (each one implying a major expense for three sacrifices). But a reference to the hair on his head is for him a reference to a big tuft of hair, just as a reference to the dust of the earth is to a mound of earth. The Sages hold that the language used implies a reference to a multitude of separate things (hairs, dust particles, sand grains).</i>.”<br>“I am a <i>nazir</i> a house full<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Arukh</i>, <i>s. v.</i> פונדק, the reading is מְלֹא חָבית “an amphora full”.</i>, or a chest full.” One checks him out. If he said, I made one large vow of <i>nazir</i>, he is a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days. But if he said, I made an unspecified vow of <i>nazir</i>, one considers the chest as filled with mustard seed and he is a <i>nazir</i> all his days<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">And obligated for a triple sacrifice every 30 days. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.4.1">Note 64</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> If somebody says, I am obligated as <i>nazir</i>, etc. Rebbi Manni said, one increases the severity<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">This belongs to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.4.1">Mishnah 5</a>. In asking the person making the vow, one starts suggesting that he imagined the box (or amphora) full of citrus fruit. If he denies this, one continues with pomegranates, then walnuts, etc., going from larger items such as etrogim to smaller and finally minuscule fruits, such as mustard seeds, to increase the number of pieces contained in the same amphora, as a simile of increasing the number of <i>neziriot</i>, ending with practically limitless number.</i>. At the start one looks at it as if full of <i>etrogim</i>, after that pomegranates, after that walnuts, after that filberts, after that pepper kernels, after that sesame seeds, after that mustard seed.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: If somebody sets a term to his state of <i>nazir</i>, one does not allow him to shave<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">But he has only one period of <i>nezirut</i>, at the end of which he brings his sacrifices and shaves, and this even if the term he indicated probably exceeds his lifetime.</i>. And it was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>. A similar text (1’000 instead of 200) in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 7a" href="/Nazir.7a">Babli (7a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 8b" href="/Nazir.8b">8b</a>) 7.</i>: “I am a <i>nazir</i> all my days, I am a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">By an error of the corrector, “he is a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity” is missing here; it does appear in the parallels.</i>; 100 years, or 200 years, he is not a <i>nazir</i> in perpetuity<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">He is a <i>nazir</i> for 100 or 200 years, not obligated for any sacrifices but not allowed to shave in the meantime.</i>.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am a <i>nazir</i> from here to place X.” One estimates how many days it is from here to place X. If less than thirty days, he is a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days, otherwise for the count of the days.<br>“I am a <i>nazir</i> according to the count of the days of the year, he counts <i>nezirut</i> in the count of the days of a year. Rebbi Jehudah said, this happened, and after he had finished, he died.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ “I am a <i>nazir</i> from here to place X,” etc. Where do we hold? If in the count of a solar year, 365 <i>neziriot</i> following the count of a solar year. If in the count of a lunar year, 354 <i>neziriot</i> following the count of a lunar year. But “the count of the days of a year” is problematic<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could have in mind either an actual Julianic year of 365 or 366 days, or an actual Jewish lunar-solar year which according to the calendar in current use is between 353 and 385 days (with somewhat greater variations in the Mishnaic calendar, described in the appendix to the author’s <i>Seder Olam</i>, Northvale 1998.) In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 8b" href="/Nazir.8b">Babli, 8b</a>, and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta, 1:3</a>, the cases of solar and lunar years are stated as a <i>baraita</i>. The formulation of the Yerushalmi Mishnah, referring simply to “a year” is not found in any of these parallel sources.<br>At this point, the scribe of the ms. wrote<br>גרש ההלכתא קדמייתא דרישא דנדרים עד מטי סופא דהילכתא<br>“One reads here from the the first Halakhah at the start of Nedarim until the end of the Halakhah.”<br>This was deleted by the corrector who prepared the ms. for the printer and inserted the text (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.14"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1, Notes 93–102</a>). The differences in readings are due to the corrector who probably was influenced by his training in the Babylonian Talmud. The main <i>baraita</i>, the story of Simeon the Just, also appears in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 4:7" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).4.7">Tosephta, 4:7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 4b" href="/Nazir.4b">Babli, 4b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 9b" href="/Nedarim.9b"><i>Nedarim</i> 9b</a>; <i>Sifry Naśo</i> 12; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 10:7" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.10.7"><i>Num</i>.<i>rabba</i> 10(7</a>).</i>.
It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah: This man was destined for death, only his <i>nezirut</i> suspended it.
The Mishnah follows Rebbi Jehudah <i>since it was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah</i>, the ancient pious ones desired to bring a purification offering, but the Omnipresent did not let a sin happen to them; so they made a vow of <i>nazir</i> in order to be able to bring a purification offering. Rebbi Simeon says, they became sinners because they made a vow of <i>nazir</i>, for it was said: “He shall atone for himself for what he sinned about the person,” that one sinned against his own person because he barred himself from [drinking] wine. It turns out that the position of Simeon the Just parallels Rebbi Simeon. As it was stated: Simeon the Just said, I never ate the reparation offering of a <i>nazir</i> except once. Once a man came to me from the South, I saw that he was handsome, with beautiful eyes and good looks, and his hair in waves. I said to him, my son, what induced you to cut off that beautiful hair? He said to me: Rabbi, I was a shepherd in my village and I went to fill the water vessel with water when I saw my mirror image in the water and my instinct rushed over me and tried to remove me from the World<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation is 29 days, the 30th day automatically is the day of celebration.</i>. I said to it, wicked! You are rushing me to something which is not yours; it is upon me to sanctify you to Heaven! I embraced him, kissed him on his head and said, my son, there should be many more in Israel who fulfill the Omnipresent’s will like you. About you the verse says, “man or woman, if he clearly articulates vowing a vow of <i>nazir</i>, to be a <i>nazir</i> for the Eternal.” Rebbi Mana asked: Why following Simeon the Just, even following Rebbi Simeon? Did Simeon the Just never eat a purification offering for suet? Did Simeon the Just never eat a purification offering for blood? Simeon the Just holds that people make a vow while they are upset. Since they make the vow while they are upset, in the end, they wonder. But if he wonders, his sacrifices become similar to one of those who slaughtered profane animals in the Temple courtyard. But this one made a well thought-out dedication, when his mouth and his thoughts were in unison.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> [abstaining] from dried figs and fig cake<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Figs and all their derivatives are permitted to a <i>nazir</i>.</i>,” the House of Shammai say, he is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If he said “I shall be a <i>nazir</i>”, he became a <i>nazir</i>. The qualification he appended is irrelevant. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 9a" href="/Nazir.9a">Babli, 9a</a>, this argument is attributed to R. Meïr, who thinks that “people do not say nonsensical things.”)</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is no <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>nazir</i> is permitted figs, his statement makes no sense and nobody can become a <i>nazir</i> by a nonsensical statement since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a> requires that the vow of <i>nezirut</i> be “clearly stated.”</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, when the House of Shammai expressed an opinion, it was about one who said, they are <i>qorban</i> for me<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">He disputes the Mishnah; nobody can become a <i>nazir</i> by vowing to abstain from figs and fig products. But anybody can make a vow to abstain from figs by declaring them <i>qorban</i> for himself (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Nedarim</i>.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> [abstaining] from dried figs and fig cake,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of the House of Shammai: because he mentioned the state of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If he said “I shall be a <i>nazir</i>”, he became a <i>nazir</i>. The qualification he appended is irrelevant. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 9a" href="/Nazir.9a">Babli, 9a</a>, this argument is attributed to R. Meïr, who thinks that “people do not say nonsensical things.”)</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, because of substitutes of substitutes<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the House of Shammai accept very far-fetched comparisons and substitutes for a vow of <i>nezirut</i>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.7">Chapter 1, Note 19</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta, 2:1</a>, this is explicitly given as the reason of the House of Shammai. The Tosephta must have been unknown to the editors of the Yerushalmi.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi said, a verse supports Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “So says the Eternal, as cider is found in the grape bunch, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 65:8" href="/Isaiah.65.8"><i>Is</i>. 65:8</a>.</i>.” The Torah called a grape bunch “cider”. And people call a dried fig cider, because of substitutes of substitutes. What is the difference between them? If he said, “I shall be a <i>nazir</i>[abstaining] from dried figs and fig cake.” In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion he is a <i>nazir</i>, in Rebbi Simeon’s opinion he is not a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This is difficult since the House of Shammai declare in the Mishnah that he is a <i>nazir</i> and the entire discussion only proceeds according to the House of Shammai. One has to assume that the person making the vow was asked what he understood by “dried figs.” If he answered, dried figs, R. Simeon ben Laqish cannot consider this as substitutes of substitutes, but for R. Joḥanan he still pronounced the word <i>nazir</i>. (This interpretation is that of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Menachot 103a:7:2" href="/Tosafot_on_Menachot.103a.7.2"><i>Tosaphot Menaḥot</i> 103a, <i>s.v.</i> הריני</a> in the name of Rabbenu Tam.)</i>. “I shall be a <i>nazir</i>[abstaining] from a loaf of bread,” in Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion he is a <i>nazir</i>, in Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion he is not a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A loaf of bread is not a grape derivative by any stretch of the imagination.</i>. “From a loaf of bread,” he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since the word <i>nazir</i> was not used. One has to assume that the expression הֲרֵינִי was used, since הֲרֵי עָלַי כִכָּר would be a vow to abstain from bread as <i>qorban</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.4">Chapter 1, Notes 44–45</a>).</i>. Rebbi Uqba asked before Rebbi Mana: The opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seems to be inverted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 12:3" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.12.3">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 12:3</a>. A voluntary flour offering is prescribed as an offering of fine wheat flour. Barley is prescribed only for some purification offerings which cannot be voluntary.</i>: “ ‘I take upon myself the obligation to bring a flour offering from barley.’ He shall bring from wheat.” And Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, because he mentioned “flour offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">One would have expected R. Simeon ben Laqish to hold that the vow of the offering was invalid since it was impossible. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 9b" href="/Nazir.9b">Babli, 9b</a>, his opinion is quoted as generally accepted.</i>” And here, he says so? He accepts one and he accepts the other. He accepts<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">He does not dispute the reason given by R. Joḥanan but only adds a second reason.</i> because he mentioned the state of <i>nazir</i>, and he accepts because of substitutes of substitutes. You should know that it is so since we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.3.1">Mishnah 2:3</a>. This discussion now also proceeds according to the House of Hillel.</i>: “If he said, the cow said.” She did not say anything; it is because he mentioned the state of <i>nazir</i>, and here he mentioned the state of <i>nazir</i>.
Any expressions can be used for <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If otherwise they are appropriate.</i> except the expression <i>qorban</i>. Any expressions can be used for <i>qorban</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If otherwise they are appropriate.</i> except the expression <i>nezirut</i>. If he said about a bunch of grapes, “I am locked away from you, I am separated from you, I am prevented from you, I am <i>nazir</i> from you,” he is a <i>nazir</i>. “It is for me <i>qorban</i>,” he only forbade it for himself as <i>qorban</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot use any of the grapes of the bunch but is permitted any other grapes in the world. If he said, “grapes are <i>qorban</i> for me,” he is forbidden all grapes and their derivates but is not a <i>nazir</i>.</i>. If he said about a loaf of bread, “I am locked away from it, I am separated from it, I am prevented from it, it is <i>qorban</i> for me,” he only forbade it for himself as <i>qorban</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since nothing connected with a loaf of bread has any relation with the rules of <i>nazir</i>, any restriction must be interpretated as a vow of <i>qorban</i>.</i> “I am <i>nazir</i> from it,” he is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Because he used the word <i>nazir</i>, not because of any connection with the loaf.</i>. “Prevented” implies both <i>nezirut</i> and <i>qorban</i>. If somebody said about a bunch of grapes, “I am prevented from it,” if he wanted to eat it, one tells him, is it not holy for its money’s worth? If he redeemed it, one tells him, are you not a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">An ambiguous vow has to be interpreted restrictively in all respects. If the vow could be interpreted as <i>qorban</i> or <i>nazir</i>, it is both (and, in fact, is a triple vow since <i>qorban</i> may mean either a prohibition “as if it were a <i>qorban</i>” or an offering to the Temple.) Therefore, when the bunch of grapes was redeemed and its value given to the Temple, the person making the vow still cannot eat it since he might be a <i>nazir</i> (even though one vow cannot be both <i>qorban</i> and <i>nazir</i>.)</i>?
Any expressions can be used for redemption except the expression “exchange”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Animals dedicated for use on the altar acquire what is called קְדֻשַּׁת הַגּוּף “bodily sanctity.” An animal used for the altar must be without blemish. Such an animal cannot be redeemed. It also should not be exchanged for another unblemished animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>). While any attempt to redeem the animal while unblemished, i. e., to subtract it to profane status, is simply impossible, the substitution of one unblemished animal for another, while sinful, nevertheless is possible and results in both animals being dedicated to the altar. If an altar animal develops a blemish, its bodily sanctity is reduced to קְדֻשַּׁת דָּמִים “holiness of monetary value.” The animal must be redeemed, i. e., its sanctity transferred to the money paid for it, and the money then used to buy a replacement animal. In this case, a substitution is impossible; there is no shortcut to avoid the redemption process.<br>Anything donated to the Temple treasury has only קְדֻשַּׁת דָּמִים from the start; it can be redeemed but not substituted.</i>. Any expressions can be used for exchange except the expression “redemption.” If he said about dedications to the altar: “this one is for that one, exchange for that one, barter for that one,” it is an exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Sinful but valid.</i>. “This is redeemed for that one,” it is no exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">If an unblemished animal was offered as exchange for an unblemished altar animal but the language of redemption was used, the transaction is void; the animal offered remains profane.</i>. If he said about dedications for the upkeep of the Temple: “this one is for that one, barter for that one,” its money’s worth is engaged<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The object originally given to the Temple has been redeemed by the object offered as redemption (provided that the monetary value of the thing given in redemption was stated; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 5:5" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.5.5">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 5:5</a>).</i>. “Exchange for that one,” he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since תְּמוּרָה is impossible for anything but unblemished animals dedicated as sacrifices.</i>. The other [expressions] serve for redemption and exchange. Dedications to the altar are subject both to redemption and to exchange. [Animals] dedicated for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This text is impossible since objects dedicated for the upkeep of the Temple are not subject to exchange but only to redemption. Unblemished animals may not be offered for the upkeep of the Temple; they automatically would be offered to the altar. Therefore, a “dedication for the upkeep of the Temple whose dedication preceded its defect” is an altar animal which developed a blemish and, therefore, has the reduced status of קְדֻשַּׁת דָּמִים and can be redeemed. Blemished animals cannot be dedicated to the altar; any such dedication is invalid.</i> who were dedicated before developing a defect, “this one is for that one,” if he wants to sacrifice a perfect animal, one tells him that its sanctity is for its money’s worth. If he wants to eat it after it developed a blemish, one tells him that it is holy as exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">If an animal originally destined for the altar but later disqualified was redeemed not by money but by the offering of an unblemished animal and use of a term which can be interpreted to mean either redemption or substitution, the original animal is redeemed but the other animal is both a substitute and a redemption. It cannot be sacrificed since it is a redemption; one cannot wait until it develops a blemish with age because it is a substitution. It must be redeemed to eliminate the “holiness of monetary value” and then be sacrificed on the altar.</i>: “Itself and its exchange shall be holy<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Isaac ben Eleazar said, since he knows that anybody who exchanges is whipped, it never occured to him to exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">He objects to the construction of a case in which an expression was used that might mean both redemption and substitution. Since redemption is required but substitution is sinful, it is obvious that only redemption was intended.</i>.
Any expressions can be used for valuation<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">“Valuation” is the sum fixed for vows in which a person promises to pay his or another person’s valuation to the Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:1-8" href="/Leviticus.27.1-8"><i>Lev</i>. 27:1–8</a>). The valuation depends on age and gender; it is fixed by the Biblical text (assuming a known ratio of the Biblical <i>šeqel</i> to the local currency.) “Money’s worth” of a person is the value he would fetch if sold as a slave. This worth can be promised by a vow, but not by a valuation.</i> except the expression “money’s worth”. Any expressions can be used for money’s worth except the expression “valuation.” If he said about a human, “I shall pay his cost, I shall pay his settlement, I shall pay his estimate, I shall pay his valuation,” he has to pay his valuation<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If an expression is ambiguous but “paying the valuation” is one of the meanings, in the absence of a definite indication to the contrary that is the legal meaning.</i>. “I shall pay his money’s worth,” he has to pay his money’s worth. “Estimate” is used as an expression both for valuation and money’s worth. A human has both valuation and money’s worth. If he said about a human, “I shall pay his estimate,” if he was good looking, he pays his money’s worth; if he was ugly, he pays his valuation<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In every case, he pays the larger sum.</i>.
The House of Shammai say, he is bound by a vow and is a <i>nazir</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is neither bound by a vow nor is he a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah. The person made an inappropriate vow of <i>nazir</i>, referring to food permitted to a <i>nazir</i>. The House of Shammai hold that he is forbidden all kinds of dried figs because of his vow, and he is a <i>nazir</i> since he used the word <i>nazir</i>. The House of Hillel hold that an inappropriate choice of words invalidates both vow and <i>nezirut</i>. This rejects R. Jehudah’s interpretation.<br>The parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 9b" href="/Nazir.9b">Babli, 9b</a>, shows that this is a <i>baraita</i>. There, it is given in two versions (of which the Yerushalmi text is a combination). In one, the House of Shammai say, he is bound by a vow and is a <i>nazir</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is bound by a vow but not a <i>nazir</i>. In the other, the House of Shammai say, he is bound by a vow but not a <i>nazir</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is neither bound by a vow nor is he a <i>nazir</i>.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he said: “this cow said, I shall be a <i>nezirah</i> if I be standing up,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The rancher has trouble to make the cow get on her feet; he says, it seems to me that the cow made a vow to be <i>nezirah</i> if she be standing up. For the House of Shammai, he used the word <i>nazir</i>; for the House of Hillel what he said was meaningless.</i>” or “this door said, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if I be open,” the House of Shammai say, he is a <i>nazir</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is no <i>nazir</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, when the House of Shammai expressed an opinion, it was only about one who said, this cow shall be <i>qorban</i> for me if she gets up<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Then the cow is forbidden to him as <i>qorban</i>, but he is no <i>nazir</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he said: ‘this cow said,’ ” etc. If he saw a Gentile passing by and said, “look what this Gentile said.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">It is understood that he said: “look what this Gentile said, I shall be a <i>nazir</i>.”</i>” Then he is a <i>nazir</i>.
