Does this mean that he is a <i>nazir</i> because Gentiles cannot make a vow of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.1.1">Mishnah 9:1</a>. Therefore, any vow of <i>nazir</i> must be the Jew’s.</i>? He has not said anything! Let us hear from the following: “If he said: ‘this cow said’ ”. You said this only because he spoke the word <i>nezirut</i>. And here, he spoke the word <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The rancher has trouble to make the cow get on her feet; he says, it seems to me that the cow made a vow to be <i>nezirah</i> if she be standing up. For the House of Shammai, he used the word <i>nazir</i>; for the House of Hillel what he said was meaningless.</i>. If he saw a Jew passing by and said, “look what this Jew said.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">He attributed to him a vow of <i>nazir</i>.</i>” Then he is a <i>nazir</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The passer-by.</i> had not said anything. Does this mean that he is a <i>nazir</i> because Jews make a vow of <i>nazir</i>? Should we say that nevertheless he is a <i>nazir</i> or is he studying the case? So I am saying, “for example, if he was reading the Torah and mentioned <i>nazir</i>,<i>naziq</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.3">Chapter 1, after Note 10</a>. Since in that case he is not a <i>nazir</i>, he cannot be a <i>nazir</i> in the present case.</i>”
Can a man be taken to <i>nezirut</i> by language appropriate for a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should translate: “a vow formulated in the feminine”; but the explanation given “there” (in Babylonia) then does not quite fit.</i>? There, they say, “a <i>nezirah</i> was passing by<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with <i>editio princeps</i> <i>איעבר</i> “passed by” (m.) for אי עבר “did not pass”. This refers to the case discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.1">Halakhah 1:1 (Notes 2</a>,23) that a person points to a <i>nazir</i> and indicates that he wants to be like him. It is admitted that he can point to a female.</i>”. Can a man be taken to <i>nezirut</i> by masculine language? Rebbi Yose said, the notion of <i>nezirut</i> is defined in the masculine: “… or a woman if he clearly makes a vow.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a>: “A man or a woman, if he clearly makes a vow”. Since a verb referring both to a male and a female is used in the masculine by traditional grammatical rules, he infers that the masculine can be used in all cases.</i>”
Unless the cow stood up, unless the door was opened<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Under which circumstances will the House of Shammai insist that the maker of the vow is a <i>nazir</i>? If the cow does stand up or if she never stands up?</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.4.1">Mishnah 5:6</a>: “If people dispute facts; A says that he will be <i>nazir</i> if a fact is true, B says that he will be <i>nazir</i> if it is not true, C says that he will be <i>nazir</i> if both A and B are wrong. The House of Shammai say, …”</i>: “The House of Shammai say, they are all <i>nezirim</i>. But the House of Hillel say, only those are <i>nezirim</i> whose words turned out not to be true.” So is the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The text is garbled here (and somewhat garbled in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.4.4">Halakhah 6:6</a>): “So is the Mishnah: ‘Whose words turned out to be true;’ it is formulated in the opposite, so as not to bury her son.” The rancher’s statement, that his cow said, “I shall be a <i>nezirah</i> if I be standing up”, for the House of Shammai has to be interpreted as a vow of <i>nazir</i> if the cow <i>never</i> stands up, since the cow standing up is the desired outcome; just as a woman who is afraid her son might die will formulate it saying her son certainly will not die. (It is possible to read a similar interpretation into the text of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 10b" href="/Nazir.10b">Babli, 10b</a>.)</i>: “Whose words turned out not to be true;” it is formulated in the opposite, as not to say that she should not bury her son. Even if the cow did not stand up, even if the door was not opened<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">For the House of Shammai, he is a <i>nazir</i> in any case.</i>.
The House of Shammai say, he is bound by a vow and is a <i>nazir</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is neither bound by a vow nor is he a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah. The person made an inappropriate vow of <i>nazir</i>, referring to food permitted to a <i>nazir</i>. The House of Shammai hold that he is forbidden all kinds of dried figs because of his vow, and he is a <i>nazir</i> since he used the word <i>nazir</i>. The House of Hillel hold that an inappropriate choice of words invalidates both vow and <i>nezirut</i>. This rejects R. Jehudah’s interpretation.<br>The parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 9b" href="/Nazir.9b">Babli, 9b</a>, shows that this is a <i>baraita</i>. There, it is given in two versions (of which the Yerushalmi text is a combination). In one, the House of Shammai say, he is bound by a vow and is a <i>nazir</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is bound by a vow but not a <i>nazir</i>. In the other, the House of Shammai say, he is bound by a vow but not a <i>nazir</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is neither bound by a vow nor is he a <i>nazir</i>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a cup of wine was prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Mixed with water.</i> for somebody who then said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> [abstaining] from it”, he is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>nezirut</i> cannot be partial. Once he mentions <i>nezirut</i> for one cup of wine, he is forbidden all wine, all grapes, all haircuts, and any impurity of the dead.</i>. It happened that a cup of wine was prepared for a woman who already was drunk, when she said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> [abstaining] from it”. The Sages said that she only intended to say, “it shall be <i>qorban</i> for me.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since she was drunk, she certainly did not want to forbid all wine to herself, but only that particular cup which was too much for her.</i>”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a cup of wine was prepared,” etc. The Mishnah applies if he is not used to it<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The first part, in which the person who declares himself <i>nazir</i> for one cup is actually a <i>nazir</i> applies only to people who are not used to getting drunk.</i>; but if he is used to it, that is what we have stated: “It happened that a cup of wine was prepared for a woman who already was drunk when she said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> [abstaining] from it”. The Sages said that she only intended to say, “it shall be <i>qorban</i> for me.” Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: It is not even an expression of <i>qorban</i>. Why? Because one can neither use an expression of <i>nezirut</i> for <i>qorban</i> nor an expression of <i>qorban</i> for <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.8">Halakhah 1, Note 16</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am a <i>nazir</i> on condition that I may drink wine or become impure for the dead,” he is a <i>nazir</i> and forbidden everything<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>nezirut</i> is defined in the Torah and any stipulation contradicting a biblical law is void (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.1.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:1, Note 5</a>).</i>. “I knew that there are <i>nezirim</i> but I did not know that wine is forbidden to the <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">A person who declared himself a <i>nazir</i>, and when told that wine was forbidden to him declares that at the moment of the vow he was ignorant of its implications.</i>”; wine is forbidden to him, but Rebbi Simeon permits<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Because the vow was made in error and such a vow is excluded by the requirement that the vow be clearly enunciated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a>).</i>. “I knew that wine was forbidden to the <i>nazir</i> but I thought that the Sages would permit me because I cannot live without wine, or because I am an undertaker;” he is permitted but Rebbi Simeon forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">For the majority, the vow is in error; for R. Simeon it is a frivolous vow.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah follows Rebbi Meïr, since Rebbi Meïr says, one has to double one’s stipulation<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 3:3. A legal stipulation must follow the example of the stipulation between Moses and the tribes of Gad and Reuben (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 32" href="/Numbers.32"><i>Num</i>. 32</a>), which covers both the positive (if they fulfill the stipulation, they will acquire the land in Transjordan), and the negative (if they fail to fulfill the stipulation, they will be given land in Cisjordan). Since in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> the person declaring himself a <i>nazir</i> failed to state that if he could not be a <i>nazir</i> on his terms, he would not be a <i>nazir</i>, his stipulation is invalid and he is a <i>nazir</i>.</i>. It follows everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The waiving of any stipulation in violation of biblical law (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.4.1">Note 51</a>) does not depend on R. Meïr’s opinion.</i>; one tells him: Watch and keep discipline<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:28" href="/Deuteronomy.12.28"><i>Deut</i>. 12:28</a>; a poetic formulation of the principle of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.4.1">Note 51</a>.</i>. The Mishnah follows Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.4.2">Note 57</a>.</i> or Rebbi Jehudah ben Tema<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Who holds that an impossible condition is considered nonexistent. Since a stipulation against biblical law is impossible, the condition mentioned in the first sentence of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> is considered nonexistent.</i>, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:12" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.12">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:12</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 84a" href="/Gittin.84a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 84a</a>.</i>: “This is your bill of divorce, on condition that you not fly in the air, that you not cross the Sea on your feet, that is a bill of divorce. On condition that you fly in the air, that you cross the Sea on your feet, that is no bill of divorce; Rebbi Jehudah ben Tema said, it is a bill of divorce.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, you should realize that he seeks a subterfuge for the bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">He thinks that he can doubly hurt his wife, by preventing her to remarry and not paying her <i>ketubah</i>, since he may claim that he is waiting for her to satisfy the stipulation. By declaring the stipulation invalid, the court will permit her to remarry and force him to pay.</i>, since he attached conditions that cannot be satisfied. What is Rebbi Jehudah ben Tema’s reason? Since he attached conditions that cannot be satisfied, it is as if the condition attached to the bill of divorce were satisfied<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In Tosephta and Babli: R. Jehudah ben Tema states as a principle that any stipulation which cannot be satisfied is only intended as a delaying tactic.</i>.
Does the Mishnah follow Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Simeon is quoted as disagreeing in two of the three cases quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>, does one have to assume that he agrees in the first case?</i>? For “Rebbi Simeon declares him free, because his offering was not according to the way of offerers.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 12:3" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.12.3">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 12:3</a>. If somebody vows an offering of barley flour (which cannot be a voluntary offering), the rabbis require him to bring an offering of wheat flour but “R. Simeon declares him free, because his offering was not according to the way of offerers.” One should assume that R. Simeon declares a person who wants to be <i>nazir</i> on condition that he may drink wine as “offering not according to the way of offerers.” Why does he not disagree in the first case?</i>” And Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, there is a difference because he reserved shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since the person making the vow did not stipulate that he may shave, the vow is valid for the prohibition of shaving and a partially valid vow is valid.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If it is because he reserved shaving, does not the following state “it is forbidden to him, but Rebbi Simeon permits.” Did he not reserve shaving [and] impurity but Rebbi Simeon frees him<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The vow is partially valid; why is it not valid?</i>? There is a difference, because of an opening for the vow<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since he bases his vow on an argument which will automatically grant him a revocation of the vow (cf. <i>Nedarim</i> Chapter 9), the vow is automatically revoked. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 11b" href="/Nazir.11b">Babli, 11b</a>, this argument is attributed to Rav Assi. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta, 2:3</a>, it is explicitly attributed to R. Simeon.</i>. If it was because of an opening for the vow, does not the following state “he is permitted but Rebbi Simeon forbids”? Rebbi Simeon does not recognize it as an opening for the vow but the rabbis recognize it as an opening for the vow. Why? Because he connects his vow with his life<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If he needs wine for medical purposes, the use of wine must be permitted to him.</i>. One understands, to drink wine. The defile oneself for the dead? It is his profession to bury the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The only person who can have a vow of <i>nazir</i> annulled because he has to defile himself for the dead is the undertaker.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> and obligate myself to shave<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">“To shave a <i>nazir</i>” means to pay for the three required sacrifices which enable a <i>nazir</i> to shave his head. It seems that most <i>nezirim</i> were poor and depended on charity to pay for their sacrifices; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 7:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.7.2.7"><i>Berakhot</i> 7:3, Notes 78–89</a>.</i> a <i>nazir</i>,” if another heard him and said: “I also shall be and I obligate myself to shave another <i>nazir</i>,” if they are clever, they will shave one another<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">They will have fulfilled their vows without added expenditure.</i>; otherwise they have to shave other <i>nezirim</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> and obligate myself to shave a <i>nazir</i>,” etc. This “I also”, what do you subsume under it? Does “I also” refer to the entire sentence<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the second person by saying “I also” would accept the obligations of being a <i>nazir</i> and of paying for another person’s sacrifices. If then he adds a vow to pay for another person’s sacrifices, he should be held liable for two sets of sacrifices.</i>, or does “I also” only refer to part of the sentence<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">One has to assume that it refers only to the first obligation pronounced by the first person.</i>? If “I also” refers to the entire sentence, he says “I also am a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">And he has to pay for another person’s sacrifices.</i>.” If “I also” only refers to part of the sentence, he said “I am a <i>nazir</i>.” It was stated in the House of Rebbi: “ ‘I also’ refers to the entire sentence<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 11b" href="/Nazir.11b">Babli, 11b</a>, proves from the wording of the Mishnah that “I also” only refers to the first obligation pronounced by the first person.</i>.” Rebbi Yose said, this implies that if some person said, I am a <i>nazir</i> for 100 days, and another person heard him and said, “I also”; the first one is a <i>nazir</i> for 100 days, the other is a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Since without further specification, “I also” only refers to the obligation of <i>nazir</i>, not to the added term.</i> unless he says, “I am like him, I am the same as he is.” Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: “I am obligated to shave half [a <i>nazir</i>]. Then he said, I am a <i>nazir</i>. If he shaved after 30 days he has fulfilled his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation to pay for a set of sacrifices precedes his own vow of <i>nazir</i>, he can fulfill his obligation by applying the sacrifices to himself.</i>.” Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah implies this: “ ‘I shall be a <i>nazir</i> and obligate myself to shave a <i>nazir</i>,’ if another heard him and said: ‘I also shall be and I obligate myself to shave another <i>nazir</i>,’ if they are clever, they will shave one another.” But not themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vow of <i>nazir</i> implies the obligation of the sacrifices, a later vow to pay for sacrifices establishes a separate obligation; it cannot be used retroactively for his own obligation.</i>. Because he said, “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> and obligate myself to shave a <i>nazir</i>”. But if he said, “I obligate myself to shave half a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.6.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>” and then he said, “I shall be a <i>nazir</i>,” if he shaved himself he has acquitted himself of his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation to pay for a set of sacrifices precedes his own vow of <i>nazir</i>, he can fulfill his obligation by applying the sacrifices to himself.</i>. One understands that the second one can shave the first, but can the first shave the second<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">By the preceding argument, a vow to pay for sacrifices can be used for later vows of <i>nazir</i>, not for earlier ones.</i>? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: This means that a person can take upon himself the sacrifice of a <i>nazir</i> who only in the future will make his vow<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">It really is used in the inverse direction. If somebody makes a vow to pay for sacrifices, it does not matter whether or not the sacrifices were already due at the moment the vow was made. It is only for one’s own sacrifices that the order in which the vows were made does matter.</i>. Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira inferred three [statements]: It implies that if he shaved himself he acquitted himself. It implies that a person obligates himself for another’s <i>nezirut</i> sacrifices of a future vow. It implies that a person chooses another’s <i>nezirut</i> sacrifices without the other’s knowledge. But he cannot dedicate [the animals] without the other’s knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since an animal cannot be brought to the Temple without having been dedicated, and the dedication has to be for a specific obligation, the <i>nazir</i> whose sacrifices are paid for must be informed of the dedication for it to be valid. The same statement is in <i>Pesaḥim</i> 8:1(35d 1.13), but is denied in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 36a" href="/Nedarim.36a">Babli, <i>Nedarim</i> 36a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Mana asked before Rebbi Yudan: If he said “I am obligated for the sacrifices of a <i>nazir</i>,” might he<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The person B whose sacrifices A just has vowed to pay.</i> make the vow of <i>nazir</i> in the future?If he made the vow without explaining<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">A did not put any condition on his vow; when he comes to pay for it, he simply pays for any needy <i>nazir</i> whose sacrifices are due on that day.</i>, he may shave any <i>nazir</i>, whether he already made the vow or whether he would make it in the future. If he explained<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">May he specify that his vow is valid only for a <i>nazir</i> who was not yet a <i>nazir</i> at the time of his vow?</i>? It can be compared to the following, as Rebbi Levi ben Ḥayyata asked: If he wrote to her<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A husband, who normally would have the administration of his wife’s property, writes a disclaimer to his wife in which he renounces all interest in her property. The details of this question are given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.1.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:1, Notes 22–23</a>.</i>, “I shall have nothing to do with the properties which you might inherit in the future.” May a person make a condition on things not yet in existence<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since Talmudic law does not admit futures contracts on produce which does not yet exist, there can be no valid vow for obligations that do not yet exist.</i>?

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am taking upon myself to shave half a <i>nazir</i>,” and his neighbor heard it and said, “I also am taking upon myself to shave half a <i>nazir</i>,” each one of them shaves an entire <i>nazir</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sacrifice of a <i>nazir</i> must either be brought whole or not at all; the vow for half the sacrifices (which would be 1½ animals) is impossible. Since he made the vow, it has to be interpreted as a vow for a whole set of animals.</i>. But the Sages say, each of them shaves half a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The vow simply was for paying half the expenses of one <i>nazir</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I am taking upon myself to shave half a <i>nazir</i>,” etc. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rav Ḥisda both say that they disagree if it was not spelled out. What are we dealing with? If he said, every half head<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">If he vows half of every animal, he has to give three animals since half an animal is no sacrifice.</i>, everybody agrees that he completely shaves a <i>nazir</i>. If he says, half of the obligation, everybody agrees that he shaves half a <i>nazir</i>. But we are dealing with the case that he says: “half”. Rebbi Meïr says, as if he said half a head, but the rabbis said, as if he said, half of the obligation. Rebbi Yudan said, it turns our that leniency is a restriction. In the opinion of Rebbi Meïr who said, as if he had said half a head, he brings one sacrifice since the <i>nazir</i> can shave on one sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.5.1">Mishnah 6:7</a> states that a <i>nazir</i> can shave even if he brings only one sacrifice. (Note that the vow is interpreted as meaning “half a head”, not “every half head”).</i>. In the opinion of the rabbis who said, half of the obligation, he has to pay for a sacrifice and a half, half of the obligation of a person.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if I have a son,” when a son is born to him, he is a <i>nazir</i>; if a daughter, a sexless<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">He has neither penis nor vagina.</i>, or a hermaphrodite<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">He has both penis and vagina. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.6.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 8:6</a>; Notes 224–237; <i>Niddah</i> 1:1, Notes 27,28.</i>, he is not a <i>nazir</i>. If he said, “when I see a child of mine,” even if a daughter, a sexless, or a hermaphrodite were born to him he is a <i>nazir</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if I have a son,” etc. A daughter counts for nothing, a sexless or a hermaphrodite are questionable<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the first part of the Mishnah. If the vow was conditional, a daughter certainly does not count as a son, but sexless or hermaphrodite might be crypto-males. This creates a problem only for R. Simeon who in the Mishnah holds that a vow of <i>nazir</i> whose validity is in doubt must be kept. There is no problem for the majority who hold that a vow of <i>nazir</i> whose validity is in doubt is void. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 13a" href="/Nazir.13a">Babli, 13a</a>, disagrees and accepts the Mishnah as everybody’s opinion.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">For whom he made the vow described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.7.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> had a miscarriage, he is not a <i>nazir</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, he should say: If it was a viable child, I am a <i>nazir</i> as an obligation, if not, I am a <i>nazir</i> voluntarily<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that a vow of <i>nezirut</i> in doubt must be kept.</i>. If afterwards she had a child, he is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since according to the anonymous majority he did not observe <i>nezirut</i> after the stillbirth.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, he should say: If the earlier one was a viable child, I earlier was a <i>nazir</i> as an obligation, and now I am a <i>nazir</i> voluntarily; otherwise, I earlier was a <i>nazir</i> voluntarily, and now I am a <i>nazir</i> as an obligation.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If his wife had a miscarriage,” etc. Should one not be afraid that he might have been viable? Rebbi Joḥanan said, [the Mishnah] is Rebbi Jehudah’s, since Rebbi Jehudah said, any doubt of <i>nezirut</i> is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta 2:8</a>: “ ‘I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if I have a son,’ and there was a son born to him but it is doubtful whether he is viable or not. R. Jehudah frees him since a doubt of <i>nezirut</i> is permitted. R. Simeon obligates him since a doubt of <i>nezirut</i> is forbidden.”</i>. It was said to him that Rebbi Jehudah said so only for the end; but for the start even Rebbi Jehudah will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">That a vow of <i>nazir</i> has to be kept.</i>. What is beginning and what is end? Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, everybody agrees that “I do not know whether I made a vow of <i>nazir</i> or not,” is at the beginning, “whether it was for 30 or 50 days”, is at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since a vow of <i>nazir</i> had to precede the statement of duration.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbu Abun said: Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba and Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac disagreed: Everybody agrees that “I do not know whether I made a vow of <i>nazir</i> or not,” is at the beginning, “whether it was for 30 or 50 days”, is at the end. Where do they disagree? If it was once or twice<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Whether he vowed one or two periods of <i>nezirut</i>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba considered it the end, Rebbi Samuel considered it the beginning. Rebbi Mana: The problem for Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac about one or two times is whether it is the beginning or the end<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, R. Ḥiyya bar Abba had no problem with this case.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am a <i>nazir</i> and a <i>nazir</i> when a son is born to me.” If he started counting for himself when a son was born to him, he finishes his own<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Then he brings his sacrifices and shaves.</i> and then counts for his son. “I am a <i>nazir</i> when a son is born to me, and a <i>nazir</i>.” If he had started counting for himself when a son was born to him he interrupts his own, counts for his son<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Then he brings his sacrifices and shaves.</i>, and then finishes for himself<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is after shaving, he has to complete 30 days.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ “I am a <i>nazir</i> and a <i>nazir</i> when a son is born to me,” etc. Rebbi Yose asked: If he said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> for these 30 days and those 30 days.<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since he vows twice for the same period of time, may he observe his <i>nezirut</i> for 30 days and then bring two sets of sacrifices to fulfill both vows?</i>” Rebbi Ze‘ira said before Rebbi Mana: Is that not the Mishnah? “He interrupts his own, counts for his son, and then finishes for himself.” Not even if his wife is in the process of giving birth<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">If he made any of the vows described in the Mishnah while his wife was in labor, the Mishnah still requires him to first observe <i>nezirut</i> for his son and only then his own even though they might start on the same day.</i>? He said to him, his <i>nezirut</i> is not comparable to his son’s <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the Mishnah, the two periods cannot start together since it is possible that his wife’s labor be prolonged and there is a 50% chance that the child will be a girl.</i>, but to the case that he said, “I am already a <i>nazir</i> and a <i>nazir</i> after twenty days.<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Where he has to start the second period of <i>nezirut</i> not after 20 days but after 30 days and after he acquitted himself of his obligatory sacrifices.</i>” Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina both say: If he finished his <i>nezirut</i>, his son’s <i>nezirut</i> cannot start for him before he was shaving with a sacrifice. About a <i>nazir</i> who became impure, the opinions of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina seem contradictory. There, he says, if a person impure by the impurity of the dead made a vow of <i>nazir</i>, his seventh day is counted<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">If the person was purified by water containing some ashes of the Red Cow on the 3<sup class="footnote-marker">rd</sup> and 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> days of his impurity, he immerses himself in water on the 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day and is pure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>). There is no reason why he cannot start a period of <i>nezirut</i> on that same day. Quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 17a" href="/Nazir.17a">Babli, 17a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 18a" href="/Nazir.18a">18a</a>.</i>. If a <i>nazir</i> becomes impure, his seventh day is not counted<sup>112</sup><i class="footnote">It is a biblical decree that he has to bring a sacrifice on the 8<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day and start counting anew from that day (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:10" href="/Numbers.6.10"><i>Num</i>. 6:10</a>).</i>. In our case<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Of the father expecting a baby boy. His vow is a new one, not a continuation of an old one as in the case of the impure <i>nazir</i>.</i>, is he not like a person impure by the impurity of the dead, who made a vow of <i>nazir</i>? And is not the seventh day counted for a person impure by the impurity of the dead, who made a vow of <i>nazir</i>? Would this obligate him to bring a sacrifice for his impurity? But if a <i>nazir</i> who becomes impure, his seventh day is not counted; does it not obligate him to bring a sacrifice for his impurity? And here, because he is obligated to bring a sacrifice (of impurity)<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The text is corrupt: He is obligated to bring a sacrifice for the end of his first <i>nezirut</i>, but it is not a sacrifice for impurity.</i>, the seventh day is [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">The text is corrupt: The count of the new <i>nezirut</i> cannot start before he has acquitted himself of his prior obligations; therefore the 30<sup>th</sup> day of his <i>nezirut</i>, which corresponds to the 7<sup>th</sup> day of impurity, cannot be counted.</i> counted for him.
Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he polluted himself during his son’s <i>nezirut</i> but was warned because of his own <i>nezirut</i>, he is whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>nazir</i> is forbidden by biblical law to pollute himself with the impurity of the dead (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:6-7" href="/Numbers.6.6-7"><i>Num</i>. 6:6–7</a>), if he pollutes himself intentionally and his criminal intent was ascertained because he was warned by two witnesses not to pollute himself and he did it anyway, he is punished in criminal law. In order to lead to prosecution, the warning must correctly state the law which might be broken.</i>. If he dedicated his sacrifices, they are sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 14a" href="/Nazir.14a">Babli, 14a</a>, in a different context, R. Joḥanan holds that his two <i>neziriot</i> are only one extended vow. There, it is disputed by R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>. One does not consider what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: “His sacrifice to the Eternal for his <i>nezirut</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:21" href="/Numbers.6.21"><i>Num</i>. 6:21</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta 2:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.2.2">Halakhah 3:2</a> (52c 1. 37), <i>Šebuot</i> 4:4 (35c 1. 58) {<i>Num. rabba</i> 10:42}. In the parallel sources, the statement is accepted as practice.</i>” i. e., his vow of <i>nazir</i> should precede his sacrifice, rather than that his sacrifice precede his vow of <i>nazir</i>.
Rebbi Judah asked before Rebbi Yose: Why should his <i>nezirut</i> not precede that of his son<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second part of the Mishnah. If he had started his own <i>nezirut</i> when his son was born, why should he not finish his own and only then start the <i>nezirut</i> honoring his son’s birth?</i>? Did not Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “This animal shall be dedicated as elevation sacrifice after 30 days.” If he sold it within the thirty days, it is sold; its dedication sanctifies<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">If the animal dedicated as one kind of sacrifice after 30 days was dedicated and used for another sacrifice within this period, it is perfectly legal since the prior dedication was inactive in the intervening period. (Quoted in <i>Qiddušin</i> 3:1, 63c 1. 42.)</i>. He said, is that not, if one said “This”. Perhaps if he said: “I have the obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">If a person dedicates an animal saying: “this animal is dedicated”, and anything happens to the animal before it is sacrificed, the owner does not have to look for a replacement. But if he says, “it is my obligation to bring”, he has to bring the dedicated animal and, if anything happens to it, the owner must provide a replacement. Therefore, if the animal was dedicated after 30 days but no longer exists, no replacement is due. But a vow of <i>nazir</i> is personal and has to be dealt with under the rules of personal responsibility.</i>”? But the one who says, “I am”, is like one who says, “I have the obligation.”
Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: “<i>Nazir</i> after twenty days and <i>nazir</i> 100 days from now on.” He counts twenty days, interrupts and counts another 30 days, and counts another 80 to complete his first <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta 2:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 14a" href="/Nazir.14a">Babli 14a</a>. A person makes a vow to be a <i>nazir</i> after 30 days and simultaneously makes a vow immediately to be a <i>nazir</i> for 100 days. Then he starts his immediate <i>nezirut</i> for 20 days, continues to count the 30 days of his other <i>nezirut</i>, brings his sacrifices and shaves, and counts the remaining 80 days of his immediate <i>nezirut</i> and brings the second set of sacrifices. Since the continuation of the interrupted <i>nezirut</i> is for more than 30 days, it is a valid <i>nezirut</i> and he does lose the initial 20 days.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if a son is born<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">An unspecified term, i. e., 30 days.</i> to me and a <i>nazir</i> for 100 days.<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">He starts this <i>nezirut</i> immediately.</i>” If a son is born to him in less than 70 [days], he should not lose anything<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:9:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.9.5">Note 122</a>, he starts his <i>nezirut</i>, after his son’s birth he starts counting 30 days, brings his sacrifices and shaves, and then finishes the count of the days missing for the vow of 100 days.</i>. After 70 [days], he reduces to 70 since no shaving is for less than 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">If less than 30 days were left in his count of 100, he must observe <i>nezirut</i> for 30 days after his celebration for his son.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> ““I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if a son is born to me,” etc. It is obvious that the end of a day is counted as a full [day]<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">If the son was born towards the end of a day, that day counts as day 1 of the son’s <i>nezirut</i> which by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.9.1">Mishnah 9</a> starts automatically at the moment of birth.</i>. Is the start of a day counted as a full day<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">If the son was born during daytime and the father started the day as <i>nazir</i> on his own count, does the day also count as a full day for the father?</i>? Is that not the Mishnah: “after 70 [days], he reduces to 70,” not even a part<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">If part of the day is not counted as a full day then if the son was born on the 71<sup>st</sup> day, no day would be lost. Since the Mishnah says that even in this case, a day is lost, it implies that without this rule the 71<sup>st</sup> day would count for both <i>neziriot</i>. Therefore, the 70<sup>th</sup> day counts for both. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 15a" href="/Nazir.15a">Babli, 15a</a>, this a statement attributed to Rav.</i>? This implies that the start of a day is counted as a full day.
If he was born on the eightieth day, he eliminates ten<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Since only the first 70 days of the father’s first <i>nezirut</i> are counted, the last 10 are disregarded. (A gloss in Tosaphot 13b, s.v. <i>לאחר</i> has a different explanation.)</i>. If he was born on the ninetieth day, he eliminates twenty. If he finished his <i>nezirut</i> and came to complete his son’s <i>nezirut</i> and became impure within the first ten days, he eliminates everything<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">If the son was born on the 90<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day of his <i>nezirut</i> and he starts counting his son’s days, as long as his 100 days are not completed, any impurity of the dead will invalidate all his <i>nezirut</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.4.1">Mishnah 3:4</a>). If the impurity occurs after he has counted 100 days, his days are immunized; the question is only what happens to the days he counts for his son.</i>. Within the last twenty days? Rebbi Abba in the name of Rab and Rebbi Joḥanan both say, he eliminates thirty<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">He has to keep 30 days in purity for his son and afterwards another thirty to finish his <i>nezirut</i> as prescribed in the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Samuel said, he eliminates seven only<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Of his own completed count, only the seven days of impurity of the dead are invalidated, following R. Eliezer (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:3</a>).</i>. Samuel bar Abba asked before Rebbi Yose: Does Rebbi Joḥanan think that eliminating by a shaving knife is identical with substantial eliminating<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>nazir</i> is forbidden to shave. If he shaves anyway, he loses 30 days of his count (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:5</a>). But impurity of his body, called here “substantial”, invalidates everything from the start.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, if Rebbi Joḥanan thought that eliminating by a shaving knife is identical to substantial eliminating, why would he say that he eliminates thirty<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, if the <i>nazir</i> for 100 days becomes impure (being defiled by a corpse) at any time before he has completed his count, he has to start a new count.</i>? Should he not invalidate everything? Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya said before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Explain it if he was born on a day unsuitable to bring a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that a defiled <i>nazir</i> has to start anew if he was defiled before he could have offered his sacrifices. Could one think of a situation where he had finished his count and still could not have offered sacrifices? The answer is negative.</i>. Think of it, if he was born in the night<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">No sacrifice can be offered in the night. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 3:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.3.4.2-3"><i>Megillah</i> 3:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 3:5:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.3.5.2-4">6</a> (73c 1. 8,10), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 20b" href="/Nazir.20b">Babli 20b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 18 7" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_18.7"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 18(7</a>), based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:38" href="/Leviticus.7.38"><i>Lev</i>. 7:38</a>.</i>, is that not unsuitable to bring a sacrifice? It is suitable; the night caused it. Think of it, if he was born on the Sabbath, is that not unsuitable to bring a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The only sacrifices possible on the Sabbath are those which are explicitly commanded for that day.</i>? It is suitable; the Sabbath caused it. If he had finished his <i>nezirut</i> but did not manage to shave when [the son] was born on the Sabbath, is that not unsuitable to bring a sacrifice? It is suitable; the Sabbath caused it. If he had finished his <i>nezirut</i> but did not manage to shave before his son was born<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">And automatically his new <i>nezirut</i> starts on the day of the birth. He now cannot bring the sacrifices for thr prior <i>nezirut</i> since this would involve shaving his hair.</i>, he celebrates one shaving for both. If he had dedicated his sacrifices but did not manage to shave before his son was born. There<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia, they hold that not the dedication but rather the actual Temple ceremony determines the end of his <i>nezirut</i>.</i>, they say that he celebrates one shaving for both. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he shaves and then shaves a second time. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: <sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta 5:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 60b" href="/Nazir.60b">Babli 60b</a>, <i>Sifry Num</i>. 38 (best <i>Sifry</i> text in <i>Yalqut Šim‘ony Num</i>. #709).</i>“They asked<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry</i>, the persons who ask are R. Eleazar ben Šamua‘ and R. Joḥanan the Alexandrian.</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai: Assume that he was both a <i>nazir</i> and a sufferer from scale disease<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">While suffering from the disease, he is impure. The ritual of purification (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14" href="/Leviticus.14"><i>Lev</i>. 14</a>) requires the healed patient to shave all his hair after a preliminary ceremony (14:8), undergo a week of quarantine, shave again, including even his eyebrows (14:9), and offer his sacrifices the next day (14:10). The only impurity forbidden to the <i>nazir</i> is the impurity of corpses; therefore, it is quite possible for a sufferer from scale disease to be a <i>nazir</i> (even though such a person could never shave before he is healed since he cannot enter the Temple precinct.)</i>, may he shave once and have it counted for his <i>nezirut</i> and his scale disease? He said to them: If he shaved<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Probably, the text of Tosephta and <i>Sifry</i> is better: “If each of them shaved …”. In the Babli: “If both of them shaved to grow or both of them to remove.”</i> to remove hair, you would be correct. But the <i>nazir</i> shaves to remove hair whereas the sufferer from scale disease shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">After the preliminary ceremony, he shaves to have his hair grow to be shorn after a week.</i> to have hair grow. They said to him, if it cannot be counted for the days of his completeness<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">The impurity of the sufferer from scale disease varies with his status. It is a minor impurity as long as the diagnosis of his sickness is only tentative (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13" href="/Leviticus.13"><i>Lev</i>. 13, <i>passim</a></i>), it is severe while his diagnosis is complete (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:45" href="/Leviticus.13.45"><i>Lev</i>. 13:45</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:46" href="/Leviticus.13.46">46</a>), and it is minor again during his quarantine in the process of purification. What Yerushalmi, Tosephta, and <i>Sifry</i> call גמרו “his completeness (of diagnosis)”, the Babli calls חֲלוּטוֹ “his absoluteness”.</i>, should it not be counted for the days of his count<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Of his quarantine, after which he has to shave again. (But then he shaves after 7 days, not at least 30 days as required for a <i>nazir</i>.)</i>? Both of them shave to remove hair. (They said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">This must read: He said to them (as in Tosephta and <i>Sifry</i>).</i>. If both of them shaved before the sprinkling of the blood, you would be correct. But the <i>nazir</i> shaves before the sprinkling of the blood and the sufferer from scale disease shaves after the sprinkling of the blood! They said to him, if it cannot be counted for the days of his completeness, should it not be counted for the days of his count? Both of them shave before the sprinkling of the blood!)<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">The passage in parentheses is hopelessly corrupt. It is missing in Tosephta and <i>Sifry</i>. One should read with the Babli: “But the <i>nazir</i> shaves <i>after</i> the sprinkling of the blood and the sufferer from scale disease shaves <i>before</i> the sprinkling of the blood.” The <i>nazir</i> shaves after the sacrifices have been brought (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num</i>. 6:18</a>) while the person healed from scale disease shaves the day before he brings his sacrifices. The problem is that the students declare that both the <i>nazir</i> and the sufferer from scale disease in his preliminary purification shave <i>before</i> the sprinkling of the blood; this seems to presuppose the uncorrected reading in the preceding sentence. The only way to make sense of the statement is also to emend this sentence: “Both of them shave <i>after</i> the sprinkling of the blood,” and to refer to the blood of the bird used in the preliminary ceremony of the sufferer from scale disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:7" href="/Leviticus.14.7"><i>Lev</i>. 14:7</a>).</i> He said to them, if both shaved before they immerse themselves in water, you would be correct. But the <i>nazir</i> shaves before he immerses himself in water and the sufferer from scale disease shaves after he immerses himself in water<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read (with the parallel sources): “But the <i>nazir</i> shaves <i>after</i> he immerses himself in water and the sufferer from scale disease shaves <i>before</i> he immerses himself in water.” The <i>nazir</i> shaves in the Temple, therefore he had to immerse himself in water to be able to enter the Temple. The sufferer from scale disease is required to shave before he immerses himself (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:9" href="/Leviticus.14.9"><i>Lev</i>. 14:9</a>).</i>. They said to him, the matter is settled. It cannot be counted for the days of his completeness, it cannot be counted for the days of his count. It cannot be counted for purification; it cannot be counted for impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read with Tosephta and Sifry: תַּקִּין הַדָּבָר. לֹא עוֹלֶה בִּימֵי גְמָרוֹ עוֹלֶה בִּימֵי סְפִירוֹ. לֹא עוֹלֶה בְטָהֵר עוֹלֶה בְטָמֵא. “It does not count in the days of his completeness; it counts in the days of his count. It does not count if [the <i>nazir</i>] is pure; it counts if he is impure.” The impure <i>nazir</i> shaves on the seventh day of his purification; this may be before he immerses himself. He has to shave before he brings his sacrifice on the eighth day. Therefore, a healed sufferer from scale disease who also is an impure <i>nazir</i> may combine his first shaving for his healing together with the shaving for his impure <i>nezirut</i>.</i>. That is, if he was a <i>nazir</i> and sufferer from scale disease. But if he was a <i>nazir</i> and <i>nazir</i>, he may shave once for both.<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence, which is not in any of the other sources, supports the Babylonians and contradicts R. Joḥanan.</i>” What does Rebbi Joḥanan do with this? He explains that they disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>baraita</i> is declared to be R. Simeon ben Laqish’s personal opinion, it is a minority opinion which does not represent practice.</i>.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody said, “I am a <i>nazir</i>,” he shaves on the 31<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> day, but if he shaved on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, he has fulfilled his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">An unspecified <i>nezirut</i> is 30 days (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.1">Mishnah 1:3</a>), and part of a day is counted as a full day (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.10.2">Chapter 2, Note 127</a>).</i>. “I am a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If he spelled out “30 days”, it has to be interpreted as meaning “30 full days”.</i>,” if he shaved on the 30<sup>th</sup> day, he did not fulfill his obligation.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody said, “I am a <i>nazir</i>,” etc. This supports Bar Qappara<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.2">Chapter 1:3, Note 93</a>.</i>, that if he shaved on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, he did not fulfill his obligation. This supports Rebbi Jonathan<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.2">Chapter 1:3, Note 94</a>.</i>, that if he shaved on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, he did fulfill his obligation. Both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">One anonymous Mishnah cannot represent two contradictory opinions.</i>? It is only one opinion, “this testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>. R. Pappaias testified that a person who vowed two <i>neziriot</i> and shaved for the first on the thirtieth day may shave the second time on the 60<sup>th</sup> day (since the 30<sup>th</sup> day is counted for both; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.1.1">Note 1</a>.) Since the testimony is formulated conditionally, “if he shaved on the 30<sup>th</sup> day”, not as an absolute statement, “he shaves on the 30<sup>th</sup> and 60<sup>th</sup> days”, it follows that shaving on the 30<sup>th</sup> day is irregular. The Mishnah follows neither Bar Qappara nor R. Jonathan but the practice proclaimed by R. Pappaios.</i>”. They knew that it was forbidden to shave on the 30<sup>th</sup>; they thought that if he shaved on the 30<sup>th</sup> day, he did not fulfill his obligation. He came to testify that if he shaved on the 30<sup>th</sup> day, he fulfilled his obligation.
Something happened<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A close relative died. By rabbinic convention, the mourner cannot leave his house for the first seven days after burial; he does not shave for thirty days. R. Immi considered the 30 days’ period of the <i>nazir</i> as a paradigm for the 30 day period of the mourner.</i> to Rebbi Immi and he shaved on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, and something happened to Rebbi Immi and he shaved on the 31<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> day<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">He was inconsistent in his actions.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqa said, Rebbi Immi learned this from the Mishnah, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>: “If somebody vowed two <i>neziriot</i>, he shaves for the first on the 31<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> day, for the second on the 61<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> day<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is from the part of the Mishnah which is not quoted: “But if he shaved for the first on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, he shaves for the second on the 60<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.1.2">Note 6</a>).</i>.” Rebbi Yose said, there when it happened, here from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose criticizes R. Immi. The Mishnah requires the <i>nazir</i> to shave on his 31<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> day; it only legitimizes shaving on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> after the fact. But R. Immi shaved on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> on his own initiative. R. Zeriqa seems to hold that what is acceptable after the fact in biblical rules is permitted from the start in rabbinic usage.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah instructed Rebbi Isaac Aṭoshia, and some say, Rav Ḥiyya bar Rav Isaac Aṭoshia, to shave on the 30<sup>th</sup> day, following the Mishnah<sup>12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mo‘ed qaṭan</i> 3:5. “If somebody buried his relative three days before a holiday, the seven-day rule is waived for him. Eight days, the 30 day rule is waived for him, as they said: Sabbath is counted but does not interrupt, holidays interrupt but do not count.” If somebody was mourning for three days before the holiday, he does not have to continue the intense mourning period after the holiday; he continues with the remainder of the 30-day period. Similarly, if he was keeping at least one day of mourning after the seven-day period, the holiday cancels the remainder of the 30-day mourning period. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 19b" href="/Moed_Katan.19b">Babli, <i>Mo‘ed qaṭan</i> 19b</a>, reduces the 8 day period to 7 since “part of a day is like a whole day” and the 30-day period starts on the seventh.)</i>: “Eight days, the decree of 30 days is waived for him.” The eighth has the same status as the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The argument here goes as follows: If the 8th day of mourning was holiday eve, the mourner can shave in the afternoon in preparation for the holiday. The time elapsed from dawn to the afternoon is counted as a full day for him. Therefore, the person who shaves on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day can nevertheless count the entire 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day as being part of his mourning period.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, there is a difference; there they permitted in order to honor the holiday. You should know this, since Rebbi Ḥelbo bar Ḥuna said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If his eighth day falls on the Sabbath, he shaves Sabbath eve. If you say that they did not permit in order to honor the holiday, then even if his 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day falls on the Sabbath, he should shave Sabbath eve<sup>14</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody allows the mourner to shave on the 29th day. (Quoted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Moed Katan 19b:7:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Moed_Katan.19b.7.1">Tosaphot <i>Mo‘ed qaṭan</i> 19b, <i>s. v</i>. הלכה</a>).</i>. In addition, from what Rebbi Joḥanan had said<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Moed Katan 3:5:2-28" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Moed_Katan.3.5.2-28"><i>Mo‘ed qaṭan</i> 3:5</a> (82b 1. 10), 3:8 (83d 1. 23); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 22b" href="/Nazir.22b">Babli 22b</a>.</i>: “For all deceased<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Except for father and mother. The first sign of mourning required is to tear one’s garment. For the seven-day period, the mourner is required to wear the torn garment. He can stitch together the tear <i>after</i> the end of the seven-day period (after 30 days for father or mother) and invisibly mend it <i>after</i> thirty days (never mending invisibly for father or mother.) Why does one not allow stitching or mending on the last day of a period if “part of the day is counted as a whole day”?</i> he stitches together after seven days and mends after 30.” Why should he not stitch on the seventh day and mend on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, this has been transmitted in this way and that has been transmitted in that way<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Except for father and mother. The first sign of mourning required is to tear one’s garment. For the seven-day period, the mourner is required to wear the torn garment. He can stitch together the tear <i>after</i> the end of the seven-day period (after 30 days for father or mother) and invisibly mend it <i>after</i> thirty days (never mending invisibly for father or mother.) Why does one not allow stitching or mending on the last day of a period if “part of the day is counted as a whole day”?</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody vowed two <i>neziriot</i>, he shaves for the first on the 31<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> day, for the second on the 61<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> day<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases in this Mishnah, “part of a day is counted as an entire day” and the last day of a period is at the same time the first of the next period, whether the period be 30 or 31 days.</i>, but if he shaved for the first on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, he shaves for the second on the 60<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, and if he shaved on the day before the 60<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup><sup>18</sup><i class="footnote">Compare Latin usage to replace 19 by 20-1, etc.</i>, he has fulfilled his obligation. The following testimony did Rebbi Pappaias give<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Any “testimony” mentioned in the Mishnah was given in Yabneh and accepted by the Synhedrion.</i>: if somebody vowed two <i>neziriot</i>, he shaves for the first on the 31<sup>st</sup> day, for the second on the 61<sup>st</sup> day, but if he shaved for the first on the 30<sup>th</sup> day, he shaves for the second on the 60<sup>th</sup>, and if he shaved on the day before the 60<sup>th</sup>, he has fulfilled his obligation since the 30<sup>th</sup> day is counted for him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody vowed two <i>neziriot</i>,” etc. If he finished his first period of <i>nezirut</i> and started to lean<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “to lean on” for “going to start” is found only here.</i> on the second, when they did not find an opening for the first while they found an opening for the second<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>nezirut</i> presupposes a vow, any <i>nezirut</i> can be eliminated by an Elder who finds “an opening” to declare the vow invalid; cf. <i>Nedarim</i>, Introduction, p. 422; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:1:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.1.2-3">Chapter 3:1, Notes 6–9</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.1.2-7">Chapter 9:1</a>.</i>, the second can be used for the first<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If he had sacrifices prepared for the second <i>nezirut</i> but none for the first, those of the second may be used for the first.</i>. Where do we hold? If he said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> twice,” a vow which is partially annulled is totally annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.6.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 9:6</a>. In this case, no sacrifice is due since there is no <i>nezirut</i>; if he wishes, he can shave without formality.</i>. If he said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> for these 30 days and those 30 days,” in this case the second cannot be used for the first<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If he made two separate vows, rather than one vow covering separate periods, the sacrifices are not transferable since they are dedicated for separate vows.</i>. But we must hold that he said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> and <i>nazir</i>.” If he said, “these are for <i>nezirut</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to the vow but not to the particular instance mentioned in the vow. Then the sacrifice can be brought for any obligation implied by the vow.</i>” But if he said, “these are for my <i>nezirut</i>; those are for the other <i>nezirut</i>,” in this case the second cannot be used for the first<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If he made two separate vows, rather than one vow covering separate periods, the sacrifices are not transferable since they are dedicated for separate vows.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, if he finished the first <i>nezirut</i>, as soon as he brought a sacrifice and shaved, the first is credited for the second<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar deals with the opposite case. He made the double vow, finished the first period, brought a sacrifice, and then went to ask an Elder about his vow. If the first <i>nezirut</i> was annulled, he can count the <i>nezirut</i> he kept as second <i>nezirut</i> and be freed from his obligation. This is essentially stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta 2:15</a> (quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 17b" href="/Nedarim.17b">Babli, <i>Nedarim</i> 17b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 27b" href="/Shevuot.27b"><i>Šebuot</i> 27b</a>): “If somebody vowed two <i>neziriot</i>, finished the first one but did not bring his sacrifices, went to an Elder to ask to annul his first [<i>nezirut</i>], the second <i>nezirut</i> is fulfilled by the first.”</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa commanded the colleagues: If you hear anything formulated by Rebbi Eleazar, you should know that Rebbi Joḥanan disagrees<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Eleazar was R. Joḥanan’s student, if he felt impelled to formulate a statement by himself, not referring to R. Joḥanan, it is safe to assume that R. Joḥanan disagreed.</i>: not unless he brings all his sacrifices, following the rabbis; but following Rebbi Simeon even if he brings only one sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan does not disagree with the main thrust of R. Eleazar’s statement, that the <i>nezirut</i> can be counted as the one covered by his vow, but he objects to the formulation referring to “a sacrifice”, which implies that a <i>nazir</i> can shave and drink wine after one sacrifice. This is R. Simeon’s opinion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.9.1">Mishnah 6:11</a>. R. Joḥanan insists that all three sacrifices be brought in order to be valid; this is the opinion of the anonymous majority which in R. Joḥanan’s opinion always defines practice (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:11:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.11.8"><i>Yebamot</i> 4:11, Note 177</a>).</i>. If he dedicated both together, he has only one in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a different situation, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta 2:15</a>: “If somebody vowed two <i>neziriot</i>, finished both of them, and brought the sacrifices for both of them together, he has only one in his hand.” Since the sacrifices of a <i>nazir</i> presuppose a period of at least 30 days for the growth of his hair, it is impossible for one person to bring sacrifices for multiple periods of <i>nezirut</i> together. He has to be <i>nazir</i> for another 30 days and bring another set of sacrifices.</i>. If he dedicated each of them separately or<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This must read “and”; then the text becomes the last sentence in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta 2:15</a>.</i> brought each of them for the other, he did not acquit himself [of his obligation]. This means that as far as holiness goes, they became holy<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If one of the <i>neziriot</i> was dissolved by an Elder, why should the sacrifice attached to it be holy? If the vow was in error, the dedication is in error.</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.9.3">Chapter 2:9, Note 118</a>.</i>: “His sacrifice to the Eternal for his <i>nezirut</i>,” i. e., that his vow of <i>nazir</i> should precede his sacrifice, rather than that his sacrifice precede his vow of <i>nazir</i>. It is different, because he still is in <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Ḥiyya is not applicable to our case. It is clear that a person cannot dedicate animals to satisfy a future vow of <i>nazir</i> since that would be “stipulating about non-existence” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.5.3">Chapter 2:5, Note 89</a>). But in the present case, the vow exists.</i>. One understands the second for the first<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The case treated at the start of the Halakhah.</i>. The first for the second<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The case discussed by R. Eleazar. Does the argument of R. Ḥiyya not apply in this case, when the dedication of the sacrifice for the first period preceded the start of the second.</i>? Rebbi Jehudah said, this supports Rebbi Eleazar. But did not Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa command the colleagues: If you hear anything formulated by Rebbi Eleazar, you should know that Rebbi Joḥanan disagrees; not unless he brings all his sacrifices. They wanted to say, following the rabbis. But here, we hold with Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since the disagreement between R. Eleazar and R. Joḥanan only refers to the disagreement between R. Simeon and the rabbis, it is clear that R. Eleazar follows R. Simeon and the second period substituted for the first both in time and in sacrifices.</i>. Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rebbi Phineas: Explain it for his <i>nezirut</i> and the <i>nezirut</i> he pledged for his son. In that, the second can be used for the first<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He rejects the previous explanation and restricts the possibility of substituting the second period for the first to the case that both are occasioned by the birth of a son, one period for himself and one for his son’s birth. In this case, it was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.9.3">Chapter 2:9 (Note 118</a>) that R. Ḥiyya’s argument is disregarded; both <i>neziriot</i> combined constitute one vow.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, did we not argue about one who said, “I am a <i>nazir</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">It had been decided that the substitution of sacrifices was possible only for the person who said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> and <i>nazir</i>.” It would be a reasonable interpretation to say that the second “and <i>nazir</i>” was said for emphasis rather than duplication. Therefore, the second <i>nezirut</i> is a rabbinic obligation, not an original vow, and there should be no discussion of legalistic niceties in this case.</i>? He really should be a <i>nazir</i> only once. You were severe with him and obligated him to be a <i>nazir</i> twice. Is it not enough that you were severe with him and obligated him to be a <i>nazir</i> twice, that now you say, he has nothing in his hands?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says, “I am a <i>nazir</i>” and became impure on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Before he offered his sacrifices.</i>, he invalidated everything<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">He has to wait 7 days to cleanse himself from the impurity of the dead, shave, bring the sacrifices prescribed for the impure <i>nazir</i>, and be a <i>nazir</i> another 30 days in purity.</i>; Rebbi Eliezer says, he invalidated only seven<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could have brought the sacrifices on the 30th day, he has to wait 7 days to cleanse himself from the impurity of the dead, shave, bring the sacrifices prescribed for the impure <i>nazir</i>, wait another 7 days to have some hair to shave, and then brings his 3 sacrifices and shaves in purity.</i>. “I am a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days,” if he became impure on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, he invalidated everything<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer agrees that he has to start anew since he must wait until the 31<sup>st</sup> day to bring his sacrifices.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says, ‘I am a <i>nazir</i>’,” etc. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Eliezer learned from the sufferer from skin disease, for whom we find seven days between shaving and shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.10.3">Chapter 2:10, Note 143</a>.</i>. Why does he not learn from the impure <i>nazir</i>? The sufferer from skin disease shaves repeatedly; the impure <i>nazir</i> does not shave repeatedly. The rabbis from Caesarea say, they disagree explicitly: Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Eliezer learned from the sufferer from skin disease; Rebbi Eleazar says, Rebbi Eliezer learned from the impure <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Who shaves after 7 days, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num</i>. 6:9</a>.</i>. What difference does it make for Rebbi Eliezer whether the vow for <i>nazir</i> was implicit rather than explicit<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Whether he says “I am a <i>nazir</i>” and it is understood that he will be a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days, or he says “I am a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days”?</i>? If the vow for <i>nazir</i> was implicit, he does not invalidate if he tears his hair out<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num</i>. 6:5</a> spells out first a prohibition, “a shaving knife shall not touch his head”, followed by a positive commandment “he shall let the hair of his head grow wildly.” If the order had been inverted, it would have been clear that only shaving with a knife was forbidden. Now that the commandment of letting the hair grow is separated from the prohibition of using a knife, the majority opinion (<i>Sifry Num</i>. 25; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta 4:3</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:2:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.2.2-5">Halakhah 6:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 39b" href="/Nazir.39b">Babli 39b</a>) holds that removing any hair is forbidden and the simultaneous removal of any two hairs during the period of <i>nezirut</i> invalidates the <i>nezirut</i> and requires a fresh start. Only on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, tearing out a hair has no consequences since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num</i>. 6:5</a> also states: “until the days are completed he shall be holy,” and on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day of the implicit vow they are completed.</i>, and his seventh day<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If he became impure by the impurity of the dead, he has to shave on the 7th day itself.</i> is counted for him; if the vow for <i>nazir</i> was explicit<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Then his days were not yet completed when he became impure.</i>, he invalidates if he tears his hair out and his seventh day is not counted for him.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am a <i>nazir</i> for 100 days,” if he became impure on day 100 he invalidated everything but Rebbi Eliezer said, he invalidated only 30<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">And the implicit vow of a <i>nazir</i> run for 30 days.</i>. If he became impure on day 101, he invalidated 30; Rebbi Eliezer said, he invalidated only seven<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could have brought the sacrifices on the 30th day, he has to wait 7 days to cleanse himself from the impurity of the dead, shave, bring the sacrifices prescribed for the impure <i>nazir</i>, wait another 7 days to have some hair to shave, and then brings his 3 sacrifices and shaves in purity.</i>.
