<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> ““I am a <i>nazir</i> for 100 days,” etc. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The reason of Rebbi Eliezer: “This is the teaching for the <i>nazir</i> on the day of his fulfilling;<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Then his days were not yet completed when he became impure.</i>” if he becomes impure on the day of his fulfilling, one gives him the teaching for the <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">And the implicit vow of a <i>nazir</i> run for 30 days.</i>. Samuel bar Abba asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: If he becomes impure in those days<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If he became impure on the last day of his <i>nezirut</i>; according to R. Eliezer, how many days does he lose if he becomes impure again while keeping the 30 days of <i>nezirut</i>.</i>, what is their status, to be given the teaching for the <i>nazir</i>? Rebbi Shammai said, he is obligated by the teaching for the <i>nazir</i> if he becomes impure on the day of his fulfilling, and for one who becomes impure on the day of his fulfilling the seventh day is not counted<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The problem arises only if he becomes impure on the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day since before it is obvious that he has to start anew. If he is considered to have finished his <i>nezirut</i>, then the 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day of purification can also be the day he brings his sacrifices since “part of the day is counted as an entire day” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.10.2">Halakhah 2:10, Note 129</a>). But if he is under the rules of the <i>nazir</i> who shaves on the 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> but brings his sacrifice only on the 8<sup>th</sup> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num</i>. 6:9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:10" href="/Numbers.6.10">10</a>), the 7<sup>th</sup> day obviously cannot be the day on which he can conclude his <i>nezirut</i>.</i>. Rebbi Mana asked: If he becomes impure at fulfilling, why should he invalidate 30, should he not invalidate only seven<sup>52</sup><i class="footnote">Since the entire argument follows R. Eliezer, why does he make a difference between the cases of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.3.1">Mishnaiot 3</a> and 4?</i>? But if somebody became impure after fulfilling<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">If he becomes impure on day 101, R. Eliezer will apply his ruling of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>, the seventh day is counted for him.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody made a vow of <i>nazir</i> while he was in a cemetery<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>nazir</i> may not be in a cemetery, in one opinion the vow cannot be activated until he leaves the cemetery; in the other opinion the vow is activated but the days cannot be counted since the <i>nazir</i> is not pure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 16b" href="/Nazir.16b">Babli 16b</a>).</i>, even if he stayed there for thirty days, they are not counted and he does not bring a sacrifice for impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The vow is activated the moment he leaves the cemetery. While the <i>nazir</i> is forbidden to defile himself by the impurity of the dead, it is not forbidden to vow to be a <i>nazir</i> while one is impure. He has to untergo the seven-day purification ritual; these days are counted as regular days of <i>nezirut</i>.</i>. If he left and re-entered, they are counted and he has to bring a sacrifice for impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The special sacrifices prescribed for the <i>nazir</i> who became impure, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:10-11" href="/Numbers.6.10-11"><i>Num</i>. 6:10–11</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer said, not on that day, since it is said: “The earlier days fall away<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num</i>. 6:12</a>. Since a plural indicates at least 2, no sacrifice is due unless the person has been a <i>nazir</i> for at least 2 days.</i>,” until he has earlier days.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody made a vow of <i>nazir</i> while he was in a cemetery,” etc. If he made the vow while he was among grave sites<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">He is not in an open cemetery but in a graveyard consisting of burial caves. A cave forms a “tent”; the impurity of the dead is transmitted by the tent even without any touching (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:14" href="/Numbers.19.14"><i>Num</i>. 19:14</a>). But in the open space before the caves, no impurity is transmitted; it is possible there to start a vow of <i>nazir</i>. It is assumed that the person making the vow participated in a burial and is impure at the moment of the vow.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan said, one warns him about wine and shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to require that he be pure from the moment of the vow, since he is impure. R. Joḥanan holds that in all other respects the vow is valid immediately.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, since one cannot warn him because of impurity, one does not warn him about wine and shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">For him, the vow is suspended until the <i>nazir</i> has undergone the ritual of purification; once he is pure he can be punished for drinking wine or shaving.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Joḥanan seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.4.2">Halakhah 6:4</a>. The text there consists simply of a referral to the present Halakhah. <i>A nazir</i> who does not observe the rules of <i>nezirut</i> has to be warned and can be punished for every infringement. There, R. Joḥanan requires that even a <i>nazir</i> who comes to drink wine has to be warned about wine, impurity, and shaving. Then it should be impossible to punish a <i>nazir</i> for infringing the rules of wine and shaving, if he is not punishable for impurity.</i>, he says, one warns him about wine, impurity, and shaving. And here, he says so? The rabbis from Caesarea: They disagree about the whole, for Rebbi Joḥanan said, one warns him about wine and shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">It is understood: because from the start one also warns him about impurity.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, since one cannot warn him because of impurity, one does not warn him either about wine or about shaving.
If he is still there<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the Mishnah. What is the situation of the person who vowed in the cemetery to be a <i>nazir</i>.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan said, one warns him about everything for every possible leaving,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan holds that the vow becomes effective the moment it is uttered. Then the <i>nazir</i> is informed that he has to leave the cemetery immediately (and refrain from wine and shaving). If he does not obey, he can be repeatedly warned and the disregard of every warning is a new, punishable offence.</i> and he is whipped. Rebbi Eleazar said, he does not accept [warning] unless he leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the vow becomes effective only when the <i>nazir</i> leaves the cemetery. Then also the warning becomes relevant for him and he can be punished if he returns to the cemetery.</i> and returns. Rebbi Abba said: So did Rebbi Joḥanan answer Rebbi Eleazar: Is it not written, “he shall not come” and “he may not defile himself”<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">If <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:6" href="/Numbers.6.6"><i>Num</i>. 6:6</a>, there is a general prohibition, “to any dead person he shall not come.” In v. 7, there is a particular prohibition; for close relatives “he may not be defiled.” R. Joḥanan interprets this to mean: even in a case where he does not defile himself, because he was defiled before he made the vow, he violates the separate prohibition of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:6" href="/Numbers.6.6">v. 6</a>.</i>? He said to him, if they warned him because of “he shall not come”, he is whipped; because of “he shall not defile himself” he is not whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the verses as they are written. The <i>nazir</i> can be warned, and is whipped, for an active <i>coming</i> to corpses. But nobody can be whipped for a prohibition formulated in the passive voice.</i>. Rebbi Hila said, Rebbi Joḥanan learned from prostrating, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 2:3. A person who comes to the Temple precinct and belatedly remembers that he is impure, has to leave immediately. If he tarries long enough for an act of prostration, he is punished.</i>: “If he prostrated himself or stayed there long enough to prostrate himself.” Rebbi Mattaniah said, we thought that was where they do disagree? About lashes, but not about a sacrifice. Since Rebbi Hila said, Rebbi Joḥanan learned from prostrating<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Where the main thrust of the entire Chapter in the Mishnah is the obligation to bring a sacrifice to purify himself from the inadvertent sin.</i>, that means that lashes and sacrifices are one and the same. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “A <i>nazir</i> who drank wine the entire day is guilty only once.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:4</a>. This presupposes that he was warned only once.</i>” He explains it, that his throat was never empty<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>nazir</i> actually never stopped drinking the entire day, he could not have been warned more than once. The Mishnah is irrelevant for the statement that separate warnings imply separate punishments.</i>. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “If he was defiling himself for the dead the entire day, he is guilty only once<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:4</a>. This presupposes that he was warned only once.</i>.” He explains it about one who waits before every leaving, who is whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">He explains the Mishnah, if there was only one warning. But if he was warned repeatedly, each action represents a new offense. (Whether tarrying plays a role in this case remains an open question in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 17a" href="/Shevuot.17a">Babli, <i>Šebuot</i> 17a</a>.)</i>. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: If a Cohen was standing in a cemetery<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Legitimately, when burying a close relative.</i> and they were handing another corpse to him, could he accept? The verse says, “the husband shall be defiled for his family<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:4" href="/Leviticus.21.4"><i>Lev</i>. 21:4</a>. The verse really reads: “The husband shall not be defiled for his family, to be profaned,” meaning that the Cohen cannot defile himself for a wife he was forbidden to marry. This implies that he can be defiled only for the benefit of his legal family.</i>.” If he accepted it, I could think that he was guilty. The verse says, “to be profaned”. One who adds impurity to the impurity; that excludes him who does not add impurity to his impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If he already is impure, touching another corpse does not change his status. (In the Babli, the Babylonian authorities disagree, 42b.)</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, Rebbi Neḥemiah said, “to be profaned”, that excludes him who does not add impurity to his impurity, lest he say, because I became defiled for my father I may go and collect the bones of X. “To be profaned”, at the time of death; Rebbi said, also “in their death.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:7" href="/Numbers.6.7"><i>Num</i>. 6:7</a> prohibits the <i>nazir</i> from being defiled for his close relatives “in their death”, meaning that he does not have to leave the house when they lie dying, but only after they are dead. The same <i>baraita</i> is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 43a" href="/Nazir.43a">Babli, 43a</a>.</i>” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The authorities disagreeing with Rebbi require the <i>nazir</i> to leave the house when they lie dying.</i>. It follows that Rebbi Simeon bar Abba follows Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. When Simeon bar Abba was dying, he said, this should be taken out here, that should be taken out there<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid that vessels become impure at his death.</i>.
If he left and re-entered<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He left the cemetery and then re-entered as <i>nazir</i>.</i>, Rebbi Ṭarphon frees him from prosecution, Rebbi Aqiba declares him guilty. Rebbi Ṭarphon said to him, what did this one add to his desecration<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since he already was defiled when he made his vow, how can he be prosecuted for defiling himself?</i>? Rebbi Aqiba said, as long as he was there, he was defiling himself by the impurity of seven days<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">As long as he was in contact with a corpse or forming a “tent” over it and he touched another person, that person becomes impure and has to undergo the 7 day ritual.</i>. When he left, he was defiling himself by the impurity of evening<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If one who is impure by the impurity of the dead touches another person, that person becomes impure in a derivative way. He has to immerse himself in water and becomes pure at sundown.</i>. When he re-entered, defiling himself by the impurity of (evening)<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This is clearly a scribal (corrector’s) error and must read: “the impurity of 7 days.” In the cemetery, he at least acquires the capability of transmitting the impurity of 7 days. Therefore, his status of impurity has changed; he can be prosecuted.</i>. Rebbi Ṭarphon told him, Aqiba! Any who leaves you is as if he left his life.
Rav said, when he has left, he counts his <i>nezirut</i> in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The person who vowed in the cemetery to be a <i>nazir</i> and who will be impure for the next 7 days does nevertheless count his valid days of <i>nezirut</i> from the moment he leaves the cemetery.</i>. If he entered again on his seventh day, he brings a sacrifice of impurity for that day; Rebbi Eliezer said, not for that day<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If he entered the cemetery again on the day he regained his purity, he is considered a <i>nazir</i> who became impure on the first day of his <i>nezirut</i>, obligated for a sacrifice according to the rabbis but not R. Eliezer.</i>. Cahana asked before Rav: Does he not need the sprinkling of the third and seventh<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The purification rite prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>: Being sprinkled with water containing some of the ashes of the Red Cow on the 3<sup class="footnote-marker">rd</sup> and 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day, and immersing himself on the 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>. He becomes pure only after immersion.</i>? He answered him, the Torah called “pure” the one who leaves the grave: “After his purity, seven days shall be counted for him.<sup>87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 44:26" href="/Ezekiel.44.26"><i>Ez</i>. 44:26</a>; one would have expected: “Seven days shall be counted until his purity.” Even though this is a verse by a prophet and therefore without legal standing, it can be applied to show usage.</i>” Samuel said, after he left, sprinkled, and repeated, immersed himself, and entered again on that day, he brings a sacrifice of impurity for that day; Rebbi Eliezer said, not for that day<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel disagrees with Rav; the rules of purity start only on day seven, after the completion of the purification. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 19a" href="/Nazir.19a">Babli, 19a</a>, Rav agrees with Samuel.</i>.
Ulla bar Ismael said, what is his problem<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to the statement of R. Eliezer in the Mishnah. R. Eliezer requires at least two days of <i>nezirut</i> only from a <i>nazir</i> who made his vow in impurity. If the vow was made in purity, he agrees that even if he becomes impure on the first day, he is required to bring a sacrifice.</i>? Of an impure <i>nazir</i>. But for a pure <i>nazir</i>, even Rebbi Eliezer agrees if he has nothing to omit. Samuel bar Abba asked: May one day of his <i>nezirut</i> and one day of <i>nezirut</i> for his son be combined<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In the case described in Mishnah 3:9: He started his own <i>nezirut</i>; at the son’s birth his count is interrupted automatically and his son’s begins.</i>? Where do we hold? If his son was born today and tomorrow he enters, he has two days for his<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This must read: His son’s. The remainder of the day of birth and the start of the next day are counted as two days; if he becomes impure he has to bring a sacrifice even if we reject the interpretation of Ulla bar Ismael.</i> <i>nezirut</i>. If his son was born the next day and he enters the next day, he has two days for his son’s<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">This must read: His own.</i> <i>nezirut</i>! Rebbi Mana said, explain it at twilight<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Then the entire first day is the father’s only.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, even if you say in the middle of the day, did we not intend to say that the start of the day is counted for him, the end of the day is counted for his son<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Halakhah 3:10, the first part of the day of birth is counted as a full day for the father’s <i>nezirut</i>; the second part, after the birth, is counted as a full day for the <i>nezirut</i> on account of the son. It is not unreasonable to expect that for R. Eliezer the day also is counted as two.</i>?
Rebbi Yose said, what is his problem<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to the statement of R. Eliezer in the Mishnah. R. Eliezer requires at least two days of <i>nezirut</i> only from a <i>nazir</i> who made his vow in impurity. If the vow was made in purity, he agrees that even if he becomes impure on the first day, he is required to bring a sacrifice.</i>? Of a pure <i>nazir</i>. But for an impure <i>nazir</i>, it is obvious to him that they cannot<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">From R. Mana’s argument, it seems that one has to read שֶּׁהֵן “they are” instead of שֶׁאֵין “they cannot”. If the Italian scribe wrote from dictation, he would not hear the difference between the two words.</i> be combined. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose: Is that not an argument of “so much more”? Since for days which are counted neither for his nor for his son’s <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The days of impurity.</i>, you say that they are combined, days which are counted for his and for his son’s <i>nezirut</i>, it would only be logical that they should be combined! Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The reading “R. Mana” should be confirmed as <i>lectio difficilior</i>. It is not unique that a person who raises a difficulty should answer it himself.</i> said, there<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If he is impure, he cannot be warned not to become impure. It does not matter in which <i>nezirut</i> he is at the moment.</i> he is not in a state to receive a warning, but here<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">If he is pure, the person who warns him not to become impure must deliver separate warnings depending on him being a <i>nazir</i> for himself or for his son. Therefore it is not automatic that the days may be combined.</i> he is in a state to receive a warning. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: Since you say there about Rebbi Eliezer, at the start only if he has something to omit; at the end even if he has nothing to omit<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Num</i>. 30 on <i>Num</i>. 6:12: “The earlier days fall away,” can be said only if there are later days. If he made a vow to be a <i>nazir</i> for 100 days and became impure on day 101, one would expect R. Eliezer to rule that no sacrifice for impurity is due.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira said before Rebbi Mana, is that not a Mishnah? “If he became impure on day 101, he invalidated 30; Rebbi Eliezer said, he invalidated only seven<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since he has to keep another 7 days even for R. Eliezer, R. Abun bar Ḥiyya’s question should become moot.</i>.” He said, we hear that he invalidates; did we hear that he brings a sacrifice?

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody vowed<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Somewhere in the diaspora. Then he should have come to the Land immediately since outside he is automatically impure in the impurity of "the land of the Gentiles" which has aspects of the impurity of corpses.</i> a lengthy <i>nezirut</i>, finished his <i>nezirut</i>, and then came to the Land, the House of Shammai say, he is a <i>nazir</i> for 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The minimum needed for <i>nezirut</i> in genuine purity which permits the <i>nazir</i> to offer his sacrifices.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, he is a <i>nazir</i> from the start. It happened to Queen Helena<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Of Adiabene.</i> that her son went to war and she said, if my son returns from the war, I shall be a <i>nezirah</i> for seven years. Her son returned from war and she was a <i>nezirah</i> for seven years. At the end of seven years, she came to the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">According to Josephus, she arrived about 43 C.E.</i>, and the House of Hillel instructed her to be a <i>nezirah</i> for another seven years. At the end of seven years she became impure and was <i>nezirah</i> for a total of 21 years. Rebbi Jehudah said, she was a <i>nezirah</i> only for fourteen years<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Ilai, R. Jehudah’s father, was a student of R. Eliezer who followed the House of Shammai, R. Jehudah tells us that Helene followed the House of Shammai, became impure at the end of 30 days, and had to restart her original <i>nezirut</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody vowed,” etc. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph and Rebbi Joḥanan disagreed. One said, Rebbi Jehudah follows the House of Shammai. But the other said, Rebbi Jehudah thinks that she never became impure. If you say, Rebbi Jehudah follows the House of Shammai, should he not have stated 30 days and forteen years? One does not state months while reporting years<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 20a" href="/Nazir.20a">Babli disagrees, 20a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If two groups of witnesses were testifying against a person, one group say that he vowed <i>nazir</i> two times, the others say that he vowed <i>nazir</i> five times. The House of Shammai say, the testimony is split<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Following the rules of criminal procedure by which contradictory testimony has to be disregared.</i> and there is no <i>nezirut</i> here. But the House of Hillel say, five contains two<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Following the rules of civil procedure. If one group testifies that A owes 500 while the other group testifies that he owes 200, he has to pay 200. An identical Mishnah is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 4:11" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.4.11"><i>Idiut</i> 4:11</a>.</i>; he should be a <i>nazir</i> twice.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If two groups of witnesses,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels are in <i>Yebamot</i> 15:5 (<b>י</b>), Notes 115–134, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 5:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.5.2.2-4"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 5:2</a> (<b>ס</b>).</i> Rav said, they differ in the overall testimony. But in detail, everybody agrees that five contains two, and that he has to be a <i>nazir</i> for two periods. Rebbi Joḥanan said, they differ in counting. But in an overall testimony, everybody agrees that the testimonies contradict one another and there is no <i>nezirut</i>. What is overall and what is counting? Overall, this one says two, the other one says five. Counting, this one says one, two, the other one says three, four, five. Rav said, if testimony was contradictory in its essence, the testimony is not void. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if testimony was contradictory in itself, the testimony is void. In the opinion of everybody, if testimony was contradictory in some aspects that belong after the fact, the testimony is not void. Rebbi Joḥanan is consistent in what he said, since Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya, said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, if it was agreed that he counted but one [witness] said, he counted from a wallet and the other said, he counted from a bundle, that contradicts the essence of the testimony, and Rav will agree that the testimony is void. Where do they disagree? If there were two groups of witnesses, these say he counted from a wallet and the others say he counted from a bundle. That contradicts the essence of the testimony, the testimony is void, but according to Rav, the testimony is not void. If one [witness] said, he killed him with a mace, the other [witness] said, he killed him with a sword, that contradicts the essence of the testimony; the testimony is void and Rav will agree that the testimony is void. Where do they disagree? If there were two groups of witnesses, these say he killed him with a mace and the others say, he killed him with a sword. That contradicts the essence of the testimony; the testimony is void, but according to Rav, the testimony is not void. If these say, he ran away to the South and those say, he ran away to the North, everybody agrees that the testimony was contradictory in some aspects that refer [to]<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Read: “after”; cf. the variant readings.</i> the facts, the testimony is not void. The strength of Rav comes from the following: “Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon say, since both agree that he is not alive they can remarry.” He did nor hear that Rebbi Eleazar said, Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Read: “R. Simeon and R. Jehudah”; cf. the variant readings.</i> concedes in the case of witnesses. What is the difference between witnesses and the co-wife? They do not consider the co-wife’s words compared to that of her companion. A Mishnah disagrees with Rav: “Both in investigations and in cross examinations, if they contradict one another their testimony is void.” Rebbi Mana said, Rav will explain that as referring to single witness against single witness. Rebbi Abun said, even if you say groups and groups. There is a difference in criminal cases, as it is written: “Justice, justice you shall pursue”.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">An alternative text of the Mishnah. In Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 20a" href="/Nazir.20a">Babli 20a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta 3:1</a>): “R. Ismael, son of R. Joḥanan ben Baroqa, says, the Houses of Shammai and Hillel do not disagree about …” The text shows that this is the correct version.</i>: “Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Aqiba do not disagree about a person about whom two groups of witnesses testify, that he should be <i>nazir</i> according to the minimal testimony. Where do they disagree? About two witnesses, where the House of Shammai say, the testimony is split<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Following the rules of criminal procedure by which contradictory testimony has to be disregared.</i> and there is no <i>nezirut</i>, but the House of Hillel say, five contains two and he shall be a <i>nazir</i> twice.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Following the rules of civil procedure. If one group testifies that A owes 500 while the other group testifies that he owes 200, he has to pay 200. An identical Mishnah is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 4:11" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.4.11"><i>Idiut</i> 4:11</a>.</i>”

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody said “I am a <i>nazir</i>” and another person heard it and said, “and so am I”, “and so am I”, “and so am I”,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A<sub>1</sub> declares to be a <i>nazir</i>, A<sub>2</sub> hears A<sub>1</sub> and says “so am I”, A<sub>3</sub> hears A<sub>2</sub> and says “so am I”, A<sub>4</sub> hears A<sub>3</sub> and says “so am I”.</i> all of them are <i>nezirim</i>. If the first one was permitted, all are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If the first went to an Elder who dissolved his vow, the others did refer to a non-existent vow; their vows are non-existent. Cf. Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 9:7.</i>; if the last was permitted, the last is permitted and all others forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In general, if A<sub>i</sub> had his vow dissolved, automatically all vows of persons A<sub>j</sub>, j &gt; i, are dissolved.</i>. “I am a <i>nazir</i>” and another person heard it and said, “my mouth is as his mouth and my hair as his hair,” he is a <i>nazir</i>. “I am a <i>nazir</i>” and his wife heard it and said, “and so am I”, he can dissolve hers<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the initiative is his, it does not limit his power to dissolve his wife’s vows (cf. <i>Nedarim</i> Chapter 10).</i> but his vow remains. “I am a <i>nezirah</i>” and her husband heard it and said, “and so am I”, he cannot dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody can dissolve his own vow. If he could dissolve his wife’s vow, his own would be dissolved, as explained in the second sentence of this Mishnah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody said ‘I am a <i>nazir</i>’ and another person heard it and said, ‘and so am I’,” etc. So is the Mishnah: “and so am I”, “and so am I”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This version has only two people responding, with most of the Babli and some Mishnah mss. The paragraph is discussed in detail by J. N. Epstein, <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>מבוא לנוסח המשנה, p. 477–479.</i>. Who is the Tanna of conjunctions? Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that there is a connection between the statements only if they are formulated as conjunctions; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.2">Chapter 1:2, Note 40</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.6.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 9:7, Notes 85–101</a>.</i>. But following Rebbi Meïr, “I am,” “I am”. Does the Mishnah require that all of them follow immediately after the speaking of the first<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If the number of additional <i>nezirim</i> is reduced to 2, is that because both A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>3</sub> refer to the original vow, not that A<sub>3</sub> refers to that of A<sub>2</sub>? In that case, there would not be time for three additional people to express their vow if the rule at the end of the paragraph be followed.</i>? It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The same <i>baraita</i> is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 21a" href="/Nazir.21a">Babli, 21a</a>, for the same conclusion.</i>: If the middle one was permitted, all following him are permitted, all preceding him are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is only possible if A<sub>i+1</sub> refers to A<sub>i</sub>’s vow, not to A<sub>1</sub>’s. Therefore, the original text of the Mishnah is correct and essential.</i>. The Mishnah applies even if not all of them follow immediately after the speaking of the first, but each one follows immediately after the speaking of the preceding person<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This is explicit in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).3.2">Tosephta 3:2</a>: “If somebody said ‘I am a <i>nazir</i>’ and another said ‘so am I’ but not immediately after him, the first is forbidden but the second is permitted.”</i>. What means “to follow immediately after the speaking”? <sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.1.15"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:1 (Notes 50–52</a>), <i>Moʻed qaṭan</i> 3:7 (83c 1. 37). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 20b" href="/Nazir.20b">Babli 20b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 6a" href="/Makkot.6a"><i>Makkot</i> 6a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 73b" href="/Bava_Kamma.73b"><i>Baba qama</i> 73b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 32a" href="/Shevuot.32a"><i>Šebuot</i> 32a</a>. In the Babli, the longer version has an additional word: “peace upon you, my teacher and master.”</i> Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The time needed for greeting between two people. Abba bar bar Ḥana in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The time needed for greeting between teacher and student, that he say to him, “peace upon you, my master.”
If one said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> for 100 days, and another heard it and said, “so am I for 100 days”, and repeated<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear who is speaking, whether the second is making an additional vow or the first is repeating after the second. In the first case, if the first had his vow permitted, the second was not permitted; the “main vow”, his hitching on to the first person’s, becomes an accessory to the second vow which needs separate permission. In the second case, the first vow of the first person (the “main”) cannot become permitted unless the Elder also permits the secondary vow (the “accessory”.)</i> and said, “so am I”, the main [statement] becomes an accessory. If one said, “I am a <i>nazir</i>;” somebody said “and I” following immediately the first, another heard it and said, “so am I” following immediately the second. If the first became permitted, so does the second<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">And the third.</i>. If the second became permitted, the third was not permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna of this <i>baraita</i> disagrees with the argument of the preceding section and holds that, since the vow of the second is predicated on the first, the third also depends on the first but not on the third. Therefore, if the first vow is annulled, all are annulled; if any other vow is annulled, that alone is annulled but nothing else.</i>. If one said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> twice,” and two people heard it and said, “so are we”, are they two <i>nezirim</i> or is each of them a <i>nazir</i> twice?<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered. The answer is obvious both for R. Jehudah and for R. Simeon (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.8.2">Chapter 2, Note 100</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta 2:8</a>).</i> If he was permitted, they were permitted. <sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that a sentence is missing: “If he was permitted, they did not become permitted.”</i> He was not permitted. If two said, “we are <i>nezirim</i>,” another heard it and said, “so am I,” did he make a vow regarding both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Then he would have vowed two <i>neziriot</i>.</i> or did he make a vow parallel to each of them?<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered. The answer is obvious both for R. Jehudah and for R. Simeon (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.8.2">Chapter 2, Note 100</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta 2:8</a>).</i> If they were permitted, [he]<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Reading הוּתַּר “he was permitted” for incomprehensible הַיּוֹם “today”.</i> was permitted; if [he]<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Reading הוּא “he” for הֵן “they”.</i> was permitted, they were not permitted.
“My mouth [shall be <i>nazir</i>] from wine<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah, where one person says “my mouth shall be like his mouth.” Somewhere it must have been mentioned that the mouth is the organ to which wine is forbidden. Similarly, if head or foot are mentioned, reference must be to the corresponding taboo imposed on the <i>nazir</i>. Then it is a valid vow of <i>nazir</i> by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.1">Mishnah 1:2</a>.</i>, my head from shaving, my hand from impurity, my foot from impurity; my head shall be <i>nazir</i>; my liver shall be <i>nezirah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is a separate set of rules. If somebody says, my <i>x</i> shall be <i>nazir</i>, he is a <i>nazir</i> as long as <i>x</i> is a body part necessary for survival (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 21b" href="/Nazir.21b">Babli 21b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).3.3">Tosephta 3:3</a>).</i>” he is a <i>nazir</i>. “My walking shall be <i>nazir</i>; my talking shall be <i>nazir</i>”; he did not say anything. Why? Because he referred the vow to something on which life depends. “Vow, vow<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">One invokes the rule of <i>gezerah šawah</i>, “equal cut” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.20"><i>Berakhot</i> 1:1, Note 70</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.2"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1, Note 18, 1:4, Note 159</a>.) The <i>nazir</i> starts his obligation with a vow, mentioned “here” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a>). Another personal obligation accepted by a vow is that of paying “the valuation of living persons”, mentioned “there”, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:2" href="/Leviticus.27.2"><i>Lev</i>. 27:2</a>). In a different formulation, the argument is in <i>Sifra Beḥuqqotay Parašah</i> 3(6).</i>.” Just as “vow” mentioned there refers to something on which life depends, so “vow” mentioned here refers to something on which life depends.
“ “I am a <i>nazir</i>, and you,” etc. If he was permitted, she is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This must refer to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>, the case that the wife says “and so am I”. Then her vow is dependent on his; if his vow is declared non-existent, so is hers. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, saying “amen” makes her vow independent of his; if his vow were permitted, hers would still exist.</i>. If she was permitted, he is not permitted.
What about, “and so am I<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The last case in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>, if the husband says “so am I” to his wife’s vow.</i>,” how do you treat this? As “amen, it is confirmed for you,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Then he confirmed his wife’s vow. The confirmation of a wife’s vow by her husband is irrevocable.</i>” or “you did well<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This is talk, not confirmation.</i>”? The elder [Rebbi] Ḥiyya and the elder Rebbi Hoshaia. It is stated: If a woman made a vow to be a<i>nazir</i>; her husband heard and said to her, “why did you make a vow of <i>nazir</i>? What did you do to make a vow of <i>nazir</i>? I would not like that you made a vow of <i>nazir</i>! There is neither vow nor oath here;” he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The only language which the husband is empowered to use is that of dissolution, הפרה. Any other language is invalid; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.8.5"><i>Nedarim</i> 10:10, Note 95</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 77b" href="/Nedarim.77b">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 77b</a>).</i>. But if he said, “you did well that you did make a vow of <i>nazir</i>; I liked that you did make a vow of <i>nazir</i>; if you had not made a vow of <i>nazir</i> I would have told you to make one”, Rebbi Ḥiyya stated on this, in all of these cases he cannot dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, confirmation of a vow is not restrictedr to a fixed formula, but dissolution would be.</i>. But Rebbi Hoshaia stated: In all these cases, he can dissolve unless he said “amen” or “it is confirmed for you”, then he can no longer dissolve.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am a <i>nazir</i>, and you<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The husband makes a vow and invites his wife to join him. As the Halakhah explains, he makes his vow conditional on her concurrence.</i>?” If she said “amen”, he may dissolve hers<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">By asking her, he did not waive his privilege of dissolving her vows.</i>, and his is void<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since his vow is conditional on hers, his disappears with hers. “Void” is also the reading of Maimonides (in his Mishnah Commentary and his Code, <i>Nedarim</i> 13:14), against the Babli in Mishnah and Gemara which reads “confirmed”. Y. Qafeḥ in his edition of Maimonides’s Commentary notes (in his grandfather’s name) that the disagreement between Rav Jehudah and Abbai in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 22b" href="/Nazir.22b">Babli (22b</a>) may be based on different readings in the Mishnah.</i>. “I am <i>nezirah</i>, and you<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The wife makes a vow and invites her husband to join her.</i>?” If he said “amen”<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">By this act, not only did he become a <i>nazir</i> but he confirmed her vow and, therefore, lost his power of dissolution.</i>, he cannot dissolve.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> If she is permitted, he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to the first sentence in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> and explains why his vow disappears with hers. The explanation is that given by Abbai in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 22b" href="/Nazir.22b">Babli, 22b</a>.</i>. If he is permitted, she is not permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since her vow was an independent act.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because he makes his vow conditional on hers, if he says, on condition that you [accept]<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">But if the Mishnah is taken as formulated, that the husband’s vow is absolute and the wife is only invited to participate, if she has her vow revoked by an Elder, his vow is not touched.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Text from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.8.5"><i>Nedarim</i> 10:10, Note 95</a>.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The husband who said “there is no vow, there is no oath,” did not say anything. Also the Elder who said “it is dissolved for you, it is voided for you,” did not say anything. But everybody has to follow his own rules. The husband says “it is dissolved for you, it is voided for you,” and the Elder says, “there is no vow, there is no oath”.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a woman had made a vow of <i>nazir</i> but drank wine or defiled herself for the dead, she receives forty [lashes]<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since she violates biblical prohibitions, is duly warned by two witnesses, and persists in her action, she is subject to the biblical punishment of at most 39 lashes.</i>. If her husband had dissolved her vow but she did not know that he had dissolved her vow<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband can dissolve his wife’s vows in her absence.</i> when she drank wine or defiled herself for the dead, she does not receive forty [lashes]<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">While there was criminal intent, there was no crime committed. The husband had legitimized her actions.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, if she does not receive forty, let her receive blows of rebelliousness<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic punishment, usually reserved for transgression of rabbinic rules.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a woman had made a vow of <i>nazir</i>,” etc. Biblical whippings are 39 lashes<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.3.7.1">Mishnah <i>Makkot</i> 3:10</a>.</i>. One evaluates him; if he can stand it, one whips him, if not, one does not whip him. Blows of rebelliousness: one strikes him until he accepts<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">To follow rabbinic rules.</i> or until he dies.
It is written: “The Eternal will forgive her<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:6" href="/Numbers.30.6"><i>Num</i>. 30:6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:9" href="/Numbers.30.9">9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:13" href="/Numbers.30.13">13</a>. The verses refer to girls or women whose vows are dissolved by father or husband. The verses assume that they felt impelled to make vows for some hidden guilt. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 23a" href="/Nazir.23a">Babli, 23a</a>, the verse is interpreted to refer only to the case of the woman violating her vow, not knowing that it had been dissolved. The speaker there and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a> is R. Aqiba.</i>.” This tells that she needs forgiveness. When Rebbi Jacob came to this verse, he used to say: If somebody needs atonement having intended to get pig’s meat but happened to get kosher [animal’s] meat, so much more one who had the intent to get pig’s meat and got pig’s meat.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta 3:10</a>; Babl1 22a.</i>: “A woman made a vow as <i>nazir</i> and her friend heard it and said, ‘so am I;’ if the first’s husband heard and told her, ‘it is permitted to you,’ the first one is permitted but the second forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli explains that in contrast to an Elder, who has the power to retroactively annul a vow from the start, the husband can only annul his wife’s vow from the moment he heard about it. Therefore, for the second woman, the reference was to a valid vow and she is a <i>nezirah</i>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, if she said, my intention was only to be like her, in her state, the second also is permitted.”
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 5:3: “If 5 people claim a deposit which he holds for them and he swears that he holds nothing of theirs, and this was false, he can be prosecuted for one perjury. But if he said, an oath that I hold nothing of yours, and of yours, etc. (using the singular in each case), he can be prosecuted for 5 perjuries. R. Eleazer says, [he can be prosecuted for 5 perjuries] only if he mentioned ‘oath’ at the end (to make it clear that he meant an oath for each of them singly.) R. Simeon says, only if he mentioned an oath in every case,” i. e., an oath that I hold nothing of yours, and an oath that I hold nothing of yours, etc.<br>The following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.1">Mishnah 5:4</a> deals with the case that one claimant demands from him wheat, barley, and spelt which he holds, and he swears falsely that he holds nothing, he is prosecuted for one perjury. But if he swears that he holds neither wheat, nor barley, nor spelt, he is prosecuted for each item separately; R. Meïr says, even if he swore that he did not have wheat, barley, spelt. For this part of the Mishnah, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.4.2-3"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:4, Notes 79–86</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Eleazar says, only if he mentions ‘oath’ at the end. Rebbi Simeon says, only if he mentions ‘oath’ for every item.” <sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, a parallel text is in <i>Šebuot</i> 5:4 (36b 1. 46).</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, [Rebbi Simeon implies]<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel text and the quote later in this paragraph show that the text in parentheses belongs here.</i> if it turns out that he had no wheat, he is not prosecutable for the rest<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the preceding Mishnah, R. Simeon held that the “oath” only referred to the first item on the list; if there was no perjury for the first item, there was no perjury. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 103a" href="/Menachot.103a">Babli agrees, <i>Menaḥot</i> 103a</a>.) As long as he did not say, “an oath for barley, and an oath for spelt”, he cannot be prosecuted for lying about these items.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, even Rebbi Jehudah will agree with this<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">It is established in <i>Šebuot</i> that R. Jehudah is the anonymous Tanna of these Mishnaiot. He agrees that no prosecution is possible if the oath regarding the first item of the list was true.</i>. If it turns out that he had no wheat, does [the oath] refer to the other kinds? The colleagues said, it does not<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">When she said, “so am I”, she only referred to the first woman in her present state, as <i>nezirah</i>, without further thought. This supports the colleagues’ interpretation.</i>; Rebbi Ze‘ira said, it does. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, the <i>baraita</i> supports the colleagues: “A woman made a vow as <i>nazir</i> and her friend heard it and said, ‘so am I;’ if the first’s husband heard and told her, ‘it is permitted to you,’ the first one is permitted and the second forbidden. Rebbi Simeon says, if she said, my intention was only to be like her, in her state, the second also is permitted.” Because she said “to be like her, in her state;” therefore, if she did not say “to be like her, in her state,” the first is permitted and the second forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Interpreting R. Simeon’s opinion, they hold that any vow or oath only refers to the item mentioned as its object.</i>. Since there, where there was no original <i>nezirut</i>, you say it applies, here, where there was an original oath, not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Jacob bar Aḥa is rejected. If an accessory vow is interpreted maximally, an original oath cannot be intepreted minimally.</i>? When Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon implies that if it turns out that he had no wheat, he is not prosecutable for the rest, if he attaches<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">He explicitly attaches the second and third items to the first. Then it is clear that he cannot be prosecuted for perjury if he swore correctly on the first item.</i>: If he says, [my obligation for] barley shall be like that for wheat; [my obligation for] spelt shall be like that for wheat.
The following is obvious: If her husband did not dissolve for the first [woman] and she transgressed her vow, she is whipped. Can the second be whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">If the second woman stated that “my intention was only to be like her, in her state,” and the first violated her vow and now cannot be a <i>nezirah</i> while she undergoes the purification rite, when the second also violated her vow.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, since one is whipped, the other also is whipped. Rebbi La said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose’s statement.</i> follows Rebbi Simeon. Can the second not be considered like one who said, I am a <i>nezirah</i> after twenty days<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">That the vow should count again after the first woman was purified.</i>? Rebbi Simeon follows his own opinion; for Rebbi Simeon declares him free, because his offering was not according to the custom of offerers.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 12:3" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.12.3">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 12:3</a> (formulated in the masculine), cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.4.3">Halakhah 2:4, Note 66</a>. There does not exist an intermittent vow of <i>nazir</i>; if the second woman’s vow was valid, it remains valid.</i>

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A woman who had made a vow of <i>nazir</i> and designated her animal<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Really three animals for her prescribed sacrifice.</i> when her husband dissolved her vow, if the animal was his, it leaves and grazes with the herd<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since one cannot dedicate anybody else’s property, the animals are not dedicated.</i>. But if the animal was hers<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">If the spouses had signed a contract of separation of properties by which the husband renounced his right of administration and usufruct. Then the wife can dedicate her property without asking the husband’s consent.</i>, the purification offering shall die<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Since an animal designated as purification offering can never be redeemed.</i>, the elevation offering shall be brought as an elevation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since elevation offerings can be brought as voluntary gifts; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1" href="/Leviticus.1"><i>Lev.</i> 1</a>.</i>, the well-being offering as a well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Most well-being offerings are voluntary gifts. But those are eaten during two days and the intervening night (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:16" href="/Leviticus.7.16"><i>Lev</i>. 7:16</a>) while the <i>nazir</i>’s well-being offering follows the rules of thanksgiving offerings which may be eaten only during one day and the following night (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:15" href="/Leviticus.7.15"><i>Lev</i>. 7:15</a>). The <i>nazir</i>’s offering also needs an accompanying gift of bread, similar to the thanksgiving offering. But since the woman is no <i>nezirah</i> after the husband’s dissolution of her vow, the bread cannot be offered. (The bread is baked on the day it is offered; since the husband has to dissolve the vow before it has run its course, there is no dedicated bread.)</i>, to be eaten on one day; it does not need bread. If she had money not designated<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Money put aside for her sacrifices, without specifying which money should be used for which animal.</i>, it should be given as a donation<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Given to the special Temple account, to be used for elevation sacrifices if the altar otherwise would be vacant.</i>. If the monies were designated, the value of the purification offering shall be thrown into the Dead Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Where it will be quickly dissolved by the chemicals in the water. “Throwing something into the Dead Sea” means: “making sure it cannot be used.”</i>; one may not use it<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is designated for a sacrifice.</i> but there can be no larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sacrifice cannot be offered, misappropriation of the money cannot be prosecuted; the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a> cannot be applied.</i>. For the value of the elevation offering, they shall bring an elevation offering; it is subject to the law of larceny. For the value of the well-being offering, they shall bring a well-being offering, to be eaten on one day; it does not need bread<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Well-being sacrifices remain the property of the offerer; they may be <i>sancta</i>, but are not “The Eternal’s <i>Sancta</i>”, never subject to the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A woman who had made a vow of <i>nazir</i>,” etc. Does this mean it became holy by dedication<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the absence of a contract to the opposite, all property of the wife’s is administered by her husband, how can the wife dedicate animals for her sacrifices without asking her husband to do it for her?</i>? But if a third person gave her a gift and said, on condition that your husband have no right of disposition over it<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This is one scenario in which the husband has no say in what she does. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:8:2-9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.8.2-9.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 11:8, Notes 69–70</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 24b" href="/Nazir.24b">Babli 24b</a>.)</i>, then it is hers. Rebbi Mattaniah said, if he gave her power over his properties<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The husband gives her the right to sign for everything concerning their properties. In this case, she is able to dedicate the animals but he retains veto power. In the scenario described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.1">Note 63</a>, he has no veto power.</i>. If he comes to protest, it did not become holy; otherwise, it became holy.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.1">Mishnah 5:3</a>: If an Elder annuls the vow for which animals already had been reserved, the animals are profane. Why are the wife’s animals not profane if her husband dissolves her vow?</i>, you find it possible to say, “it should go grazing”, and here, you say so? There, the Elder eliminates the vow from the start; here, he<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The husband. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 19a" href="/Nazir.19a">Babli, 19a</a>, the question of the reach of the husband’s action remains unanswered. Only for the Elder, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 74b" href="/Ketubot.74b">Babli agrees, <i>Ketubot</i> 74b</a>.</i> eliminates only from that moment onwards.
Who dissolves her obligations<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">It is agreed that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30" href="/Numbers.30"><i>Num</i>. 30</a> gives the husband the right to dissolve his wife’s vows. But where is it written that the husband can eliminate the sacrifices which are implied by his wife’s vow?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, it is a decree of Scripture: “He dissolved her vow;” he dissolved her obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:9" href="/Numbers.30.9"><i>Num</i>. 30:9</a>: “If on the day of his hearing he stops her and dissolves her vow [and] obligations.”</i>. The one who dissolved her vow dissolved her obligation.
It was stated: They<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifices offered after a vow of <i>nazir</i> was dissolved. The Mishnah mentions that no bread is offered; it is a logical consequence that the officiating Cohen does not receive a foreleg from the goat offered as well-being sacrifice since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:19" href="/Numbers.6.19"><i>Num</i>. 6:19</a> makes the gift of the foreleg dependent on the gift of bread.</i> need neither bread nor foreleg. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: Do well-being sacrifices which are brought after death<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If the estate of a deceased person included animals dedicated for well-being sacrifices, the heirs have to offer these animals in the Temple.</i> need bread? He turned around and said, is it not the same during his lifetime, do they not come for nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A person made a vow for sacrifices and afterwards dedicated certain animals to satisfy his vow. If then these animals were lost or ran away, he dedicated other animals and offered them; when afterwards the original animals are found, they have to be sacrificed; this is “for nothing” since the vow already was fulfilled.</i>? You say, they need bread, and here they need bread. Rebbi Yose said, it is obvious for Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya that well-being sacrifices which are brought after dissolution do not need bread<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is stated in our Mishnah. The question is why did R. Abun bar Ḥiyya not conclude (with the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 24b" href="/Nazir.24b">Babli, 24b</a>) from the Mishnah that well-being sacrifices after the dedicator’s death do not need bread?</i>. What is the difference between death and dissolution? In the case of death, they already were prepared to need bread; In the case of dissolution, they never were prepared to need bread<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of animals dedicated to fulfill a vow of well-being offerings, they can immediately be brought to the Temple and offered. But a well-being offering for a vow of <i>nazir</i> cannot be brought before the period of the vow has expired; before that time the dedication is potential, rather than actual.</i>. It was stated: There is a reparation sacrifice after dissolution<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife became impure before her vow was annulled, she incurred the obligation of a reparation sacrifice. If the husband then dissolves her vow, the obligation is not dissolved since his action is not retroactive (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.3">Note 77</a>).</i>; there is no reparation sacrifice after death<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Reparation sacrifices are parallel to purification sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:7" href="/Leviticus.7.7"><i>Lev</i>. 7:7</a>). Since purification sacrifices after death are impossible, so are reparation sacrifices.</i>. If she died, her reparation sacrifice is not offered, since there is no reparation sacrifice after death. If he dissolved for her, her reparation sacrifice is offered since that reparation sacrifice does not come for nothing.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:4:5-5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.4.5-5.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:6</a>.</i>: “Leftovers for <i>nezirim</i> are for <i>nezirim</i>; what is left over from a <i>nazir</i> is for donation<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">If moneys were collected to help indigent <i>nezirim</i> with the expenses of their sacrifices but not all was used, the remainder has to be kept in trust to be used in the future for the same purpose. But if a person dedicated his own money for his sacrifices and had money left over, that should go to the account for offerings (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.1">Note 68</a>).</i>.” Rav Ḥisda said, only if his purification offering was presented last. But if his well-being offering waspresented last, what is left over is for a well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:4:5-5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.4.5-5.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:6</a> also states that leftovers of monies for purification offerings go to the offerings account, but leftovers for well-being offerings must be used for well-being offerings.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, even if his well-being offering was presented last, it is a general rule for a <i>nazir</i> that his leftover be for donation<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Overriding the general rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:4:5-5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.4.5-5.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:6</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 25a" href="/Nazir.25a">Babli, 25a</a>, this rule is attributed to R. Joḥanan (and, therefore, declared to be practice to be followed.)</i>. A <i>baraita</i> supports one and a <i>baraita</i> supports the other. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ze‘ira: “The following are undesignated monies: any which contain money for purification offerings that should die. Even if he designated money for purification offerings that should die, it remains undesignated.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah <i>Meʻilah</i> 3:2 which decrees that money left over from unspecified funds set aside for <i>nazir</i>’s sacrifices must be given to the Temple’s offering account. Such money can be used for elevation or well-being offerings only if absolutely no money of purification offerings is mixed in (since one has to avoid the danger that the value of purification offerings be totally lost if anything goes wrong with that kind of sacrifice.)</i>” A <i>baraita</i> supports Rav Ḥisda: “This is for my purification offering and the rest for my <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The required elevation and well-being offerings.</i>. Then he died. One commits larceny with all of them but not with part of them<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since well-being offerings are not subject to larceny by its owner, misappropriation cannot be prosecuted unless one can prove that money for elevation offerings is involved.</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">A different version of this <i>baraita</i> is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 26b" href="/Nazir.26b">Babli, 26b</a>; Tosephta <i>Meʻilah</i> 1:10.</i> It does not say, when he died it should be given for donation.
Rav Ḥisda said, a <i>nazir</i>’s leftover bread shall be left to decay. Rebbi Yose said, that is correct. You cannot sacrifice it by itself since (reparation sacrifices)<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of אָשָׁם “reparation sacrifice” one has to read לֶחֶם “bread”. While there are all kinds of offerings of flour and baked goods (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2" href="/Leviticus.2"><i>Lev</i>. 2</a>), no offering of bread alone is authorized.</i> cannot be brought alone. You cannot sacrifice it together with another <i>nazir</i>’s since no <i>nazir</i> sacrifices without bread<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Also, nowhere do we find a procedure to redeem sacrificial bread.</i>. Therefore, it was necessary to say that a <i>nazir</i>’s leftover bread shall be left to decay. They wanted to say, the same rule applies to his leftover bread as to his leftover wine offering<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Required in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:15" href="/Numbers.6.15"><i>Num</i>. 6:15</a>, following the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:1-16" href="/Numbers.15.1-16"><i>Num</i>. 15:1–16</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, his leftover wine offering is most holy; it should be given to donation accounts<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since all leftovers of sacrifices characterized as “most holy” are to be given to the donation account of the Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.4.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:5</a>).</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, Samuel, Rav Ḥisda, and Rebbi Eleazar, all three said the same. Rav Ḥisda, as quoted here<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">About wine offerings, in the interpretation of R. Yose ben R. Abun.</i>. Samuel, as Rebbi Yasa<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">For historical reasons, one has to read ר׳ יסא instead of ר׳ יוסי.</i> said, when I still was there<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>, I heard the voice of Rav Jehudah asking our teacher Samuel: If he designated his <i>sheqel</i> and died? He said, it should be given as donation<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">But leftover money to be used for the Temple tax of a living person is profane (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.4.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:5</a>).</i>. The leftover of his tenth of an <i>epha</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">To buy the required flour offering belonging to a purification offering.</i>: Rebbi Joḥanan said, he should bring it to the Dead Sea; Rebbi Eleazar said, it should be given as donation<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah (Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:5).</i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: Money can be non-designated, no animal can be non-designated<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Money can be used to buy any animal; an animal usually defines the sacrifice for which it can be used: A female exclusively for a purification offering, a young male for an elevation offering, an older animal for a well-being offering. The same argument is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 26b" href="/Nazir.26b">Babli, 26b</a>.</i>. Rav Sheshet said, it was stated thus: <sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Wayyiqra II Parašah</i> 6(2), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 27b" href="/Nazir.27b">Babli 27b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 27b" href="/Keritot.27b"><i>Keritut</i> 27b</a>.</i>“I might think that one could not satisfy his obligation with his father’s sacrifice, with an animal which he dedicated, whether from a minor sin for a major sin<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">A minor sin is one not punished either by divine exstirpation or by capital punishment. The latter are major sins. Since all purification offerings are the same, if it were possible to use one’s father’s designated purification offering for oneself, it would not matter for which sin his father designated the animal.</i>, or from a major sin for a minor sin, or from a minor sin for a minor sin, or from a major sin for a major sin, since he cannot shave for his <i>nezirut</i> with his father’s animal; but he could satisfy his obligation with his father’s sacrifice, with money he dedicated, whether from a minor sin for a major sin, or from a major sin for a minor sin, or from a minor sin for a minor sin, or from a major sin for a major sin, since he can shave on money for his <i>nezirut</i>, any time it is not designated, but not if it is designated. The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">For purification offerings, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:29" href="/Leviticus.4.29"><i>Lev</i>. 4:29</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:32" href="/Leviticus.4.32">32</a>. For the <i>nazir, Num</i>. 6:14. The mention of his sacrifice in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:32" href="/Leviticus.4.32"><i>Lev.</i> 4:32</a> is redundant; this is given as motivation for the restrictive interpretation of all three verses.</i>, ‘<i>his</i> sacrifice’, he can only satisfy his obligation with his sacrifice, not with his father’s sacrifice.” Talking about money, one mentions “not designated”.<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">But non-designated animals are not mentioned. This is Rav Sheshet’s support for Rebbi Abba’s statement.</i>
An animal is not subject to the rules of the non-designated at the beginning<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.8">Note 106</a>.</i>, but it is subject to the rules of the non-designated at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">If an animal develops a defect and can no longer be offered on the altar, it must be redeemed and the redemption money may be used for any type of offering.</i>. Money is subject to the rules of the non-designated at the beginning, but it is not subject to the rules of the non-designated at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.9">Note 121</a>.</i>. Money is subject to the rules of the non-designated at the beginning, as we have stated: “If he had money not designated, it should be given as donation.” An animal is subject to the rules of the non-designated at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">As written, this is a <i>non sequitur</i>. It seems that one has to read: An animal is <i>not</i> subject to the rules of the non-designated at the <i>beginning</i>.</i>, as we have stated: “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if he brought three animals but did not explain.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.10.1">Mishnah 6:12</a>. If somebody brings his three animals to the Temple without specification, the officiating priests do not have to ask him to designate the animals for specific offerings since the situation is clear, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.8">Note 106</a>.</i>” They are subject to the rules of the non-designated at the beginning,<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This must read: <i>Money</i> is <i>not</i> subject to the rules of the non-designated at the <i>end</i>.</i> as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Meʻilah</i> 1:10. The text there is more complete: “ ‘These are for my purification offering, the remainder is for the rest of my <i>nezirut</i>’, when he died. <i>The money’s worth of the purification offering shall be thrown into the Dead Sea; one may not use it but there can be no larceny. The remainder may be used for either elevation or well-being offerings</i>. One commits larceny about all but one cannot commit larceny about a part.”<br>If the moneys were under the rules of the non-designated, any surplus would have to be used for donation, i. e., to buy elevation offerings. Since it may also be used for well-being offerings, it is not subject to these rules.</i>: “ ‘These are for my purification offering, the remainder is for the rest of my <i>nezirut’</i>, when he died. One commits larceny about all but one cannot commit larceny about a part<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since no money destined for well-being offerings can be misappropriated by its owner.</i>.” It is not subject<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read: “An animal is subject”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.9">Note 112</a>.</i> to the rules of the non-designated at the end, when he brought money and redeemed it for an animal. If he designated a donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">An animal unfit as sacrifice is simply representing its money’s worth.</i>, it is as if he designated money. If he designated an ox<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">A sacrificial animal in general, but not for a <i>nazir</i> who is required to offer one female and one male sheep, and one male goat.
