The Babli rules that the animal must be treated as a sacrifice unless it be blemished, when it follows the rules of money (27b).</i>, Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun and Rebbi Ḥiyya ben Julianus, both speak in Shammai’s name. One says, he is like one who designates money; the other says, he is like one who designates an animal<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">It cannot be redeemed unless it develops a blemish; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one of the bloods was sprinkled for her<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The essence of any sacrifice is that its blood should be sprinkled on the walls of the altar. Once at least one of the sacrifices was presented, the <i>nezirut</i> is completed. The husband cannot dissolve a vow which no longer is active.</i>, he cannot dissolve. Rebbi Aqiba says, even if one of the animals was slaughtered for her, he cannot dissolve. When has this been said? If she shaves in purity. But if she shaves in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">And then has to start anew.</i> he may dissolve since he can say, I cannot stand an unseemly wife<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Since as a <i>nezirah</i> she is forbidden to comb her hair, lest she tear one out.</i>. Rebbi says, he may dissolve even if she shaves in purity, since he can say, I cannot stand a shorn wife<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">While she could wear a wig after shearing off her hair, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 28b" href="/Nazir.28b">Babli (28b</a>) explains that the husband can object to her wearing a wig, which is difficult to keep clean. Therefore, a vow which will cause his wife to have to wear a wig is one which causes pain to the husband and is one which he may dissolve (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.1.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 11:1</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one of the bloods was sprinkled for her,” etc. He dissolves for her because of her hair<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">While she could wear a wig after shearing off her hair, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 28b" href="/Nazir.28b">Babli (28b</a>) explains that the husband can object to her wearing a wig, which is difficult to keep clean. Therefore, a vow which will cause his wife to have to wear a wig is one which causes pain to the husband and is one which he may dissolve (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.1.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 11:1</a>).</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: It is a decision of Scripture: “He dissolved her vows,” he dissolves what is on her<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees and holds that living with a shorn wife imposes no hardship on the husband and, therefore, the general rules of vows do not permit the husband to dissolve his wife’s vow of <i>nazir</i>. But he finds a special dispensation which empowers the husband to dissolve his wife’s vow of <i>nazir</i>. The verse, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:9" href="/Numbers.30.9"><i>Num</i>. 30:9</a>) is slightly misquoted; instead of הפר נדריה it should be והפר את נדרה. As in most cases, the argument is from the part of the verse which is not quoted: “But if on the day on which her husband hears it, he prevents her, and <i>dissolves her vow which is on her …</i>” This gives the husband the power to dissolve any vow she makes regarding what is on her; i. e., her hair. (In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 153, the verse is interpreted to mean that the husband has a say only over vows which are <i>on her</i>, i. e., after she made them. A preëmptive dissolution is invalid just as the Mishnah here states that a dissolution after the end of the period is invalid.)</i>. Whenever he dissolves her vow, he dissolves what is on her. Rebbi Eleazar said, it<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, which considers the <i>nezirut</i> completed once the blood of a single animal was sprinkled on the altar in the final ceremony.</i> follows Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is everybody’s opinion, after she was transferred from the prohibition to the positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">From the moment when she starts the final ceremony, she is no longer forbidden to shave her hair but ordered to shave the hair and burn it in the fire under the well-being sacrifices. Since shaving the hair cannot be prohibited and required at the same time, the <i>nezirut</i> is completed according to everybody.</i>. The rabbis say, “afterwards, the <i>nazir</i> shall drink wine,” after all these actions<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.7.1">Mishnah 6:9</a>. In their opinion, the <i>nazir</i> may shave only after the conclusion of the entire ceremony.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, even after a single action<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.10.1">Mishnah 6:12</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.5.2">Note 128</a>.</i>. Ḥizqiah said, the Mishnah supports Rebbi (Bevai)<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Read: Eleazar.</i>. “When has this been said? If she shaves in purity. But if she shaves in impurity, he may dissolve since he can say, I cannot stand an unseemly wife.” Therefore, shaving in purity does not make her unseemly. Who holds that shaving does not make unseemly<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore “unseemly” cannot mean “shaven”; it must refer to the fact that she cannot drink wine. Since R. Simeon allows the <i>nazir</i> to drink wine after the offering of one animal, before he shaves, he asserts that shaving and not drinking wine are separate matters.</i>? Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Yose bar Abun said, even Rebbi thinks so: “Rebbi says, he may dissolve even if she shaves [in purity], since he can say, I cannot stand a shorn wife.” Should he not say, I cannot stand an unseemly and shorn wife<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the argument that the husband suffers if his wife does not drink wine is applicable only if her vow was not completed and she shaves in impurity.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi (Jehudah)<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Read: Aqiba; the reference is to the Mishnah.</i> said that only for the purification sacrifice, since a purification sacrifice would be invalid if not in her name<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband were able to dissolve his wife’s <i>nezirut</i> after the slaughter of her purification offering, that offering would become invalid and would have to be burned outside the Temple precinct. Both elevation and well-being offerings may be used if the original intent became void.</i>. That means, as long as it is alive it is surrendered to Heaven only by slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Even though we say (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a>) that “a promise to Heaven is like a delivery to a person”, an animal dedicated as purification offering becomes Heaven’s property only by its slaughter, in contrast to all other sacrifices for which the dedication also equals delivery.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A man can declare his son<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">His underage son. Why a father should have such power is a matter of disagreement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 28b-29a" href="/Nazir.28b-29a">Babli, 28b/29a</a>.</i> a <i>nazir</i> but a woman cannot declare her son a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Since rabbinic law knows no <i>materna potestas</i>.</i>. How is this? If he shaved him or relatives shaved him<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">If either the son or some relatives provided the sacrifices required while the father already had dedicated either animals or the monies needed for them.</i>; if he protested or relatives protested<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">If either the son or relatives protested the father’s action, the child’s <i>nezirut</i> is voided. If the father already had dedicated either animals or the monies needed for them, they have to be treated according to the rules detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> in the case of the woman who had prepared her sacrifices when her vow was dissolved by her husband.</i>, if he had designated animals, the purification offering shall die; the elevation offering shall be brought as elevation offering; the well-being offering shall be brought as elevation offering; it may be eaten for one day and does not need bread. If he had money not designated, it should be given as donation. If the monies were designated, the money’s worth of the purification offering shall be thrown into the Dead Sea; one may not use it but there can be no larceny. For the value of the elevation offering, he shall bring an elevation offering; it is subject to the law of larceny. For the value of the well-being offering, he shall bring a well-being offering, to be eaten on one day; it does not need bread.<br>A man may shave on the basis of his father’s <i>nezirut</i>, but a woman may not shave on the basis of her father’s <i>nezirut</i>. How is this? If his father was a <i>nazir</i> and had set aside unspecified money for his <i>nezirut</i> when he died, and he said, I am a <i>nazir</i> on condition that I may shave on my father’s money, Rebbi Yose said, the money shall be given as donation, for he cannot shave on his father’s money. Who may shave based on his father’s <i>nezirut</i>? If both he and his father were <i>nezirim</i> and his father had set aside unspecified money for his <i>nezirut</i> when he died; this one shaves on his father’s <i>nezirut</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A man can declare his son a <i>nazir</i>,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah deals with a difference between a man and a woman, it quotes another difference, regarding people afflicted with skin disease. The text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:8:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.8.7"><i>Soṭah</i> 3:9, Note 228</a>.</i>“A man”. This refers not only to a man; from where for a woman? The verse says, “afflicted with skin disease,” whether man, woman, or minor. If it is so, why is “a man” written? For the next theme, “a man is dishevelled and has open seams, no woman is dishevelled and has open seams.”
A man can declare a <i>nazir</i>, and a man can shave<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">A man can declare his son to be a <i>nazir</i>, and the son can shave on the offerings which his father brings for him.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Meïr: <sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eduyot 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Eduyot.2.2">Tosephta <i>Idiut</i> 2:2</a>.</i> In 24 matters are the House of Shammai lenient but the House of Hillel stringent, and this is one of them: The House of Shammai say, a man cannot declare his son to be a <i>nazir</i> but the House of Hillel say, a man can declare his son to be a <i>nazir</i>. It was stated about those who are <i>nazir</i> from the womb<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence seems to be incomplete.</i>. Some want to say, until he grows two pubic hairs<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the father can declare his son to be a <i>nazir</i> until the latter reaches adulthood.</i>. Some want to say, until he reaches the time of vows<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Niddah</i> 5:6: “The vows of a girl 11 years and one day of age or a boy 12 years and one day of age are checked.” If they know what they are doing, their vows are valid.</i>. Everybody agrees that he can no longer declare him a <i>nazir</i> once he reaches the time of vows<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Once a boy’s own vows are valid, his father can no longer make vows for him. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 29b-30a" href="/Nazir.29b-30a">Babli, 29b/30a</a>, the matter remains undecided.</i>.
“If he shaved him or relatives shaved him; if he protested or relatives protested,” in any language it is a valid protest<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">No particular language is prescribed for these protests.</i>. If he sat before a barber<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The protest must be verbal; a silent action does not count.</i>, it is not a protest; may a relative protest<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">If the child sits in a barber’s chair, may a relative protest for him? The question needs no answer since the relative can protest anywhere.</i>?
May his own vow of <i>nazir</i> and that of his father fall on him together<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">If he is between childhood and adulthood and it is not clear which vow would apply. The positive answer is given by the story.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 29b" href="/Nazir.29b">Babli, 29b</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Niddah 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Niddah.5.6">Tosephta <i>Niddah</i> 5:15</a>.</i> It happened that Rebbi Ḥanina ben Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, Ḥananiah ben Ḥananiah. In the Babli, R. Ḥaninah.</i>’s father made him a <i>nazir</i> and Rebbi Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">In both other sources, Rabban Gamliel. Since the story presupposes the existence of the Temple, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel would have to be the president of the revolutionary government in the war against the Romans, Rabban Gamliel his father.</i> checked him whether he had grown two pubic hairs. He said to him, why are you checking me? If my father’s <i>nezirut</i> is on me, I am a <i>nazir</i>; otherwise, I declare being a <i>nazir</i>. Rabban Gamliel stood up and kissed him on his head and said, I am sure that you will not die from old age before you taught instruction in Israel. Rebbi Eleazar bar Ṣadoq<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the first generation at Jabneh.</i> said, I saw him sitting and explaining in Jabneh.
Rebbi Aḥa: Rebbi Immi said that Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina asked: May his bird be eaten when its neck was broken<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">If the child who was declared <i>nazir</i> by his father became impure, his father has to bring a couple of birds for him. Both birds (pigeons or turtle doves) are killed by having their necks broken by the Cohen’s thumbnail (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:8" href="/Leviticus.5.8"><i>Lev</i>. 5:8</a>). The meat of one of them, offered as purification sacrifice, should be eaten by the priests. If the boy’s vow is valid in biblical law, there is no problem. But if it is valid only rabbinically, then the priest who eats this meat commits two sins, since (a) he slaughters in the Temple what is not a legitimate sacrifice and (b) he eats meat forbidden outside the Temple precinct since it was not ritually slaughtered.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 29a" href="/Nazir.29a">Babli, 29a</a>, R. Joḥanan holds that the vow is biblically valid; there are no problems. R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that the vow is rabbinic; his position is that articulated by R. Mani in this paragraph.</i>? Instead of asking about a bird whose neck was broken, why do you not ask about a slaughtered bird<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is forbidden to bring profane animals into the Temple precinct, one could ask not about birds, but about the animals required for the <i>nazir</i> who finishes his term in purity, whether his purification offering could be eaten by the priests.</i>? There is one doubt, here are two doubts<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifices of the pure underage <i>nazir</i> have only the problem of the validity of his vow in biblical law. The sacrifice of the impure in addition has the problem that the consumption of meat from a bird whose neck was broken is forbidden to everybody except priests in the Temple in the line of duty.</i>. Rebbi Mani said, here also it is one doubt, as it was stated: One who perforates or tears out is not guilty because of profane slaughter in the Temple courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to ritually slaughter profane animals in the Temple precinct. If the animals are killed in other ways than by slaughter, that prohibition was not violated (other prohibitions may have been violated). Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 86a" href="/Chullin.86a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 86a</a>. There remains only the problem of eating the meat; the irregular slaughter in the Temple precinct is not an infraction.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: There is not even one doubt, following him who says that slaughtering birds is not clear from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Ritual slaughter of four-legged animals is clearly prescribed in the Torah. In the desert, all non-sacrificial slaughter was forbidden, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:1-7" href="/Leviticus.17.1-7"><i>Lev</i>. 17:1–7</a>. When the Israelites entered the Land, profane slaughter was permitted, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:21" href="/Deuteronomy.12.21"><i>Deut</i>. 12:21</a>. Profane slaughter has to be executed “as I commanded you”, i. e., by the method of slaughter used in the Tabernacle. In all of <i>Lev</i>., slaughter is described by the verb שׁחט, Arabic سحط “to cut the throat”. According to this argument, birds should be killed by breaking their neck, rather than by cutting their throat.</i>: “They spread them a spread,”<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 11:32" href="/Numbers.11.32"><i>Num</i>. 11:32</a>, speaking of the quail. In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 98, the metathesis שחט - שטח is attributed to R. Jehudah.</i> they spread out slaughtered [birds].
So far, if they are equal. If he was <i>nazir</i> once, his son <i>nazir</i> twice? He and his son <i>nazir</i> once, [but] he was a pure <i>nazir</i>, his son an impure <i>nazir</i>, [or] he an impure <i>nazir</i>, his son a pure <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>. As explained in the next paragraph, R. Yose requires that the son’s vow precede the father’s dedication. How would R. Yose deal with the case that the vow of the son’s second <i>nezirut</i> precedes the father’s dedication but the actual realization of the vow starts only after the father’s death? Both for the anonymous Tanna, who allows the son unrestricted access to his father’s dedicated money, and for R. Yose, the question arises whether the money can be used for a sacrifice for which it was not intended (money for the offerings in purity for the reparation offering of the impure or vice versa)? The questions are not answered, neither here nor in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 30b" href="/Nazir.30b">Babli (30b</a>).</i>?
What is Rebbi Yose’s reason<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">That the son’s vow must precede the father’s dedication.</i>? “His offering to the Eternal for his vow”<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:21" href="/Numbers.6.21"><i>Num</i>. 6:21</a>.</i>, that (his sacrifice precede his vow)<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">The two parentheses have to be interchanged. The correct text is copied in <i>Num. rabba</i> 10(42), <i>Yalqut</i> #709.</i> but not (that his vow precede his sacrifice). They wanted to say that Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 3:18" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).3.18">Tosephta, 3:18</a>, it is explained that R. Jehudah and R. Meïr hold that any unspecified money in the estate can be used by the son for a future <i>nezirut</i> (but specified money has to be treated by the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.6.1">Mishnah 6</a>.) The opinion of R. Yose is shared by R. Eleazar (ben Shamua) and R. Simeon (ben Ioḥai). The problems with the text of Tosephta and Babli are treated at length by S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta ki-Fshutah</i> p. 537–538).<br>The Yerushalmi does not treat the anomaly that, by tradition, the daughter cannot use the father’s dedicated money even if she is the only heir (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 30" href="/Nazir.30">Babli 30a/b</a>).</i> would agree with Rebbi Yose. It was found said that neither of them agrees with the other.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The house of Shammai say, dedication in error is dedication, but the House of Hillel say, dedication in error is not dedication. How? If one said, the black ox which comes out of my house first shall be dedicated, and a white one came out; the house of Shammai say, it is dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that he simply wanted to dedicate one of his animals as a sacrifice and since most of his animals were black, he mentioned black. If he had said explicitly, “the first ox which comes out of my house shall be dedicated <i>if</i> it be black,” the House of Shammai will agree that there is no dedication. Since oxen are possible as sacrifices, the dedication mentioned here is dedication as sacrifice.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, it is not dedicated.<br>The gold denar which first comes into my hand shall be dedicated, but it was a silver one; the house of Shammai say, it is dedicated, but the House of Hillel say, it is not dedicated.<br>The wine amphora which first comes into my hand shall be dedicated, but it was a one of oil; the house of Shammai say, it is dedicated, but the House of Hillel say, it is not dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Even though all three Mishnaiot illustrate the same principle, the statements imply that the positions of the Houses of Hillel and Shammai apply to all kinds of dedications. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> exemplifies dedications for the altar, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.1">Mishnah 2</a> money donations, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.1">Mishnah 3</a> things which could be brought to the altar as accessories but never are sacrifices on their own. The three Mishnaiot are an introduction to the <i>nazir</i> vow made in error treated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The House of Shammai say, dedication in error is dedication.” There<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 3:8. The following two paragraphs are Halakhah <i>Terumot</i> 3:8, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.1-2">Notes 85–92</a>.</i>, we have stated: “If somebody intends to say heave but says tithe, tithe but says heave, fire offering but says well-being offering, well-being offering but says fire offering.” Rebbi Jeremiah said, if he intends to say “profane” and says “fire sacrifice”, he dedicated it. Rebbi Yose said, we consider only whether he intended to dedicate but erred because of something else. What is the status of this Mishnah? In the opinion of Rebbi Jeremiah it is in dispute, in the opinion of Rebbi Yose it is everybody’s opinion.
“With his lips but not in his mind.” I could think that I exclude him who decides in his mind; the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>): “To articulate”. But Samuel said, he who decides in his mind is not obligated until he pronounces with his lips. But did we not state: “(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 35:5" href="/Exodus.35.5"><i>Ex</i>. 35:5</a>) Everyone who volunteers in his mind,” that is he who decides in his mind. You say, that is he who decides in his mind, but maybe that is he who pronounces with his lips? When he says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:24" href="/Deuteronomy.23.24"><i>Deut</i>. 23:24</a>): “What comes out from your lips you have to keep,” that speaks about him who pronounces with his lips. Therefore, how can I confirm “every one who volunteers in his mind?” That is he who decides in his mind. What Samuel said refers to a sacrifice.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:3</a>.</i>, we have stated: “If somebody collects coins and says, ‘these are for my Temple tax<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Half a biblical <i>šeqel</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:13" href="/Exodus.30.13"><i>Ex</i>. 30:13</a>). While the relation of the biblical <i>ŝeqel</i> to currency in circulation may have varied from time to time, at the beginning of the tax season the amount was clearly stated and known to everybody (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:4</a>). Therefore, nobody who mentions the Temple tax will intend to dedicate more than the stated amount. In contrast, a purification sacrifice can be a sheep or a goat (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev</i>. 4</a>), in special circumstances also a couple of birds or a flour offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5" href="/Leviticus.5"><i>Lev</i>. 5</a>).</i>,’ the House of Shammai say, the excess should be given as a donation<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that the entire amount was dedicated even if only part can be used for the purpose stated.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, the excess is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.4.1">Mishnah 2:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.4">Note 13</a>.</i>. ‘That I shall be able to pay my Temple tax,’ they agree that the excess is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation makes it clear that the amount in excess of the required tax is not dedicated.</i>. ‘These [monies] are for my purification offering’, they agree that the excess is profane.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The text is shortened so much as to be unintelligible. The Mishnah reads: “ ‘These are for a purification sacrifice,’ they agree that the excess should be a donation (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.6">Chapter 4, Note 90</a>). ‘That I shall be able to bring a purification sacrifice,’ the excess is profane (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.4">Note 8</a>).”</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the next paragraph, the text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.3.2-3">Halakhah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:3</a>. The Yerushalmi text reproduced in the Babli (<i>editio princeps</i>) is too closely adapted to Babylonian spelling to be useful for variant readings.</i> Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: When do they disagree? If he collects little by little<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">He starts a cache of small coins with the declared intention of collecting money for either his future Temple tax or purification sacrifice.</i>. But if he says “these<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">He declares that all his coins shall be dedicated to the stated purpose. The House of Hillel will agree that everything is dedicated and if it cannot be used for the stated purpose, it should be given to the Temple’s donation account.</i>,” everybody agrees that the excess should be given as a donation. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Bevai in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: When do they disagree? If he collects little by little. But if he says “these,” everybody agrees that the excess is profane. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Šeqalim</i> 2:4.</i> supports Rebbi Bevai: “Rebbi Simeon says, what is the difference between Temple tax and purification sacrifices? Only that the Temple tax is a fixed amount<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Half a biblical <i>šeqel</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:13" href="/Exodus.30.13"><i>Ex</i>. 30:13</a>). While the relation of the biblical <i>ŝeqel</i> to currency in circulation may have varied from time to time, at the beginning of the tax season the amount was clearly stated and known to everybody (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 2:4</a>). Therefore, nobody who mentions the Temple tax will intend to dedicate more than the stated amount. In contrast, a purification sacrifice can be a sheep or a goat (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev</i>. 4</a>), in special circumstances also a couple of birds or a flour offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5" href="/Leviticus.5"><i>Lev</i>. 5</a>).</i>.” Where do we hold? If he says, “that I shall use it for the Temple tax,” everybody agrees that the excess should be given as a donation<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Read with the <i>Šeqalim</i> text: “Is profane” (as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.4">Note 13</a>).</i>. If he says, “that I shall use it for my purification offering,” everybody agrees that the excess is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Read with the <i>Šeqalim</i> text: “Should be given as a donation” (as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.4">Note 13</a>).</i>. But we are considering one who says “these<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The monies are already collected; he declares them dedicated for a given purpose.</i>.” Since the Temple tax has a fixed rate from the Torah, the excess is profane. Since purification offerings do not have a fixed rate from the Torah, the excess should be given as a donation<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.4.6">Chapter 4, Note 90</a>.</i>. How does Rebbi Yose handle this? He explains it if he collects little by little<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Simeon applies only if the stated intent is to collect money for the Temple tax, not if the declaration is made on monies already available. In the latter case, R. Simeon may agree that the excess is earmarked for donation.</i>, following the House of Hillel.
If somebody put aside his Temple tax in the belief that he owed it and it turned out that he did not owe<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">After the dedication he remembered that he already had paid.</i>, it was not dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In the next paragraph, it will be determined that this is the opinion of the House of Hillel.</i>. If he put aside two in the belief that he owed twice and it turned out that he owed only once, how do you treat the second<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case, the entire dedication was in error; it is invalid. But in the second case, there is a valid dedication. In order to be able to speak of a second, the amounts must have been dedicated one after the other. But if two animals were dedicated simultaneously, it is clear that a choice of one over the other in this case would be an act of retroactive validation, which is not accepted in biblical law; both coins certainly remain dedicated.</i>? If he believed that he owed it and it turned out that he did not owe<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the dedication is removed as being made in error.</i>, [or as] if he said: “these”<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to R. Yose’s opinion in the preceding paragraph, implying that the money should be donated to the Temple.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šeqalim</i>, this is introduced with a referral clause, “let us hear from the following.” That clause is correctly missing in the present text since the argument from <i>Šeqalim</i> is not presented here.</i> If somebody put aside a purification sacrifice in the belief that he owed it and it turned out that he did not owe, it was not dedicated. If he put aside two in the belief that he owed two and it turned out that he owed only one, how do you treat the second? As if he believed that he owed it and it turned out that he did not owe, or as if he had said “these”<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">By contrast, in <i>Šeqalim</i> it is stated that the dedication is not removed automatically, that the second animal has to be sent to graze until it develops a defect or becomes too old to be an acceptable sacrifice. The implied conclusion seems to be that the redemption money for that animal is a donation to the Temple.</i>?
There<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 12:2" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.12.2">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 12:2</a>.</i>, we have stated: “If somebody says, ‘I undertake [to bring] on a pan<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A cereal offering fried in oil on a flat clay pan, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:5-6" href="/Leviticus.2.5-6"><i>Lev</i>. 2:5–6</a>.</i>’ and he brought in a deep vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">A cereal offering cooked in boiling oil in a deep clay vessel, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:7" href="/Leviticus.2.7"><i>Lev</i>. 2:7</a>.</i>, in a deep vessel and he brought on a pan.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">“What he brought is acceptable but he did not fulfill his vow.”</i>” Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: This is the House of Shammai’s<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Why should a flour offering be acceptable if it was not properly dedicated? Since it is forbidden to bring profane food into the Temple precinct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:26" href="/Deuteronomy.12.26"><i>Deut</i>. 12:26</a>), the House of Hillel should hold that an offering which does not fulfill the specification of the donor’s vow has to be rejected by the officiating priests. But for the House of Shammai, who hold that a dedication is always valid, even if made in error, the nonconforming offering is not profane.</i>, since the House of Shammai say, “dedication in error is dedication.” Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Yose was R. Ze‘ira’s student’s student, one has to read: R. Yasa (Assi).</i>: Why do we not explain it according to everybody, if he said, “I said, on a pan”?<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">If the person who made the vow agrees that the present offering does not satisfy his vow, there is no reason why it should not be accepted as a separate offering.</i> But if he said, “I undertake [to bring] on a pan,” and then he changed his mind<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Immediately.</i> and said, “in a deep vessel”, he fulfilled his duty. Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read “R. Ḥinena”.</i> and Rebbi Yasa came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: it is everybody’s opinion. Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he said, “I undertake [to bring] on a pan or in a deep vessel,” turned around<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Immediately.</i> and said, “on a pan”, and turned around and said, “in a deep vessel”?<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since he demonstrates that he did not make up his mind, do we hold him to his last statement or can he satisfy his vow with any of the kinds mentioned as possibilities?</i> Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Hama in the name of Rebbi Yose: He determines even orally<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This is an independent statement. From the Mishnah in <i>Menaḥot</i> it follows that the donor’s statement determines the kind of cereal sacrifice he is required to bring, even if as yet he owes no flour.</i>. They thought to say, even holidays determine<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">If he vows the sacrifice for a holiday, he cannot satisfy his vow on a workday.</i>, even vessels determine<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">If he vowed a cereal sacrifice without specifying its kind and then put the flour into one of the acceptable vessels without saying a word, the vessel determines the kind of offering he vowed and he cannot change it any longer.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 2:3" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.2.3">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 2:3</a>.</i>, we have stated: “Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, He<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">God, in instituting the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a> ff.</i> made error equal to intent for substitution, but not for sacrifices.” Ḥizqiah said, in error: a prohibition, the one who substitutes: a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">He explains the Mishnah. Once an animal has been dedicated, it cannot be exchanged for another animal as long as it did not develop a blemish (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>). A person who intentionally violates this prohibition is whipped. It is asserted that a person who unintentionally violates the prohibition is also whipped (see the next paragraph). But a dedication in error is not a dedication for the House of Hillel.</i>. If he wants to say “profane” but said “an elevation sacrifice”, it is sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">While for vows one requires that “his heart and mouth be in unison”, for Temple dedications only the pronouncement counts (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:24" href="/Deuteronomy.23.24"><i>Deut</i>. 23:24</a>).</i>. If he wants to say “an elevation sacrifice” but said “profane”, it is not sanctified. It follows that Rebbi Joḥanan, interpreting Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah, parallels Rebbi Jeremiah’s explanation<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In the first paragraph of this <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 3:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.3.4.2">Halakhah (<i>Terumot</i> 3:8, Note 86</a>).</i> regarding the House of Shammai on sacrifices. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion, may one understand that “He made error equal to intent for substitution, but not for sacrifices,” if somebody dedicates a firstling?<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question for Ḥizqiah. Without his comment, we would have read the Mishnah in <i>Temurah</i> to state that substitution in error is substitution, dedication in error is not dedication. But he insists that for R. Yose ben R. Jehudah, substitution in error is criminally punishable. Now everybody agrees that dedication in error is not punishable, but where do we find that intentional dedication should be punishable, to justify R. Yose ben R. Jehudah’s formulation? The answer is that the dedication of a firstling (which must be given to a Cohen) for any other sacrifice is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:26" href="/Leviticus.27.26"><i>Lev</i>. 27:26</a>) and, therefore, should be subject to criminal prosecution.</i> Can it be dedicated? Did not Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Aḥa, and Rebbi Yasa say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody dedicated a firstling and it was brought to the altar, it is not sanctified even for its money’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The firstling is sanctified at birth and never becomes the rancher’s property. The rancher has to raise the calf or lamb for 30 days and then is obligated to deliver it to a Cohen. Since nobody can dedicate what is not his, any dedication of a firstling by the rancher is void, not only invalid. In the case described, any Cohen can come and take the firstling from the altar and eat it (since only the blood of a firstling is given to the altar but nothing of its meat). There can be no prosecution for a nonexisting act.</i>. If somebody dedicated a blemished animal for the altar, even as a substitute it is not sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The example of dedication of a blemished animal cannot be used as illustration of R. Yose ben Jehudah’s statement. A blemished animal cannot be dedicated; therefore, it neither can become a substitute sacrifice. The rules of substitutions and dedications are identical for blemished animals.</i>. But if somebody dedicated an animal with a temporary blemish. Is that consistent<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">(Logically) connected. This technical term appears as מְחֻוָּר “whitish, clear” in the Babli, pointing to differences in the pronunciation of ב, β in Galilee and ب in Babylonia. Cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Die Aussprache des “Bet” in talmudischer Zeit</i>, Bulletin, Verband jüdischer Lehrer und Kantoren der Schweiz Nr. 21, 1977, pp. 4–5.</i> with whipping<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since a temporary blemish does not permanently disqualify the animal from the altar, the dedication cannot be a crime. Again, there is no difference between dedication and substitution; R. Yose ben R. Jehudah cannot refer to this case.</i>? Rebbi Yudan, Rebbi Mattaniah’s father, said, explain it if he gave unblemished animals for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Donating unblemished cattle, sheep, or goats for the upkeep of the Temple clearly violates the injunction of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9" href="/Leviticus.27.9"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9</a> to reserve such animals for the altar. However, that rule is formulated as an obligation, not as a prohibition and, therefore, involves no prosecutable offense. In the next paragraph it is established that R. Jehudah and his son found a biblical source which allows one to find a prohibition in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9" href="/Leviticus.27.9"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9</a>. The rule of R. Yose ben Jehudah is valid only for him and his father, not for most other Sages.</i>.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah follows his father’s method: If somebody dedicated unblemished animals for the upkeep of the Temple, he violates a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9" href="/Leviticus.27.9"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9</a>.</i>. Why also a prohibition? The verse says: “The Eternal spoke to Moses לאמר”<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:1" href="/Leviticus.27.1"><i>Lev</i>. 27:1</a>. It is assumed that this heading applies also to the paragraph <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9-34" href="/Leviticus.27.9-34"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9–34</a> since v. 9 starts with a connecting ו.</i>: a prohibition was pronounced<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">“He said ‘no’ ”. The underlying pronunciation of לאמר cannot be recovered from the indications given here.</i>, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi was sitting and studying the rule that “a firstling cannot be substituted” when Bar Pedaiah’s father was passing by. Rebbi saw him, and said, do I know what I should say here, “the Eternal spoke to Moses לאמר,” could one explain the words of Rebbi Jehudah? Is it written thus? <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.1.2-5"><i>Soṭah</i> 5:1</a> (<b>ס</b>), explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.4">Notes 8–10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.3.2-3"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:3</a> (<b>ה</b>).</i> It follows what Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: For interpretation, one removes from its beginning to its end. Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Even a middle word<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In the application here, the word is split by duplication of a letter, לאמר as אמר לא.</i>. “You have to pour oil on.” You have to pour oil on a flour offering, to subject all flour offerings to pouring.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 6:1. The argument refers to the part of the Mishnah which is not quoted: “The public sale of orphans’ property goes on for 30 days, the public sale of Temple property goes on for 30 days, and one publicly announces mornings and evenings. If somebody dedicates his property while the lien if favor of a wife’s <i>ketubah</i> was in effect, Rebbi Eliezer says, if he would divorce her, he has to make her vow not to have any usufruct from him; Rebbi Joshua says, it is not necessary. Similarly, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says regarding a guarantor of a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> whose husband divorces her, that he shall make him execute a vow of usufruct lest he could plot against his property and take his wife back.”</i>: “The public sale of orphans’ property<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The administrator of an estate whose beneficiaries are underage can sell real estate to satisfy claims against the estate only under supervision by the court. There has to be a 30 day public notice of the land being up for sale; at the end of the period the parcel is sold to the highest bidder.</i> goes on for 30 days, the public sale of Temple property<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Sale of real estate donated to the Temple.</i> goes on for 30 days, and they are publicly announed mornings and evenings.” Rebbi Mana said, Rebbi Eliezer is afraid of trickery<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife has to vow not to have any future usufruct from her past husband in order to collect her <i>ketubah</i> from the Temple, she cannot remarry him. R. Eliezer suspects that a husband who donates his property to the Temple might want to get it back by divorcing his wife, waiting until she has collected her <i>ketubah</i>, then remarrying her and receiving the <i>ketubah</i> money as dowry.</i>, Rebbi Joshua is not afraid of trickery. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Eliezer follows the House of Shammai and Rebbi Joshua the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with R. Mana and holds that their differences are systemic. This is the only opinion quoted in the Babli, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 23a" href="/Arakhin.23a"><i>‘Arakhin</i> 23a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer follows the House of Shammai, since the House of Shammai say, a person may ask about his dedication; could he say that he does not have to vow usufruct?<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This text seems to be corrupt. Since in our Mishnah, the House of Shammai hold that dedication in error is valid, it is clear that they must hold that a vow of dedication cannot be abrogated by an Elder (cf. <i>Nedarim</i>, Introduction p. 422, Chapter 9). Therefore, the text must read: דְּבֵית שַׁמַּי אוֹמְרִים. אֵיו אָדָם נִשְׁאַל עַל הֶקְדֵּשׁוֹ. וְהוּא דַהֲוָה אָמַר. אֵינוֹ צָרִיךְ לְהַדִיר הֲנָייָה. וּדְרִבִּי יְהוֹשֻׁעַ כְּבֵית הִלֵּל. דְּבֵית הִלֵּל אוֹמְרִים. אָדָם נִשְׁאַל עַל הֶקְדֵּשׁוֹ. וְהוּא דַהֲוָה אָמַר. צָרִיךְ לְהַדִיר הֲנָייָה. “Rebbi Eliezer follows the House of Shammai, since the House of Shammai say, a person may <i>not</i> ask about his dedication; could he say that he does not have to vow ususfruct? Rebbi Joshua follows the House of Hillel, since the House of Hillel say, a person <i>may ask</i> about his dedication; could he say that he has to vow usufruct?” The “vow of usufruct” is a vow never to have any usufruct from the person designated in the vow.</i> Rebbi Joshua follows the House of Hillel, since the House of Hillel say, a person may not ask about his dedication; could he say that he has to vow usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could ask an Elder about his vow, he does not need any tricks.</i>? In any case, could not a man ask about his vow of usufruct?<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer should agree that a man who cannot have his dedication annulled may try to have his vow of usufruct annulled.</i> Rebbi Joshua agrees that a guarantor must execute a vow of usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua will agree that the husband of a woman whose <i>ketubah</i> is collected from a third party has to promise never to take her back. It is not that the guarantor has to vow; he has to ask the divorcing couple for their vows.</i>. What does Rebbi Joshua say about a gift? Since he gives voluntarily, he does not have to vow usufruct, or since [the recipient’s] power is small and [the donor] may change his mind, does he have to vow usufruct? Let us hear from the following: The husband of a relative of Rebbi Ḥaggai owed on a document<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χάρτης, Latin <i>charta</i>, “papyrus, roll of papyrus”.</i>. The creditor came and foreclosed. The case<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">After the foreclosure, the husband divorced his wife and she went to court to foreclose on the foreclosed parcel since the lien of her <i>ketubah</i> preceded the creditor’s loan document.</i> came before Rebbi Aḥa, who said, he<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The husband owes a vow of usufruct which will forbid him to remarry his wife in order to permit the wife to collect her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> owes a vow of usufruct. Rebbi Yose said, he does not have to vow usufruct. The colleagues said before Rebbi Aḥa [and] Rebbi Yose: Does Rebbi Aḥa say it correctly? Since if he takes her back, does not the creditor come and foreclose<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband should remarry his divorcee, would not her property become the husband’s property as dowry, and could not the creditor then foreclose it for his claim? It seems that the creditor loses nothing if there is no vow.</i>? Rebbi Yose said to them, she turns it into jewelry or keeps it as additions to her dowry<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.8.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:10, Note 218</a>. Before the marriage, they sign a stipulation that the husband shall have no rights to the property. Then the creditor would be left without recourse.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, by Moses! Rebbi Yose says it correctly. It was executed following Rebbi Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In this and similar cases, the husband has to deliver a vow which forbids him any future usufruct from his divorcee.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.1">Mishnah 4</a>. If a person makes a vow of <i>nazir</i>, designates an animal as his sacrifice at completion, and then asks about his vow and has it annulled, the animal becomes profane.</i>, you say: “It shall leave and graze with the herd.” And here, you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Should not the House of Shammai hold that the animal must remain dedicated if it was designated even in error?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there he asked about his vow of <i>nazir</i>. Because of the vow of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">There never was a direct dedication of the animal, only a designation as <i>nazir</i> sacrifice. If there is no <i>nazir</i>, there is no sacrifice. The Babli rejects this argument and holds (9a) either that for the House of Shammai there can be no dissolution of a vow of <i>nazir</i> because there can be no dissolution of a dedication, or (32a) for the House of Hillel substitutions can be revoked.</i> did his sacrifices become profane.
Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥuna; Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Aḥa, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Everybody agrees that nobody can ask about his substitution<sup class="footnote-marker">75,</sup><i class="footnote">There never was a direct dedication of the animal, only a designation as <i>nazir</i> sacrifice. If there is no <i>nazir</i>, there is no sacrifice. The Babli rejects this argument and holds (9a) either that for the House of Shammai there can be no dissolution of a vow of <i>nazir</i> because there can be no dissolution of a dedication, or (32a) for the House of Hillel substitutions can be revoked.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">No Elder has the power to annul a (forbidden) substitution.</i>. Where do they disagree? About his dedication; for the House of Shammai say, since a person cannot ask about his substitution, he cannot ask about his dedication, but the House of Hillel say, a person can ask about his dedication but not about his substitution<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to read <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a> according to the House of Hillel but the interpretation is confirmed by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 31a" href="/Nazir.31a">Babli, 31a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Bar Qappara: You catch him at the mention of “ox”, at the mention of “first”<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">He denies that the reason of the House of Shammai be a comparison with the rules of substitutions but holds that they interpret any statement of dedication for the maximum benefit of the Temple (similar to the position of Rav Papa in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 32a" href="/Nazir.32a">Babli, 32a</a>).</i>. “The black ox which comes out of my house first,” if a white one came out and the black followed him, you catch him because he is the leader of the black ones. “The white ox which comes out of my house first,” if a black one came out and the white followed him, you catch him because he is the leader of the white ones. “The ox which stands at the manger” and it was lying down, “lying down” and it was standing, should be like what Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he wanted to give heave from wheat and he gave from barley, during nighttime he did not do anything, during daytime, what he did is done<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Since at night he could not see what he did, we have to take his word as expressing his intent. Therefore, the heave designated during nighttime is not sanctified. But during daytime, his action overrides his words and the heave from barley is sanctified. Similarly, the House of Shammai will sanctify the ox if the dedication was made under circumstances in which his action can override his words.</i>. Brown grain and it turned out to be white<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">For the spelling and definition of these words, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.4.2"><i>Peah</i> 2:5, Notes 85–86</a>. אגרו, אגדו is human food, שחמתית שמתית usually is animal feed.</i>, even during daytime he did not do anything. “A ram” is nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">If he dedicated some kind of cattle but the animal coming out of his house was a goat or sheep, even the House of Shammai will agree that there was no dedication.</i>. “A calf” yes, since it is written: “A calf of cattle as purification offering.” “A sheep” is nothing. “Tetradrachmas” is nothing. “Change” is nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.1">Mishnah 2</a>. If he dedicated the first “denar” which he takes out of his wallet, the House of Shammai will agree that if he takes out only small change, none of which carries the denomination “denar”, it is not dedicated. Also tetradrachmas are never called “denar”. {<i>Denar</i> might mean “money” or “coin” in general. Denominations in late provincial usage may not correspond to standard values.}</i>. “A gold denar”<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Even though an unspecified "denar" in normal speech means "silver denar", the House of Hillel might agree that in a dedication it does apply to an aureus, a gold denar (in honest coin worth 25 silver denars) even though it is always referred to as "gold denar", not simply "denar".</i> was sanctified.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person who made a vow of <i>nazir</i>, asked the Sages and they forbade, counts from the moment of his vow<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">He regretted his vow. When the vow was confirmed, the time of his regret is also counted.</i>. If he asked the Sages and they permitted, if he had an animal designated, it leaves and grazes with the herd<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.1">Mishnah 4</a>. If a person makes a vow of <i>nazir</i>, designates an animal as his sacrifice at completion, and then asks about his vow and has it annulled, the animal becomes profane.</i>. The house of Hillel said to the House of Shammai: Do you not agree that this is dedication in error, it leaves and grazes in the herd<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the principle announced in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> does not always apply.</i>? The House of Shammai anwered, do you not agree that if somebody erred and designated the ninth as the tenth, or the tenth as ninth, or the eleventh as tenth, it is sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 9:8" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.9.8">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 9:8</a>. Every tenth newborn animal of cattle, sheep, and goats has to be brought as sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev</i>. 27:31</a>). The sacrifice is eaten by the rancher and his family; the priests have no part in it. The verse requires that the animals be counted with a staff and the tenth be designated. If the rancher erred between 9, 10, 11, all animals involved are sanctified. But if the error was by a count of more than one, there is no dedication and the animals remain profane.</i>? The House of Hillel anwered, not the staff sanctified it, for if he erred and put his staff on the eighth or the twelth, did he do anything? But the verse which sanctified the tenth sanctified the ninth and the eleventh<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev</i>. 27:31</a>, interpreted in the Halakhah, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 61a" href="/Bekhorot.61a">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 61a</a>, and <i>Sifra Beḥuqqotay Pereq</i> 13(2).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person who made a vow of <i>nazir</i>,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 3:19, referring to the first sentence of the Mishnah.</i>: The House of Shammai say, from the moment he asked; but the House of Hillel say, from the moment he vowed. Where do we hold? If he scoffed<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">For גלגל = לגלג cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:47" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.47"><i>Peah</i> 1:1, Note 178</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.2.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:3, Note 130</a>).</i> at his vow, everybody agrees from the moment he asked<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">If he did not keep his vow, the time elapsed cannot be counted. The questions to be raised here are treated at the end of the paragraph.</i>. If he did not scoff at his vow, everybody agrees from the moment he vowed. But we have to deal with one who is going to ask. The House of Shammai say, since he decided to ask, he is now scoffing. But the House of Hillel say, if he were scoffing, he would not ask. If he made a vow of <i>nazir</i> and scoffed at his vow, one does not let him ask about it unless he kept its prohibitions for the number of days he did not keep the prohibitions, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Yasa said, when has this been said? If he vowed a lengthy period<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">More than 30 days.</i>. But if he vowed a short period, thirty days are sufficient for him. What is the difference between a lengthy and a short period<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In any case, one requires that he keep his vow the number of days for which he vowed.</i>? But it must be: When has this been said? If he scoffed at his vow for a lengthy period. But if he scoffed at his vow for a short period, thirty days are sufficient for him<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The minimum of a <i>nazir</i> vow.</i>. Where are we holding? If he scoffed in impurity, everybody agrees that he invalidated everything<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since the impurity of the dead invalidates a vow of <i>nazir</i> by biblical decree.</i>. If he scoffed in shaving, everybody agrees that he invalidated only 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>nazir</i> for many periods shaves every thirty days, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:4</a>.</i>. But we must deal with the case that he scoffed with wine<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">This is forbidden for the <i>nazir</i>; he is whipped if his transgression is observed by two witnesses after due warning. Biblical law contains no instructions which would require him to continue his vow for additional days. R. Jehudah’s rule which requires him to continue for an additional time, equal to that in which he disregarded the prohibition of wine, is purely rabbinical.</i>. That is, if he is still within his period of <i>nazir</i>. But after his period of <i>nazir</i>, he invalidated everything<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">It follows from R. Jehudah’s rule that he has to start anew if he disregarded the prohibition of wine during the entire prior period of his vow.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 9:8" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.9.8">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 9:8</a>. This starts the discussion of the rules of animal tithes (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.1">Note 87</a>).</i>, we have stated: “If he called the ninth tenth, and the tenth ninth, and the eleventh tenth, all three are sanctified. The ninth may be eaten when it develops a defect<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">As explained later in this paragraph, the ninth is holy but is not dedicated as a sacrifice. Therefore, it is barred from becoming a sacrifice. The owner has to wait until it develops a blemish (which will be automatic with age); then it can be redeemed and eaten under the rules of profane slaughter.</i>, the tenth is tithe, the eleventh is brought as well-being sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">It is holy and a sacrifice, but not tithe. Therefore, the priest’s parts are due from it.</i>.” “It shall be holy”<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:32" href="/Leviticus.27.32"><i>Lev</i>. 27:32</a>: “About tithes of cattle and flocks, anything which passes under the staff, the tenth shall be holy for the Eternal.” Since the determination of which animal is the tenth is not automatic, any which either is the tenth or is designated as the tenth by the rancher’s staff is holy.</i>, this teaches that holiness falls on the ninth and the eleventh. <sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 61a" href="/Bekhorot.61a">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 61a</a>, <i>Sifra Wayyiqra Pereq</i> 16(4).</i> I could think that both of them should be sacrificed, the verse says “cattle”<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3:1" href="/Leviticus.3.1"><i>Lev</i>. 3:1</a>, speaking of well-being sacrifices.</i>, “cattle” to include the eleventh. “From the cattle”<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">In all situations, the expression מן “<i>from</i>” is interpreted to mean: “Not all.” It is then a problem of rabbinic interpretation to determine which kinds are excluded. Since the expression “<i>from</i> the cattle” mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:3" href="/Leviticus.1.3"><i>Lev</i>. 1:3</a> was used to exclude animals used for sinful purposes [<i>Sifra Wayyiqra Parsheta</i> 2(7)], the expression used here can be taken to apply to other situations.</i>, to exclude the ninth. What reason do you have to include the eleventh and to exclude the ninth? After the verse included, it excluded. You can argue, when are sanctified [animals] subject to substitution<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Substitutions of tithe animals arc mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:33" href="/Leviticus.27.33"><i>Lev</i>. 27:33</a>.</i>? Before<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">There can be no substitution for an animal which has not yet been designated as sacrifice since that would be a purely profane operation.</i> or after? One has to say, afterwards. I am including the eleventh which is after sanctification and I am excluding the ninth which is before sanctification.
So far, if he thought that it was the tenth which he called “tenth”. If he knew that it was the ninth and called it “tenth”? The colleagues say, it is sanctified. Rebbi Yudan said, it is not sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 32a" href="/Nazir.32a">Babli, 32a</a>, the different opinions are quoted in the name of Babylonian authorities.</i>. The Mishnah supports the colleagues: “The House of Hillel said to the House of Shammai: Do you not agree that it leaves and grazes with the herd? The House of Shammai anwered them, do you not agree that if somebody erred and designated the ninth as the tenth, or the tenth as ninth, or the eleventh as tenth, it is sanctified.” And they do not accept it! Otherwise, they could have answered: Why do you answer us from something which is sanctified only if not in [willful] error about something which will be sanctified both in error and not in error! As Rebbi Immi said, they were rich in answer<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The Talmudim never exhaust all possible arguments but quote only one. The text is better in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:1:19-2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.1.19-2.3"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 6:2</a> (32b): עֲשִׁירִין הֲווּ בִתְשׁוּבוֹת.</i>, or as Rebbi Nasa said, like somebody who has two reasons and he responds by giving one of them.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person vowed to be a <i>nazir</i> and went to bring his animal<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">And from the start it was his intention to use this animal as his sacrifice at the end of the period of <i>nezirut</i>.</i> when he found that it was stolen; if he vowed before the animal was stolen he is a <i>nazir</i>, after the animal was stolen he is not a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vow was made in error.</i>. This error was made by Naḥum from Media: When <i>nezirim</i> came from the Diaspora<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The Parthian empire, which was not touched by the Jewish revolt.</i> and found that the Temple had been destroyed, Naḥum from Media asked them: If you had known that the Temple would be destroyed, would you have made a vow of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The question was legitimate for R. Eliezer (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.2.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 9:2</a>) who admits that people may ask to annul a vow because of unforseeable circumstances, but the anonymous majority forbid this kind of question.</i>? They said to him, no, and Naḥum from Media permitted them. When the case came before the Sages they said, anyone who made his vow before the Temple was destroyed is a <i>nazir</i>, after the Temple was destroyed he is not a <i>nazir</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a person vowed to be a <i>nazir</i> and went to bring his animal,” etc. Where do we hold? If he saw an animal passing on the market and said, I am a <i>nazir</i> on that animal which passed by, he is a <i>nazir</i> even if the animal was stolen<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Since the animal was not his and he could not be sure that the owners would sell it to him, his vow was not dependent on that animal (unless he would spell out that he would not be a <i>nazir</i> unless the animal was sold to him).</i>. If he thought that he had one and it turned out that he did not, would we say if he was rich and became poor, the <i>nezirut</i> would be invalidated? But we must hold that he said, I am a <i>nazir</i> on the animal I have at home, then went and found it stolen. If he vowed before the animal was stolen he is a <i>nazir</i>, after the animal was stolen he is not a <i>nazir</i>. The students of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Julianus say: Rebbi Jehudah asked: If the thieves returned it in the night, did his <i>nezirut</i> return to him retroactively<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">From the moment of his vow.</i> or for the future<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">He has to start anew from the moment the animal was returned. No answer is given.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.2.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 9:2, Notes 48–49</a>.</i>“That was the error of Naḥum from Media.” What was his error? That he found for them an opening due to changed circumstances. “Naḥum from Media said to them: ‘Would you have made a vow to become <i>nezirim</i> if you had known that the Temple would be destroyed at some future time?’ ” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the following he should have said to them: Did you not know that the prophets already had prophesied that eventually the Temple would be destroyed? Then there are no changed circumstances. Rebbi Hila said, still it is changed circumstances. They could have said to him, we knew it, but it seemed to us that this referred to the distant future: “The vision he sees is for many years.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 7:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.7.2.7"><i>Berakhot</i> 7:2, Notes 79–90</a>.</i> It has been stated: 300 <i>nezirim</i> came in the days of Rebbi Simeon ben Shetaḥ. For 150 of them he found an opening, for 150 of them he did not find an opening. He came to king Yannai and said to him: There are here 300 <i>nezirim</i> who need 900 sacrifices. You should give half of them from your side, I shall give half from my side. The king sent him 450 animals. An informer went around and said that the other one had not given anything from his own money. King Yannai heard about it and got angry. Simeon ben Shetaḥ heard and fled. After some time, important people from the Persian empire came to king Yannai. They said to him: We remember that there was an old man who gave us a rabbinic discourse. They said to him, send and bring him! He sent and gave him his word; he came and sat between king and queen. He said to him, why did you trick me? He said, I did not trick you; you with your money and I with my learning, as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 7:12" href="/Ecclesiastes.7.12"><i>Eccl</i>. 7:12</a>) “In the shadow of wisdom, in the shadow of money.” He said to him, why did you disappear? He said to him, I heard that my lord was angry with me and I wanted to fulfill the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 26:20" href="/Isaiah.26.20"><i>Is</i>. 26:20</a>) “Hide a little bit until the rage passes;” he used about himself (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 7:12" href="/Ecclesiastes.7.12"><i>Eccl</i>. 7:12</a>): “Knowledge is an advantage, wisdom lets its possessor live.” He said to him, why did you sit between king and queen? He said to him, it is written in the book of Ben Sirach: “Esteem it and it will raise you and seat you among princes.” He said, bring him a cup that he may recite Grace. They brought him a cup and he said: “Let us give praise for the food that Yannai and his company ate.” He said to him, what should I say, “for the food that we did not eat?” He said, bring him something that he may eat. They brought, he ate and recited: “For the food that we ate.” <sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph has no place here, it refers to the discussion in <i>Berakhot</i>.</i> Rebbi Yoḥanan said, his colleagues disagree with Simeon ben Shetaḥ. Rebbi Jeremiah said, about the first action; Rebbi Abba said, about the second action. Rebbi Jeremiah seems to contradict himself! There he wondered about it and here it is obvious for him! He wondered following the Sages; it is obvious to him following Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. We have stated about this: If one came, was lying on a couch, and dipped with them, even if he did not eat grain the volume of an olive one ‘invites’ with him, the words of the Sages. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yoḥanan: One never ‘invites’ anyone unless he has eaten grain the volume of an olive. But did we not formulate, “two [eating] bread and one [eating] vegetable, then one ‘invites’?” This <i>baraita</i> follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If they were walking on the road and a person came towards them when one said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> unless he is Mr. X”, and another said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if it is not he”; “I am a <i>nazir</i> unless one of you is a <i>nazir</i>”, “unless both of you are <i>nezirim</i>”, “unless all of you are <i>nezirim</i>”. The House of Shammai say, they are all <i>nezirim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">By their rule, anybody who said “I am a <i>nazir</i>” is a <i>nazir</i>, even if his condition was not satisfied.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, only those whose assertions prove wrong are <i>nezirim</i>. Rebbi Ṭarphon said, none of them is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a> requires that a vow of <i>nazir</i> be clearly expressed, but these people did mention <i>nazir</i> only to emphasize their statements, there is no valid vow.</i>.<br>If he suddenly returned, no one is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This is a continuation of the previous Mishnah. The object of the disagreement of the travelers suddenly disappears and it is not possible to determine who is right and who is wrong, who should be a <i>nazir</i> and who should not.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, one<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Everyone whose vow is in doubt. On the one hand, not to fulfill one’s vow is a grave sin; on the other hand, the sacrifices at the end of the period of <i>nezirut</i> can be offered only if they are due, otherwise the animals would be sinful profane offerings in the Temple. Therefore, it is necessary to offer a new vow which takes care of all possibilities.</i> should say: If it was as I said, I am a <i>nazir</i> by obligation, otherwise I am a <i>nazir</i> voluntarily. If one saw a <i>koy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">An animal neither wild nor domesticated, which partially follows the rules of both kinds (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.6.1">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 2:9</a> ff., Note 154).</i> and said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if this is a wild animal”, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if this is not a wild animal”<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">These are statements by different prople walking together.
