Since all assertions are more or less true, all persons involved are <i>nezirim</i>.</i>, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if this is a domestic animal”, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if this is a not a domestic animal”, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if this is a wild and domestic animal”, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if this is neither a wild nor a domestic animal”, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if one of you is a <i>nazir</i>”, “if one of you is not a <i>nazir</i>”, “if both of you are <i>nezirim</i>”, then all of them are <i>nezirim</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If they were walking on the road,” etc. Should the Mishnah not read: “whose assertions are correct”?<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">If Mishnah 6 is translated strictly literally, it reads: “I am a <i>nazir</i> if he is Mr. X”, and another said, “I am a <i>nazir</i> if he is not”; “I am a <i>nazir</i> if one of you is a <i>nazir</i>”, “if both of you are <i>nezirim</i>”, “if all of you are <i>nezirim</i>”. In that case, the House of Hillel should state that the ones whose statements are correct are <i>nezirim</i>.</i> It is language of opposites, “that she did not bury her son.”<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">One does not want to express anything negative. A woman who is afraid for the life of her son will assert that she will not bury her son. A different explanation of the paragraph is given by J. N. Epstein, מבוא לנוסח המשנה<sup>2</sup> pp. 332–335, more in accordance with the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 32b-33a" href="/Nazir.32b-33a">Babli (32b–33a</a>).</i>
It was stated: “Rebbi Jehudah said in the name of Rebbi Ṭarphon: None of them is a <i>nazir</i> since <i>nezirut</i> exists only by warning.”<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 34a" href="/Nazir.34a">Babli 34a</a>, Tosephta 3:19, “<i>nezirut</i> exists only by הפלאה ‘clear statement’ ”. The meaning is the same here; a legal warning for a breach of the vow could only be issued if the vow was clearly stated.</i> That is what Rebbi Jehudah said, “doubtful <i>nezirut</i> is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 4:12" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.4.12">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i> 4:12</a>.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.4.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> Where do we hold? If one says, Reuben, and the other says, Simeon, as you take it, if he is Reuben, one is a <i>nazir</i>, if he is Simeon, one is a<i>nazir</i>. But we must hold that one says, Reuben, and the other says, Simeon. He suddenly disappeared, he was neither Reuben nor Simeon; nobody is a <i>nazir</i>.
That is what Rebbi Simeon said, he cannot bring a sacrifice unless it exists<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Simeon parallels his own in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.8.1">Mishnah 2:8</a>, regarding the man who vowed to be <i>nazir</i> if his wife bore a son and she had a miscarriage.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, 3:19, this refers to people who meet a hermaphrodite and quarrel whether or not he is a man, a woman, or a man and a woman. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 34a" href="/Nazir.34a">Babli, 34a</a>, there is a question whether to read “9 <i>neziriot</i>”, or “9 <i>nezirim</i>”. The formulation of the Mishnah in the Yerushalmi clearly speaks of only two people who dispute and pile vow onto vow.</i>: “All of them count nine <i>neziriot</i>”. Are they not ten?<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The questioner has a slightly different reading in the Mishnah which exhausts all logical possibilities: Wild animal or not, domestic or not, wild and domestic or not, neither wild nor domestic, one or none or both <i>nezirim</i>.</i> It is impossible that the words of any of them should not be correct. Rebbi Yasa said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta which counts 9 possibilities.</i> is the House of Shammai’s, since the House of Shammai say, dedication in error is dedication.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Three kinds are forbidden for the <i>nazir</i>: Impurity, shaving, and anything coming from the vine. Everything coming from the vine is added together<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The verse prohibiting “anything coming from the vine” to the <i>nazir</i> makes it clear that the most minute amount is forbidden. But a <i>nazir</i> cannot cleanse himself by a purification sacrifice (or, if duly warned by two witnesses, cannot be criminally prosecuted for desecrating his holy status) unless he consumed more than the legal minimum. For most matters of food, the legal minimum is the size of an average olive used for the production of oil (cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, Northvale 1995, pp. 326–328). For beverages, the standard is the <i>revi‘it</i>, definined by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:11" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.11">Mishnah (<i>Kelim</i> 17:11</a>) as Roman <i>quartarius</i>, 133 ml (<i>loc. cit</i>. pp. 212–213).</i>. He is only guilty when he eats grapes in the volume of an olive; according to the early Mishnah if he drinks a <i>quartarius</i> of wine<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Or eats an amount of grapes from which a <i>quartarius</i> of juice could be extracted.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, even if he dipped his bread in wine for a total volume<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The bread plus the wine absorbed in it.</i> of an olive, he is guilty.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Three kinds are forbidden for the <i>nazir</i>,” etc. Impurity, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:6" href="/Leviticus.6.6"><i>Lev</i>. 6:6</a>.</i>: “During all the days he vowed to the Eternal he shall not come close to a human corpse.” Shaving, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:5" href="/Leviticus.6.5"><i>Lev</i>. 6:5</a>.</i>: “During all the days of his <i>nazir</i> vow, a shaving knife shall not come onto his head.” Anything from the vine, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:4" href="/Leviticus.6.4"><i>Lev</i>. 6:4</a>.</i>: “During all the days of his vow, of anything coming from the wine-vine [he shall not eat.]”
<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are from <i>Šabbat</i> 7:2 (9c, 1. 11 ff.), as will be seen in the commentary. The variant readings refer to that text. The introductory section is from <i>Šabbat</i> 7:1 (9a, 1. 20–24), the one variant in spelling there is noted by: <b>א</b>.<br>Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 7:2 states that on the Sabbath, 39 different activities are forbidden. This means that a person who violates the Sabbath unintentionally may be liable for up to 39 purification sacrifices. The question then appears whether in other cases multiple sacrifices also are necessary.</i> Rav Zakkai stated before Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody sacrificed, burned incense, and poured a libation in one forgetting<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">He committed idolatry but forgot that sacrificing, burning incense, and pouring libations are forbidden as idolatrous actions, or he was conscious that these acts are part of idolatry but forgot that idolatry was forbidden.</i>, he is guilty for each action separately<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 72a" href="/Shabbat.72a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 72a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 62a" href="/Sanhedrin.62a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 62a</a>, the positions of R. Joḥanan and R. Zakkai are switched.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan told him, Babylonian! You crossed three rivers with your hands<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Tigris, Euphrates, and Jordan.</i> and were broken. He is guilty only once! <sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The sentences in braces are unintelligible here; they refer to and are quoted from a discussion in <i>Šabbat</i> 7:1 (fol. 9a) which deals with the introductory sentence to the chapter of purification offerings, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:2" href="/Leviticus.4.2"><i>Lev</i>. 4:2</a>: “Speak to the Children of Israel, saying: If a person sins unintentionally against any commandments of the Eternal that are not to be broken, and did <i>from any one, from those</i>.” This implies that sometimes a purification offering is due for violating <i>one</i> prohibition, and sometimes one sacrifice is valid for a number of <i>those</i>. In general, the answer depends on what was unintentional. If a person does not know that today is Sabbath, for all he does wrong he owes one sacrifice. If he knows that it is Sabbath but forgot what is forbidden, he owes one sacrifice for each category of forbidden work. The problem is first whether this principle also applies to idolatry, the sacrifice for which is not described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev</i>. 4</a> but in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:22-26" href="/Numbers.15.22-26"><i>Num</i>. 15:22–26</a>, and second what is the status of the details enumerated in the Second Commandment, in particular why a detail, “do not prostrate yourself before them” is mentioned before the principle “do not serve them”.</i>{Before he broke<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The reference to “breaking” here is a continuation of R. Joḥanan’s criticism of Rav Zakkai (who in the Babli is Rebbi Zakkai): If the Second Commandment is considered a unit, there are no “those” to be applied to idolatry. If all activities mentioned are separate rules, how can one bring only one sacrifice?</i> in his hand there is “one” but not “those”; after he broke in his hand there are “those” but not “one”.} Rebbi Abba bar Mamal asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Should he not be guilty for each action separately? As you say for the Sabbath: “Do not perform any work<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:10" href="/Exodus.20.10"><i>Ex</i>. 20:10</a>.</i>,” principle. “Do not light fire in any of your dwelling places,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 35:3" href="/Exodus.35.3"><i>Ex</i>. 35:3</a>.</i>” a detail. Was not lighting fire subsumed under the principle, but it is mentioned separately from this principle! Since lighting fire is special in that it is the work of a single individual<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A forbidden action on the Sabbath which is executed only by the common effort of several people is not prosecutable.</i> and one would be guilty for it alone, so everything for which alone one is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Needs a separate sacrifice. This is an application of the 9th hermeneutical principle of R. Ismael: Any detail which was subsumed under a principle but is mentioned separately in order to instruct, was not mentioned for itself but to explain the entire principle [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 2" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.2"><i>Sifra Introduction</i> 2</a>; <i>Pereq</i> 1(1)]. In the text this is called “principle and detail”, which in the technical language of the Babli refers to the completely different rule No. 5 [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 1" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.1"><i>Sifra Introduction</i> (1</a>,7)]. In <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i> p. 347 the argument is attributed to R. Jonathan (who in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 70a" href="/Shabbat.70a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 70a</a>, appears as R. Nathan.)<br>Whether there is a connection between rules 5 and 9 is left open in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 85a" href="/Bava_Kamma.85a">Babli, <i>Baba qama</i> 85a</a>, decided in the negative in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 55b" href="/Menachot.55b"><i>Menaḥot</i> 55b</a>. Menahem Cahana, in an exhaustive study of the problem (קווים לתולדות התפתחותה של מידת כלל ופרט בתקופת התנאים p. 173–216 in: Studies in Talmudic and Midrashic Literature in Memory of Tirzah Lifshitz) holds that the original Tannaïtic theory knew only of two principles, one which corresponded to the later (Babli, Sifra, Sifry) rules entitled “principle and detail”, “detail and principle”, “principle and detail and principle”; the other one referring to all rules which in Babylonian formulation start with “any detail which was subsumed under a principle”. His arguments support the thesis of the present commentary that Mekhilta, Sifra, Sifry (and Tosephta) in our hands are essentially Babylonian editions.</i>. Also here<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Regarding idolatry.</i>: “Do not worship them,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:5" href="/Exodus.20.5"><i>Ex</i>. 20:5</a>.</i>” a principle. “Do not prostrate yourself,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:5" href="/Exodus.20.5"><i>Ex</i>. 20:5</a>.</i>” a detail. Was not prostrating itself included in the principle and why was it mentioned separately? To infer, to tell you that prostrating oneself is special in that it is the work of a single individual and one would be guilty for it alone, so everything for which alone one is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Needs a separate sacrifice. This is an application of the 9th hermeneutical principle of R. Ismael: Any detail which was subsumed under a principle but is mentioned separately in order to instruct, was not mentioned for itself but to explain the entire principle [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 2" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.2"><i>Sifra Introduction</i> 2</a>; <i>Pereq</i> 1(1)]. In the text this is called “principle and detail”, which in the technical language of the Babli refers to the completely different rule No. 5 [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 1" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.1"><i>Sifra Introduction</i> (1</a>,7)]. In <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i> p. 347 the argument is attributed to R. Jonathan (who in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 70a" href="/Shabbat.70a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 70a</a>, appears as R. Nathan.)<br>Whether there is a connection between rules 5 and 9 is left open in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 85a" href="/Bava_Kamma.85a">Babli, <i>Baba qama</i> 85a</a>, decided in the negative in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 55b" href="/Menachot.55b"><i>Menaḥot</i> 55b</a>. Menahem Cahana, in an exhaustive study of the problem (קווים לתולדות התפתחותה של מידת כלל ופרט בתקופת התנאים p. 173–216 in: Studies in Talmudic and Midrashic Literature in Memory of Tirzah Lifshitz) holds that the original Tannaïtic theory knew only of two principles, one which corresponded to the later (Babli, Sifra, Sifry) rules entitled “principle and detail”, “detail and principle”, “principle and detail and principle”; the other one referring to all rules which in Babylonian formulation start with “any detail which was subsumed under a principle”. His arguments support the thesis of the present commentary that Mekhilta, Sifra, Sifry (and Tosephta) in our hands are essentially Babylonian editions.</i>. He answered<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ze‘ira, answering R. Abba bar Mamal. The translation follows the text in <i>Šabbat</i>.</i>: For the Sabbath, he mentioned the principle at one place and the details at another place. For idol worship, the principle is found close to the detail<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In the same sentence. If “prostrating” had been mentioned after “serving”, the 5th hermeneutical principle would imply that the two notions are identical in intent. As the verse stands, it cannot be interpreted as “principle and detail”.</i>. He retorted: Is it not witten: “Do not prostrate yourself before another power”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:14" href="/Exodus.34.14"><i>Ex</i>. 34:14</a>.</i>? He did not state the principle and the detail at the same spot! He said, since you do not infer anything from it close up, you cannot infer anything from afar<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since 34:14 does not teach anything not contained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:5" href="/Exodus.20.5"><i>Ex</i>. 20:5</a>.</i>. The colleagues say, it makes no difference; whether He gave the principle at one place and the detail at another, or gave principle and detail at the same place, it is a matter of principle and detail. For the Sabbath, He first gave the principle and then the detail. For idolatry, He gave the detail and only later the principle<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the 9th principle does not apply to idolatry since the detail does not follow after the principle.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, it makes no difference whether <sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Text from <i>Šabbat</i>.</i>[He first gave the principle and then the detail or He gave the detail and only later the principle, or He gave principle, detail, and principle<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This really is the case for the Second Comandment.</i>]; it is a matter of principle and detail. For the Sabbath, He gave a general prohibition of work, followed by details; for idolatry, He was indeterminate regarding its worship but detailed the worship of Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition refers to performing for idolatry any ceremony commanded for the worship of Heaven. The case of R. Zakkai really has no connection with the argument about the status of the mention of prostrating oneself in the Second Commandment.</i>.
Rebbi Mana said, lighting fire was mentioned unnecessarily<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since the prohibition of making fire is implied in the Fourth Commandment in any reasonable interpretation. Therefore, making fire is a detail which can be used to characterize <i>all</i> work forbidden on the Sabbath.</i>; prostrating oneself was mentioned by necessity to explain about itself since it is not work<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing is changed or produced by prostrating oneself; it is not obvious that it should be forbidden under any circumstances.</i>. This follows what Ḥizqiah stated: “He who sacrifices to powers shall be banned<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:19" href="/Exodus.22.19"><i>Ex</i>. 22:19</a>. This explains the punishment for idolatrous acts forbidden in the Second Commandment. This is the interpretation in all of talmudic literature (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 60b" href="/Sanhedrin.60b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 60b</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i> p. 310, <i>dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> p. 210.) (Nowhere in rabbinic literature does one find the more obvious explanation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:19" href="/Exodus.22.19">Ex. 22:19</a>: “Anyone sacrificing to <i>the Elohim</i> (God as Creator, Ruler of the physical world) shall be banned, only to YHWH (God the Merciful and Dispenser of Grace) alone.” In all of <i>Lev</i>. and <i>Num</i>., there is never any mention of a sacrifice to <i>Elohim</i>.}</i>.” Sacrificing was mentioned separately to teach about everything<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since punishment for sacrificing is spelled out separately, any punishment for an act of idolatry must be given separately by the 9th rule, supporting R. Zakkai against R. Joḥanan.</i>, prostrating oneself to explain about itself since it is not work. Rebbi Jeremiah said, lighting fire was mentioned by necessity, to teach that courts should not sit on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 6b" href="/Yevamot.6b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 6b</a>, this is a Tannaïtic statement from the school of R. Ismael, appended to an argument also quoted in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i>, ויקהל.</i>. What is the reason? It says here, “in all your settlements” and it says there, “these should be rules of law for your generations, in all your settlements<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:29" href="/Numbers.35.29"><i>Num</i>. 35:29</a>. The quote is correct in <i>Šabbat</i>.</i>.” Since “settlements” mentioned there refers to courts, “settlements” referred to here also refers to courts. Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon said, even if you say that it was mentioned by necessity, it is as if it were mentioned unnecessarily<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since the argument is based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:29" href="/Numbers.35.29"><i>Num</i>. 35:29</a>, not on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:19" href="/Exodus.22.19"><i>Ex</i>. 22:19</a>, the latter verse can be used in an application of the 9th rule.</i>, and anything mentioned unnecessarily teaches.
But here, skins and seeds were understood in the principle, and were listed separately<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num</i>. 6:4</a>, it is started that a <i>nazir</i> is forbidden “everything coming from the vine”, followed by “skins and seeds”. Since skins and seeds of grapes come from the vine, this is “principle and detail” and the question arises why the Mishnah prescribes that all that comes from the vine be counted together; should not every kind be counted separately?</i>. Should they not be separate rather than common? But there<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of the Sabbath.</i>, the principle is at one place and the details are at another place. But did not the colleagues say: It makes no difference: Whether he stated the principle and then a detail or the detail and after that the principle. And here, he stated the principle and then a detail. But did not Rebbi Yose say, there is no difference whether He gave principle, detail, and principle, it is the principle and then a detail, it is counted as the principle and then a detail. There, the detail was not necessary. Why were they detailed? To exclude leaves and twigs<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Which may not be directly edible. But vine leaves are used in cooking, supporting R. Eliezer.</i>. But was it not stated in the name of Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 34b" href="/Nazir.34b">Babli 34b</a>, <i>Sifry Num</i>. #24; rejected in the Babli.</i>: “From anything coming from the wine-vine, from skins to seeds, he shall not eat;” leaves and twigs are also understood. There<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">While this paragraph is not found in <i>Šabbat</i>, it seems to have originated there since “there” is here, the discussion of the rules of <i>nazir</i>, but “here” is <i>Šabbat</i>, the discussion of the mention of making fire. In the case of the <i>nazir</i>, everything that comes from the vine is added together (one minimum quantity, one sacrifice) since everything mentioned (including in v. 3 wine, liquor, vinegar, grapes and raisins) is necessary as explained in <i>Sifry Num</i>. #23–24.</i>, they are mentioned for a need, but here, they are mentioned without need. Why? For additions<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Reading לְצֵירוּפִין for לצירוכין of the text.</i>.
Warning<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">An infraction of a biblical law is prosecutable only if the prohibition is mentioned at least twice in the text, once as “warning” to spell out the prohibition and once to specify the punishment for infraction. If no punishment is specified, whipping is intended; nevertheless, the second mention is necessary. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 11:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.11.1.11"><i>Yebamot</i> 11:1, Note 47</a>.</i> for one who eats carcass meat, from where? “You shall not eat any carcass meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>” That covers carcass meat; from a “torn”<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭerephah</i> is a technical term, originally meaning an animal which cannot survive an attack by a predator. The meaning has been extended to include all animals who cannot survive for any length of time, including dangerously sick animals and those born with severe birth defects. (As a practical matter, slaughtered animals have to be inspected for signs of tuberculosis, which would prohibit the meat for human consumption.)</i> animal from where? Rebbi Joḥanan said, “carcass meat” and “any carcass meat”, to include the “torn” animal<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The verse must forbid more than carcass meat, otherwise the mention of “all” was superfluous. The argument is reported as tannaitic in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 104.</i>. If somebody eats flesh from a living animal which is “torn”, Rebbi Yasa said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagree. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he is guilty twice, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he is guilty only once. What is the reason of Rebbi Joḥanan? “You shall not eat any carcass meat<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>;” “you shall not eat of life with the flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">45,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:23" href="/Deuteronomy.12.23">Deut. 12:23</a>. It is forbidden to eat limbs torn from a living animal. (In rabbinic interpretation, this is the prohibition imposed on all mankind by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:4" href="/Genesis.9.4">Gen. 9:4</a>: "But meat in whose blood is life you shall not eat", meat taken when life is still carried by the blood.)</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is that in one act one may transgress two prohibitions referring to two distinct verses as warnings and, therefore, be subject to distinct punishments. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 102b-103a" href="/Chullin.102b-103a">Babli, Hulin 102b/103a</a>, the difference between the interpretations of R. Johanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish boils down to the question whether "flesh from a living animal" and "limbs from a living animal " are different prohibitions following distinct rules. (For the problems raised by the competition of laws, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.6">Terumot 7:1, Notes 6</a> ff.)</i>.” What is the reason of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose: The assertion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish parallels what Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob stated: “ ‘Flesh torn on the field you shall not eat’<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex</i>. 22:30</a>. In this interpretation, the verse forbids flesh or limbs torn from an animal (and also supports R. Joḥanan’s interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.) A similar formulation, also in the name of R. Eliezer ben Jacob, is in <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i>, p. 214.</i>, you shall not tear from an animal and eat in the way you tear from the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Vegetables.</i> and eat.” What is the reason of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? The colleagues before Rebbi Yose: Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish does not hold with Rebbi Joḥanan about the “torn” animal; if he did hold with him, one should be twice guilty. He said to them, even if he held with him, one should be guilty only once. There is a difference, because He repeated it and combined it<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to say that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex</i>. 22:30</a> does not contain a prohibition of meat from “torn” animals, since this is the obvious meaning of the text. But since following R. Eliezer ben Jacob, the verse also prohibits flesh torn from living animals, there is no separate “warning” for eating meat from “torn” animals. The offender can be prosecuted either on basis of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a> or of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex</i>. 22:30</a>, but not of both together. (Since in the desert, consumption of any non-sacrificial meat of domesticated animals was forbidden, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:4" href="/Leviticus.17.4"><i>Lev</i>. 17:4</a>, the mention of carcass meat would have been out of place in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22" href="/Exodus.22"><i>Ex</i>. 22</a>.)</i>. They objected: “Suet you shall not eat,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:24" href="/Leviticus.7.24"><i>Lev</i>. 7:24</a>.</i>” “and blood you shall not eat,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:26" href="/Leviticus.7.26"><i>Lev</i>. 7:26</a>.</i>” and it is written: “Any suet and any blood you shall not eat.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3:17" href="/Leviticus.3.17"><i>Lev</i>. 3:17</a>.</i>” Then because He repeated it and combined it, one should be guilty only once! He said to them, if it were written “suet and blood”, you would be correct. But it is written “<i>any</i> suet and <i>any</i> blood,” to declare him guilty for each case separately. But it is not written: “Anything soaked with grapes he shall not drink<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:3" href="/Numbers.6.3"><i>Num</i>. 6:3</a>.</i>,” and it is written, “from skins to seeds he shall not eat<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num</i>. 6:4</a>.</i>.” Then because He repeated it and combined it, one should be guilty only once!<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">But <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.2.1">Mishnah 6:2</a> will state that the <i>nazir</i> can be punished separately for each item on the list.</i> He said to them, if it were written “skins and seeds”, you would be correct. But it is written “skins <i>unto</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">A redundant word, not really required by the context.</i> seeds,” to declare him guilty for each case separately.
Rebbi [Yose]<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in ms. and <i>editio princeps</i>; needed by the context and historical considerations.</i> said to the colleagues: You should know that Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagree: If somebody split a limb from a living animal and ate it, everybody agrees that he is free from prosecution<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">He has an olive-sized piece of flesh taken from a living animal and splits it in two. Neither piece has the minimum size that would make prosecution possible. He eats both pieces in one meal. As Rashi explains in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 103b" href="/Chullin.103b"><i>Ḥulin</i> 103b (<i>s.v.</i> מהו</a>): “In general, undersized parts of forbidden food consumed in the same meal are added together (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 80b" href="/Yoma.80b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 80b</a>). But the prohibition of limbs from a living animal is particular since in general inedible sinews and bones (of carcass and “torn” meat) are not forbidden food. By contrast, here one would be guilty since there is no limb without sinews and bones. One may say that its rules are separate and if one eats it in one piece one is guilty, since this is the normal way of eating, but not in minute pieces.”</i>. Where do they disagree? If he split it in his mouth before he ate it. Rebbi Joḥanan considers his mouth as inside, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish considers his mouth as outside<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the Babli, for R. Joḥanan the prohibition is triggered by the palate’s enjoyment, for R. Simeon ben Laqish by the act of swallowing.</i>. They asked him, what do you say? He answered them, I informed you that mighty mountains disagree, and you ask this? If it is so, even if he split it outside and then ate, he should be guilty! Why? That is how one eats<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 103b" href="/Chullin.103b">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i> 103b</a>, this is R. Eleazar’s opinion. The configuration of the food is irrelevant.</i>. If somebody split an ant in his mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since an ant is a complete creature, it is forbidden food (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:41" href="/Leviticus.11.41"><i>Lev</i>. 11:41</a>) irrespective of size (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1, Note 17</a>). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 16b" href="/Makkot.16b">Babli, <i>Makkot</i> 16b</a>, eating an ant is counted as violating up to five prohibitions simultaneously.</i> and ate it, that is the disagreement of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Simeon ben Laqish, what he did is forbidden but not prosecutable.</i>. Rebbi Maisha asked Rebbi Ze‘ira: If he<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>nazir</i>, who can be criminallyprosecuted for eating an olive’s volume of grapes.</i> split a grape in his mouth and ate it, is that the disagreement of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? He answered, there<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">A limb taken from a living animal causes impurity (<i>Ahilut</i> 2:1); similarly, crawling animals are called “impure”. An entire crawling animal is always impure. But all parts of such an animal cut into pieces smaller than lentils are pure.</i> it is something for which there is a prohibition and impurity. In a circumstance in which its impurity disappeard, its prohibition disappeared. But here is a prohibition and no impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish will agree that for the <i>nazir</i> only the total amount consumed is relevant.</i>. Rebbi Abba ben Rebbi Mamal asked: If he split an olive-sized bit of <i>mazzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In the first night of Passover, when there is a biblical commandment to eat <i>mazzah</i> (in the minimal amount of one olive’s size); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:18" href="/Exodus.12.18"><i>Ex</i>. 12:18</a>.</i> in his mouth and ate it, is that the disagreement of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, in the other cases, his palate did not enjoy an olive sized bit<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Here also, R. Simeon ben Laqish will agree with R. Joḥanan.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea said that Rebbi Nisa asked: If he split in his mouth pomegranate berries<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The berries of the pomegranate, each containing a single seed. Such a berry is a creature (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1, Note 18</a>).</i> which are <i>‘orlah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Harvested before the tree is three years old, when all usufruct is forbidden.</i> and ate them, is that the disagreement of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? Where do we hold? If because of fluids, we already did state: “Nothing makes impure as a drink except what comes from olives and grapes.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 11:3. Therefore, the pomegranate berries are food and follow the rules of food.</i>” The rabbis of Caesarea said, explain it if he swallowed them<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A pomegranate berry is much smaller than an olive. It is agreed that eating a whole berry of <i>‘orlah</i> can be prosecuted since it means eating a complete creature. But if the berry is eaten after being split into two parts, one might assume that R. Joḥanan will agree with R. Simeon ben Laqish that one cannot be prosecuted.</i>.
A “torn” [creature] that was turned into a carcass<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If a “torn” animal was not slaughtered according to the rules, is the meat forbidden under one or two statutes?</i>. Rebbi Yasa, the son of Rebbi Yasa’s daughter, in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagree. Rebbi Joḥanan says, he is guilty twice; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he is guilty only once. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said before Rebbi Yose, the following<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Acharei Mot, Chapter 11 8" href="/Sifra,_Acharei_Mot,_Chapter_11.8"><i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 11(8</a>).</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “ ‘Any person who would eat any carcass meat<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:15" href="/Leviticus.17.15"><i>Lev</i>. 17:15</a>.</i>,’ why does the verse say ‘and torn’? If a ‘torn’ animal can survive, was ‘carcass meat’ mentioned before<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">No animal is ritually impure while alive. [Even the “impure” animals forbidden as food (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:26" href="/Leviticus.11.26"><i>Lev</i>. 11:26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:9" href="/Deuteronomy.14.9"><i>Deut</i>. 14:9</a>) are ritually pure while alive.] If a “torn” animal can survive and was ritually slaughtered, it does not become impure.</i>? If a ‘torn’ animal must die, is it not included in ‘carcass meat’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If a “torn” animal must die in the short run, it simply becomes impure as a carcass. The mention of “torn” in the verse seems superfluous; it can only be justified as adding another prohibition. (In <i>Sifra</i>, the argument is inverted to prove that a “torn” animal, if ritually slaughtered, is not impure even as it is forbidden food.)</i>? Should he not say, it is carcass meat? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: The reason of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Who seems to accept his son’s argument.</i>: “Do not defile yourself by animals and birds<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The argument refers to the part of the verse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:25" href="/Leviticus.20.25"><i>Lev</i>. 20:25</a>, which is not quoted: “Do not defile yourself by animals and birds, and anything which crawls on the ground, which I separated for you as impure.” Now anything crawling on the ground (and in the water) is forbidden as food, but impurity caused by dead bodies is restricted to mammals, birds, and the Eight Reptiles enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29-30" href="/Leviticus.11.29-30"><i>Lev</i>. 11:29–30</a>.</i>,” but only the Eight Reptiles impart impurity! But the measure of their impurities is the measure of their uses as food<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Since even in biblical usage, mammals and birds acceptable as food are called “pure” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 8:20" href="/Genesis.8.20"><i>Gen</i>. 8:20</a>). By inference, non-kosher animals are called “impure” even while alive and technically pure.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar objected<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">To R. Abbahu.</i>: May not the limbs of pure animals impart impurity in the most minute amount<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 1:7" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.1.7">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 1:7</a>.</i> but as food only in the volume of an olive? He accepted it. What is meant by “he accepted it”? Like a person who said, the opponent accepted it. Ḥiyya bar Abba said: “You shall not [eat] any carcass meat.” The Torah identified all eating together. Rebbi Ḥanina objected: Is not the impurity of the Eight Reptiles in the size of a lentil, but as food in the volume of an olive, whether for blood or for meat<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Meilah 15b" href="/Meilah.15b"><i>Me‘ilah</i> 15b</a>, R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina states, as explanation of his inference (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.1.8">Note 78</a>) that eating parts of any of the Eight Reptiles is prosecutable in amounts the size of a lentil (Maimonides <i>Ma‘akhalot asurot</i> 2:7). The identity of the rules for blood and flesh of these reptiles is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Meilah 4:3" href="/Mishnah_Meilah.4.3">Mishnah <i>Me‘ilah</i> 4:3</a>, for the rules of impurity <i>Sifra Šemini Parašah</i> 5(2), <i>Pereq</i> 7(6).</i>?
Rebbi Samuel bar Sosarti asked: Then one who eats a limb from a kosher living animal should be guilty on two counts<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">He argues against R. Joḥanan who stated that one who eats a limb from a “torn” animal violates two laws at the same time. Why should the verses quoted not apply to a completely healthy kosher animal?</i>, because of “you shall not eat any carcass meat<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since the impurity of limbs from a living animal is identical with the impurity of carcasses, and by the argument of R. Yose ben Hanina food prohibitions follow impurity.</i>;” and because of “you shall not eat of life with the flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:23" href="/Deuteronomy.12.23"><i>Deut</i>. 12:23</a>. It is forbidden to cat limbs torn from a living animal. (In rabbinic interpretation, this is the prohibition imposed on all mankind by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:4" href="/Genesis.9.4"><i>Gen</i>. 9:4</a>: “But meat in whose blood is life you shall not eat”, meat taken when life is still carried by the blood.)</i>.” Does one argue from kosher abour non-kosher animals? But did not Rebbi Eleazar argue<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">To R. Abbahu.</i> from kosher about non-kosher animals? Then one who eats a limb from a non-kosher living animal should be guilty on three counts, because of “you shall not eat any carcass meat<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>;” and because of “you shall not eat of life with the flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:23" href="/Deuteronomy.12.23"><i>Deut</i>. 12:23</a>. It is forbidden to cat limbs torn from a living animal. (In rabbinic interpretation, this is the prohibition imposed on all mankind by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:4" href="/Genesis.9.4"><i>Gen</i>. 9:4</a>: “But meat in whose blood is life you shall not eat”, meat taken when life is still carried by the blood.)</i>”; and because of “you shall not eat from their flesh.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:8" href="/Leviticus.11.8"><i>Lev</i>. 11:8</a>. The verse also connects food prohibition and impurity: “you shall not eat from their flesh nor touch their carcasses”. The prohibition of touching applies to people intending to enter the holy precinct.</i>” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: If somebody ate five ants together, in one forgetting<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Even though all sins of the same kind incurred while one was oblivious of the prohibition can be atoned for by one purification sacrifice, each creature is in a category by itself.</i>, he is guilty for each one separately because of “creature”<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since an ant is a complete creature, it is forbidden food (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:41" href="/Leviticus.11.41"><i>Lev</i>. 11:41</a>) irrespective of size (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1, Note 17</a>). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 16b" href="/Makkot.16b">Babli, <i>Makkot</i> 16b</a>, eating an ant is counted as violating up to five prohibitions simultaneously.</i>. If he fragmented and ate them, he is guilty only once, if together they amount to the volume of an olive. If he ate of the fragments in the volume of an olive, he is guilty; if he ate of the fragments in the volume of an olive and an ant, he is guilty twice<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The same argument, applied to the Babli’s opinion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.1.7">Note 62</a>), is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 16b" href="/Makkot.16b">Babli <i>Makkot</i> 16b</a>.</i>. If this is correct, then if he ate of the fragments less than the volume of an olive and an ant completed the volume of an olive, is he guilty twice<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since the ant it counted for itself, can it be counted with the volume filled by the forbidden pieces?</i>? If this is correct, if the ate an ant the size of an olive, is he guilty twice? The same rule applies to combinations<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">As enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, combining wine, vinegar, leaves, husks, pomace, etc.</i> of a <i>nazir</i>. If he ate of the combinations in the volume of an olive, he is guilty. If he ate of the combinations in the volume of an olive and a grape berry, is he guilty twice<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">For eating the volume of an olive from the produce of the vine he violates <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num</i>. 6:4</a>; for the single intact grape berry he violates <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:3" href="/Numbers.6.3"><i>Num</i>. 6:3</a>.</i>? If this is correct, then if he ate a grape berry for the volume ofan olive, is he guilty twice? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: All [food] prohibitions combine together<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 66a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.66a"><i>‘Avodah zarah</i> 66a</a>, this is a tannaitic statement derived from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:3" href="/Deuteronomy.14.3"><i>Deut</i>. 14:3</a>.</i> to be whipped for the volume of an olive, but for an ant one is guilty twice. Then if he ate prohibited food and an ant completed the volume of an olive, he is guilty twice. Then if he ate an ant the volume of an olive, he is guilty twice.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One estimates [food] prohibitions as if they concerned onions or leeks<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.1.6"><i>Terumot</i> 10:1, Notes 10–11</a>.</i>. This parallels what Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: If carcass meat disappeared among slaughtered meat<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A piece of carcass meat is mixed up with slaughtered meat, and if the volume of the carcass meat were filled by onions and leeks one could not taste the onions after cooking, then the impurity of carcass meat also has disappeared (in the opinion of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 23a" href="/Bekhorot.23a">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i> 23a</a>, only the impurity transmitted by touch has disappeared, not the impurity tranmitted by carrying without touching, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:40" href="/Leviticus.11.40"><i>Lev</i>. 11:40</a>.)</i>, its [impurity by] touch has disappeared by biblical law.
<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This and the next paragraph are from <i>‘Orlah</i> 2:6 (Notes 136–152).</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One does not whip for anything imparting taste until he tasted the forbidden thing itself. Rebbi Ḥiya bar Yosef objected before Rebbi Joḥanan: Take, for example, meat in milk, where he did not taste the forbidden thing itself and you say that he is whipped! He accepted that. What is meant by: he accepted that? Rebbi Ḥiya bar Abun said before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Like a person who listens to the argument of the opposing party he accepted it.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One does not whip for anything imparting taste except imparting taste for the <i>nazir</i>. [Rebbi Ze‘ira said, one does not whip for anything imparting taste until he tasted the forbidden thing itself]<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Missing here, added from the text in ‘<i>Orlah</i> since it is required by the following text.</i> except the <i>nazir</i> even if he did not taste the forbidden thing itself. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, for food imparting taste what is forbidden and what is permitted is not combined, but for the <i>nazir</i> forbidden and permitted do combine. A <i>baraita</i> supports one and a <i>baraita</i> supports the other. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ze‘ira: If wine in the volume of an olive fell into a dish and he ate from it, he cannot be prosecuted unless he ate the entire dish. In the opinion of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal, if he ate the volume of an olive from it he is guilty. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Abba bar Mamal: “What do we understand when it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:3" href="/Numbers.6.3"><i>Num</i>. 6:3</a>): ‘Anything in which grapes were soaked he shall not eat, and fresh or dried grape berries he shall not eat’? What did the verse leave out that was not said? But since it was said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num</i>. 6:4</a>): ‘anything made from the wine-vine, from grape skins to seeds he should not eat;’ (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:3" href="/Numbers.6.3"><i>Num</i>. 6:3</a>) ‘from wine and liquor he shall abstain.’ Why does the verse say ‘anything in which grapes were soaked he should not eat’? It means that if he soaked grapes and then soaked his bread in that, if it adds up to the volume of an olive, he is guilty. From here you argue about all prohibitions of the Torah. Since for all that comes from the vine, whose prohibition is neither permanent, nor a prohibition of usufruct, and whose prohibition can be lifted, He made taste like the thing itself; it is logical that for every prohibition of the Torah which is permanent, is a prohibition of usufruct, whose prohibition cannot be lifted, taste is treated like the thing itself. From here, the Sages inferred that everything imparting taste is forbidden.” This is difficult for Rebbi Ze‘ira: you say everywhere “unless he tasted<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The impure <i>nazir</i> cannot restart his vow if he left two hairs uncut. But the requirement of a knife also applies to a pure <i>nazir</i>.</i>”, and here you say, “even if he did not taste.”
“According to the early Mishnah if he drinks a <i>quartarius</i> of wine.” They did explain “liquor”. Since “liquor” mentioned there<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 10:9" href="/Leviticus.10.9"><i>Lev</i>. 10:9</a>, the prohibition for a priest to enter the holy precinct after he drank “wine or liquor”; <i>Sifra Šemini Parašah</i> 1(1). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 13a" href="/Keritot.13a">Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 13a</a>, the rules of priests are deduced from those of the <i>nazir</i>.</i> means a <i>quartarius</i>, so “liquor” mentioned here also means a <i>quartarius</i>. They changed to say “he shall not eat, he shall not drink.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:3" href="/Numbers.6.3"><i>Num</i>. 6:3</a>. Since both expressions appear in the same verse, they should conform to the same standard. Since the volume of an average olive is much smaller than a <i>quartarius</i>, the smaller standard in applicable in both cases.</i>” Since eating is defined by an olive’s size so drinking is by an olive’s size.
“Rebbi Aqiba says, even if he dipped his bread in wine for a total volume<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The bread plus the wine absorbed in it.</i> of an olive, he is guilty.” Rebbi Ḥanania said, only if he dipped in an olive-sized volume of wine. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: For a mixed cup<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">One third wine mixed with two thirds water.</i> one whips because of soaking. That is, if they did (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This word seems to be an error. It seems that R. Immi stated that if the <i>nazir</i> drank wine mixed with water when was he warned not to drink wine, he is whipped for drinking wine, but if he was warned not to drink anything soaked with the fruit of the vine, he is whipped for that. The next sentence then should read לֹא הִתְרוּ “did not warn” instead of הִתְרוּ “warned”.</i> warn because of soaking. But if they warned because of soaking, this does not apply. It is written: “He shall not drink anything soaked in grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:3" href="/Numbers.6.3"><i>Num</i>. 6:3</a>.</i>.” Not only soaking grapes, from where soaking grape skins? The verse says “soaked, anything soaked,” that all kinds of soaking are counted<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.1.6">Note 44</a>.</i>. Mixed wine combines with pure wine<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Half an olive’s volume pure wine and half an olive mixed wine result in an olive of forbidden drink for the <i>nazir</i>.</i>. Soaking water of grapes combines with grapes. Do soaked grapes and wine combine? If one ate half an olive’s volume of wine and half an olive’s volume of soaking water, is he not guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The context shows that this sentence is interrogatory.</i>. Separately<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Drinking the two parts at different times.</i>, he is not prosecutable. Because he combined, he is guilty. If he ate (<i>sic!</i>) an olive’s volume of wine and an olive’s volume of soaking water, he is guilty only once. Separately, he is guilty twice. Because he combined, he should be guilty only once<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">This is quite obvious; it is stated only as contrast to the preceding case, in which combining made things worse.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One is guilty for wine separately, for grapes separately, for grape skins separately, for seeds separately<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">All types of produce of the vine enumerated in the verses represent separate prohibitions; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.1.5">Note 35</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah says, he is guilty only if he eats two חרצנים and their זגים<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since a plural implies a minimum of 2.</i>. What are חרצנים and what זגים<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Both expressions are unexplained <i>hapax legomena</i>.</i>? חרצנים are the outer skins, זגים the inner (seeds), the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Yose said, that you should make no mistake<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with R. Jehudah and defines חרצן as seed, זג as skin (cf. Arabic زجّ “to be transparent”). The interpretation of R. Yose is accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 39a" href="/Nazir.39a">Babli (39a</a>) and Targum Onkelos. Targum Yerushalmi follows R. Jehudah.</i>, like an animal’s bell, the outer shell is זוג, the inner the clapper.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One is guilty for wine separately, for grapes separately,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:3" href="/Numbers.6.3"><i>Num</i>. 6:3</a>.</i>: “Also grapes, fresh or dried, he shall not eat.” One understands, since it said “grapes”, do we not know that they are fresh<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since raisins are called צִמּוּקִים.</i>? Why does the verse say, “grapes, fresh or dried”? To declare guilty for either one separately. This parallels what Ḥizqiah stated: Since at a place where He did not treat the waste of fruits like fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical rules of <i>‘orlah</i>, the prohibition of fruits for the first three years of a fruit tree, do not extend to branches, leaves, or flowers (Mishnah <i>‘Orlah</i> 1:7).</i>, He treated fresh and dried equally, here, where He treated the waste of fruits like fruits, would it not be logical that we treat fresh and dried equally? The verse said, “fresh or dried”, to declare guilty for either one separately. This parallels what Rebbi Hila said: “One may not impound the movable and the fixed part of a flour-mill.<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:6" href="/Deuteronomy.24.6"><i>Deut</i>. 24:6</a>.</i> “One understands, since it said “the fixed part”, do we not know that the entire mill is understood? Why does the verse say, “the movable and the fixed part”? To declare guilty for either part separately<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 272, the conclusion arrived at here is taken as the obvious meaning of the verse.</i>.
“Fresh”, to include unripe berries.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Num</i>. 23.</i> “Fresh”, to include the flower. The <i>baraita</i> follows Rebbi Yose, since Rebbi Yose said, the flower is forbidden for the <i>nazir</i> because it is a fruit<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mishnah <i>‘Orlah</i> 1:6, Note 182 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 36b" href="/Berakhot.36b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 36b</a>). Probably the reference is to the bottom of the flower, from which the fruit develops.</i>. It is possible to eat a bunch of grapes and to be guilty in reference to “grapes, fresh or dried, he shall not eat.<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">If some grapes in the bunch dried on the stem.</i>” If he soaked it for soaking, pressed it for wine, and they warned him about “anything that comes from the wine-vine”<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 13:14" href="/Judges.13.14"><i>Jud</i>. 13:14</a>, as substitute of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num</i>. 6:4</a>. If the <i>nazir</i> took a bunch of grapes some of which where dried up, soaked it in water to fill the raisins with water as one does to prepare raisin wine, and then presses the bunch for its juice, and drinks after being duly warned, he can be convicted of four simultaneous crimes.</i>, etc.
Rebbi Abba bar Aḥa said: The reason of Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah is because of a creature<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Even if skin and the seed inside do not fill the volume of an olive, the <i>nazir</i> who eats them is guilty for eating a complete creature.</i>. But it was stated: “Skins and seeds,” to include the third [kind] between them<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The flesh of the fruit. Since one speaks of skins and seeds as separate from the grape berry, one speaks of the husks left after pressing. Therefore, the “third” is what is left of the flesh after all juice has been squeezed out.</i>. But was it not stated in the name of Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 34b" href="/Nazir.34b">Babli 34b</a>, <i>Sifry Num</i>. #24; rejected in the Babli.</i>: “From anything made from the wine-vine”, leaves and twigs are also understood? Rebbi Eliezer parallels Rebbi Ismael, since Rebbi Ismael said, [if you have] a principle and a detail, everything is included in the principle<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources (35a; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 7" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.7"><i>Sifra Introduction</i> 7</a>), R. Ismael’s hermeneutical rule 5 is stated as: If the detail precedes the general statement, the general statement receives the maximal extension. If the general statement precedes the detail, the extension of the general statement is defined by the detail. This interpretation must be rejected by the Yerushalmi since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num</i>. 6:4</a> contains first a general statement (all that comes from the vine) and then a detail (skins and seeds). The discussion disregards that v. 3 contains a list of details only.</i>. And more than that, since Rebbi Ismael said, with a principle and a detail, everything is included in the principle; why are “skins and seeds” said? To include the third [kind] in between<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Previously (Note 56), the expression “from skins unto seeds” was meant to imply that the <i>nazir</i> is guilty separately for skins and for seeds. This was derived from the additional word <i>unto</i>. Here, the argument refers to the mention of skins and seeds themselves, which does not seem to add anything to “everything coming from the vine.” For a different formulation, cf. <i>Sifry Num</i>. 24.</i>.
What is Rebbi Yose’s reason<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose clearly must think that his interpretation of the words is linguistically correct. The question is, what are the legal implications of his definition; how would he rule differently from R. Jehudah?</i>? If one ate half the volume of an olive of seeds and peels from one grape berry, in Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah’s opinion he is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">If a single berry was squeezed dry but two seeds and the skin were left, for R. Eleazar ben Azariah this would represent a creature and the <i>nazir</i> who eats them would be guilty. For the rabbis, anything less than the volume of an olive cannot lead to a conviction. But for R. Jehudah, two חרצנים means skins from two berries; there is no creature and the <i>nazir</i> is not prosecutable..</i>, in the rabbis’ opinion he is not prosecutable. What is Rebbi Jehudah’s reason? If he ate the volume of an olive of peels and seeds from two grape berries, in Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah’s opinion he is guilty only once<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Jehudah, R. Eleazar ben Azariah’s requirement that the <i>nazir</i> must eat two חרצנים to be guilty means that even if each grape berry has the size of an olive, he must eat two of them to become guilty. For the rabbis, eating a single berry the size of an olive is a punishable offense.</i>, in the rabbis’ opinion he is guilty twice. Rebbi Abun said, it turns out that Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah holds with Rebbi Jehudah, if he ate the volume of an olive of two זגים and their חרצנים<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the statement of R. Eleazar ben Azariah cannot be reconciled with the opinion of R. Jehudah since then he should have switched the mention of חרצן and זג as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.2.5">Note 124</a>.</i>?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An unspecified <i>nezirut</i> is thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Repeated from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.3.1">Mishnah 1:3</a>.</i>. If he shaved, or robbers shaved him<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Forcibly.</i>, he starts again for thirty<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">He counts a minimum of 30 days after his shaving before he can bring his sacrifices since no <i>nazir</i> can shave after less than 30 days.</i>. A <i>nazir</i> who shaved any [hair], whether with scissors or razor knife, or cropped<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Arukh</i>: ספסף, “tearing off part of the hair, cropping,” with the root remaining in the scalp, contrasting רַט “tearing or falling out completely”. (Compare Arabic سفساف “to sift”). The commentary from the school of Rashi and Maimonides explain: “tore out.”</i>, is guilty. A <i>nazir</i> may wash his head and separate his hair but may not comb. Rebbi Ismael says, he cannot wash his hair with powder because that removes hair.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An unspecified <i>nezirut</i> is thirty days,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the first two paragraphs of this Halakhah is in rather bad shape.</i> It is written: “A shaving knife shall not pass over his head<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num</i>. 6:5</a>.</i>;” therefore, if it did pass, he is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><i>He</i> is guilty if <i>it</i> passed: even if the <i>nazir</i> is passive. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 44a" href="/Nazir.44a">Babli, 44a</a>, and <i>Sifry Num</i>. 25, the sentence is interpreted to make the shaver equally guilty with the shaved.)</i>. “His head’s hair grows wildly;” how much means growing hair? 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 1, Notes 99,100.</i>. {That refers to an impure <i>nazir</i>. A pure <i>nazir</i>? “He has to shave his head on the day be becomes pure.” Why does the verse say: “On the seventh day he shall shave all his hair”? That shows that he shaves a second time.}<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The text in braces is corrupt as it stands. The proposals for emendations create a new text; it seems better to try to understand the text as it is.<br>The verses quoted up to this point do not mention an impure <i>nazir</i>; it is possible to read with the classical commentaries: “That refers to a pure <i>nazir</i>. An impure <i>nazir</i>? ‘He has to shave his head on the day be becomes pure’ (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num</i>. 6:9</a>).” This presupposes that a pure <i>nazir</i> who shaves has to start anew; why does an impure <i>nazir</i>, who anyhow has to start anew for a minimum of 30 days, add to his period of <i>nezirut</i> if he tears out a hair?<br>The following quote, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:9" href="/Leviticus.14.9"><i>Lev</i>. 14:9</a>, and its accompanying text have nothing to do with the <i>nazir</i> but refer to the convalescent sufferer from skin disease. He has to shave a second time, 7 days after the shaving ordered in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:8" href="/Leviticus.14.8">v. 8</a>.</i> “He shaves,”<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num</i>. 6:9</a> (the impure <i>nazir</i>), 6:18 (the pure <i>nazir</i>).</i> all, not in part<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">This is a <i>non sequitur</i>. In all other sources, the rule for the <i>nazir</i> is determined in comparison with the recovered sufferer from skin disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.3.2">Note 137</a>) and the Levites when inducted into the service of the Tabernacle (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 8:7" href="/Numbers.8.7"><i>Num</i>. 8:7</a>). In both cases, the verse emphasizes the necessity to shave <i>all</i> hair, meaning that no two hairs can be left standing [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 32a" href="/Nazir.32a">Babli 32a</a>; Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:4; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Metzora, Chapter 2 6" href="/Sifra,_Metzora,_Chapter_2.6"><i>Sifra Meṣora‘ Pereq</i> 2(6</a>)].</i>. From here that if he left two hairs, he [did] nothing. “A shaving knife shall not pass over his head.<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num</i>. 6:5</a>.</i>” Not only a shaving knife, from where to treat a cropper and scissors like a shaving knife? The verse says, “shall not pass over his head.” That means not only a shaving knife; all methods of removal are understood. From here that he starts again only for a [shaving knife]<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Part of the last sentence is missing here but can be recovered from the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 39b" href="/Nazir.39b">Babli, 39b</a>, and <i>Sifry Num</i>. 25, where a text parallel to that extending the prohibition from a shaving knife to anything that shaves is attributed to R. Joshia; but R. Jonathan states that “the verse speaks of a shaving knife. Therefore, if he tore out, cropped, or went to the barber, he cannot be whipped.”<br>Since the wording of R. Joshia’s text in the Yerushalmi differs from the Babli/Sifry text, for R. Jonathan’s opinion only the meaning, but not the text, can be recovered.</i>.
Rebbi Abba bar Mamal and Rebbi Ila asked before Rebbi Yasa: They should not start again for thirty, but should start again for seven<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Since the person healed of skin disease shaves both after the initial ceremony (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:8" href="/Leviticus.14.8"><i>Lev</i>. 14:8</a>) and seven days afterwards (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:9" href="/Leviticus.14.9"><i>v</i>. 9</a>), it follows that seven days are sufficient to justify another shaving. Since shaving by means other than a knife is forbidden for the <i>nazir</i> only by implication, it seems reasonable to require only a 7 days’ growth for a <i>nazir</i> who removed hair not by a knife.</i>! No, should they restart neither for seven nor for thirty<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Jonathan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.3.2">Note 140</a>), shaving other than by a knife should not have any consequence.</i>? If you say, they should not start again for thirty, what is the difference between a pure and an impure <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> to be quoted states that the exact duration of the period to restart the growth of hair is different for a pure <i>nazir</i> who shaves and an impure <i>nazir</i> who is forced to shave.</i>? “One has stated a leniency and a restriction for a pure <i>nazir</i>; one has stated a leniency and a restriction for an impure <i>nazir</i>. A leniency for a pure <i>nazir</i> that he restart only for thirty; a restriction that he restart for seven<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">This text makes no sense. It seems that it must read with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta 4:3</a>: “He restarts only if shaved by a knife (most of his head); a restriction that he restart for 30 days.”</i> If you say, he should restart for thirty, what is the difference between a pure and an impure <i>nazir</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence has to be deleted; it belongs to the discussion after the <i>baraita</i> is quoted in its entirety.</i> A leniency for an impure <i>nazir</i> that all cases make him restart; a restriction that he restarts for seven<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">In this sentence, “leniency” and “restriction” have to be switched. At the required shaving of the impure <i>nazir</i>, “knife” is not mentioned; everybody agrees that all methods of shaving are equally valid.</i>.” Should he not restart for thirty? If you say, he should restart for thirty, what is the difference between a pure and an impure <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">And the <i>baraita</i> clearly stated that the time lost is different for the two kinds of <i>nazir</i>.</i>? Some want to say, what is the difference between scissors and a shaving knife for a pure <i>nazir</i> following Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.4.1">Mishnah 3:4</a> requires the <i>nazir</i> who fulfilled his time but shaved before he brought his sacrifice only to wait another seven days. He certainly must agree with R. Jonathan that if the <i>nazir</i> did not use a knife he does not have to wait at all.</i>?
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:4; quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 40a" href="/Nazir.40a">Babli 40a</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.3.2">Note 139</a>.</i>: “Three categories of people shave and their shaving is a commandment: the <i>nazir</i>, the sufferer from skin disease, and the Levites. All these, if they shaved not with a knife or left two hairs,did not do anything.” Rebbi Eleazar said: The Mishnah [speaks] about an impure <i>nazir</i>. But a pure <i>nazir</i>, once he shaved most of his head, even if not with a knife, has acquitted himself [of his obligation]. Rebbi Immi, following Rebbi Eleazar (ben Azariah)<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Since the author of the remark is R. Eleazar ben Pada, the words in parenthesis have to be deleted.</i>, asked: The only place where a knife is mentioned is about a pure <i>nazir</i>: “A shaving knife shall not pass over his head until the days are fulfilled.<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num</i>. 6:5</a>.</i>” Therefore, after he fulfilled them he needs a knife! Perhaps it was said only about an impure <i>nazir</i> with regard to two hairs<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The impure <i>nazir</i> cannot restart his vow if he left two hairs uncut. But the requirement of a knife also applies to a pure <i>nazir</i>.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 42a" href="/Nazir.42a">Babli, 42a</a>, the remark is attributed to R. Yose ben Ḥanina. Since R. Jacob bar Aḥa was a colleague of R. Yasa and a teacher of R. Yose, it seems that one has to read “Yasa” instead of “Yose”; the attribution of the Babli is impossible.</i> said to Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: Do you remember when we were studying <i>Nazir</i>, we said that there was no difference between scissors and a knife, and Rebbi Eleazar said: The Mishnah [speaks] about an impure <i>nazir</i>! Why not about a pure <i>nazir</i>? The pure <i>nazir</i> shaves after the blood was sprinkled<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">For all sacrifices, the sprinkling of the blood on the walls of the altar fulfills the purpose of the sacrifice; nothing that happens afterwards can invalidate the sacrifice.</i>. When that was sanctified, his vow was completed. But it<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The hair of the <i>nazir</i>’s head.</i> is as if fallen out.
You say about a pure <i>nazir</i> that most of his hair hinders him<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">If he shaved less than 50% of his head’s hair, he has not lived up to the conditions of his vow.</i>. If he shaved a third but did not manage to finish until everything grew again: Is it sufficient that he shave the remainder or does he have to shave everything?<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered, being unrealistic.</i> You say about an impure <i>nazir</i> that two hairs hinder<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The impure <i>nazir</i> cannot restart his vow if he left two hairs uncut. But the requirement of a knife also applies to a pure <i>nazir</i>.</i> or two hairs make him start again. Similarly, for a pure <i>nazir</i>, do two hairs hinder him or two hairs make him start again? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, so it is, and it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta 4:3</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 40a" href="/Nazir.40a">Babli 40a</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah said in the name of Rebbi Simeon, just as two hairs hinder him, so two hairs cause him to start again.”
