Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he shaved everything but left two hairs which were long enough each to bend its end to its root<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">A hair is considered cut if it cannot be bent so that its head touches its root.</i> twice; he shaved to reduce it to one<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">He cut all hairs to below the limit except two which he cut exactly to the limit. R. Jeremiah in general holds that the rules only state that the condition is fulfilled if the limit is not reached and violated if the limit is exceeded but at the exact limit nothing has been decided; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 22b" href="/Bava_Batra.22b">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 22b</a>, a position rejected in general.</i>. Since he cut the required length, has he fulfilled his obligation, or because he omitted what was required, did he not fulfill his obligation?
Rebbi Ila said before Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Here also one has to read: Yasa.</i>: For whipping one, for hindering two, to start again three<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>nazir</i> who cut one hair can be whipped. If he was impure and failed to cut two hairs, he cannot re-start his <i>nezirut</i>; if he was pure and failed to cut two hairs, he did not fulfill his vow. If he is pure and cut three hairs, he has to keep a minimum of 30 days of <i>nezirut</i> after that.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Here also one has to read: Yasa.</i>: “If he cropped any<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">He did not even cut one entire hair.</i>, he is guilty.” Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, Rebbi Yasa explains it by a little bit from each one<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Cropping many hairs is not comparable to cutting one hair.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of Samuel instead of R. Hila seems to be a scribal error.</i>: A person may tear out two hairs and have violated four prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe did not quite understand his text. He wrote ללוים “for the Levites” insteat of לוים, לאוים “prohibitions”, literally “no’s”.</i>: As a <i>nazir</i>, as a sufferer from sking disease, on a holiday, because of jaw stripping<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">If a person who is both a <i>nazir</i> and a sufferer from skin disease (who has to grow his hair, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:45" href="/Leviticus.13.45"><i>Lev</i>. 13:45</a>) shaves his sideburns on a holiday, he violates 4 prohibitions with one act. Since shaving on a holiday is prosecutable only if he shaves a minimum of two hairs, R. Hila’s assertion that one can be whipped for shaving a single hair is put into question.</i>. We have enumerated the prohibitions; but with regard to whippings, one even whips for one<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Things may be prohibited even if they cannot be prosecuted.</i>.
Some <i>Tannaïm</i> state: “One separates matted hair<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">קליקין is usually explained as κιλίκιον, τό “coarse cloth (of Cilician goat’s hair); covering used by soldiers and seamen”, but in this case (and the related one in Mishnah <i>Miqwa’ot</i> 9:2) one might refer to Latin <i>culcita, culcitra</i>, “sack filled with feathers, wool, or hair; cushion, mattress” (E. G.).</i>.” Some <i>Tannaïm</i> state: “One does not separate.” Rav Ḥisda said, the one who states that one separates, for healthy [hair]; the one who states that one does not separate, for weak [hair].

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A <i>nazir</i> who was drinking wine all day long is guilty only once. If he was told “do not drink, do not drink” and he did drink, he is guilty for each single infraction<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">If he was duly warned once by two witnesses, he can be prosecuted and punished for one offense.<br>If he was repeatedly warned and flouted each warning, he can be prosecuted for disregarding each warning separately. The same rules apply for all three prohibitions imposed on a <i>nazir</i>.</i>.<br>One who shaved all day long is guilty only once. If he was told “do not shave, do not shave” and he did shave, he is guilty for each single infraction.<br>One who defiled himself for the dead all day long is guilty only once. If he was told “do not defile yourself, do not defile yourself” and he did defile himself, he is guilty for each single infraction.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A <i>nazir</i> who drank wine,” etc. A <i>nazir</i> while he was in a cemetery: Rebbi Joḥanan said, one warns him about wine and shaving. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, since one cannot warn him because of impurity, one does not warn him about wine and shaving. One reads all this in the Third Chapter<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.5.2">Halakhah 3:5, Notes 58</a> ff.</i>.
If before him were two bottles, one of water and one of wine. He took and emptied the water bottle. They said to him, you should know that after you drank the water, if you then drink the wine, since its volume corresponds to ten olives you will be subject to ten whippings; he does not accept warning in this way<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not touch the wine bottle yet and gave no indication that he would take it, the warning is purely preventive. In order to be used in criminal prosecution, the warning must be given when the person to whom it is directed seems likely to violate the law.</i>. But if it was a wine bottle and he started drinking from it when they said to him, you should know that if you then drink it all, since its volume corresponds to ten olives you will be subject to ten whippings; he accepts warning in this way.
If before him were two roasting spits, one with slaughtered meat and one with carcass meat. He took and ate the slaughtered meat. They said to him, you should know that after you ate the slaughtered meat, if you then ate the carcass meat, since its volume corresponds to ten olives you will be subject to ten whippings; he does not accept warning in this way<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not touch the wine bottle yet and gave no indication that he would take it, the warning is purely preventive. In order to be used in criminal prosecution, the warning must be given when the person to whom it is directed seems likely to violate the law.</i>. But if there was a roasting spit of carcass meat and he started eating from it when they said to him, you should know that if you then ate it all, since its volume corresponds to ten olives you will be subject to ten whippings; he accepts warning in this way.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Three kinds are forbidden for the <i>nazir</i>: Impurity, shaving, and consuming produce of the vine<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Repeated from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.1.1">Mishnah 6:1</a></i>. Impurity and shaving are more severe than the prohibition of produce of the vine since impurity and shaving require him to start again, but produce of the vine does not require him to start again. The prohibition of produce of the vine is more severe than impurity and shaving since produce of the vine is never permitted but impurity and shaving are permitted for a commanded shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">If a sufferer from skin disease is a <i>nazir</i> when he is healed, his vow does not preclude the required shavings for his rehabilitation.</i> and a corpse of obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Any person, even a <i>nazir</i> or a High Priest, who comes upon an unattended corpse, has to defile himself and bring the dead to burial. The rules of the commanded burial are detailed in the next Chapter.</i>. Impurity is more severe than shaving since for impurity he has to start again from the beginning and is obligated for a sacrifice, but for shaving he has to start again for at most 30 days and is not obligated for a sacrifice.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.6">Halakhah 6:1, Notes 4–6</a>.</i> Impurity, as it is written: “During all the days he vowed to the Eternal he shall not come close to a human corpse.” Shaving, as it is written: “During all the days of his <i>nazir</i> vow, a shaving knife shall not come onto his head.” Anything from the vine, as it is written: “During all the days of his vow, of anything coming from the wine-vine [he shall not eat.]”
“Impurity and shaving are more severe than produce of the vine.” Impurity, as it is written: “All the days he vowed to the Eternal.” And shaving, as it is written: “The earlier days fall away<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.5.1">Halakhah 3:5, Note 57</a>.</i>, for his vow in impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">This verse proves that the impure <i>nazir</i> has to start again from the beginning. One is tempted, with the classical commentators, to move this verse to the previous sentence and find an appropriate verse for shaving. The problem is that no biblical verse requires the shaved <i>nazir</i> to start counting anew, as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:3:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.3.2-8">Halakhah 3</a>. The reference is to an argument, reproduced <i>in extenso</i> in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 44a" href="/Nazir.44a">Babli 44a</a> and Sifry <i>Num</i>. 31, shortened in <i>Num. rabba</i> 10(34), on exactly the quoted verse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num</i>. 6:12</a>: “ ‘For his vow is impure’, impurity requires him to start from the beginning, shaving does not require him to start from the beginning, but he has to repeat 30 days.”</i>”
“The produce of the vine is more severe than impurity and shaving.” They wanted to say, for the Four Cups (of water or)<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">This clearly is a scribal error.</i> wine<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">The 4 cups of wine one is required to drink in the Seder night (cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, pp. 185–191.) Since this is a rabbinic (or in any case, post-biblical) requirement, it cannot eliminate a biblical prohibition. (In the opinion of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 3b-4a" href="/Nazir.3b-4a">Babli, 3b/4a</a>, the <i>nazir</i> may drink wine for the Sabbath <i>qidduš</i> since the Fourth Commandment to sanctify the Sabbath cannot be pushed aside by the vow of the <i>nazir</i>. This fact is irrelevant here since impurity and shaving can be imposed on the <i>nazir</i> after his vow, but the Sabbath wine is his duty before the vow. The Yerushalmi clearly holds that <i>qidduš</i> can be recited over bread; wine is not absolutely necessary.)</i>. But if he said, “a vow that I shall drink and be a <i>nazir</i>”, “that I shall drink” comes to say that he will not drink<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Since in an oath formula, the positive stands for the negative.</i>.
He is permitted commanded shavings. Not only to signify his skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Since in an oath formula, the positive stands for the negative.</i> but also to signify his scall, to know whether it spread or did not spread<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The shavings needed for diagnostic purposes, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:33" href="/Leviticus.13.33"><i>Lev</i>. 13:33</a></i>. “And a corpse of obligation.” That is what we have stated: “If they were walking and came upon a corpse of obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.1.1">Mishnah 7:1</a>.</i>”
“Impurity is more severe than shaving.” Impurity, as it is written: “The earlier days fall away,” “and he shall bring a yearling sheep as a reparation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num</i>. 6:12</a>.</i>.” Shaving is more severe because He made the shaver equal to the shaved<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The person who shaves a <i>nazir</i> can be criminally prosecuted. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 44a" href="/Nazir.44a">Babli 44a</a>, <i>Sifry Num</i>. 25, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 4:4" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).4.4">Tosephta 4:4</a>.</i>, but in impurity He did not make the defiler equal to the defiled<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">A person who causes a <i>nazir</i> to be defiled by the impurity of the dead cannot be prosecuted.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is shaving in impurity? He was sprinkled on the third and seventh [days]<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">Since the only impurity which defiles the <i>nazir</i> is the impurity of the dead, he must have water with ashes from the Red Cow sprinkled on him on the 3<sup class="footnote-marker">rd</sup> and 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> days of his impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>).</i>, shaves on the seventh, and brings his sacrifices on the eighth<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">As prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num</i>. 6:9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:10" href="/Numbers.6.10">10</a>.</i>. If he shaved on the eighth, he may bring his sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Two birds (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:10" href="/Numbers.6.10"><i>Num</i>. 6:10</a>) and a sheep (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num</i>. 6:12</a>).</i> on the same day, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Ṭarphon asked him, what is the difference between this one and the sufferer from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:3 states that the person healed from skin disease can bring his sacrifices only after the sundown following his immersion in a <i>miqweh</i>. According to R. Aqiba [readings of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Nazir 44b" href="/Tosafot_on_Nazir.44b">Tosaphot, 44b</a> <i>s.v.</i> א״ל; Maimonides (Commentary <i>ad loc.</i>; <i>Meḥusere Kappara</i> 4:2); see the readings in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Metzora, Chapter 2 7" href="/Sifra,_Metzora,_Chapter_2.7"><i>Sifra Meṣora‘ Pereq</i> 2(7</a>); another reading in the Commentary attributed to Rashi] this implies that he has to bring his sacrifices on the 9<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> if he shaved on the 8<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>? He told him, the purification of this one is bound to his days<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">As prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num</i>. 6:9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:10" href="/Numbers.6.10">10</a>.</i>, but the purification of the sufferer from skin disease is bound to his shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:9" href="/Leviticus.14.9"><i>Lev</i>. 14:9</a> prescribes the immersion of the sufferer from skin disease <i>after</i> his shaving but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num</i>. 6:9</a> requires the impure <i>nazir</i> to shave “on the day of his purity”, i. e., after the second sprinkling and his immersion.</i>. He cannot bring his sacrifices unless the sun had set for him<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general principle that immersion in water makes ritually pure only for profane places or food; for <i>sancta</i> only the following sundown brings purity, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev</i>. 22:7</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.2.4"><i>Soṭah</i> 5:2, Note 42</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written: “He has to vow to the Eternal the days of his <i>nezirut</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num</i>. 6:12</a>. The requirement to renew his vow is written between the mention of the sacrifice of two birds and that of the sheep. The verse seems to refer to the eighth day, the day of his sacrifices.</i>” from the day he brings his sacrifices, the words of Rebbi. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, from the time of his shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., after he immersed himself and is pure for profane purposes. Since he is no longer impure, there is no reason for him not to be a fully functioning <i>nazir</i>.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 18b-19a" href="/Nazir.18b-19a">Babli, 18b/19a</a>, the verse quoted is brought as support for the majority opinion, that a resumption of the status of <i>nazir</i> only depends on his shaving, here attributed to R. Yose ben R. Jehudah, while the opinion of Rebbi, that a resumption of the status of <i>nazir</i> depends of his reparation offering, is quoted there in the name of R. Joḥanan ben Baroqa. This implies that the Yerushalmi prefers the opinion of Rebbi, the Babli that of R. Yose ben R. Yehudah.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rav Hoshaia, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Where do they disagree? If he shaved on the seventh and brought his sacrifices on the eighth. But if he shaved on the eighth and brought his sacrifices on the same day, everybody agrees on the day he brings his sacrifices. Rebbi Yose said, that is, if he immersed himself on the seventh. But if he immersed himself on the eighth, the eighth takes the place of the seventh<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Since he cannot enter the holy precinct before sundown.</i> and the seventh of the eighth<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the eighth is not different in status from the seventh.</i>; he counts only from that “seventh”<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">For Rebbi from the 9<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, for R. Yose ben R. Yehudah from the 8<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>. If he became impure and impure again<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">He became impure after he went to the <i>miqweh</i> on the 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day but before he brought his sacrifices on the 8<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 4:8" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).4.8">Tosephta 4:8</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 18b" href="/Nazir.18b">Babli 18b</a>.</i>, he brings a sacrifice for each occurrence. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, that statement follows Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 18b" href="/Nazir.18b">Babli, 18b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 52a" href="/Nazir.52a">52a</a>, “R. Eliezer”. Rebbi’s opinion is referred to as that “of the rabbis” in the Babli.</i>, but following Rebbi this person still stays in impure <i>nezirut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Since only the reparation offering eliminates his disability as <i>nazir</i>.</i>. Rebbi Hila said, where do they disagree? To count <i>nezirut</i> in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Only for the count. Rebbi will agree that if he immersed himself on the 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, any impurity he incurs after that is a new impurity even if he could not yet start counting the days of his <i>nezirut</i>.</i>. “He has to vow to the Eternal the days of his <i>nezirut</i> and bring.” Rebbi said, until he actually brought. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, even if he was enabled to bring the sacrifice for impurity. Everybody agrees that he brings a sacrifice for impurity. It was stated thus: If he became impure on the seventh, and again impure on the eighth, he brings a sacrifice for each occurrence. In Rebbi Ze‘ira’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">193,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num</i>. 6:12</a>. The requirement to renew his vow is written between the mention of the sacrifice of two birds and that of the sheep. The verse seems to refer to the eighth day, the day of his sacrifices.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., after he immersed himself and is pure for profane purposes. Since he is no longer impure, there is no reason for him not to be a fully functioning <i>nazir</i>.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 18b-19a" href="/Nazir.18b-19a">Babli, 18b/19a</a>, the verse quoted is brought as support for the majority opinion, that a resumption of the status of <i>nazir</i> only depends on his shaving, here attributed to R. Yose ben R. Jehudah, while the opinion of Rebbi, that a resumption of the status of <i>nazir</i> depends of his reparation offering, is quoted there in the name of R. Joḥanan ben Baroqa. This implies that the Yerushalmi prefers the opinion of Rebbi, the Babli that of R. Yose ben R. Yehudah.</i>, following Rebbi the first sacrifice is superseded and he brings the second; following Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah it was not superseded. In Rebbi Hila’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Only for the count. Rebbi will agree that if he immersed himself on the 7<sup>th</sup>, any impurity he incurs after that is a new impurity even if he could not yet start counting the days of his <i>nezirut</i>.</i>, everybody agrees that nothing is superseded and he brings another.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is shaving in purity? He brings three animals<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:14" href="/Numbers.6.14"><i>Num</i>. 6:14</a>.</i>, a purification sacrifice, an elevation sacrifice, and a well-being sacrifice. He slaughters the well-being sacrifice ands shaves for it<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Since shaving is mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18">v. 18</a>, after the slaughter of the well-being offering in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:17" href="/Numbers.6.17">v. 17</a>.</i>, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">In some Mishnah mss., “R. Eliezer”.</i> says, he only should shave for the purification sacrifice since that has precedence everywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the <i>nazir</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:16" href="/Numbers.6.16">v. 16</a>; in other cases of a common offering of purification and elevation offerings, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:8" href="/Leviticus.5.8"><i>Lev</i>. 5:8</a>.</i>, but if he shaved for any of the three, he satisfied his obligation.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said: “The ram he shall offer, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:17" href="/Numbers.6.17"><i>Num</i>. 6:17</a>.</i>” Why does the verse say, “he shall offer”? Start the procedure with it<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry zuṭa</i> 17, this argument is tannaïtic and attributed to R. Jehudah. It seems that he contrasts the imperfect used for sacrificing the well-being offering with the perfect used for the other offerings, to indicate beginning of an action. In the Babli and <i>Sifry Num</i>. 35, the preferred treatment of the well-being offering is deduced from v. 18.</i>. Rebbi Ḥinena objected before Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The R. Mana quoted in this Halakhah is neither R. Mana I, of the first, nor R. Mana II, of the fifth generation. Either there exists a third, otherwise unkown, Amora of this name or “Mana” is erroneous for “Yasa”, or “Ze‘ira” is erroneous for “Ezra”.</i>: But is it not written: “He shall offer his flour offering and his libation<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:17" href="/Numbers.6.17"><i>Num</i>. 6:17</a>.</i>”? Should he not start with them? How is that? Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: If he shaved for any of the three, he satisfied his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Flour offerings and libations are mentioned last in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:17" href="/Numbers.6.17">v. 17</a>. They accompany both the well-being offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:17" href="/Numbers.6.17">v. 17</a>) and the elevation offering, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:16" href="/Numbers.6.16">v. 16</a> together with the purification offering which needs neither flour nor wine. It is inferred that the order of the sacrifices is irrelevant.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Mana: Who is the Tanna of: “All purification offerings in the Torah precede the reparation offerings”? Rebbi Eleazar said, it is everybody’s opinion, “all purification offerings in the Torah precede the reparation offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 10:5" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.10.5">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i> 10:5</a>. No reparation offering is due from the pure <i>nazir</i>.</i>”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if he brought three animals but did not specify<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">No sacrifice can be offered unless it was sanctified by dedication. In general, the dedication must specify for which sacrifice the animal will be used. But for a <i>nazir</i>, dedication “for the <i>nazir</i>’s sacrifices” is sufficient since the purification offering requires a female sheep, the elevation offering a male sheep, and the well-being offering a male goat.</i>, the one proper for the purification offering shall be brought as purification offering, for the elevation offering shall be brought as elevation offering, for the well-being offering shall be brought as well-being offering. He took the hair shorn from his <i>nazir</i> head and sent it under the cooking pot<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">In general, any sacrificial meat permitted for human consumption can be eaten as desired, cooked, roasted, or scalded. Only for the well-being offering of the <i>nazir</i> does the verse prescribe cooking; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:19" href="/Numbers.6.19"><i>Num</i>. 6:19</a>. The hair is “sent” since the <i>nazir</i> shaves outside the holy precinct but the well-being offering must be cooked inside (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:19-20" href="/Numbers.6.19-20">vv. 19–20</a>).</i>; even if he shaved in the countryside<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">If he sends money so that the sacrifices should be offered on his behalf while he remains outside of Jerusalem, his hair has to be burned.</i> he sent it under the cooking pot. When has this been said? If he shaved in purity. But if he shaved in impurity, he does not send it under the cooking pot<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">His hair has to be buried; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:4" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.4"><i>Mishnah Temurah</i> 7:4</a>.</i>. Rebbi Meïr says, all send under the cooking pot except the impure in the countryside.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> They wanted to say, if he did not specify, but in his house he did specify<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">He did not dedicate specifically but when he selected the animals he specified.</i>. They said, even in his house he need not specify. Why? The animal which at the start has the rules of the indeterminate<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">An animal has to be dedicated specifically only if there can be a doubt for what it will be used. For a <i>nazir</i>, no doubt is possible.</i>.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: He puts it into the sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 45b" href="/Nazir.45b">Babli 45b</a> [<i>Num. rabba</i> 6(39], as tannaïtic text. One takes from the sauce in which the well-being offering is cooked, wets the hair with it, and puts it into the fire.</i>. What is the reason? “He puts it into the fire,<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num</i>. 6:18</a>.</i>” the sacrifice also into the fire.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Slightly different text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 45b" href="/Nazir.45b">Babli, 45b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 4:6" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).4.6">Tosephta 4:6</a>.</i>: “All send under the cooking pot except the impure in the countryside. The pure<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">This “pure” refers to a <i>nazir</i> who was impure, had to bring a reparation offering for his impurity, but now is pure after having been sprinkled with the ashes of the Red Cow and been immersed in a <i>miqweh</i>.</i> in the Temple brings it under the cooking-pot of the reparation sacrifice, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, the pure brings in any case, the impure does not bring in any case. But the Sages say, neither the impure in the Temple nor the pure in the countryside bring. Nobody sends under the cooking-pot except the one who shaves in purity.” “Outside the door of the Tent of Meeting.<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num</i>. 6:18</a>.</i>” Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, the attributions have to be switched. The rabbis allow burning only if the <i>nazir</i> is pure at the Temple gate; Rebbi Meïr allows it if either he is pure or at the Temple gate.</i> says, only if he is proper and near; but the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, the attributions have to be switched. The rabbis allow burning only if the <i>nazir</i> is pure at the Temple gate; Rebbi Meïr allows it if either he is pure or at the Temple gate.</i> say, proper even if not near, near even if not proper.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He cooked the well-being offering or scalded<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 6:1, Note 1</a>.</i> it. A Cohen takes the cooked fore-leg of the ram, one unleavened loaf from the basket, and one unleavened thin bread, places it on the <i>nazir</i>’s hands and waves it<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:19-20" href="/Numbers.6.19-20"><i>Num</i>. 6:19–20</a>.</i>. Afterwards the <i>nazir</i> is permitted to drink wine and to defile himself with the dead. Rebbi Simeon says, when one of the bloods was sprinkled, the <i>nazir</i> is permitted to drink wine and to defile himself with the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:20" href="/Numbers.6.20">v. 20</a>, the note that the <i>nazir</i> is now permitted to drink wine is an appendix to the text which deals exclusively with the Cohen, not the <i>nazir</i>. Therefore, it remains unclear whether the <i>nazir</i> is permitted only after all ceremonies or after the start of the ceremonies, when one of the sacrifices was validated by having its blood sprinkled on the walls of the altar [<i>Num. rabba</i> 6(41)].</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">The following two paragraphs are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.2-3"><i>Nedarim</i> 6:1, Notes 6–14</a>.</i> A Mishnah states that scalding is called cooking, as we have stated: “If he cooked the well-being offering or scalded it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are from <i>Šabbat</i> 7:2 (9c, 1. 11 ff.), as will be seen in the commentary. The variant readings refer to that text. The introductory section is from <i>Šabbat</i> 7:1 (9a, 1. 20–24), the one variant in spelling there is noted by: <b>א</b>.<br>Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 7:2 states that on the Sabbath, 39 different activities are forbidden. This means that a person who violates the Sabbath unintentionally may be liable for up to 39 purification sacrifices. The question then appears whether in other cases multiple sacrifices also are necessary.</i>.” A verse [states] that “roasted” is called “cooked”: “They cooked the <i>pesaḥ</i>”, etc. If you say, against the rules, Rebbi Jonah from Bostra said, “as is the rule”. A Mishnah states that scalded is called cooked: “Is one who makes a vow to abstain from cooked food permitted roasted and scalded food”? Rebbi Joḥanan said, in matters of vows one follows common usage. Rebbi Joshia said, in matters of vows one follows biblical usage. What is the difference between them? ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine on Tabernacles.’ In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan he is forbidden on the last day of the holiday. In the opinion of Rebbi Joshia, is he permitted? Rebbi Joshia also agrees that he is prohibited. Rebbi Joshia said it only for restrictions.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, Rebbi Joḥanan ate bake-meats and said, I did not taste food on that day. But did we not state: “He who made a vow not to eat food is permitted water and salt”? Explain it following Rebbi Joshia, who said, in matters of vows one follows biblical usage. And from where that everything is called food? Rebbi Aḥa bar Ulla said: “And ten female donkeys carrying grain, bread, and food, etc.” Why does the verse say, “and food”? From here that everything is called food.
It is written: “The Cohen takes the cooked fore-leg of the ram.<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:19" href="/Numbers.6.19"><i>Num</i>. 6:19</a></i>” If cooked, I could think separately<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">Since the fore-leg becomes the property of the Cohen and will be forbidden to lay people, its holiness is greater than the remainder of the well-being offering which is consumed by the <i>nazir</i> and his family. The obvious question is whether it is permissible to cook meat of different degrees of holiness together, which is answered in the following paragraphs.</i>. The verse says, “from the ram”. How is this? He cuts it off so that only a barley grain’s width remains. Does not the sanctified absorb from the profane, or the profane from the sanctified?<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">The question is answered in the following paragraphs which are paralleled in <i>‘Orlah</i> 1:4, Notes 137–154. The readings from there are noted <b>ע</b>.</i>.
Ḥilfai asked Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, do condiments forbid with more than 200? They said to him, condiments are not in more than 200. But did we not state: “Anything which sours, flavors, or creates <i>dema</i>‘?” If you say about 100 or 200, even if it does not sour, flavor, or create <i>dema</i>‘! But we deal with grapes. Rebbi Assi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, if they were not raisins, but if they were raisins they are condiments in more than 200. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, if they were not cooked, but if they were cooked they are condiments in more than 200.
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the name of Bar Pedaiah: All sources of flavor one in a hundred. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the name of Bar Pedaiah: All sources of flavor one in sixty. Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said to Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: Rebbi Yasa disagrees with you and the Mishnah disagrees with both of you: “Anything which sours, flavors, or creates <i>dema</i>‘? If you say about 100 or 200, even if it does not sour, flavor, or create <i>dema</i>‘<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the text in <i>‘Orlah</i>. Here: “Make leavened”.</i>! Therefore, we hold even more. Rebbi Jeremiah said, explain it for meat in meat. Rebbi Yose said, meat in meat is the same as all other prohibitions since Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, one estimates as if they were onion or leeks<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.1.6"><i>Terumot</i> 10:1, Notes 10–11</a>.</i>.
How is this? One says, all sources of flavor by one in 100; the other says, all sources of flavor by one in 60. For him who says all sources of taste by one in 100, you take the foreleg as one in 100 of the ram. For him who says all sources of taste by one in 60, you take the foreleg as one in 60 of the ram. For him who says one in 100, you remove the bones from the foreleg. But if you remove the bones from the foreleg, remove them from the ram! This you cannot do, as it was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the text in <i>‘Orlah</i>. Here: “Make leavened”.</i>“The waste of heave does not combine with heave to forbid<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows <i>Terumot</i> and <i>‘Orlah</i>; the text here, “to lift”, makes no sense.</i> the profane, but the waste of profane combines with the profane to lift the heave.” Rebbi Vivian asked: Does the waste of heave combine with profane to lift the heave? Since Rav Ḥuna said, the husks of what is forbidden combine to permit; that means waste of heave combines with profane to lift the heave.
Rebbi Ḥizqiah<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">The reading “Ḥiyya” probably is correct since Ḥizqiah, the collector of baraitot and son of R. Ḥiyya (the Elder) has no title.</i> stated: All I forbade to you at other places I permitted to you here. Since everywhere 100 is a prohibition, more than 100 is permitted, but here even 100 is permitted.
Rav said, waving stops the <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>nazir</i> is not permitted to drink wine or become impure unless he perfomed the waving of his well-being sacrifice. This is also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 46" href="/Nazir.46">Babli, 46a/b</a>, but is rejected there as practice.</i>. But did we not state: “The teachings for the <i>nazir</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:21" href="/Numbers.6.21"><i>Num</i>. 6:21</a>.</i>” whether or not he has wings<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 46b" href="/Nazir.46b">Babli 46b</a>. “The teaching of the <i>nazir</i>” must be applicable to everybody, whether he has arms and hands (“wings”) or not. But since the wavings have to be given “on the <i>nazir</i>’s hands”, how can they be absolutely required if the <i>nazir</i> has no hands?</i>? What Rav says, if he does, as it was stated thus: For somebody able to wave, waving stops him; for somebody unable to wave, waving does not stop him. Samuel says, measure<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">It is unclear what this means. Most authors emend “measure” to “waving”.</i> stops a <i>nazir</i>, as for the waves and thumbs of a sufferer from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">The poor sufferer from skin disease must wave his reparation offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:24" href="/Leviticus.14.24"><i>Lev</i>. 14:24</a>); every healed sufferer from skin disease must receive blood and oil on his right thumb and great toe (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:14" href="/Leviticus.14.14"><i>Lev</i>. 14:14</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:17" href="/Leviticus.14.17">17</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:25" href="/Leviticus.14.25">25</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:28" href="/Leviticus.14.28">28</a>).</i>. But did we not state: “The teachings for the sufferer from skin disease,<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:2" href="/Leviticus.14.2"><i>Lev</i>. 14:2</a>.</i>” whether or not he has thumbs? He explains it following Rebbi Eliezer who said, he puts it on their place<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:9; <i>SifraMeṣora</i>‘<i>Pereq</i> 3(11); quoted similarly in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 61b" href="/Yoma.61b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 61b</a> (cf. <i>Diqduqe Soferim Yoma</i> p. 171 Note ח). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 47b" href="/Nazir.47b">Babli <i>Nazir</i>, 47b</a>, (with a different editorial history) R. Eliezer holds that he cannot ever be purified; R. Simeon is quoted parallel to R. Eliezer in the other sources.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he shaved for one of the sacrifices and it turned out to be invalid, his shaving is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">And he has to wait another 30 days to regrow his hair.</i> and his sacrifices are not counted for him. If he shaved for the purification offering not in its name<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">A purification offering must be slaughtered explicitly for that purpose; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:33" href="/Leviticus.4.33"><i>Lev</i>. 4:33</a>.</i> but brought the other sacrifices in their names, his shaving is invalid and his sacrifices are not counted for him<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">Elevation and well-being offerings are valid and can be brought to the altar. But since the <i>nazir</i> is required to bring all three sacrifices together, this Tanna holds that they cannot be counted as fulfilling his obligation.</i>. If he shaved for the elevation or well-being offerings not in their names<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">These are valid sacrifices even if slaughtered for the wrong purpose “but are not counted for their owners to relieve them of their obligations” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i> 1:1</a>).</i> but then brought his sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">The remaining two.</i> in their names, his shaving is invalid and his sacrifices are not counted for him. Rebbi Simeon says, that sacrifice is not counted for him but the others are counted for him<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">Except for the purification offering not slaughtered for its purpose which is disqualified by biblical law.</i>. But if he shaved for all three<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">Without specifying for which one he shaved; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.7.1">Mishnah 9</a>.</i> and one of them is valid, his shaving is valid and he has to repeat the other sacrifices.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Is it counted for him and his shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">If a <i>nazir</i> shaved for an improper sacrifice when afterwards he brought two proper sacrifices, R. Simeon said that the two proper sacrifices are counted for him. But if the shaving were improper, he would have to wait another 30 days and bring a new set of sacrifices, and there would be no way in which the earlier sacrifices could be counted for him. It is proved that for R. Simeon, shaving is valid as long as one sacrifice was valid.</i>? If you would say that it is not counted for him and his shaving, then his sacrifices count for nothing. Rebbi Joḥanan asked: Following Rebbi Simeon, may he shave for his holiday well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">He shaves on a holiday and brings with him not only the three obligatory sacrifices of a <i>nazir</i> but additional sacrifices for the holiday.</i>? Is that not the Mishnah? “Rebbi Simeon says, that sacrifice is not counted for him but the others are counted for him.” When he slaughtered them<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">The well-being or elevation offerings which are acceptable as gifts to the altar if slaughtered without indicating the kind of sacrifices they should be.</i> are they not like voluntary well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">In rabbinic terminology, a sacrifice corresponds to a <i>vow</i> (נדר) if a person engages himself for a certain kind of sacrifice. Then if the animal chosen to satisfy the vow becomes unusable for any reason, the vower has to provide a replacement animal. A voluntary gift (נדבה) is an animal dedicated as sacrifice. If it becomes unusable for any reason, the owner does not have to provide a replacement. Well-being and elevation sacrifices slaughtered for the wrong purpose or no purpose fall under the category of voluntary gifts.</i>? This means that he may shave for voluntary well-being offerings following Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Yose bar Abun said<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">He corrects the tradition: the person who asks is R. Ada bar Aḥawa, not R. Joḥanan.</i>, Rav Ada bar Aḥawa and Rebbi Joḥanan. For Rav Ada bar Aḥawa it is problematic<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">But in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 46b" href="/Nazir.46b">Babli, 46b</a>, he is quoted as proving the statement attributed here to R. Joḥanan.</i>, but for Rebbi Joḥanan it is obvious.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one of the bloods had been sprinkled for him<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">Sprinkling its blood on the walls of the altar makes any sacrifice irrevocably valid.</i> when he became impure, Rebbi Eliezer says, he has to repeat everything<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">Not the <i>nezirut</i>, but all his sacrifices since he holds, against the Sages, that the sacrifices cannot be brought separately (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.1.1">Mishnah 8:4</a>).</i>. But the Sages say, he shall bring his remaining sacrifices once he became pure again<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">And can enter the Temple precinct.</i>. They told him, it happened with Miriam the Palmyrene, that one of the bloods had been sprinkled for her when she was informed that her daughter was dying; she went and found her dead<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">And defiled herself for her daughter.</i>; then the Sages said, she shall bring her remaining sacrifices once she becomes pure again<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 4:10" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).4.10">Tosephta 4:10</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The argument of Rebbi Eliezer seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.4.1">Mishnah 3:4</a>.</i>, he says: “he has to repeat only for 30,” and here, he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">If he becomes incapacitated, he has to repeat only for 30 days. If his sacrifice becomes invalid, why should he have to start everything anew?</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan says, he has to repeat all his sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">Not the <i>nezirut</i>, but all his sacrifices since he holds, against the Sages, that the sacrifices cannot be brought separately (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.1.1">Mishnah 8:4</a>).</i>. It is obvious, if it would have been valid without him becoming impure, then why is it not valid if later he becomes impure<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">Why should R. Eliezer not agree that once part of the ceremony was performed correctly, he can replace any missing part?</i>? Rebbi Ḥinena said, is that not Rebbi Eliezer’s? And Rebbi Eliezer follows the Shammaites, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 46b" href="/Nazir.46b">Babli 46b</a> (in different formulation), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 61b" href="/Yoma.61b"><i>Yoma</i> 61b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a>. As the Babli explains, the House of Shammai require him to shave, but since he cannot shave he never can terminate his <i>nezirut</i>.</i>: Concerning a <i>nazir</i> who lost all his hair, the House of Shammai say, he has to move a shaving knife over his head; the House of Hillel say, he does not need to move a shaving knife over his head.
So is the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah is formulated with a temporal inversion. The action to become pure must precede the offering of his or her sacrifices.</i>: He shall become pure again and then bring his remaining sacrifices. So is the Mishnah: She shall become pure again and then bring her remaining sacrifices.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The High Priest and the <i>nazir</i> do not defile themselves for their relatives<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The High Priest is forbidden to defile himself even for father or mother, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:11" href="/Leviticus.21.11"><i>Lev</i>. 21:11</a>; the same holds for the <i>nazir</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:7" href="/Numbers.6.7"><i>Num</i>. 6:7</a>. In the Babli and many Mishnah mss. (but not in Maimonides’s autograph) there is an additional clause: “but they have to defile themselves for a corpse of obligation”, i. e., an abandoned corpse of whose burial nobody is taking care. The first person finding the body has to bury him.</i>. If they were walking on a road and found a corpse of obligation, Rebbi Eliezer says, the High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Even the High Priest, and certainly a common priest. Similarly, the Sages require that the <i>nazir</i> defile himself but not a common priest if they stumble on the corpse together.</i> shall defile himself but the <i>nazir</i> shall not defile himself. But the Sages say, the <i>nazir</i> shall defile himself but the High Priest shall not defile himself. Rebbi Eliezer said to them, the Priest shall defile himself, who does not bring a sacrifice for his defilement, but the <i>nazir</i> shall not defile himself, who has to bring a sacrifice for his defilement. They told him, the <i>nazir</i> shall defile himself, whose holiness is temporary, but the Priest shall not defile himself, whose holiness is permanent.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The High Priest and the <i>nazir</i>,” etc. It is written: “He shall not go close to a dead body.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:11" href="/Leviticus.21.11"><i>Lev</i>. 21:11</a>, speaking of the High Priest. A parallel argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 47b" href="/Nazir.47b">Babli, 47b</a>.</i>” Where do we hold? If to forbid non-relatives, is he not also under the rules of a simple priest<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A common priest is forbidden to defile himself for any dead person other than close relatives, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:1-3" href="/Leviticus.21.1-3"><i>Lev</i>. 21:1–3</a>.</i>? If it cannot refer to non-relatives, refer it to relatives. It is written: “Not to go close to a dead body,” and you say so? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Gamda said, from here repeated prohibitions in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, the High Priest who defiles himself for a corpse violates two identical prohibitions.</i>. But it is to permit the corpse of obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the rule that “an exclusion on top of an exclusion means an inclusion.” Since in general a Cohen is forbidden to defile himself for the dead, the repetition of the prohibition for the High Priest indicates an obligation to defile himself in some cases. Since father and mother, for whom defilement is commanded to the common priest, are forbidden to the High Priest, the corpse for whom the High Priest (and by implication, any priest) has to defile himself is a non-relative, the corpse of obligation.</i>. Some understand it from the following: “<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:4" href="/Leviticus.21.4"><i>Lev</i>. 21:4</a>.</i> The man shall not defile himself, in the midst of his people” he may not defile himself<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If other Jews are present who may bury the dead, he may not defile himself. By implication, if he is alone, he must defile himself.</i>. But he defiles himself for a corpse of obligation. Some understand it from the following: “<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:4" href="/Leviticus.21.4"><i>Lev</i>. 21:4</a>.</i> To profane himself.” He may not defile himself to profane himself; he defiles himself for a corpse of obligation.
Some want to derive it from the following: “For a hanged person is blasphemy.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:23" href="/Deuteronomy.21.23"><i>Deut</i>. 21:23</a>: “You may not leave his corpse on the gallows overnight, but bury, you shall bury him on that day, for a hanged person is blasphemy …”</i>” Anybody warned about blasphemy is warned about a corpse of obligation. Anybody not warned about blasphemy is not warned about a corpse of obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This means that the details of the rules for burials apply only to Jews to whom the details of the rules of blasphemy apply.</i>. They objected, are there not Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of blasphemy is part of the “natural law” given to Adam and Noaḥ (Tosephta <i>‘Avodah zarah</i> 9:4, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 56" href="/Sanhedrin.56">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 56a/b</a>).</i>? Those executed by hanging, this exludes those executed by the sword<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The only method of execution recognized for Gentiles, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 57a" href="/Sanhedrin.57a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 57a</a>.</i>. “Bury!” A positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This is a shortened version of the explanation in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 220: “You may not leave his corpse on the gallows overnight,” a prohibition, “but bury!”, a positive commandment. A biblical commandment which is both positive and negative is the strongest of all commandments and will supersede opposing simple positive or simple negative commandments (Babli <i>Yom Ṭob</i> 8b, severely restricted in <i>Yebamot</i> 3b/4a) if written in the same verse (Yerushalmi <i>Yom Ṭob</i> 1:3, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 2:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.2.1.5"><i>Ḥallah</i> 2:1, Note 10</a>, unrestricted).</i>. From where do you add the sword he was killed with, the gallows on which he was hanged, the towel with which he was strangled? Why does the verse say, “you shall bury him”? I could think he alone is buried; the verse says “bury, you shall bury him”, a burial for him, and his gallows, and his stone<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 45b" href="/Sanhedrin.45b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 45b</a>.</i>. How does one do it? One digs down three [handbreadths], so the plough shall not unearth him. “You shall bury him”, whole and not partially. “You shall bury him”, from here that if he left anything [unburied], he did not do anything, for it is said “bury, you shall bury him”. From here that it is not a corpse of obligation unless it consists of his head with most of the body.
Rebbi Yasa stated before Rebbi Joḥanan: Just as one defiles himself for a corpse of obligation, so one defiles himself for a limb of a corpse of obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This contradicts the preceding statement.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan answered him: Is that so? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: Explain it if he returns<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If a Cohen was burying a corpse of obligation and later found another limb of the same corpse, he may bury the limb since he already is impure and the prohibition of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:1" href="/Leviticus.21.1"><i>Lev</i>. 21:1</a> refers only to him <i>becoming</i> impure, not to what he does once he is impure. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 43b" href="/Nazir.43b">Babli, 43b</a>, this is a tannaïtic statement ascribed to R. Jehudah.</i>. It was stated: Rebbi Yose said, nobody has to defile himself for a limb from his living father, but one has to defile himself for bone the size of a barley corn from his father. Rebbi Jehudah says, just as one has to defile himself for bone the size of a barley corn from his father, so one has to defile himself for a limb from his living father<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Śemaḥot</i> 4:27. In the Babylonian sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 43b" href="/Nazir.43b">Babli 43b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 13" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.13"><i>Sifra Emor Introduction</i> 13</a>, R. Jehudah represents the opinion ascribed here to R. Yose, while R. Yose denies that a Cohen defiles himself for a piece of his father’s bone. The reason given is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:3" href="/Leviticus.21.3"><i>Lev.</i> 21:3</a> (speaking of the Cohen’s unmarried sister): “For her he has to defile himself,” interpreted as “for her body, but not for her body parts.”</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 14" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.14"><i>Sifra Emor Introduction</i> 14</a>. <i>Śemaḥot</i> 4:28 (יוסף פסקס), <i>Yalqut Qohelet</i> 976 (יוסף הכהן). It is clear from the story that one speaks of a priestly family.</i>“It happened that Yose ben Paxas developed a growth<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Greek νομή “cancerous growth”.</i> on his foot. When the surgeon came to remove it, he told him to inform him when only a hair’s breadth was left<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Connecting the growth to his body.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The surgeon.</i> cut until only a hair’s breadth was left, and informed him. He<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Yose ben Paxas.</i> addressed his son Onias and told him, Onias my son, up to now you had to care for me, but leave now since nobody has to defile himself for a limb from his living father<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since the growth, once separated from his body, is impure in the impurity of the dead (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 2:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 2:1</a>).</i>. When this came before the Sages, they said, about him it was said: “It happens that a just man is lost in his merit<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 7:15" href="/Ecclesiastes.7.15"><i>Eccl</i>. 7:15</a>. The implication is that practice follows R. Yose.</i>;” the just is lost and his merit accompanies him.
What is a corpse of obligation? Anyone for whom he shouts and nobody comes<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 43b" href="/Nazir.43b">Babli (43b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 89b" href="/Yevamot.89b"><i>Yebamot</i> 89b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17b" href="/Eruvin.17b"><i>Erubin</i> 17b</a>): “Anyone who has nobody to bury him; if he calls and others answer him it is not a corpse of obligation.” The Yerushalmi version is in <i>Śemaḥot</i> 4:29.</i>. If the villagers come, he refrains<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Cohen or <i>nazir</i> refrain from defiling themselves.</i>. How many? For the carriers of the bier, their replacements, and the replacements of their replacements<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Śemaḥot</i> 4:30: Carriers of the bier and grave diggers.</i>. If he is not needed; but if he is needed, it is different<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If the dead cannot have a decent burial without the help of <i>nazir</i> or Cohen, the latter have to defile themselves.</i>. If [the deceased] is not recognized<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If the deceased is not recognized by the locals; if he is recognized the <i>nazir</i> or Cohen is precluded from defiling himself.</i>. But if he is recognized, it is different. If it is not according to his honor; but if it is according to his honor, it is different<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This is the introduction to the following paragraphs. If the prestige of the deceased is so great that priests have to defile themselve for his burial, it is obvious that the <i>nazir</i> or Cohen who stumbles on his body also has to defile himself.</i>. For the Patriarch it is his honor.
<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The same text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.22"><i>Berakhot</i> 3:1, Notes 95–101</a>. The following paragraphs (to 56a, line 68) are from the same source, Notes 64–120.</i> May a Cohen defile himself for the Patriarch? When Rebbi Judah the Prince died, Rebbi Yannai proclaimed and said: “There is no priesthood today.” When Rebbi Judah the Prince, grandson of Rebbi Judah the Prince, died, Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba did push Rebbi Zeïra<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Berakhot</i> text clearly is the correct one.</i> in the Gufna synagogue of Sepphoris and defiled him. When Yehudinai, the sister of Rebbi Judah the Prince, died, Rebbi Ḥanina sent for Rebbi Mana but the latter did not come. He said to him, if one does not defile himself for them during their lifetime, so much less in their death. Rebbi Nassa said, in their death they treated them like a corpse of obligation.
May a Cohen defile himself for the honor of his teacher? The father-in-law of Rebbi Yannai the Younger died. He was both his father-in-law and his teacher. He asked Rebbi Yose who forbade it. Rebbi Ḥama (?) heard it and said: His students should defile themselves for him. The students [of Rebbi Yose]<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">From the text in <i>Berakhot</i>, required by the context.</i> defiled themselves for him but ate meat and drank wine. Rebbi Mana told them: One of two things you cannot escape. If you are mourners, why did you eat meat and drink wine; if you are not mourners, why did you defile yourselves?
May a Cohen defile himself for the study of Torah? Rebbi Yose was sitting and teaching when a dead body was brought in. He did not say anything either to those who remained sitting or to those who left.
Rebbi Nihumi the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said: My father did not walk under the arch of Caesarea. Rebbi Ammi walked. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Cohen and Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa went walking in the streets of Caesarea. They came to the arch and Rebbi Cohen left. They reached a place of purity and he returned to them. He asked them: “What were you discussing?” Rebbi Ḥizqiah said to Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: “Do not tell him anything!” We do not know whether it was because he was angry that he left since one defiles oneself for words of Torah or whether it was because he was haughty.
It is stated: A Cohen may defile himself by leaving the Land for civil and criminal suits, for the consecration of the New Moon and intercalation  of a year, to redeem a field from a Gentile, to study Torah, and to marry a wife. Rebbi Judah says, if he has a place to study, he should not defile himself. Rebbi Yose says, even if he has a place to study, he may defile himself, since a man may not have the luck to learn from every teacher. They said about Joseph the Cohen that he followed his teacher and defiled himself following his teacher to Sidon. In truth, they said a priest should not go abroad unless one promised him a wife.
May a (High) Priest defile himself for the lifting of hands? Gabilah, the brother of Rebbi Abba bar Cohen said before Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: A Cohen defiles himself for the lifting of hands. Rebbi Aḥa heard it and said: I never told him anything. On second thoughts he said, maybe he heard from me what Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Every Cohen who stays in the synagogue and does not lift his hands transgresses a positive commandment, and he wanted to say that a positive commandment supersedes a negative commandment. I never told him anything. Bring him in and I will flog him!
Rebbi Abbahu was sitting teaching in the fortified synagogue in Caesarea; there was a coffin there. There came the time for lifting the hands and they did not ask him. There came the time for eating and they asked him. He said to them: For the lifting of hands you did not ask me, for eating you are asking me? When they heard this, each one was taking himself away and fled.
Rebbi Yannai said: A Cohen defiles himself in order to see the King. When King Diocletian visited here, Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba was seen stepping over graves at Tyre in order to see him. Rebbi Ḥizqiah and Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: There is an obligation to see great persons of government, so that when the kingdom of the dynasty of David will return one will know how to distinguish one government from the other.
May [a Cohen] defile himself in honor of his father and mother? Rebbi Yasa heard that his mother had come to Bostra. He went and asked Rebbi Yoḥanan, may I leave? He said to him, if it is because of danger on the road, leave. If it is in order to honor your mother, I do not know. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, Rebbi Yoḥanan still needs to answer. He importuned him, so he said: Since you decided to go, return in peace. Rebbi Eleazar heard this and said: there is no greater permission than that.
May a (person) [Cohen] defile himself in honor of the public? It is stated: When there are two acceptable roads, one long and pure, the other one short and impure: If the public was walking on the long one, he goes on the long one; otherwise, he goes on the short one in honor of the public. That refers to impurity by their words; also for impurity that is from the words of the Torah? From what Rebbi Zeïra said, so great is the honor of the public that it temporarily pushes aside a prohibition, that means even impurity that is from the words of the Torah.
Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Yose the Galilean, in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: One does not ask rulings on practice before the bier of a deceased. But Rebbi Yoḥanan asked Rebbi Yannai before the bier of Rebbi Simeon ben Yoẓadaq about him who dedicated his elevation sacrifice for the upkeep of the Sanctuary, and he answered him! Let us say when they were bringing him to the study hall. But Rebbi Jeremiah asked Rebbi Zeïra before the bier of (Rebbi Simeon and) Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac! Let us say that he answered him when he was far away; when he was close he did not answer him.
It is stated: The carriers may not wear sandals lest a shoelace of one of them break so he would be prevented from performing a good deed.
Rebbi Zeïra bent down during a eulogy. They wanted to straighten him up and found that he tarried. They said to him, what is this? He said to them, because we will go there, following (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 7:2" href="/Ecclesiastes.7.2"><i>Eccl</i>. 7:2</a>): “Let the one who is alive take it to heart.”
If somebody finds a corpse of obligation, he has to take care of it and bury it at its place<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of “its place” is given in the paragraph after the next.</i>. When is that? If he finds it outside the town limits<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Outside of 2000 cubits from the built-up domain of a village, or the city walls.</i>. But if he found it inside the town limits, he brings it to the cemetary to bury it.
<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Śemaḥot</i> 4:34; <i>Derekh Ereṣ zuṭa</i> 8. Quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Ketubot 17a" href="/Tosafot_on_Ketubot.17a"><i>Tosaphot Ketubot</i> 17a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 29a" href="/Megillah.29a"><i>Megillah</i> 29a, <i>s.v.</i> מבטלין</a>.</i> Rebbi Aqiba said, the start of my practice before the Sages was the following. Once I was on the road when I found a corpse of obligation; I carried it about four <i>mil</i> to a cemetery and buried it. When I came to Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua and told them, they said to me that every step that you walked is counted against you as if you were shedding blood<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since the corpse was removed from its natural place of burial.</i>. I said, if I became guilty when I intended to acquire merit, how much worse is it if I do not intend to acquire merit. From that time on I did not interrupt practicing before Sages. He used to say, anybody who did not practice before Sages deserves the death penalty.
The corpse of obligation acquired its place, four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A square of land, four cubits in each direction, can be taken to dig the grave without requesting permission from the owner, who has no claim to compensation.</i> even in a field of saffron, for on this condition did Joshua distribute the Land to Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, these rules do not apply outside the Land. The list of reservations for public use attributed to Joshua is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.1.2-7"><i>Baba batra</i> 5:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 80b-82a" href="/Bava_Kamma.80b-82a">Babli <i>Baba qama</i> 80b–82a</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.3.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 2:5, Note 73</a>.</i>. When is this? If he found it in the field. But if he found it on the road, he buries it either to the right or to the left of the road<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Use of the road is public use; use of land for burial of a corpse of obligation is public use. The later use cannot displace the prior. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17b" href="/Eruvin.17b">Babli, <i>Eruvin</i> 17b</a>, explains this <i>baraita</i> away, assuming that the corpse was lying across the narrow roadway and encroaching on the fields on either side. According to Rashi, the corpse may be moved to protect passing Cohanim from the impurity of the dead.</i>. A fallow and a ploughed field, he buries it in the fallow field<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If the corpse was found on the road between two fields.</i>. A ploughed and a sown field, he buries it in the ploughed field. A vineyard and a sown field, he buries it in the sown field. A vineyard and an orchard, some Tannaïm state, he buries it in the vineyard; some Tannaïm state, he buries it in the orchard. He who says, he buries it in the vineyard but not in the orchard, because of a tent over the corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If a grave is is the shade of a tree, the entire canopy of the tree forms a “tent” over the grave; any person passing under the tree becomes impure by the impurity of the dead (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:14" href="/Numbers.19.14"><i>Num</i>. 19:14</a>).</i>. He who says, he buries it in the orchard but not in the vineyard because of the preparation of the grape harvest<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since wine-grapes collected in a basket at harvest time will be wet from the juice of some squashed grapes, they are “prepared” for impurity (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Note 141</a>), and a grave in a vineyard makes its entire harvest irreparably impure.</i>. If he switched, Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: He transgressed “do not defile your land<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:23" href="/Deuteronomy.21.23"><i>Deut</i>. 21:23</a>.</i>.” If both were fallow, or ploughed, or sown, he buries in the one he chooses.
A Cohen and a Levite, the Levite shall defile himself<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since a Levite is not forbidden to defile himself.</i>. A Levite and an Israel, the Israel shall defile himself. No, is not the Levite like the Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In matters of impurity, the Levite has no more obligations than the Israel.</i>? Rebbi Abin said, it was taught in the time of the platform<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The platform in the Temple for the levitic singers. If the Levite was engaged in the Temple service, he must be careful not to become disabled by impurity.</i>. The Sages agree with Rebbi Eliezer that between a High Priest and a <i>nazir</i>, the <i>nazir</i> shall defile himself but not the High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This is the position of the Sages in the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer agrees with the Sages that between a High Priest and a <i>nazir</i>, the <i>nazir</i> shall defile himself but not the High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer holds to his position in the Mishnah only for a common priest, not the High Priest.</i>. Think of it, if his father sanctified him from birth<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Like Samson or Samuel. The reference is to the argument of the Sages that the <i>nazir</i> has to defile himself because his holiness is temporary. If the <i>nazir</i> is <i>nazir</i> from birth, that argument does not hold but the formulation of the Sages in the first sentence of the Mishnah does not admit of any exception.</i>. The one<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The holiness of the Cohen.</i> is from the Torah, the other is not from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The possibility of a <i>nazir</i> from birth is only given in the Prophets.</i>. Rebbi Huna in the name of Rav Joseph: Rebbi Eliezer parallels the House of Shammai. As the House of Shammai say, between holy and frequent the holy has precedence<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.1.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 8:1</a>, the House of Shammai require that on Friday nights, the benediction for the Sanctification of the Sabbath (the holy) precede the benediction for the wine (the frequent).</i>, so Rebbi Eliezer says, between holy and frequent the holy has precedence<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">For him, the <i>nazir</i> who must bring a sacrifice for his defilement is holier than the Cohen who brings no sacrifice.</i>. A <i>nazir</i> for 30 days and a <i>nazir</i> for 100, the <i>nazir</i> for 30 days shall defile himself. A <i>nazir</i> for 100 days and a <i>nazir</i> forever, the <i>nazir</i> for 100 days shall defile himself. A <i>nazir</i> forever<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">He cuts his hair (but does not shave it off) once a year and brings three sacrifices; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.2-10">Halakhah 1:2</a>.</i>, and a <i>nazir</i> of <i>neziriot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Similar to one described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:4</a>; from the argument it seems that the <i>nazir</i> referred to here has vowed many periods of more than one year.</i>, some Tannaïm state, the <i>nazir</i> forever shall defile himself; but some Tannaïm state, the <i>nazir</i> of <i>neziriot</i> shall defile himself. He who says, the <i>nazir</i> forever shall defile himself but not the <i>nazir</i> of <i>neziriot</i>, since he<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>nazir</i> forever shall defile himself since he never shaves.</i> shaves more frequently. He who says, the <i>nazir</i> of <i>neziriot</i> shall defile himself but not the <i>nazir</i> forever, since he brings more sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">If the basic period of the <i>nazir</i> of <i>neziriot</i> is more than one year, the <i>nazir</i> forever brings more sacrifices.</i>.
