If he switched, it should be as Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: He transgressed “do not defile your land<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:23" href="/Deuteronomy.21.23"><i>Deut</i>. 21:23</a>.</i>.” Here it is the same.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The <i>nazir</i> shaves for the following impurities: For a corpse, for flesh in the volume of an olive of a corpse, and for the volume of an olive of decayed matter from a corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Either decaying flesh or fluid from the corpse. If it is decayed so that it looks like dust it is counted as decay and follows the standard of the spoonful (under certain conditions). It is implied that all impurities enumerated in the Mishnah are biblical in nature.</i>, and for a spoonful of decay, for the spine and for the skull<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Even if no flesh is left.</i>, for a limb from a corpse or a limb from the living on which there is sufficient flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">“Sufficient flesh” is enough left on a limb connected to a living body that it could heal.</i>, for half a <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>qab</i> is 4 <i>log</i> or 2.13 liter.</i> of bones, and for half a <i>log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>qab</i> is 4 <i>log</i> or 2.13 liter.</i> of blood, if they are touched, or carried<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the carrier does not touch the cause of impurity.</i>, or under a tent<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If a grave is is the shade of a tree, the entire canopy of the tree forms a “tent” over the grave; any person passing under the tree becomes impure by the impurity of the dead (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:14" href="/Numbers.19.14"><i>Num</i>. 19:14</a>).</i>. Also for a bone in the volume of a barley grain if it is touched, or carried, (or under a tent.)<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The last clause, which originally was also in Babli mss., is a scribal error since only half a <i>qab</i> of bones transmits impurity in a tent but less than that transmits impurity only by touch or carrying, not in a tent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Nazir 49b:1:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Nazir.49b.1.1">Tosaphot 49b, <i>s. v.</i> על משאו</a>).</i> For these, the <i>nazir</i> shaves, he sprinkles on the third and seventh [days], he disregards the preceding days and starts to count only after he purifies himself and brings all his sacrifices.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The <i>nazir</i> shaves for the following impurities,” etc. An old man asked Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 49b-50b" href="/Nazir.49b-50b">Babli, 49b/50b</a>, the entire discussion is tannaïtic: Symmachos, a student of R. Meïr, asked R. Jehudah.</i>: If the volume of an olive from a corpse makes impure, then certainly all of it also<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation of the Mishnah is redundant. If one <i>kezayit</i> of a corpse induces the impurity of the dead, why is it necessary to say that a complete corpse induces the impurity?</i>? He said to him, to include the stillbirth which did not reach the volume of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.3.3.1">Mishnaiot <i>Niddah</i> 3:3</a> ff.</i>. He continued to ask: If a limb of a corpse makes impure, then certainly all of it also? He said to him, to include the stillbirth whose limbs did not yet jell<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">No limb has a recognizable shape.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, was that old man wise? His questions were not wise since after he asked the first question, it was not necessary to ask the second. If he wanted to ask both, he should have asked the second and after that the first<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If the fetus does not fill the volume of an olive, it is to be assumed that its limbs are not recognizable. If its limbs are not recognizable, the body still might be larger than the volume of an olive.</i>. The students of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥalaphta<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">He is “R. Yose” the Tanna; in the Babli, he gives the explanations ascribed in the Yerushalmi to R. Joḥanan.</i> asked the second but did not ask the first because it was obvious for them that if he did not reach the volume of an olive it is certain that his limbs did not yet jell. Rebbi Mana bar<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">There is no “R. Mana bar Hizqiah” mentioned otherwise in the entire talmudic/midrashic literature. Moreover, it is not mentioned who his partner was. Therefore, it is reasonable to follow all commentators and emend בר to לר׳ and read: “R. Mana (II) said to R. Ḥizqiah.”</i> Ḥizqiah said, do you remember when Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa was here, were not both questions by that old man [asked] about <i>Ahilut</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The only method of execution recognized for Gentiles, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 57a" href="/Sanhedrin.57a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 57a</a>.</i> that the <i>nazir</i> shaves for them<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose’s criticism of the old man is unjustified since it would not be inconsistent with either Mishnah to hold that a corpse induces biblical impurity but a small part of the corpse only rabbinic impurity. In that case, the double mention in the Mishnah would be justified and, as noted in the sequel, R. Joḥanan once held this opinion.</i>? In addition, from what Rebbi Joḥanan said, I thought that the stillbirths were not Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">It is generally accepted that a termination of pregnancy within 40 days of conception is simply a failed conception, not an abortion, since there is no human fetus (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.3.5.1"><i>Niddah</i> 3:5, Note 135</a>). The status of the fetus between the 41<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> day and delivery is never defined in the biblical text.</i>. But since he makes his mother sit days of purity<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnaiot <i>Niddah</i> 3:3–5 state that if there was a miscarriage and the sex of the fetus cannot be determined, the mother has to observe 14 days of impurity (for a girl) but the next 26 days are days of purity during which no genital discharge will make her impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 3:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.3.5.2"><i>Niddah</i> 3:2, Note 14</a>). Since normally any genital discharge induces the seven-day impurity of menstruation in a woman, the rules of Tractate <i>Niddah</i> imply that the impurity of a stillbirth is biblical. If the impurity were rabbinic, it could not imply immunity (by necessity biblical) from impurity.</i>, it is implied that [the rules of] stillbirths are Torah.
It was stated: Rebbi Simeon bar Ioḥai says, why did they say that a crawling animal<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29-30" href="/Leviticus.11.29-30"><i>Lev</i>. 11:29–30</a>.</i> the size of a lentil makes impure? Because the start of the creation of a crawling animal is the size of a lentil<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 52a" href="/Nazir.52a">Babli, 52a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 56a" href="/Niddah.56a"><i>Niddah</i> 56a</a>, the parallel is an anonymous tannaïtic statement, deriving the power of tradition to determine the amount which induces impurity from the change in wording from בהם in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:31" href="/Leviticus.11.31"><i>Lev</i>. 11:31</a> to מהם in 11:32. Since that change is required by the meaning of the verses, the Babli’s source is the school of R. Aqiba.</i>. Rebbi Yudan asked: Should not a corpse bring impurity even if it is less than the volume of an olive, for did not Rebbi Joḥanan say, “to include the stillbirth which did not reach the volume of an olive”<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This in itself is an irrelevant question since R. Joḥanan makes it clear, here and elsewhere, that the minimal size requirements do not apply to fully formed creatures (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1, Note 17</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 51b" href="/Nazir.51b">Babli <i>Nazir</i> 51b</a>).</i>? And a carcass<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">From an otherwise kosher animal but which dies without being slaughtered.</i> the size of a pea should cause impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">When in fact the unchallenged tradition is that a minimum volume of an olive is required.</i>, for Rebbi Ḥanina said, I saw a calf the size of a pea in a placenta! How is that? I say, [these are] sermons. Preach and receive reward<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">But rules of practice should never be derived from sermons.</i>.
What is decayed matter? Flesh of the corpse which was separated<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This text does not make much sense. The parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 50a" href="/Nazir.50a">Babli, 50a</a>, reads בָּשָׂר הַמֵּת <b>שֶׁקָּרַשׁ</b> וְהַמּוֹהַל <b>שֶׁהִרְתִּיחַ</b>, “Flesh of the corpse that coagulated and fluid that was heated.” A gloss in the <i>Arukh</i> (<i>s. v.</i> נצל) explains: “A stillbirth whose flesh coagulated but did not firm.” The quote from the Yerushalmi reads in the <i>Arukh</i>: בָּשָׂר הַמֵּת <b>שֶׁנָּצַל</b> וְהַמּוֹהַל שֶׁקָּרַשׁ which can also be translated as: “Flesh of the corpse which was separated and fluid that coagulated”, the difference being that נצל means “to separate” in an abstract sense, as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 31:16" href="/Genesis.31.16"><i>Gen</i>. 31:16</a> where Rachel and Leah congratulate themselves that God had <i>separated</i> Laban’s property from him and <i>transferred</i> it to them, while נתק means bodily separation, “tearing apart.” The suggestion by M. Margalit and J. Sussman to read נתוך instead of נתוק has no basis. It is remarkable that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.2.2">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> (2:3</a>, quoted later in our text) goes into the details of the rules of decay but except for a fleeting reference to our Mishnah (<i>Ahilut</i> 4:5) never mentions נֵצֶל. If the text is taken as it stands, one might define <i>nēṣel</i> as “fibers from the corpse and fluid that coagulated.”</i> and fluid that coagulated. Therefore not when it<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The blood in an intermediate state between fluidity and coagulation. It is unreasonable to assume that the blood should be impure as fluid and as fully coagulated matter, but not in an intermediate state.</i> is still mashed? Did not Rebbi Ḥanina say in the name of Rebbi: <sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The text is paralleled in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 50a" href="/Nazir.50a">Babli, 50a</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.4.3">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 4:3</a>. In the latter, a Babylonian scribe or compiler systematically wrote חתך for התך, היתיך (as noted by D. Pardo).</i> Fat from a corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The text is a contamination of two versions. “The fat of a corpse” may either be חֵלֶב הַמֵּת or חֶלְבּוֹ שֶׁל הַמֵּת.</i> which was melted remains impure; if he cut it<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The fat was cut in pieces smaller than olive size. Either this was done outside a “tent” (because all body parts of a corpse under one “tent” are joined together by the “tent”; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.4.3">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 4:4</a>), or small pieces of fat from two different corpses are fused together by heating. The resulting olive-sized piece is not impure since “connection by a human is not counted” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.4.3">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 4:3</a>).</i> and then melted it, it is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Then the question is, if the state of the material does not matter, why should the mashed state matter?</i>. Rebbi Yudan and Rebbi Yose, one says to exclude the fluid in the flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Any fluid which is not blood but oozes from a corpse is impure only if it became solid in an olive-sized piece.</i>; the other says that if it will fill the volume of an olive when congealed, it will be impure when still mashed<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">An exactly olive-sized piece of colloidal material will be pure since in solid form it will have shrunk.</i>.
We have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Niddah</i> 7:1.</i>: “Rebbi Yose said, dried flesh from a corpse which even if soaked<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 56a" href="/Niddah.56a">Babli, <i>Niddah</i> 56a</a>, explains soaking 24 hours in lukewarm water.</i> will not return to its former status is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 56a" href="/Niddah.56a">Babli, <i>Niddah</i> 56a</a>, this is qualified to mean that there is no impurity of flesh, but there is impurity of decay (requiring a spoonful for impurity.)</i>. What is Rebbi Yose’s reason? Did he not infer this from a carcass? From a carcass, since a dried carcass is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 56a" href="/Niddah.56a">Babli, <i>Niddah</i> 56a</a>.</i>, so also a dried corpse should be pure. Rebbi Immi asked: Since a foul-smelling carcass is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 23b" href="/Bekhorot.23b">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i> 23b</a>. The argument is based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a> which requires the animal carcass to be given to the non-Jewish resident or sold to the stranger. It is inferred that once a carcass can no longer be given away or sold, it has lost its legal standing as carcass. This corresponds to the rule, expounded at length in Tractate <i>Kelim</i>, that impure objects become pure once they have lost any commercial value.</i>, would a foul-smelling corpse also be pure? Then there can be no decayed matter for Rebbi Yose! It was found stated in Rebbi Yose’s name that there was decayed matter<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 56a" href="/Niddah.56a">Babli, <i>Niddah</i> 56a</a>, this is qualified to mean that there is no impurity of flesh, but there is impurity of decay (requiring a spoonful for impurity.)</i>. The colleagues asked before Rebbi Yose: Since a carcass has no decay<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">By the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.2.5">Note 95</a>, a decaying animal carcass has no commercial value and, therefore, is pure. “No decay” means “no impurity classified under the heading of ‘decay’ ”.</i>, so a corpse should have no decay! He told them that he inferred from an animal only for the flesh, not for the bones. There is no decay from flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of the colleagues is well taken; R. Yose accepts the impurity of human decay only for decaying bone material, not for decayed flesh.</i>, there is decay from bones, as Bar Qappara stated, “decay of bones is jealousy<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 14:30" href="/Proverbs.14.30"><i>Prov</i>. 14:30</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Yannai said, “or a grave<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:20" href="/Numbers.19.20"><i>Num</i>. 19:20</a>. This is a new statement. Touching a grave induces the impurity of the dead irrespective of the age of the grave and the person buried while in the opinion of R. Simeon ben Ioḥai only a Jewish corpse induces the impurity of a “tent” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 61a" href="/Yevamot.61a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 61a</a>.) The Babli concurs (<i>loc. cit.</i>). R. Yannai’s statement is attributed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 54a" href="/Nazir.54a">Babli, 54a</a>, to his student R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>”, even if one touched the first Adam’s grave. The colleagues say, he<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">As confirmed by a fragment of <i>baraita</i>, Bar Qappara holds that any root may represent all 6 permutations of its letters. This cannot be classified as a derivation; it is a hint.)</i> transposes the verse: “or a grave <i>qbr</i>” means “or decay <i>rqb</i>”. Bar Qappara stated, “or a grave <i>qbr</i>”, “or decay <i>rqb</i>”.
Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi said, the Holy One, praise to Him, took a spoonful from the place of the altar and created Adam from it. He said, he shall have been created from the place of the altar so that he should be able to stand up<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 14:9" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.14.9"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 14(9</a>), R. Berekhiah and R. Ḥelbo in the name of R. Samuel ben Naḥman. The altar, with definite article, is the altar in the Temple courtyard in Jerusalem which by tradition [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 34:8" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.34.8"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 34(8</a>)] is the altar used by Adam, Noah, and Abraham.</i>. That is what is written: “The Eternal Omnipotent formed Adam the first of dust from the earth<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 2:7" href="/Genesis.2.7"><i>Gen</i>. 2:7</a>.</i>”, and it is written: “You shall build for me an altar of earth<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 20:24.</i>.” Since “earth” there means an altar, here also [it means] an altar. “His days should be a hundred and twenty years.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:3" href="/Genesis.6.3"><i>Gen</i>. 6:3</a>.</i>” Adam the first lived close to a thousand years and you say, “his days should be a hundred and twenty years”! But after 120 years he returns to be a spoonful of decay. That is difficult. For Og, the king of Bashan, 120 years, and for a newborn baby 120 years? Og [becomes] a spoonful of decay, and a newborn baby becomes a spoonful of decay. “<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.2.2">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 2:2</a>; <i>Kelim Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 7:1, as legal principle independent of the story of 120 years. Quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 50b" href="/Nazir.50b">Babli, 50b</a>, but there the rabbis derive the decay from the body of the hand, without the fingers.</i> The spoonful of decay which they mentioned is from his finger joints and upwards, the words of Rebbi Meïr, but the Sages say, from a fully developed hand.” Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi was illustrating that of Rebbi Meïr one way, that of the rabbis in another way.
“Which corpse has decay<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, every corpse will eventually produce decayed matter. What is meant here is, under which circumstances does one apply the rules of “decay” which induces impurity by the spoonful, and when the rules of “grave dust” which requires more than a spoonful (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.2.2"><i>Ahilut</i> Mishnah 2:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>.)</i>? One who was buried naked in a marble coffin, or on a stone floor, or on a marble table. But if he was buried in a wooden casket or on a woooden plank; that is not decay but graves’ dust and needs somewhat more than a spoonful.”<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.2.2">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 2:3</a>; quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 51a" href="/Nazir.51a">Babli <i>Nazir</i> 51a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 27b" href="/Niddah.27b"><i>Niddah</i> 27b</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, if even the smallest attachment was buried with him, there is no decay<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">But graves’ dust. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 51a" href="/Nazir.51a">Babli, 51a</a>, this is implied by a statement of Ulla.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Of two corpses which were buried together, each one becomes an attachment for the other<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 51a" href="/Nazir.51a">Babli 51a</a>.</i>. This<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">That there is symmetry between both corpses.</i> is automatic; could one alone be an attachment to another? Abba bar Nathan<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 51a" href="/Nazir.51a">Babli, 51a</a>, a Rav Nathan states that decay combined from two corpses induces impurity.</i> objected: Think of it, that somebody brought half a spoonful from one and half a spoonful from the other! He<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yasa.</i> was considering it when he<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Abba bar Nathan.</i> was seen laughing; he<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yasa.</i> was offended by him and he<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Abba bar Nathan.</i> died. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Two generations later.</i> said, there this man is gone and we did hear nothing about it<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Whether decay from two corpses combines to induce impurity.</i>. What about it? There, neither of them will produce a spoonful of decayed matter, but here, each of them can generate a spoonful of decay<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If two corpses are buried in a common grave, neither of them can produce decay by R. Joḥanan’s definition. But pure decay from two distinct corpses can be combined.</i>.
If his foot was cut off, from above the joint<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no Yerushalmi extant to Tractate <i>Ḥulin</i>, it is impossible to know exactly what is meant. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 6:4" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.6.4">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 6:4</a> states that an animal, one of whose legs was cut above the ארכובה, is unfit for consumption since it could not survive. There is no unanimity as to which leg joint is meant, the knee or the ankle. In the language of the Babli, both are called ארכובה (root רכב by metathesis from biblical ברך) and are distinguished by appropriate adjectives. It is reasonable to identify ארכובה here as the ankle, but this is far from certain.</i> there is no “decay”<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The corpse is missing a limb; the rules of “decay” do not apply.</i>, from below the joint there is “decay”. If it<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The amputated limb.</i> was buried together with him, from below the joint it becomes an attachment to him<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could have survived the amputation, the part taken becomes an attachment. But if the part amputated is so large that by the medical standards of the day the patient could not have survived, the amputated leg is part of the corpse if buried together with it.</i>, from above the joint it does not become an attachment to him. The colleagues asked before Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon: If he bound from below the joint as attachment? He said to them, if it were so<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">If the amputated limb was sewn back to the corpse, if it were considered a separate part then any limb could be considered separate and there never would be a complete corpse. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 63b" href="/Nazir.63b">Babli, 63b</a>, does not require sewing but holds that a grave makes a complete corpse whole even if it was brought to the grave in pieces.</i>, even if it was not cut I should consider it as if cut and it should be an attachment! Since it is connected to him, it forms one body. If there is something missing, can it have “decay”? Let us hear from the following: If it is imcomplete, there is no “decay”, it does not require the surrounding earth, and it is not part of a row of graves<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This is also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 51b" href="/Nazir.51b">Babli, 51b</a>.<br>It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.3.1">Mishnah 9:3</a> that a person who finds an unattended corpse must bury it together with all <i>surrounding earth</i> which might have soaked up his blood. This is now qualified to apply only to complete corpses.<br>If one finds three graves next to one another they form a cemetery which may not be disturbed. The rules governing possible removal of graves are detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:3:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.3.2-8">Halakhah 9:3</a>. The rules arc restricted here to graves containing complete corpses.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan asked, how much must be missing that it have no “decay”? Might it follow what we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 2:3" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.2.3">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 2:3</a>. In the list of body parts of a corpse which cause impurity by touch and carrying but not in a “tent” appears an incomplete skull. The skull is considered incomplete by the House of Shammai if it exhibits a hole made by a surgeon’s drill, but by the House of Hillel only if the missing piece was large enough so “that he would die if it was removed.”</i>: “That he will die if it was removed.” Is it the same here? Think of it, if the esophagus was perforated, nothing was missing but he could not survive. You have only the following: If his foot was cut off, from below the joint there is “decay”, from above the joint there is no “decay”. If it was buried together with him, from below the joint it becomes an attachment to him, from above the joint it does not become an attachment to him.
Do stillbirths generate “decay”? For him who says that their blood induces impurity by a <i>quartarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">For him a fetus is human; a stillbirth follows all rules of human corpses.</i> they generate “decay”; for him who says that their blood does not induce impurity by a <i>quartarius</i>, do they generate “decay”? Let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 16:1" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.16.1">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 16:1</a>: “Small heaps close to a town or to a road, whether new or old, are impure since women bury there their stillbirths and lepers their limbs. Far away, new ones are pure, old ones impure; I am saying that there had been a road nearby.”</i>“Why is the dust from small heaps impure? Because women bury there their stillbirths.” Rebbi Yudan, the father of Rebbi Mattaniah, said: Would it not be reasonable otherwise, that lepers bury their limbs there?<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, the Tosephta in this formulation was unknown to the editors of the Yerushalmi. The full Tosephta text is paraphrased in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 20b" href="/Ketubot.20b">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 20b</a>.</i> You must say that it is because of “decay”, not because a bone the size of a lentil, since it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">If some decay is replaced by pure dust from another place, the latter becomes an attachment and by the rules of the previous paragraph, the earth lost its character of “decay”. But if the impurity were caused by a human bone, its impurity would be original biblical impurity; the new dust would have become impure immediately as a “tent” over the bone. Therefore, the second opinion expressed in the first part of the Tosephta is inconsistent with the rule stated at the end.</i>: “If he took some dust from it and replaced it, [the small heap] is pure.” Explain it following Tannaïm, as it was stated: If one removed it<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">If the entire little heap was levelled (without there appearing any bones in it), it remains impure if one assumes that some bone is buried below the levelled surface; it is pure if one assumes that it consisted only of “decay”.</i>, it remains impure; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel declares it pure.
Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">This name attribution cannot be correct since R. Simeon ben Eleazar was a Tanna, student of R. Meïr. A statement of his appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 2:4" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.2.4">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 2:6</a>, denying that people with a trepanned skull can survive a winter. Probably one has to read: R. Eleazar said.</i>, it was formulated following Rebbi Simeon, as it was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah and explains why spine and skull were mentioned separately when “half a <i>qab</i> of bones” is also mentioned and both a skull and a spine fill the required volume of about 1 liter. A similar (anonymous) text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.2.3">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 2:5</a>: “A spine of which bones were removed is pure even if its outline still exists; But in a grave it is impure, even if broken, even if crushed, since the grave unites them.”</i> The spine and the skull are impure even if crushed, even disconnected, because the grave unites them as “a human in a tent”<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:14" href="/Numbers.19.14"><i>Num</i>. 19:14</a>.</i>.
The Mishnah follows Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Probably both “Eleazar ben Azariah” and “Simeon ben Eleazar” should read simply “Eleazar”.</i>, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 4:7" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.4.7">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 4:13</a>: “Rebbi Eleazar said, earlier the Elders were divided; some said, a <i>quartarius</i> of blood, a quarter (<i>qab</i>) of bones; some said, half a <i>qab</i> of bones and half a log of blood. The later court said: a <i>quartarius</i> of blood, a quarter (<i>qab</i>) of bones for heave and sacrifices, a half a <i>qab</i> of bones and half a log of blood for the <i>nazir</i> and the Temple.” It is difficult to correct the Yerushalmi text by the Tosephta since our text clearly puts Temple and sacrifices in the same category. In a parallel text to the Tosephta in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 53a" href="/Nazir.53a">Babli, 53a</a>, the speaker is R. Eliezer. Instead of “Temple” (meaning “access to the Temple”), he formulates the equivalent, “people who come for the Passover sacrifice”.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, earlier the courts were divided; some said, a <i>quartarius</i> of blood, a quarter bones; some said, half a <i>log</i> of blood, a half a <i>log</i> of bones, for <i>nezirut</i> and the impurity of the Sanctuary and its sacred offerings.” Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>: They said this<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The determination of the minimum amounts inducing impurity was the result of prophetic inspiration, rathet than of logical derivation. In the Babli, <i>loc. cit.</i>, this is a statement of R. Jacob bar Idi himself.</i> as explanation from the mouths of Ḥaggai, Zachariah, and Malachi.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> But for overhanging branches<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>nazir</i> walks under a tree under which a grave is suspected.</i>, or protuberances<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Standing out from a building or a fence and it is suspected that a grave may be buried underneath.</i>, or broken fields<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">“The house of the broken-off piece” is a field which contained a grave ploughed under. Not only is the suspected place of the grave impure by biblical standards but the entire field is rabbinically impure since the plough might have caught a bone and transported it to another part of the field.</i>, or Gentile territory<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Which is rabbinically impure even in the Holy Land.</i>, or the cave door, or its frame<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">גּוֹלֵל “the roller” is a large circular stone which closes the entrance to a burial cave. דּוֹפֵק “the knocker” is the frame which keeps the roller in place. The expressions were later transferred to burial in the earth, where “the roller” became the stone plate covering the grave and “the knocker” the stone frame on which it rests. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.1">Note 142</a>.</i>, or a <i>quartarius</i> of blood<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">One-half of the amount which induces biblical impurity.</i>, or a tent<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Everything inside a tent in which there is a corpse is impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:14" href="/Numbers.19.14"><i>Num</i>. 19:14</a>). The tent confines the impurity; outside of the tent everything is pure. The impurity of a person touching a tent (or a “roller” or a “knocker”) from the outside is purely rabbinical. But cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.4">Note 164</a>.</i>, or a quarter (<i>qab</i>) of bones<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">One-half of the amount which induces biblical impurity.</i>, or objects that touched the corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">An object inside a tent in which there is a corpse is a source of original impurity. But an object touching a corpse under the open sky becomes impure in a derivative way. Anybody touching such an object becomes impure in a secondary way which in most cases is only rabbinical; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Note 137</a>.</i>, or the days of his counting or his being absolute<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the sufferer from skin disease. The <i>nazir</i> is enjoined from becoming impure by the impurity of the dead; in general, impurity generated by the <i>nazir</i>’s own body has no influence on his status as <i>nazir</i>. “The days of his counting” are the eight days which the healed sufferer from skin disease has to observe between the preliminary and the final expiation ceremonies. These days do not interfere with his status as <i>nazir</i> but they cannot count since he has to shave <i>all</i> his hair on the first and seventh days. “The days of his being absolute” is the time in which the sufferer is declared to be certainly impure. Then he is required not to shave his hair (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:45" href="/Leviticus.13.45"><i>Lev</i>. 13:45</a>); nevertheless, these days do not count towards the fulfillment of his vow.</i>, the <i>nazir</i> does not shave<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">If his impurity is caused by a corpse and either is rabbinical or questionable.</i> but sprinkles on the third and seventh days<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:12" href="/Numbers.19.12"><i>Num</i>. 19:12</a>.</i>, does not disregard the preceding<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">The time he is in any state of impurity caused by the dead, whether rabbinical or questionable, cannot be counted towards fulfillment of his vow of <i>nazir</i>. But the preceding and following days count and after purification he simply completes the count; he does not start anew.</i>, starts counting immediately, and has no sacrifice. In truth<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">This expression is a label for an old, pre-Mishnaic, rule.</i>, the days of a male or female sufferer from flux<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15" href="/Leviticus.15"><i>Lev</i>. 15</a>. As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.1">Note 144</a>, this impurity generated by the <i>nazir</i>’s own body has no influence on his state of <i>nezirut</i>. “The days of quarantine” are those in which the possible sufferer from skin disease is put under observation before his final status is determined (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:4" href="/Leviticus.13.4"><i>Lev</i>. 13:4</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:5" href="/Leviticus.13.5">5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:21" href="/Leviticus.13.21">21</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:26" href="/Leviticus.13.26">26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:31" href="/Leviticus.13.31">31</a>).</i> and the days of quarantine of the sufferer from skin disease are counted for him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “But for overhanging branches, or protuberances,” etc. What is the status of the undistributed middle<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> spells out for which biblical impurities the <i>nazir</i> has to shave; the implication is that for anything less he does not have to shave. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> has a list of rabbinic impurities for which the <i>nazir</i> does not have to shave; the implication is that for anything more he has to shave. We are left without instructions for cases which fall in between.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, the undistributed middle is judged leniently<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Anything not covered by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> is not biblical; the <i>nazir</i> is prevented from shaving.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the undistributed middle is judged restrictively<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Anything not exempted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> requires shaving and a new start. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 53b" href="/Nazir.53b">Babli, 53b</a>, reports the same opinions, limited to the case taken here as illustration.</i>. What is the undistributed middle? That is a limb from a corpse or a limb from a living body which is not sufficiently<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">“Sufficient flesh” is enough left on a limb connected to a living body that it could heal.</i> covered by flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 1:8" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.1.8">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 1:8</a> spells out that such a limb induces impurity by touch or carrying but not in a tent.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">He questions R. Simeon ben Laqish’s position.</i>: From where [do we infer that] a bone [induces impurity in the size of] a barley grain? Not from that verse, “or a person’s bone<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:16" href="/Numbers.19.16"><i>Num.</i> 19:16</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:18" href="/Numbers.19.18">v. 18</a>, only “bone” is mentioned but not “human”. This is interpreted in <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num.</i> #127,129 to cover bones coming from both living or dead persons; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 54a" href="/Nazir.54a">Babli 54a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:16" href="/Numbers.19.16">Targum Pseudo-Jonathan <i>Num.</i> 19:16</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:18" href="/Numbers.19.18">18</a>.</i>”? Here you require a barley grain, there you do not require a barley grain<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">For a bare bone, everybody agrees that a barley grain represents the minimum size which induces impurity. According to R. Simeon ben Laqish, a bone fragment with some flesh is not subject to a legal minimum.</i>! Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon stated: “A slain one”, anything from a slain person<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Without a minimum; <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num.</i> #127.</i>, that is a limb from a corpse or a limb from a living body which is not sufficiently covered by flesh.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, overhanging branches and protuberances are biblical for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">If heave was transported under overhanging branches or protuberances which might cover a grave, it becomes impure and forbidden as food; it must be burned.</i> even though the <i>nazir</i> does not shave. Rebbi Jeremiah asked<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Is there any practical difference whether the impurity imputed to the heave is biblical or rabbinical?</i>, if to burn it, one burns even for a rabbinic doubt; maybe to whip<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Implying that a person can be whipped for exposing heave to the impurity induced by overhanging branches or protuberances.</i>? Rebbi [Yose]<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the text; identified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4">Halakhah 4 (Note 219</a>).</i> from Sidon stated before Rebbi Jeremiah and disgagrees with Rebbi Jeremiah: “For any impurity deriving from a corpse for which a <i>nazir</i> shaves one is guilty if entering the Sanctuary, but any impurity deriving from a corpse for which a <i>nazir</i> does not shave one is not guilty if entering the Sanctuary.<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>. If entering the Temple is not prosecutable, neither is exposing heave to this impurity.</i>”
Rebbi Joḥanan said, layers of a tree which cover four-by-four [handbreadths]: the <i>nazir</i> shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">If a tree has a thick crown which forms a roof over an area of at least 4 by 4 handbreadths so that the sky is not visible, and impurity derived from a corpse is under this roof; a <i>nazir</i> which passes under the tree is in a “tent” together with the impurity and has to shave.</i>. Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Joḥanan said, if one of his hands is on the other side and one hand is in the layer, the <i>nazir</i> does not shave<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>nazir</i> stands outside the tree and only one of his hands is in the crown, he does not become impure by the “tent” formed by the tree. In general, the roof of a “tent” covering an impurity shields anything above it from impurity. The only exception is טוּמְאָה רְצוּצָה “squeezed impurity”: If the roof of the tent is less that a handbreadth above the impurity, it does not shield but the impurity rises up to the sky and below into the ground (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.7.1">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 7:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 14:7" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.14.7">14:7</a>).</i>. Rebbi Yose asked: If it separates from impurity it also should separate from purity; if it does not separate from impurity, it should not separate from purity<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">This question is directed towards <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 6:1</a> which states that “humans and objects form tents to make impure but not to make pure.” In the Mishnah, R. Eliezer disagrees and holds that any roof which brings impurity shields everything above it from impurity. R. Yose notes that the position of R. Eliezer is the only rational one.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if the corpse is in the bedroom and the <i>nazir</i> under the bed, the <i>nazir</i> shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Since a bed can be moved, it is an object and brings impurity, even if the <i>nazir</i> is more than one handbreadth below the bottom of the bed.</i>. Certainly if the corpse is under the bed and the <i>nazir</i> in the bedroom, does the <i>nazir</i> not shave<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the bed is one handbreadth above the corpse it does not form a “tent” to protect the <i>nazir</i>. The ceiling of the room is the “tent” and everything below it is impure.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, if the corpse and the <i>nazir</i> are under the bed frame<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Not in a room, and the frame is not holding bedding. This is a situation similar to that of standing under the gutters attached to a building.</i>, under the belly of a camel<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">A moving “tent” which only transmits rabbinic impurity.</i>, under the width of a gate, under the gutters<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μέλαθρον “roof-tree, beam, roof”.</i>, the <i>nazir</i> does not shave, nor for anything which is there for protection<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">But not for dwelling.</i>. Ḥizqiah heard it and said, they purified the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Since Ḥizqiah was R. Joḥanan’s teacher, practice cannot follow R. Joḥanan.</i>! Rebbi Joḥanan said, if the corpse is in the anteroom and the <i>nazir</i> in the dining hall, the <i>nazir</i> shaves; certainly if the corpse is in the dining hall and the <i>nazir</i> in the anteroom, the <i>nazir</i> shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 4:3" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.4.3">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 4:3</a>: “If [impurity] was in the entrance with the door open, the house is pure; if it was in the house, everything in the house is impure, <i>since it is the way of impurity to leave but not to enter</i>.” If the dining hall had no windows (or its windows were narrow slits through which no corpse could be transported), so that the only way out was through the door of the anteroom, it is obvious that everybody in the anteroom was biblically impure if the corpse was in the dining hall. If the corpse was in the outer room, it still was on the same floor in the same building as the <i>nazir</i>, who would be made biblically impure by the “tent”, the ceiling of his room.</i>. Rebbi Mana asked, if it is a tent to separate it should be a tent to draw along; if it is not a tent to draw along, the impurity should disappear<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding Note, the second case is the more natural and should have been mentioned first. In the formulation given, should one not ask whether in the first case the <i>nazir</i> was not biblically impure since impurity is supposed to leave?</i>! Rebbi Joḥanan said, if the impurity is under the outer half of the wall, the <i>nazir</i> shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">This is a commentary on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 6:3" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.6.3">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 6:3</a>. If the wall of a house is rather thick and the “squeezed” impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.4">Note 165</a>) is under the inner part of the wall, the house is impure but anybody standing on the roof is pure since the roof intercepts the impurity. But if the “squeezed” impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.4">Note 165</a>) is under the outer part of the wall, the house is pure but anybody standing on the wall is impure since a roof does not intercept a “squeezed” impurity. R. Joḥanan asserts that this rule is biblical.</i>. Rebbi Joshia said, if impurity is hidden in the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of “squeezed” impurity.</i> of the house, the <i>nazir</i> shaves. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joshia: The <i>nazir</i> does not shave for most of the skeleton<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">The spine and skull (assuming they do not amount to half a <i>qab</i>); cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 53a" href="/Nazir.53a">Babli 53a</a>.</i> or most of the bones<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">125 bones (assuming they do not amount to half a <i>qab</i>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 2:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 2:1</a>.</i>.
“In truth;” Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">178a</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is found also in Sabbat 10:4 (12c, 1. 46). But in the other occurrences in the Yerushalmi, Sabbat 1 (3b 1. 69), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1.9">Kilaim 2:2 Note 36</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 2:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.2.1.5">Terumot 2:1 Note 16</a>, the name is "Eleazar" (Lazar). In the Babli the quote is anonymous in Sabbat 92b; it appears in the name of R. Eleazar in Baba mesi'a 60a.</i> said that every place where they stated “in truth,” refers to practice going back to Moses on Mount Sinai.
“They shall fall away, for his vow of <i>nazir</i> is impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num.</i> 6:12</a>.</i>” From here that the days of impurity fall away<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">The days of certified skin disease cannot count as days of <i>nezirut</i> even though the sufferer from skin disease is also required to let his hair grow.</i>. Then should he invalidate<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah states that a <i>nazir</i> who develops skin disease simply waits until he is healed and then finishes his count. Why does he not start anew as in the case of impurity of the dead?</i>? He invalidates only the days of [impurity of] the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical law is quite clear that only the impurity of the dead makes him lose the earlier days of his count.</i>. Why should they not be counted? If you say that days in which he causes [impurity to] couch and seat are counted, days in which he does not cause [impurity to] couch and seat are certainly counted<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 1:4" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.1.4">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 1:4</a> states that the impurity of the sufferer from skin disease is more severe than the impurity of the female sufferer from flux. For the latter, it is stated explicitly (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:26" href="/Leviticus.15.26"><i>Lev.</i> 15:26</a>) that any couch and any seat used by her becomes a source of original impurity. No direct biblical source exists for declaring the sufferer from skin disease to cause this kind of impurity; it is derived indirectly in <i>Sifra Meṣora‘ Parashah</i> 2(6). This derivation is accepted at face value by Maimonides both in his <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 1:4" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.1.4">Mishnah Commentary (Kelim 1:4</a>) and in his Code (<i>Turn‘at Ṣara‘at</i> 10:11). The commentators of the Babli (Rashi, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 67b <i>s. v.</i> זב, Ravad, Commentary to <i>Sifra</i>) have difficulties in accepting the <i>Sifra</i> since it seems to contradict the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 67b" href="/Pesachim.67b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 67b</a>, but a student of the Yerushalmi does not have to consider this, in particular since Ravad does not object to Maimonides’s ruling in his Code. For impurity there is no difference between a sufferer from skin disease in quarantine and one positively declared infirm (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 1:7</a>, <i>Nega‘im</i> 8:8).<br>The argument given here refers to Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:2 which states that the healed sufferer from skin disease in his days of counting, between the preliminary and the definitive purification, is free from all severe impurities and does not cause more impurity than a dead reptile (the slightest of impurities, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 1:1</a>). It does not seem to make any sense to accept the days of the severely impure quarantined but not to accept the slightly impure counting sufferer from skin disease (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.1">Note 144</a>).</i>! What did you see to say that they are not counted? Rebbi []<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">There are no sources which would permit filling in the lacuna.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “For a wild growth of his head’s hair”<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:5" href="/Numbers.6.5"><i>Num.</i> 6:5</a>.</i>. Days of hair growth are counted, days preparing for shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">For the final purification of the sufferer from skin disease.</i> are not counted. So far in his days of counting; in the days of his definite status? Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: “Please do not let her be like a corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 12:12" href="/Numbers.12.12"><i>Num.</i> 12:12</a>, speaking of Miriam who was punished for calumniating Moses by becoming a clear sufferer from skin disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 12:10" href="/Numbers.12.10">v. 10</a>), not a case of quarantine.</i>.” Since the days of a corpse are not counted, the days of quarantine are not counted. A student quoted this saying cf Rebbi Joḥanan’s before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, who did not accept it. He said to him: Here, you call it quarantine, but there, you want to call it absolute; you cannot do that. For Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “Please do not let her be like a corpse; let her be quarantined<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 12:14" href="/Numbers.12.14"><i>Num.</i> 12:14</a>.</i>.” Just as the days of the dead are seven, so the days of quarantine are seven<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">While the case of Miriam was clearly not one of quarantine, the verse treats it as such by (1) calling her exclusion from the camp “quarantine” and (2) exempting her from the cleansing ritual which is required of  the absolute sufferer but not the quarantined (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 1:8</a>, <i>Nega‘im</i> 8:8). The verse cannot be applied to the absolute sufferer.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said in the name of Rebbi Joshua: For any impurity caused by a corpse for which the <i>nazir</i> shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>, one is guilty if entering the Sanctuary, but for any impurity caused by a corpse for which the <i>nazir</i> does not shave<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 11:4" href="/Mishnah_Parah.11.4">Mishnah Parah 11:4</a> the formulation is: “For any impurity which requires immersion in water by biblical decree, one is guilty if entering the Sanctuary, but for any impurity which does not require immersion in water by biblical decree, one is not guilty if entering the Sanctuary.”</i>, one is not guilty if entering the Sanctuary. Rebbi Meïr said, this should not be less than the impurity of a dead reptile<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Which requires immersion in water by biblical decree, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:31" href="/Leviticus.11.31"><i>Lev.</i> 11:31</a>, and a person is guilty if he enters the Sanctuary when impure, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev.</i> 5:2</a>.</i>! Rebbi Aqiba said, I argued before Rebbi Eliezer: If for a barley-grain sized bone, which does not cause impurity to a human in a tent<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 2:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 2:1</a>.</i>, a <i>nazir</i> shaves if he touches or carries it<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>, should it not be logical that for a <i>quartarius</i> of blood, which causes impurity to a human in a tent<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.2.2">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 2:2</a>. The <i>nazir</i> does not shave, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>, a <i>nazir</i> should have to shave if he touches or carries it? He said to me, what is that, Aqiba? This is not a place for an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>! When I came and expounded that before Rebbi Joshua, he said to me, you have a good argument, but that is what they said was practice<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">“Practice” here means an old tradition, whose origin can no longer be ascertained and which cannot be overruled. The relationship of this kind of practice to the interpretation of biblical verses is not amenable to logical analysis.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Eleazar said in the name of Rebbi Joshua,” etc. There<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 1:1</a>.</i>, we have stated: “Two are impure from the dead, one is impure for seven days and one is impure by the impurity of evening<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">As explained at the end of the Mishnah, a person touching a corpse becomes a source of original impurity. A second person touching the first becomes impure in the first degree. The first is subject to the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num.</i> 19</a>, the second can cleanse himself by immersion in water; then he will become pure in all respects at sundown (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev.</i> 22:7</a>). For degrees of impurity, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Note 137</a>.</i>. Three are impure from the dead, two are impure for seven days and one is impure until nightfall<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 1:2" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.1.2">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 1:2</a>: An object touching the corpse becomes impure like the corpse itself (<i>Sifry</i> <i>Num.</i> 130). A second object touches the first; it becomes a source of original impurity; both need the ritual of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>. A third object or a human touching the second object becomes impure in the first degree and can become pure at sundown.</i>. Four are impure from the dead, three are impure for seven days and one is impure until nightfall<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 1:3" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.1.3">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 1:3</a>: An object which touches the corpse becomes impure like the corpse itself. A human touches the first object, becoming a source of original impurity; a second object touches the human, also becoming a source of original impurity. A third object or a human touching the second object becomes impure in the first degree and can become pure at sundown.</i>. How is it for two? Any person who touches a corpse is impure for seven days; a person who touches him is impure until nightfall,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: All are biblically [impure] for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Not only for heave but also for sacrifices: anything with a status of sanctity. But for profane food, humans, or objects, anything touching a corpse becomes a source of original impurity; the person touching it or him becomes impure in the first degree. In biblical law, no human can become impure by derivative impurity. No person touching anything more than once removed from the corpse can become biblically impure.</i>, but for entering the Sanctuary only the second impure who touched the first impure. What is the reason? “A person who would be impure and did not purify himself<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:20" href="/Numbers.19.20"><i>Num.</i> 19:20</a>: “This person will be extirpated from the congregation, for he desecrated the Eternal’s Sanctuary”.</i>;” anybody needing purification is guilty for entering the Sanctuary; anybody not needing purification is not guilty for entering the Sanctuary. They objected: But a person who touches objects which touched a corpse needs purification, but is he the second<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">A person who touched objects which touched objects which touched the corpse is a third in line who is impure by biblical standards and guilty if he enters the Sanctuary unpurified.</i>? Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya said, for impurity of a person from a person<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Joḥanan refers to impurity of a person induced by a person.</i>, not for impurity of a person from objects. The statement of Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya [implies that] only the first is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Anybody needing the ritual of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num.</i> 19</a> but entering the Sanctuary without it is guilty of a deadly sin, as stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:20" href="/Numbers.19.20"><i>Num.</i> 19:20</a>. The person only impure in the first degree, not subject to this ritual, is guilty of a sin but not a deadly one. As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.4.2">Note 199</a>, if the impurity is transmitted by an object, the human may be the second in the sequence.</i>. Since [for impurity of] a person from a person only the first is guilty, so here the first is guilty. Rebbi Yose said, only if he immersed himself. That is a statement of Rebbi, since Rebbi said, all impure persons remain impure until the are immersed in water<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">The person impure in the first degree may still commit a deadly sin by entering the Sanctuary (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:3" href="/Leviticus.22.3"><i>Lev.</i> 22:3</a>) without immersing himself in water. But if he enters (or eats sanctified food) between immersion and sundown, he commits a minor sin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev.</i> 22:7</a>).</i>.
Rebbi Illa said: He himself, who touched the corpse, is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that a deadly sin is only committted if a person impure by contact from body to body is entering the Sanctuary, not if the impurity was transmitted by contact with an object as explained in Notes 197,198.</i>. He said it and gave its reason: “who became impure<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:20" href="/Numbers.19.20"><i>Num.</i> 19:20</a>: “This person will be extirpated from the congregation, for he desecrated the Eternal’s Sanctuary”.</i>,” that refers to the impurity of a human. But is there not a third<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">How could it be that a person needs purification by the rites of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num.</i> 19</a> and not be guilty entering the Sanctuary unpurified?</i>? As Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, for impurity of a person from a person a man is guilty for impurity in the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The verse does not deal with the impurity transmitted by objects; its status remains indeterminate.</i>. They objected: If objects touch the corpse, a human the objects, and objects the human<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">As explained at the end of the Mishnah, a person touching a corpse becomes a source of original impurity. A second person touching the first becomes impure in the first degree. The first is subject to the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num.</i> 19</a>, the second can cleanse himself by immersion in water; then he will become pure in all respects at sundown (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev.</i> 22:7</a>). For degrees of impurity, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Note 137</a>.</i>. For impurity of a person from a person, not for impurity of a person from objects<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The verse does not deal with the impurity transmitted by objects; its status remains indeterminate.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqa in the name of Rav Hamnuna: The Tanna there<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Kelim Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 1:5 says the same in different words.</i> disagrees with Rebbi Illa: “For an object made half of cement־earth<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">Probably clay is meant; earth of a consistency from which objects can be made. Clay objects are the paradigm of objects which can become impure; they cannot be purified except by being broken.</i> and half of dung<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">Objects made of dried camel or cow dung cannot become impure.</i> one is not guilty for coming to the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">If such an object was brought to the Sanctuary after being exposed to impurity, no sin was committed.</i>” Because it is half of earth and half of dung; but if it were totally of earth, one would be guilty. Who is guilty? Not the one who touches it? Is he not the second who touches the first<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">Since an object cannot be guilty, the human who touches it must be guilty. This contradicts both R. Ila and R. Abin bar Ḥiyya.</i>? Rebbi Phineas said before Rebbi Yose: Explain it if he threw it<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">Somebody threw an impure clay object into the Sanctuary grounds. Then there is no question of a human entering the holy precinct.</i>. He answered him, about him who threw it we stated: For washing his body he is subject to extirpation, for washing his garments to the forty<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">A person is impure in original impurity who transmits impurity to his garments. If he enters the Sanctuary while impure, he commits a deadly sin with his body. But at the same time, he carries his impure garments into the holy precinct. This is a separate offense, punishable by 39 (= 40–1) lashes. The implication is that a person throwing an impure object into the Sanctuary is whipped but not subject to Divine extirpation.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said before Rebbi Yose, do we not have the Mishnah in support of that first Tanna<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ila, an Amora.</i>, as is stated: “Rebbi Eleazar said in the name of Rebbi Joshua: For any impurity caused by a corpse for which the <i>nazir</i> shaves, one is guilty if entering the Sanctuary, but for any impurity caused by a corpse for which the <i>nazir</i> does not shave, one is not guilty if entering the Sanctuary.” For the first, for which the <i>nazir</i> shaves, one is guilty if entering the Sanctuary; for the second, for which the <i>nazir</i> does not shave, one is not guilty if entering the Sanctuary. Rebbi Joḥanan said<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan holds that the entire discussion up to this point is based on a false premise. It is clear to him that anybody impure in at least the first degree is forbidden access to the Sanctuary on penalty of extirpation. R. Joshua’s statement only deals with the impurity generated by the corpse. Such impurity prohibits the person contaminated by it (or any of its derivatives up to the level of first degree impurity) from entering the Sanctuary if and only if it forces the <i>nazir</i> to shave.</i>, it is needed from what comes from the corpse: For a <i>quartarius</i> of blood or a quarter <i>qab</i> of bones, for which the <i>nazir</i> does not shave, one is not guilty if entering the Sanctuary; for half a <i>log</i> of blood and half a <i>qab</i> of bones, for which the <i>nazir</i> shaves, one is guilty if entering the Sanctuary. Rebbi Jeremiah said, we were of the opinion that they disagree about a sacrifice, but one whips even the third, even the fourth<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">He tries to salvage the opinions of R. Illa and R. Abin ben Ḥiyya, that everybody would agree that any person entering the Sanctuary in impurity commits a sin; the question would only be about a sacrifice for an unintended infraction. But for wilful transgression, the Mishnah in <i>Ahilut</i> is quite clear that it is punishable even for the third and fourth person.</i>. Rebbi Yose from Sidon stated before Rebbi Jeremiah in opposition to Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">He confirms the interpretation given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.4.3">Note 217</a>.</i>: “For any impurity caused by a corpse for which the <i>nazir</i> shaves, one is guilty if entering the Sanctuary, but for any impurity caused by a corpse for which the <i>nazir</i> does not shave, one is not guilty if entering the Sanctuary.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Yannai, Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">The order of the last two names has to be inverted: R. Yose ben R. Bun said in the name of R. Joḥanan who heard it from R. Yannai.</i>: It is needed from what comes from the corpse: For a <i>quartarius</i> of blood or a quarter <i>qab</i> of bones, for which the <i>nazir</i> does not shave, one is not guilty; for half a <i>log</i> of blood and half a <i>qab</i> of bones, for which the <i>nazir</i> shaves, one is guilty.

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a person said to two <i>nezirim</i>: I saw one of you becoming impure but I do not know which one of you it was, they bring<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">They purify themselves from the impurity of the dead and at the end of their period of <i>nezirut</i> they shave and bring the sacrifices in common.</i> one sacrifice of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Two birds and a sheep.</i> and one of purity<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A male and a female sheep and a ram.</i>. One of them says, if I am impure, the sacrifice of impurity is mine and that of purity is yours; otherwise the sacrifice of purity is mine and that of impurity is yours. Then they count another thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that both had vowed the standard period of 30 days. Otherwise they would have to repeat the longer of the respective periods of <i>nezirut</i>.</i> and bring one sacrifice of purity; (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.1.1">Mishnah 2</a>) one of them says, if I was impure, the sacrifice of impurity was mine and that of purity was yours, hence this is my sacrifice of purity; otherwise the sacrifice of purity was mine and that of impurity was yours, hence this is your sacrifice of purity.<br>If one of them died<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Then the survivor is in danger of never being able to conclude his <i>nezirut</i> since, not knowing which sacrifice to bring, he can bring neither.</i>, Rebbi Joshua said, he should seek out a person from the general public who would make a vow of <i>nazir</i> corresponding to his needs and say: If I was impure, you are a <i>nazir</i> immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">And the sacrifice of purity will be yours.</i>, but if I was pure, you are a <i>nazir</i> after 30 days. They count 30 days and bring one sacrifice of impurity and one of purity (Mishnah 3) and he says: If I am impure, the sacrifice of impurity is mine and that of purity is yours; otherwise the sacrifice of purity is mine and that of impurity is questionable<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice of impurity consists of two birds. The one offered as elevation sacrifice can be given as obligation, if there was impurity, or as voluntary gift, if there was none. The bird offered as purification sacrifice cannot be eaten by the priests; after its blood was used, it has to be burned outside the Temple precinct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:6" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.6">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 7:6</a>).</i>. Then they count another thirty days, bring one sacrifice of purity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.1.1">Mishnah 4</a>) and he says: If I was impure, the sacrifice of impurity was mine and that of purity was yours, hence this is my sacrifice of purity; otherwise the sacrifice of purity was mine and that of impurity was questionable; hence this is your sacrifice of purity.<br>Ben Zoma said to him, who would accommodate him to make a vow of <i>nazir</i> corresponding to his needs? But he brings a bird as purification offering and an animal as elevation offering and says: If I was impure, the purification offering is for my obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">While this offering is incomplete, it is sufficient to let him shave and restart his <i>nezirut</i>.</i> and the elevation offering is voluntary<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Of all kinds of offerings required from a pure or impure <i>nazir</i>, only the elevation offering can be voluntary.</i>. But if I was pure, the elevation offering is for my obligation and the purification offering is questionable. Then he counts 30 days and brings one sacrifice (of impurity and one)<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This clause in parenthesis is obviously false; it is missing in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss.</i> of purity<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A male and a female sheep and a ram.</i> (Mishnah 5) and says: If I was impure, the first elevation offering was voluntary and the one now is for my obligation. But if I was pure, the first elevation offering was for my obligation and now I am bringing the remainder of my sacrifices. Rebbi Joshua said, then it turns out that he brings his sacrifices piecemeal<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If he was pure, he brings the first offering 30 days before the other two, but the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:14" href="/Numbers.6.14"><i>Num.</i> 6:14</a>) calls all three sacrifices together “his sacrifice” in the singular, meaning that they have to be offered together.</i>! But the Sages accepted the words of Ben Zoma<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">As the only reasonable solution to an intractable problem.</i>.
