<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a person said to two <i>nezirim</i>,” etc. Not only if he said to one of them, “I saw one of you becoming impure but I do not know which one of you it was,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In this case it can really happen that the witness knew of a “tent” impurity unknown to the <i>nezirim</i>.</i>” but even if he said, “I saw one of you vowing to be a <i>nazir</i> but I do not know which one of you it was,” both of them have to follow the rules of <i>nazir</i> because of his testimony. If they do not contradict him<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In matters of ritual law, the uncontested testimony of a single witness is valid.</i>. But if they contradict him, this does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A contested testimony of a single witness is worthless, not even admissible as supporting evidence.</i>. If both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the text, שניהם, is an error for שְׁנַיִם, “two [witnesses].” The concurrent testimony of two witnesses must be believed by biblical standards.</i> contradict, it parallels the following: “If one witness says, he became impure, but he says, I did not become impure, he is pure. If two witnesses say, he became impure, but he says, I did not become impure, the two are believed more than he is, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, everybody is believed about himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 5:9" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.5.9">Mishnah <i>Ṭaharot</i> 5:9</a>.</i>” Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: So is the Mishnah: Rebbi Meïr declares impure, Rebbi Jehudah and the Sages declare pure<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In this matter of the standing of two witnesses, the Sages fully accept the position of R. Jehudah.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, so is the Mishnah: Rebbi Meïr declares impure, Rebbi Jehudah declares pure<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">But the Sages disagree with R. Jehudah; the Mishnah should have mentioned R. Jehudah and not the Sages. In his interpretation, the Sages follow R. Jehudah in accepting the statement of a person accused of a sin even against two witnesses but not in cases of impurity.</i>. The rabbis’ words support Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Gurion said in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Rebbi Jehudah said this only about an old impurity, for he can say to them, I had been impure but I purified myself<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If at least one evening has passed since the possible impurity and the person in question denies that he was impure, we read from his disclaimer that he admits having incurred impurity but eliminated it quickly by immersion in a <i>miqweh</i>.</i>, and we have stated: The same holds for eating suet; the same holds for entering the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">A single witness can force a person to bring a purification sacrifice for committing a sin. But since for an intentional sin, no purification sacrifice is possible, the person accused of committing the sin can always assert that he committed the sin intentionally and, therefore, cannot bring a sacrifice. Then he also must be believed if he asserts that he did not commit the sin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 12a" href="/Keritot.12a">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 12a</a>). (Talmudic law does not accept confessions since testimony of relatives is excluded and “everybody is related to himself.” Therefore, the person asserting that he intentionally committed the sin attributed to him by a single witness, cannot be prosecuted.)</i>. Should they not accept his position for impurity? Because of the impurity of a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>nazir</i> brings a sacrifice for becoming impure by the impurity of the dead whether incurred willingly or involuntarily. The sacrifice is a public act; he cannot claim to have purified himself in private.</i>, could they not say, where is the sacrifice which you brought? Then should they not accept his position for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">If an unauthorized person is accused of eating heave, he can always claim to have done it intentionally; so he does not have to pay the 25% fine (Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 7:1).</i>? So far the questions of Rebbi Ḥanina.
Rebbi Mana understood it<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">That the Sages and R. Jehudah do not agree about the weight of a person’s disclaimer as against two witnesses to his actions.</i> from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 3:1" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.3.1">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 3:1</a>.</i>: “They told him, if he wishes, he says that he did it intentionally.” What can he say for an assigned slave girl<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20-22" href="/Leviticus.19.20-22"><i>Lev.</i> 19:20–22</a>, the case of a man sleeping with a slave girl assigned as a future wife to another free man. As long as the girl is not totally freed, she cannot marry the man to whom she is assigned. Therefore, her relations with another man are not adultery. A reparation offering is required from the man. This is one of the few cases in which a sacrifice is possible for deliberate sin.</i>? In error<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If in the dark he thought that she was his wife.</i>, he is obligated; intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This is the case treated by the verse. If he denies the accusation by two witnesses, one cannot take his denial as assertion that he did it but already had remedied the situation.</i>, he is obligated! He can tell him, I touched her but did not finish<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This is a first explanation: A sacrifice is due only if there was an ejaculation of semen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20" href="/Leviticus.19.20"><i>v</i>. 20</a>). If he took the slave girl to bed but stopped before there was an ejaculation, no sacrifice is due.</i>, or as Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, I was forced to it by the spell she put on me<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">He disclaims responsibility by reason of temporary insanity caused by the girl’s charms. This is enough to support his disclaimer against even two witnesses.</i>. For a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Exposed to the impurity of the dead; the case of the Mishnah.</i> what can you say? In error, he is obligated; intentionally, he is obligated; forced, he is obligated! “Suddenly”, to include in error, “suddenly”, to include intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This is proof that the <i>nazir</i> must bring his sacrifices even if forced. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num.</i> 6:9</a>. In all other sources, <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num.</i> 28 [= <i>Num. rabba</i> 10(31)], <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 9a" href="/Keritot.9a">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 9a</a>, the inference is from the double expression “if a person should die near him <i>suddenly</i>,<i>unexpectedly …</i>” “Suddenly” is taken to refer to accidental impurity, “unexpectedly” to outside force (<i>Sifry</i>) or outside force and intention (Babli).</i>. “There was a condition in my mind that if I should become impure, my <i>nezirut</i> should burst away from me and a new <i>nezirut</i> would fall on me”; in any case he was not obligated to be a <i>nazir</i> until now<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vow of <i>nezirut</i> could have been formulated in a way that eliminates the possibility of a sacrifice for impurity, the testimony of the witnesses can be explained away.</i>. (“There was a condition in my mind that if I should become impure, my <i>nezirut</i> should burst away from me and a new <i>nezirut</i> would fall on me”; in any case he was not obligated to be a <i>nazir</i> until now.)<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A case of dittography.</i> What do you have in case of an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If two witnesses tell a person that he owes a sacrifice because he has violated an oath imposed on him by other people (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev.</i> 5:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4">4</a>). How can he be believed if he denies the accusation?</i>? In error, he is obligated; intentionally, he is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.2.2">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 2:2</a>.</i>! A condition may apply to words<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If he undertakes anything, he may add conditions. If others (usually a court of law) impose an oath on him, he swears according to their understanding, rather than his own.</i>; there is no condition for oaths! It follows what Rebbi Abba said, Rav Jehudah: For error, its sacrifice, for intention, its sacrifice. But if he said, I thought that this was no oath, he is free<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Here, there may be a case in which no sacrifice can be demanded if the interested party denies their obligation.</i>. Therefore, all these subjects cannot be stated, but the following can be stated<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases discussed so far, the Sages cannot disagree with R. Jehudah; that is possible only in the cases dealt with in the next paragraph.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">From <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.2"><i>Soṭah</i> 6:2, Notes 34–42</a>. The readings from the Leiden ms. in <i>Soṭah</i> are noted <b>ל</b>, those from a Genizah text <b>ג</b>. The sentence which really refers to the discussion here is missing in the text; the scribe omitted from the first אמר לו אחד to the next occurrence; cf. <i>Soṭah</i> p. 252.</i> From what Rebbi Yose said, “X ate suet, and they warned him,” he is not whipped. If one person said to him, you are a <i>nazir</i>, and based on this testimony he behaved like a <i>nazir</i>, when then he drank wine or became impure in the impurity of the dead, if two warned him, he is whipped. Is not the main testimony given by a single witness? From what Rebbi Mana said, “X is the wife of a Cohen and whored; her husband, a Cohen, then had relations with her but I had warned him;” he is [not] whipped. If she went to a secluded place in the presence of two, and one said, I saw her that she became impure; when her husband, the Cohen, had relations with her but two had warned him, he is whipped. Is not the main testimony given by a single witness?
“They bring one sacrifice of impurity and one of purity.” That is, if they are at the end of thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah seems to indicate that immediately after being informed of the impurity, the <i>nezirim</i> bring the sacrifices. This impression is rectified here; the first set of sacrifices is due only if both have satisfied the terms of their original vows.</i>. But if one is in the middle of thirty days, one has to wait till the end of thirty days. If both of them were <i>nezirim</i> for thirty days. But if one was a <i>nazir</i> for thirty and the other a <i>nazir</i> for 100, one has to wait for 100. Rebbi Jacob the Southerner asked before Rebbi Yose: If one of them became impure during these days, what is the rule? He said to him, like one who has to seek out a person from the general public<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>nazir</i> who is not impure without a doubt observes the rules (either following R. Yose or Ben Zoma) for a person possibly impure whose companion <i>nazir</i> has died (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.1.1">Mishnaiot 2–4</a>).</i>.
If he drank wine within the first 30 days, he is (not) whipped; within the last 30 days, he is [] whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Here begins the discussion of Mishaniot 2 ff. The scribe (or the text he copied from) switched the places of “whipped” and “not whipped”. If one of the <i>nezirim</i> died, the other certainly has to finish the period of his vow and then has to start a second period because he might have been impure and the <i>nezirut</i> of part of his first period might have been invalid. Since the first period was in fulfillment of his vow of <i>nazir</i>, any transgression is prosecutable. But since he keeps the second period only because of a doubt, even if there are two witnesses both to his vow of <i>nazir</i> and to his drinking wine, a prosecutor could not prove that a crime certainly had been commited. Nobody can be found guilty by a human court if the crime was not proven.</i>. The one coming from the general population cannot be whipped, either for the (second) or the last period<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since his <i>nezirut</i> in both periods is conditional, the certainty of his crime is not provable.</i>. So far, if he was warned separately for each period<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the crime was proven, a conviction is possible only if criminal intent was proven by two witnesses who testify that the perpetrator was warned of the criminality of his undertaking; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.1.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 8:1, Note 9</a>.</i>. If they warned him for both together<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that either the first or the second period of <i>nezirut</i> are in fulfillment of his vow, even if it is not known which period counts in biblical law. If the <i>nazir</i> was warned for both during his first period after it was determined that he had to keep a second period, and he drank wine during both periods, the fact of the crime has been established. (The separate problem, that the accused might have forgotten the warning if it preceded the action by a long time, is not considered here.)</i> this is the disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">פלוגתא is the Babylonian form for Galilean תפלוגתא.</i> between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who disagreed about the two days of holiday in the diaspora<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Before the publication of the computed calendar, communities which could not be informed by messengers about the determination of the first days of the months of Nisan and Tishri, kept two days of holidays to account for possible variations in the dates. Since each day was only one of a possible two, no work on the holidays could be prosecutable unless the warning was given for both days, the infraction occured on both days, and this kind of long־term conditional warning was accepted in court. [After the publication of the calendar computations, the first day of a holiday is of biblical character; the second day is of purely rabbinic character and is kept only because the algorithm was published on condition that its users continue to keep the second day (<i>Eruvin</i> 3:10 21c 1. 24; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 4b" href="/Beitzah.4b">Babli <i>Beṣah</i> 4b</a>).]</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one accepts warning in case of a doubt; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one does not accept warning in case of a doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 11:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.11.7.6"><i>Yebamot</i> 11:7 (Note 171</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:4:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.4.2-12"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 5:4</a> (32s 1. 5); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 16a" href="/Makkot.16a">Babli <i>Makkot</i> 16a</a>.</i>.
“I made a vow of <i>nazir</i> but I forgot whether it was for now or for a later date<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">From the context it seems that he vowed a standard 30 day <i>nezirut</i> but does not remember whether it was to begin immediately or after 30 days. Then he has to keep 60 days and bring one set of sacrifices after 60 days.</i>.” They told him, this [warning] is for now, and that for the later date. If he drank wine within the first 30 days, he is [] whipped; within the last 30 days, he is (not) whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Here again, the statements are switched. He cannot be convicted during the first 30 days since it cannot be proven that he is obligated during these days.</i>. Should one not say that the first days may be within the period of his vow, the later ones are after the period of his vow<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">How can he be convicted for drinking wine during the second 30 days when it cannot be proven that these were intended by his vow (unless he drank during both periods)?</i>? “All days of his status as <i>nazir</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num.</i> 6:4</a>. As spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:20" href="/Numbers.6.20">v. 20</a>, the <i>nazir</i> is forbidden to drink wine until he has brought his sacrifices. Since he did not bring sacrifices after the first 30 days, during the second 30 days he certainly is guilty, either because of his vow of the second 30 days or as an extension of his vow of the first 30 days for which the sacrifice was not yet offered.</i>” to make the days after he finished his term like the days before he finished his term with respect to wine, impurity, and shaving.
Why can he not bring his sacrifices one by one? Not to preclude answers in the house of study<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the objection R. Joshua voiced to the method proposed by Ben Zoma. There is no reason why the sacrifices could not be brought at different times; R. Joshua offered his objection as a training exercise to his students, lest they accept any statement without subjecting it to criticism and also that they should not be afraid to propose hypothetical answers which could be disproved by analysis.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph presents textual difficulties. Since the following paragraph makes sense only in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:8:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.8.2-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 8:8</a>, the present paragraph also must be parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:8:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.8.2-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 8:8</a> (36b 1. 43 ff.) But the text there is quite different from the text here; they are not versions of a common original. In the text, the words “the community” and “a (single) person” have switched places. In <i>Pesaḥim</i>, different questions are asked, and the comparison is between “the community” and “a <i>nazir</i>”, which would make better sense here.</i>(The community) [A person] who became impure by a doubt relating to a private domain<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general principle that doubts about impurity are resolved by treating doubts in a private domain as certain impurity, in a public domain as certain purity (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.5"><i>Soṭah</i> 1:2, Note 88</a>). The rule becomes ineffective if the doubt arises in a domain whose status is in doubt.</i> on Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The 14th of Nisan, the day on which the <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifice has to be slaughtered in the afternoon.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia said, he should be pushed to the Second Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The 14th of Iyyar, instituted for someone who either was impure or “on a far journey” on the 14th of Nisan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:9-14" href="/Numbers.9.9-14"><i>Num.</i> 9:9–14</a>). R. Hoshaia treats him as certainly impure; he can stay near the Temple precinct and nevertheless celebrate the “Second Passover”.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one sends him on a far journey<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Joḥanan the case remains one of doubt. If he stayed near the Temple precinct, he could celebrate neither the first (not being pure) nor the second (not being impure) Passover. Therefore, one sends him away so he can celebrate the Second Passover under a different qualification.</i>. This follows what Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he became impure by the impurity of the abyss<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Pesaḥim</i>, this is called “the impurity of a broken field” (cf. Mishnah 7:3, Note 138); it applies to all impurities rabbinic in character. If the person incurs the impurity during the 14th of Nisan, he cannot offer the First Passover because he becomes pure after immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> only at sundown. He cannot offer the Second Passover because he was not biblically impure on the 14th of Nisan. He has to leave Jerusalem to be sufficiently “far away” in the afternoon of the 14th of Nisan.</i>, one sends him on a far journey. (A <i>nazir</i>) [The community] who became impure by a doubt relating to a private domain on Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The Second Passover was instituted only for individuals; if the majority of the people were impure, the Passover sacrifice was offered in impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.4.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 7:4</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.8.1">6</a>).</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it should be presented in their doubt; Rebbi Hoshaia said, it should be made in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since the community offers the First Passover in impurity, the difference between the two opinions is only that for R. Joḥanan the pure minority offers its sacrifices in purity whereas it seems that for R. Hoshaia the minority is obligated to follow the majority. This interpretation is rejected in the next sentence so that there remains no material difference between the two statements.</i>. The great Rebbi Hoshaia also agrees that it should be presented in their doubt; what Rebbi Hoshaia said referrred only to restrictions.
<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:8:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.8.2-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 8:8</a> (36b, 1. 55ff.) It belongs there and has no direct connections with the topics discussed here. A parallel discussion is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 71b" href="/Yevamot.71b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 71b</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Benaiah: One sprinkles on an uncircumcised Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The person impure by the impurity of the dead has to be sprinkled with water containing ashes of the Red Cow on the third and the seventh day of his impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num.</i> 19</a>). The uncircumcised is barred from the Passover sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:48" href="/Exodus.12.48"><i>Ex</i>. 12:48</a>). It is asserted that the uncircumcised can purify himself from the impurity and, being circumcised later, participate in the Passover celebration.</i>, for we find that our forefathers in the desert<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Not in the desert, but after crossing the Jordan on the 10<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 4:19" href="/Joshua.4.19"><i>Jos</i>. 4:19</a>). They were then circumcised (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 5:2-8" href="/Joshua.5.2-8"><i>Jos</i>. 5:2–8</a>) and celebrated Passover on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 5:10" href="/Joshua.5.10"><i>Jos</i>. 5:10</a>).</i> received sprinkling when uncircumcised. Rav Ḥisda said, following him who said that they circumcised on the eleventh<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">If they were circumcised on the 11<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> they had to be sprinkled on the 10<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, when they still were uncircumcised, in order to become pure on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>. But for him who said that they circumcised on the tenth, they did not count sprinkling when uncircumcised. Rebbi Abin said, did they not have to count for sprinkling when uncircumcised in any case<sup>68</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Ḥisda’s argument has to be rejected since if sprinkling on an uncircumcised person is ineffective, the underlying counting (of the third, seventh days) also must be ineffective. But for the Passover on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, the counting had to start on the 7<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>, when according to the biblical text everybody (except Joshua, Caleb, and possibly Levites and/or Priests) were uncircumcised. He will hold that the first Passover in Canaan was held in impurity. This argument, represented in the Babli, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>., by Mar Zuṭra, there is rejected by Rav Ashi.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: It happened that an uncircumcised Cohen was sprinkled<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Pesaḥim</i>: “he sprinkled” (active verb form).</i> and the sprinklings were declared valid. It was stated: Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, there were soldiers<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στρατιώτης.</i> and gate keepers in Jerusalem who immersed themselves and ate their Passover sacrifices in the evening<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">They were Gentiles who converted to Judaism, were circumcised on the 14th of Nisan, immersed themselves in a <i>miqweh</i> and participated in Passover, not needing cleansing by sprinkling following the House of Shammai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.8.1">Mishnah Pesaḥim 8:8</a>).</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A <i>nazir</i> who was doubtfully impure and doubtfully definitely [suffering from skin disease]<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">A person who has completed the 30 days of his <i>nazir</i> vow but is not sure whether or not he became impure from the impurity of the dead in his period of <i>nezirut</i>. Also, he was recently healed from skin disease. The sufferer from skin disease is called “definitively suffering from the disease” only if the conditions for impurity apply and he was declared impure <i>by a Cohen</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:8" href="/Leviticus.13.8"><i>Lev</i>. 13:8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:11" href="/Leviticus.13.11">11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:20" href="/Leviticus.13.20">20</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:22" href="/Leviticus.13.22">22</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:25" href="/Leviticus.13.25">25</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:27" href="/Leviticus.13.27">27</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:30" href="/Leviticus.13.30">30</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:44" href="/Leviticus.13.44">44</a>). A definitively suffering person has to be cleansed by the ritual of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-32" href="/Leviticus.14.1-32"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–32</a> which includes repeated shavings of all his hair. In the case treated in the Mishnah, the person was in a group where one was declared definitively affected, but the Cohen does not remember whom in particular he declared impure.</i> may eat sacrificial meat after 60 days<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">After he was healed from skin disease, purified from his impurity, and has completed the 30 days of his vow, he shaves the first time for his rehabilitation from skin disease. He cannot do it before he has completed the 30 days since he might be a pure <i>nazir</i> for whom it is forbidden to shave for a possible impurity of skin disease, as explained at the end of the Mishnah. He then should count seven days and shave a second time, but he cannot do that since he might have been impure and the shaving was not for his skin disease but for his impurity. Therefore, he can shave a second time and finish his purification from skin disease after an additional <i>nazir</i> period of 30 days; then he is able to partake of sacrificial meat.</i> and drink wine and become impure by the dead after 120 days<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot drink wine after 60 days since he might have been definitively sick but pure from the impurity of the dead; the period of his impurity as a sufferer from skin disease is not counted for his <i>nezirut</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.2-6">Halakhah 7:3</a>), which he has to keep for another 30 days. He cannot drink wine after 90 days since he might have been both definitively sick and impure from the impurity of the dead; his three shavings take care of the impurities both of skin disease and of the impure <i>nazir</i>; he has satisfied his obligations after 120 days and four shavings. The details of the required procedures and sacrifices are explained in the Halakhah.</i>, since the shaving for skin disease supersedes the shaving of the <i>nazir</i> when it is certain, but when it is in doubt it does not supersede.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Who is doubtfully definitely [suffering]? Two who came to him<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">To a Cohen knowledgeable in the diagnosis of skin disease.</i>. He declared one of them definitively affected but before he could declare the second one definitively affected, they were changed for him<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Since he does no longer recognize them, he cannot declare the second one definitively impure, even though he realizes that both of them satisfy all criteria of impurity, since the Cohen may not see two cases together (Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 3:1), and by now both are healed (cf. S. Lieberman, <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Nezirut</i> p. 563.)</i>. That is a doubtfully definitely [suffering person]. This is the one about whom Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta <i>Nazir</i> 6:1</a>. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Zevachim 76a" href="/Tosafot_on_Zevachim.76a">Tosaphot <i>Zebaḥim</i> 76a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 105a" href="/Menachot.105a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 105a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 70a" href="/Niddah.70a"><i>Niddah</i> 70a</a>, all <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. למחרת.)</i>: A <i>nazir</i>, in doubt whether impure or pure, but certainly he was a <i>nazir</i>. A sufferer from skin disease, in doubt whether impure or pure, but certainly he was a sufferer from skin disease. He eats from sacrificial meat after 60 days, drinks wine and defiles himself for the dead after 120 days. How is that? One said to him, you are an impure <i>nazir</i>, go count seven [days]. He sprinkled, repeated, shaved, and brought<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The offerings of two birds required from the impure <i>nazir</i>. The sentence can also be interpreted as imperative: Go count seven [days], sprinkle, repeat (on day 7), shave (on day 7), and bring! The Erfurt text of the Tosephta is self־contradictory; it is partially vocalized הַיזה ושנה וגַילח; the consonantal text is in the imperfect, the punctuation in the imperative.</i>. He then counted seven [days] and wanted to shave. One said to him, impure <i>nazir</i>, go count seven [days] and continue for 30. He counted 30 [days] and wanted to shave. One said to him, you are a pure <i>nazir</i>, does not a pure <i>nazir</i> shave on kinds of blood<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot shave without an appropriate sacrifice. In the Tosephta, the sentence is declarative: וְאֵין נָזִיר טָהוֹר מְגַלֵּחַ אֶלָּא עַל הַדָּמִים “a pure <i>nazir</i> shaves only on a bloody sacrifice”.</i>? He brings an elevation offering and shaves on it, a bird purification offering and stipulates, saying: “If I was impure, the purification offering is my obligation and the elevation offering is voluntary. If I was pure, the elevation offering is my obligation and the purification offering is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice of impurity consists of two birds. The one offered as elevation sacrifice can be given as obligation, if there was impurity, or as voluntary gift, if there was none. The bird offered as purification sacrifice cannot be eaten by the priests; after its blood was used, it has to be burned outside the Temple precinct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:6" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.6">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 7:6</a>).</i>” He shaves his head, his beard, and his eyebrows in the way the sufferer from skin disease shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:9" href="/Leviticus.14.9"><i>Lev.</i> 14:9</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta <i>Nazir</i> 6:1</a>. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Zevachim 76a" href="/Tosafot_on_Zevachim.76a">Tosaphot <i>Zebaḥim</i> 76a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 105a" href="/Menachot.105a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 105a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 70a" href="/Niddah.70a"><i>Niddah</i> 70a</a>, all <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. למחרת.)</i> How does one deal with him in a restrictive way? He brings two swallows and slaughters one of them over a clay vessel filled from running water; one digs und buries it, for it is forbidden for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:1 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:4-5" href="/Leviticus.14.4-5"><i>Lev.</i> 14:4–5</a>).</i>. He shaves his head, beard, and eyebrows in the way the sufferers from skin disease do. He counts 30 days and brings another animal as elevation offering, saying: “If I was impure, the first elevation offering was voluntary and this one<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This translation follows the Tosephta text. In ms. and <i>editio princeps</i> at both places אוֹ “or” instead of וְזֶה “and this one”.</i> is obligatory, and the purification offering is my obligation. If I was pure, the first elevation offering was my obligation, this one<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This translation follows the Tosephta text. In ms. and <i>editio princeps</i> at both places אוֹ “or” instead of וְזֶה “and this one”.</i> is voluntary, and the purification offering is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice of impurity consists of two birds. The one offered as elevation sacrifice can be given as obligation, if there was impurity, or as voluntary gift, if there was none. The bird offered as purification sacrifice cannot be eaten by the priests; after its blood was used, it has to be burned outside the Temple precinct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:6" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.6">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 7:6</a>).</i>” He shaves his head, his beard, and his eyebrows in the way the sufferer from skin disease shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:9" href="/Leviticus.14.9"><i>Lev.</i> 14:9</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta <i>Nazir</i> 6:1</a>. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Zevachim 76a" href="/Tosafot_on_Zevachim.76a">Tosaphot <i>Zebaḥim</i> 76a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 105a" href="/Menachot.105a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 105a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 70a" href="/Niddah.70a"><i>Niddah</i> 70a</a>, all <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. למחרת.)</i>Rebbi Simeon said: On the next day<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">As required in the purification ritual for the sufferer from skin disease, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:10" href="/Leviticus.14.10"><i>Lev.</i> 14:10</a>.</i>, he brings his reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The healed person has to bring three sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:10" href="/Leviticus.14.10"><i>Lev.</i> 14:10</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21-22" href="/Leviticus.21-22">21–22</a>), but only the sheep as reparation sacrifice is needed in the purification ceremony.</i> and a <i>log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>log</i> is .533 1.</i> of oil with it and says: If I was impure, this is my obligatory offering, otherwise it is my doubtful offering<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">This text cannot be accepted; it makes no sense and contradicts the last statement in the Halakhah. The text should be replaced by the corresponding one in the Tosephta: וְאִם לָאו הֲרֵי זֶה שַׁלְמֵי נְדָבָה "otherwise, it should be a voluntary well-being offering". It is impossible to bring a reparation offering except for an obligation but a well-being offering is voluntary in almost all cases.</i>. He slaughters in the North<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since a reparation offering is “most holy”, it must be slaughtered to the North of the altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:2" href="/Leviticus.7.2"><i>Lev.</i> 7:2</a>). The Tosephta adds that the sacrifice also needs the obligatory gifts accompanying a well-being offering.</i>, it needs giving on the thumbs<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Blood of the sacrifice and oil from the flask have to be put on the convalescent’s right earlobe, right thumb, and right great toe (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:14" href="/Leviticus.14.14"><i>Lev.</i> 14:14</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:17" href="/Leviticus.14.17">17</a>).</i>, and it is eaten by male Cohanim at any place<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Any place within the Temple precinct; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:6" href="/Leviticus.7.6"><i>Lev.</i> 7:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 5:5" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.5.5">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i> 5:5</a>).</i> during the day and the following night until midnight. But the Sages did [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Text of the Tosephta, required by the context.</i> agree with Rebbi Simeon because he causes sacrifices to become invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Well-being sacrifices may be eaten by all pure Israelites for two days and the intervening night, reparation sacrifices only by priests and only for a day and half a night. There is a likelyhood that some of the meat of the reparation sacrifice will become invalid after midnight; it has to be burned outside the Temple precinct. This is an alienation from the original purpose and has to be avoided.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta <i>Nazir</i> 6:1</a>. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Zevachim 76a" href="/Tosafot_on_Zevachim.76a">Tosaphot <i>Zebaḥim</i> 76a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 105a" href="/Menachot.105a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 105a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 70a" href="/Niddah.70a"><i>Niddah</i> 70a</a>, all <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. למחרת.)</i> He cannot bring an animal as purification sacrifice since he does not have the right to an animal purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta is more explicit: “Because an animal purification sacrifice cannot be offered in case of doubt.”</i>. He cannot bring a bird as purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The offering of the poor person healed from skin disease, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:22" href="/Leviticus.14.22"><i>Lev.</i> 14:22</a>.</i> since a rich person who brought the sacrifices prescribed for a poor person did not fulfill his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:12.</i>. What can he do? He shall write his property over to another person<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Then he is poor.</i> and bring the sacrifices prescribed for a poor person; this enables the rich person to bring a bird as a purification sacrifice. He stipulates and says: If I was impure, that is my obligation. Otherwise, this is in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice of impurity consists of two birds. The one offered as elevation sacrifice can be given as obligation, if there was impurity, or as voluntary gift, if there was none. The bird offered as purification sacrifice cannot be eaten by the priests; after its blood was used, it has to be burned outside the Temple precinct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:6" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.6">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 7:6</a>).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta <i>Nazir</i> 6:1</a>. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Zevachim 76a" href="/Tosafot_on_Zevachim.76a">Tosaphot <i>Zebaḥim</i> 76a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 105a" href="/Menachot.105a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 105a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 70a" href="/Niddah.70a"><i>Niddah</i> 70a</a>, all <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. למחרת.)</i> He may eat sacrificial meat immediately. He cannot drink wine or defile himself for the dead since his <i>nezirut</i> is not counted during the days of his [impurity of] skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot drink wine after 60 days since he might have been definitively sick but pure from the impurity of the dead; the period of his impurity as a sufferer from skin disease is not counted for his <i>nezirut</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.2-6">Halakhah 7:3</a>), which he has to keep for another 30 days. He cannot drink wine after 90 days since he might have been both definitively sick and impure from the impurity of the dead; his three shavings take care of the impurities both of skin disease and of the impure <i>nazir</i>; he has satisfied his obligations after 120 days and four shavings. The details of the required procedures and sacrifices are explained in the Halakhah.</i>.
He counts another 30 days and brings all his sacrifices, stipulates, and says: If I was impure, the first elevation offering was obligatory and this one is voluntary. But if I was pure, the first elevation offering was voluntary and this one is obligatory. The purification offering is in doubt. He shaves his head, beard, and eyebrows, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to make sense of this text (which has no parallel in the Tosephta). The last sentence is certainly out of place since the ceremonies at the end of 60 days disposed of all possible impurities of the sufferer from skin disease. He cannot bring the entire set of sacrifices of the pure <i>nazir</i> before 120 days, as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.2.6">Note 74</a>. In addition, since an impure <i>nazir</i> has to bring a bird as elevation offering, rather than a four-legged animal, it is clear that after 120 days the declaration must read: “If I was impure, the first elevation offering <i>was voluntary and this one is obligatory</i>. But if I was pure, the first elevation offering was <i>obligatory and this one is voluntary</i>.” It seems that a paragraph is missing, describing the offering after 90 days (which is parallel to the one after 30 days for the person who might have been impure by the impurity of the dead, Mishnah 3) and that the duly corrected present paragraph describes the sacrifices after 120 days.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">This text is hopelessly garbled. It seems that the original text was close to the Tosephta:<br>כֵּיצַד תַּעֲשֶׂה לוֹ כְדִבְרֵי בֶּן זוֹמָא. סוֹפֵר שְׁלֹשִׁים יוֹם וּמֵבִיא עוֹלַת בְּהֵמָה וּמְגַלֵּחַ וּמֵבִיא חַטַּאת הָעוֹף וּמַתְנֶה עָלֶיהָ וְאוֹמֵר אִם טָמֵא אֲנִי חַטָּאת מֵחוֹבָתִי וְעוֹלָה נְדָבָה אִם טָהוֹר אֲנִי עוֹלָה מֵחוֹבָתִי וְחַטָּאת סָפֵק. וְסוֹפֵר שְׁלֹשִׁים יוֹם וּמֵבִיא קָרְבְּנוֹתָיו שְׁלֵמִים וּמֵבִיא עוֹלַת בְּהֵמָה וּמַתְנֶה עָלֶיהָ וְאָמַר אִם טָמֵא אֲנִי עוֹלָה הָרִאשׁוֹנָה חוֹבָה וְזוֹ נְדָבָה וְחַטַּאת הָעוֹף מֵחוֹבָתוֹ. אִם טָהוֹר אֲנִי עוֹלָה הָרִאשׁוֹנָה נְדָבָה וְזוֹ חוֹבָה וְחַטַּאת הָעוֹף סְפֵיקִי וְשׁוֹתֶה יַיִן וּמִטַּמֵּא לַמֵּתִים מִיָּד. בַּמֶּה דְבָרִים אֲמוּרִים בִּזְמַן שֶׁנָּזַר שְׁלֹשִׁים יוֹם אֲבָל נָזַר שְׁנֵים עָשָׂר חֹדֶשׁ אוֹכֵל בַּקֳּדָשִׁים לְאַחַר שְׁתֵּי שָׁנִים. וְשׁוֹתֶה יַיִן וּמִיטַּמֵּא לַמֵּתִים אַחַר אַרְבַּע שָׁנִים.<br>How does one treat him according to Ben Zoma<sup class="footnote-marker">a</sup><i class="footnote">Ben Zoma disagrees with R. Simeon and holds that the healed sufferer from skin disease can cleanse himself by the bird purification sacrifice of the poor, without a reparation sacrifice. The Tosephta text also is defective, since Ben Zoma also agrees that complete purification is possible only after four periods. The original statement also must have contained four parts, not two as in the current text.</i>? He counts 30 days, brings an animal elevation offering and shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">b</sup><i class="footnote">As Tosaphot explains in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 76a" href="/Zevachim.76a"><i>Zebaḥim</i> 76a</a>, it is assumed that he already cleansed himself from the impurity of the dead by sprinkling. This shaving is valid if he was not impure from skin disease; it is useless if he was impure from skin disease since then it was not done in the correct framework and <i>nezirut</i> is not counted during his skin disease impurity.</i>, a bird as purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">c</sup><i class="footnote">After another 30 days.</i>, stipulates, and says: “If I was impure, the purification offering is for my obligation and the elevation offering is voluntary. If I was pure, the elevation offering is for my obligation and the purification offering is in doubt.” Then he counts 30 days, brings the completion of his sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">d</sup><i class="footnote">The well-being offering of the pure <i>nazir</i>. He cannot bring the animal purification offering since this may not be offered in cases of doubt. Naturally, he also shaves for this sacrifice, and he has to stipulate that all sacrifices are either voluntary or in doubt in case he was already completely purified by the prior shavings</i> and an animal elevation offering, stipulates, and says: <sup class="footnote-marker">e</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from the Vienna ms. and the <i>editio princeps</i>. The Erfurt ms. reads “If I was impure, the prior purification offering was <i>obligatory</i>, the present one is <i>voluntary</i>, and the bird purification offering is obligatory. If I was pure, the prior elevation offering was <i>voluntary</i> and the present one <i>obligatory</i>; the purification offering is in doubt.” It seems that the two texts are not variations of one another (which would make the Erfurt text obviously erroneous) but residues of two different paragraphs of the original four-part text.</i> “If I was impure, the prior purification offering was voluntary, the present one is obligatory, and the bird purification offering is obligatory. If I was pure, the prior elevation offering was obligatory and the present one voluntary; the purification offering is in doubt.” <sup class="footnote-marker">f</sup><i class="footnote">The forth period; its sacrifices and declarations are missing.</i> He immediately drinks wine and defiles himself for the dead. When had this been said? If the vow of <i>nazir</i> was for 30 days. But if his vow was for 12 months, he eats sacrificial meat after two years, drinks wine, and defiles himself for the dead after four years.</i> How does one treat him according to Ben Zoma? He brings an animal elevation offering and shaves for it, a bird as purification offering, stipulates, and says: “If I was impure, the purification offering is for my obligation and the elevation offering is voluntary. If I was pure, the elevation offering is for my obligation and the purification offering is in doubt.” He shaves his head, beard, and eyebrows, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is out of place here.</i>
He may drink wine and defile himself for the dead immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">There is a possibility that the original text underlying the Halakhah was not that of the Tosephta but referred to the situation mentioned by R. Ze‘ira in the next Halakhah.</i>. When has this been said? If he made the vow of <i>nazir</i> as a <i>nazir</i>, but if he made the vow of <i>nazir</i> as a sufferer from skin disease he might eat sacrificial meat after two years, drink wine, and defile himself for the dead after four years<sup class="footnote-marker">97,</sup><i class="footnote">This text is hopelessly garbled. It seems that the original text was close to the Tosephta:<br>כֵּיצַד תַּעֲשֶׂה לוֹ כְדִבְרֵי בֶּן זוֹמָא. סוֹפֵר שְׁלֹשִׁים יוֹם וּמֵבִיא עוֹלַת בְּהֵמָה וּמְגַלֵּחַ וּמֵבִיא חַטַּאת הָעוֹף וּמַתְנֶה עָלֶיהָ וְאוֹמֵר אִם טָמֵא אֲנִי חַטָּאת מֵחוֹבָתִי וְעוֹלָה נְדָבָה אִם טָהוֹר אֲנִי עוֹלָה מֵחוֹבָתִי וְחַטָּאת סָפֵק. וְסוֹפֵר שְׁלֹשִׁים יוֹם וּמֵבִיא קָרְבְּנוֹתָיו שְׁלֵמִים וּמֵבִיא עוֹלַת בְּהֵמָה וּמַתְנֶה עָלֶיהָ וְאָמַר אִם טָמֵא אֲנִי עוֹלָה הָרִאשׁוֹנָה חוֹבָה וְזוֹ נְדָבָה וְחַטַּאת הָעוֹף מֵחוֹבָתוֹ. אִם טָהוֹר אֲנִי עוֹלָה הָרִאשׁוֹנָה נְדָבָה וְזוֹ חוֹבָה וְחַטַּאת הָעוֹף סְפֵיקִי וְשׁוֹתֶה יַיִן וּמִטַּמֵּא לַמֵּתִים מִיָּד. בַּמֶּה דְבָרִים אֲמוּרִים בִּזְמַן שֶׁנָּזַר שְׁלֹשִׁים יוֹם אֲבָל נָזַר שְׁנֵים עָשָׂר חֹדֶשׁ אוֹכֵל בַּקֳּדָשִׁים לְאַחַר שְׁתֵּי שָׁנִים. וְשׁוֹתֶה יַיִן וּמִיטַּמֵּא לַמֵּתִים אַחַר אַרְבַּע שָׁנִים.<br>How does one treat him according to Ben Zoma<sup class="footnote-marker">a</sup><i class="footnote">Ben Zoma disagrees with R. Simeon and holds that the healed sufferer from skin disease can cleanse himself by the bird purification sacrifice of the poor, without a reparation sacrifice. The Tosephta text also is defective, since Ben Zoma also agrees that complete purification is possible only after four periods. The original statement also must have contained four parts, not two as in the current text.</i>? He counts 30 days, brings an animal elevation offering and shaves<sup class="footnote-marker">b</sup><i class="footnote">As Tosaphot explains in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 76a" href="/Zevachim.76a"><i>Zebaḥim</i> 76a</a>, it is assumed that he already cleansed himself from the impurity of the dead by sprinkling. If I was pure, the prior elevation offering was <i>voluntary</i> and the present one <i>obligatory</i>; the purification offering is in doubt.” It seems that the two texts are not variations of one another (which would make the Erfurt text obviously erroneous) but residues of two different paragraphs of the original four-part text.</i> “If I was impure, the prior purification offering was voluntary, the present one is obligatory, and the bird purification offering is obligatory. Ze‘ira in the next Halakhah.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, these are Ben Zoma’s words, but the words of the Sages: Forever is he definitively [impure] until he heave<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:12" href="/Leviticus.14.12"><i>Lev.</i> 14:12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:24" href="/Leviticus.14.24">24</a>.</i> the reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The entire purification of the convalescent from skin disease is by the blood of the reparation sacrifice and the olive oil; the other sacrifices are dependent on the reparation sacrifice; cf. Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:11.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Why is he definitively [impure] until he heave the reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This would imply that the <i>nazir</i> who may be impure from the dead and may be definitively impure from skin disease can never cleanse himself for sacrificial meat since R. Simeon’s approach was rejected. The purification of the convalescent in all other respects only depends on the ceremonies described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-9" href="/Leviticus.14.1-9"><i>Lev.</i> 14:1–9</a>, which do not require any sacrifice (Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:3; Maimonides <i>Tum’at Ṣara‘at</i> 11:2).</i>? Rebbi Yose said to him, why not? We find several reparation sacrifices which are pushed aside because of doubts! As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 8:3" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.8.3">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i> 8:3</a>. The animals should be put out to graze until they develop a blemish, when they can be sold and the money used for new sacrifices. In the meantime, the owners of the sacrifices can fulfill their obligations only by offering substitute animals.</i>: “If a reparation sacrifice was mixed up with a well-being sacrifice, Rebbi Simeon says that both should be slaughtered in the North and eaten following the more restrictive rules. They said to him, one does not cause sacrifices to become invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Well-being sacrifices may be eaten by all pure Israelites for two days and the intervening night, reparation sacrifices only by priests and only for a day and half a night. There is a likelyhood that some of the meat of the reparation sacrifice will become invalid after midnight; it has to be burned outside the Temple precinct. This is an alienation from the original purpose and has to be avoided.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob the Southerner asked before Rebbi Yose: Could they not arrange something for his wine<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Why does the <i>nazir</i> have to wait until the problems arising from the skin disease are resolved, which in the best case takes 2 periods of <i>nezirut</i> and in the worst forever? Why can he not shave twice within eight days to remove the impurity of skin disease (for profane purposes and heave) and then proceed directly to resolve the problem of <i>nezirut</i>?</i>? He said to him, “the <i>nazir</i> shaves at the gate of the Tent of Meeting<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num.</i> 6:18</a>. The <i>nazir</i> cannot proceed to liquidate his vow as long as he is barred from holy places. (Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:2:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.2.13">Note 110</a>).</i>;” if he is able to appear at the gate of the Tent of Meeting. This excludes him who is unable to appear at the gate of the Tent of Meeting.
What is the status of his hair<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The shavings of a <i>nazir</i> are forbidden for usufruct; they must be burned within the Temple precinct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num.</i> 6:18</a>). But nothing is prescribed for the shavings of the convalescent sufferer from skin disease; therefore, these are permitted for usufruct.</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagreed. Rebbi Joḥanan said, his hair is forbidden; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, his hair is permitted. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: Do they disagree about a sufferer from skin disease who made a vow of <i>nazir</i> or do they disagree about a <i>nazir</i> who became a sufferer from skin disease? If you say that they disagree about a sufferer from skin disease who made a vow of <i>nazir</i>, then for a <i>nazir</i> who became a sufferer from skin disease everybody agrees that it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since his hair was forbidden already before he contracted the disease.</i>. If you say that they disagree about a <i>nazir</i> who became a sufferer from skin disease, then for a sufferer from skin disease who made a vow of <i>nazir</i> everybody agrees that it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Any shavings for his convalescence are subject to the rules of skin disease.</i>. For Rebbi Jeremiah it was obvious that they disagreed about a sufferer from skin disease who made a vow of <i>nazir</i>. Therefore, for a <i>nazir</i> who became a sufferer from skin disease everybody agrees that it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">For consistency, one should read here: “forbidden.”</i>
Does the obligatory shaving push aside the rules of voluntary shaving<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:27" href="/Leviticus.19.27"><i>Lev.</i> 19:27</a> forbids to “shear off the corners of your head and to destroy the corners of your beard.” The <i>nazir</i> must shave his entire head, including its corners; the sufferer from skin disease must shave off all his hair, including corners of head and beard. There is no question that a person certainly impure, either from a corpse in the case of the <i>nazir</i>, or being declared impure as a sufferer from skin disease, must shave as required. The problem is only how far a shaving because of doubt really pushes aside the general rules about forbidden shaving.</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.2.4">Halakhah 4</a>, speaking of a case of double doubt.</i>: “He shaves his head, his beard, and his eyebrows in the way sufferers from skin disease shave.” But there one could say, maybe it was an obligatory shaving. <sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this text does not belong here. It belongs to the paragraph before the last (Notes 102, 103), where R. Ze‘ira notes that the <i>nazir</i> cannot begin counting for his vow unless he is able to enter the Temple precinct. He brings an additional proof from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.9.1">Mishnah 6:11</a>, which notes that permission to drink wine is given to the <i>nazir</i> if and only if one of his sacrifices was offered in the Temple, whether he shaved or not. Therefore, his proceedings as <i>nazir</i> cannot be based on the shavings as a convalescent alone, disregarding his required sacrifice. There can be no shortcut.</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Yudan: The Mishnah says so; “for Rebbi Simeon says, when one of the bloods was sprinkled, the <i>nazir</i> is permitted to drink wine and to defile himself with the dead.” Is he not forbidden because of “do not destroy”? Samuel bar Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that reference is made not to R. Samuel bar Abba, the student of R. Joḥanan, but to Samuel (bar Abba bar Abba) the Babylonian who in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 57b" href="/Nazir.57b">Babli, 57b</a>, restricts the applicability of the rules of shavings to women and minors, to whom <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:27" href="/Leviticus.19.27"><i>Lev.</i> 19:27</a> does not apply.</i> said, explain it if he was a eunuch<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Since we are speaking of a person who certainly had made a vow of <i>nazir</i>, there can be no question that he is entitled to shear “the corners of his head”; the only problem is to shave “the corners of his beard” when it is not clear that it is really required. If the person in question has no beard, there is no problem.</i>. But did we not state “the beard”? How can you say, a eunuch? You have only what Rebbi Jacob said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he removed it from bottom to top it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">By implication it would not be forbidden if the head was shaved totally before the beard was.</i>. Rebbi Yose bar Abin said, there is no difference between bottom to top or top to bottom<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding statement is rejected. It follows that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:27" href="/Leviticus.19.27"><i>Lev.</i> 19:27</a> forbids to “shear off the corners of your head and to destroy the corners of your beard” but not to shave off <i>all</i> hair on the head and <i>all</i> hair on the chin in case of need.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta 6:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 60b" href="/Nazir.60b">Babli 60b</a>. The text as it stands here is mostly unintelligible; the correct numbers are given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 6" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).6">Tosephta 6</a>/Babli:<br>טָמֵא בְסָפֵק וּמוּחְלַט בְּוַדַּיי אוֹכֵל בַּקֳּדָשִׁים לְאַחַר שְׁמוֹנָה יָמִים שׁוֹתֶה יַיִן וּמִיטַּמֵּא לַמֵּתִים לְאַחַר שִׁשִּׁים וְשִׁבְעָה יוֹם. טָמֵא בְּוַדַּאי וּמוּחְלָט בְּסָפֵק אוֹכֵל בַּקֳּדָשִׁים לְאַחַר שְׁלֹשִׁים וְשִׁבְעָה יוֹם שׁוֹתֶה יַיִן וּמִטַּמֵּא לַמֵּתִים לְאַחַר שִׁבְעִים וְאַרְבַּע יוֹם. טָמֵא בְּוַדַּאי וּמוּחְלַט בְּוַדַּיי אוֹכֵל בַּקֳּדָשִׁים לְאַחַר שְׁמוֹנָה יָמִים וְשׁוֹתֶה בַּיַּיִן וּמִיטַּמֵּא לַמֵּתִים לְאַחַר ארְבָּעִים וְאַרְבָּעָה יוֹם.<br>If he was doubtfully impure and certainly definitely [suffering from skin disease], he eats from sacrificial meat after <i>8</i> days<sup class="footnote-marker">a</sup><i class="footnote">The eight days of purification of the sufferer from skin disease.</i>, drinks wine and defiles himself for the dead after <i>67</i> days<sup class="footnote-marker">b</sup><i class="footnote">The sixty days for two periods of <i>nezirut</i> plus seven days of the sufferer from skin disease which do not count for his <i>nezirut.</i></i>. If he was certainly impure and doubtfully definitely [suffering from skin disease], he eats from sacrificial meat after <i>37</i> days<sup class="footnote-marker">c</sup><i class="footnote">He waits 7 days and shaves, both for his impurity of the dead and since he might have been impure with skin disease. He then keeps 30 days of <i>nezirut</i> in purity and shaves. This shaving certainly counts for the second shaving of the sufferer from skin disease; afterwards he may eat sacrificial meat. Since the thirty days would not count for his <i>nezirut</i> if he actually was a sufferer from skin disease, he keeps another 7 days for his impurity of the dead followed by 30 days of <i>nezirut</i> in purity, shaves, and is permitted wine.</i>, drinks wine and defiles himself for the dead after <i>74</i> days. If he was certainly impure and certainly definitely [suffering from skin disease], he eats from sacrificial meat after eight days<sup class="footnote-marker">a</sup><i class="footnote">The eight days of purification of the sufferer from skin disease.</i>, drinks wine and defiles himself for the dead after 44 days<sup class="footnote-marker">d</sup><i class="footnote">Seven days of the convalescent sufferer from skin disease, seven days of the person defiled by the impurity of the dead, and 30 days of pure <i>nezirut.</i></i>.</i>“If he was doubtfully impure and certainly definitely [suffering from skin disease], he eats from sacrificial meat after 60 days, drinks wine and defiles himself for the dead after 120 days. If he was certainly impure and doubtfully definitely [suffering from skin disease], he eats from sacrificial meat after 20 days, drinks wine and defiles himself for the dead after 44 days. If he was certainly impure and certainly definitely [suffering from skin disease], he eats from sacrificial meat after eight days, drinks wine and defiles himself for the dead after 44 days.”
Rebbi Abbamari, the brother of Rebbi Yose said, this shows that the shaving knife destroys seven for an impure <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the Tosephta/Babli text is also understood here. In no case are the seven days counting for the convalescent counted for the <i>nazir</i>.</i>.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Gentiles cannot take a vow as <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, Gentiles may vow under Jewish law. Only the special vow of <i>nazir</i> is restricted to Jews.</i>. Women and slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Who had been circumcised and become Jewish by immersion in a miqweh. When manumitted, such a slave will be a full member of the Jewish community. While still in servile status, he is obligated to follow all rules imposed on women.</i> can take a vow as <i>nazir</i>. A man’s power is restricted for his wives more than for his slaves since he can force his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If the slave makes any vow which inconveniences his master, the latter can veto the execution of the vow as long as the slave is under his authority.</i> but he cannot force his wife. His power is restricted for his slaves more than for his wives since he can dissolve his wife’s vows<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nedarim</i> Chapters 10,11.</i> but not his slave’s vows<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If the master vetoed the implementation of his slave’s vow, that vow becomes dormant but not cancelled. Once the master’s authority is lifted, the vow is automatically activated, as explained in the next sentence.</i>. If he dissolved his wife’s, they are eternally dissolved. If he dissolved his slave’s, once the slave gains his freedom he has to fulfill his <i>nezirut</i>. If [the slave]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Who made a vow of <i>nazir</i> against his master’s wishes.</i> is not in the presence of his master, Rebbi Meïr says, he cannot drink<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Wine. Similarly, R. Meїr will hold that the slave may not defile himself for the dead without specific instruction by his master.</i>, but Rebbi Yose says, he may drink.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Gentiles cannot vow as <i>nazir</i>,” etc. Israelites can vow as <i>nazir</i>, Gentiles cannot vow as <i>nazir</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Section 7 2" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Section_7.2"><i>Sifra Emor Parašah</i> 7(2</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 72b" href="/Menachot.72b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 72b</a>. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:18" href="/Leviticus.22.18"><i>Lev.</i> 22:18</a>: “Every man of the House of Israel, and of the sojourners in Israel, who would bring their sacrifices for all their vows and all their gifts, to present them to the Eternal as elevation offerings.” In the same Chapter, v. 25 makes it clear that what is acceptable from the Israelite is accptable from the Gentile both as vow (in which a person engages himself to dedicate an animal) and a gift (in which a person dedicates an animal, in which case he does not have to supply a replacement if anything should happen to the animal before it could be sacrificed.)</i> Since you say, “a man”, why does the verse mention “a man”<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">“Every person” is in Hebrew אִישׁ אִישׁ “man, man”. The repetition has to be explained.</i>? To include Gentiles, who make vows and offer voluntary gifts like Israelites! Why should one not say the same here<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num.</i> 6:2</a> reads: “A man or a woman, if he makes a clear vow of <i>nazir</i> to the Eternal.” “Man” should include Gentiles by the preceding argument.</i>? There is a difference, for it is written “He shall atone for him.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:11" href="/Numbers.6.11"><i>Num.</i> 6:11</a>. Since Gentiles are not subject to the rules of impurity, the rituals of purification cannot apply to them.</i>” This refers to one to whom atonement applies. It excludes Gentiles, to whom atonement does not apply.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 1:2.</i>, we have stated: “The Non-Jew, Rebbi Meїr says, is evaluated but does not evaluate<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:1-8" href="/Leviticus.27.1-8"><i>Lev.</i> 27:1–8</a>, where “evaluation” amounts are specified to be offered to the Sanctuary for persons of specified age and sex. According to R. Meïr, a Jew may vow to pay a Gentile’s evaluation but a Gentile cannot make such a vow. According to R. Jehudah, a Gentile may vow to pay a Jew’s valuation but a Jew cannot offer to pay the Gentile’s evaluation.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says, he evaluates but is not evaluated. Both agree that they make vows and are objects of vows<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Both Jew and Gentile may make a vow to pay to the Sanctuary the amount another person would fetch were he to be sold on the slave market.</i>.” Rebbi Jeremiah asked: For him who says there that he evaluates, here can he declare a <i>nazir</i>? For him who says there that he is evaluated, here can he be declared a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The texts used to introduce a vow of evaluation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:2" href="/Leviticus.27.2"><i>Lev.</i> 27:2</a>) and a vow of <i>nazir</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num.</i> 6:2</a>) are completely parallel. It is to be assumed that the rules derived from the texts are also parallel; if a Gentile can cause an obligation of payment of an evaluation, he also can cause an obligation of <i>nazir</i>; if a Gentile can be the object of an obligation of payment of an evaluation, he also can be the object of an obligation of <i>nazir</i>.</i>? How would it be possible for him to [be declared a <i>nazir</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The context requires to read ניזור for מזיר and vice-versa.</i>? A Israelite can declare a Gentile to be a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Tentative answer.</i>. Would he listen to him<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Rejection of the tentative answer.</i>? But the Israelite said, “I am a <i>nazir</i>,” the Gentile heard it and said, “I am responsible for what this one said.” It is only as if he paid a debt<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile may well vow to the Jew’s obligatory sacrifices since by biblical law, Gentiles’ sacrifices are accepted. But this does not make the Gentile a <i>nazir</i>.</i>. How would it be possible for him to [declare a <i>nazir</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The context requires to read ניזור for מזיר and vice-versa.</i>? A Gentile can declare an Israelite to be <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Tentative answer.</i>. Since an Israelite cannot declare [another] Israelite to be a <i>nazir</i>, a Gentile cannot declare an Israelite to be a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Rejection of the tentative answer.</i>. But it must be that the Gentile said, “I am a <i>nazir</i>”, the Israelite heard it and said, “I am taking upon me what this one said.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Gentile’s vow is invalid, so is the Jew’s.</i>” What did you exclude from him<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This now is R. Jeremiah’s real question. Since it was shown that the power to evaluate is not related to the power to vow for <i>nazir</i>, the question arises whether in general the power of Gentiles to make vows is equal to that of Jews or not.</i>? An Israelite is subject to “not to desecrate”<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num.</i> 30:3</a>; <i>Sifry zuṭa</i> 30:2. Since a Gentile is not subject to biblical laws other than those given to Adam and Noah, he cannot be held responsible if he “desecrates his word,” i. e., does not keep his vow.</i>, a Gentile is not subject to “not to desecrate”. Rebbi Jonah said, Israelites can be relieved by a Sage<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">An Elder can annul the vow; cf. Tractate <i>Nedarim</i>, Introduction and Chapter 9.</i>, Gentiles cannot be relieved by a Sage<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since Gentiles are not subject to the Law, they cannot profit from the Law. He will hold that Gentiles, whose informal marriages have biblical sanction, never can be divorced; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.10"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:2, Notes 33</a> ff.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, Israelites need a Sage to be relieved, Gentiles do not need a Sage to be relieved<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since Gentiles are not subject to the Law, they do not need the Law. He will hold that since Gentiles are not under the biblical obligation of formal marriage, they never need a divorce but can validly terminate a companionship through unilateral action by either one of the parties.</i>. It follows that Rebbi (Jonah) [Yose] follows Rebbi Abbahu. A Gentile woman came before Rebbi Abbahu, who said to Abime bar Tobi: Go and find her an opening by unforeseen circumstances<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Which is forbidden to Jews, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.2.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 9:2</a>. He ordered the lady’s vow to be dissolved to give her peace of mind, not because it would have been necessary.</i>. And Rebbi Jonah follows Rebbi Aḥa, who said that Laban was <i>Kushan-Riš‘ataim</i>. Why was he called “Kushan the doubly bad”? Because he committed two evils. One that he desecrated his oath, the other that he subjugated Israel for eight years<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 105b" href="/Sanhedrin.105b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 105b</a>, as tannaïtic text. The story would make Laban live more than 300 years.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The argument about the wife’s vow is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.1.8"><i>Nedarim</i> 11:1, Notes 23–25</a>.</i> Why can he not force his wife? Did not Rebbi Huna say, [if she vowed] any benefit from me [shall be forbidden] to you, he forces her and sleeps with her. Any benefit from you [shall be forbidden] to me, he hasto dissolve. There is a difference because it is a benefit for him and her. He should not be able to force his slave! There is a difference, “because his God’s crown is on his head<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:7" href="/Numbers.6.7"><i>Num.</i> 6:7</a>.</i>,” a person who has no other master. This excludes the slave who has another master<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The master has the power to force the slave to disregard the vow. The slave in obeying his master does not commit any sin.</i>. If he comes to protest his master’s word, one says to him: this is practice<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">He has to follow his master’s command.</i>, obey your master’s orders! If his master pushed him and he became impure, does he have to bring a sacrifice of impurity? Is he a <i>nazir</i>, did not you decide for him that he should become impure<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">If his master’s action invalidates the slave’s vow, there is no valid vow of <i>nazir</i>. The status of the slave reverts to profane; the slave is not responsible for the lifting of the status of <i>nazir</i> from him. If there were anything sinful in this action, it would be the master’s responsibility.</i>? You say, he brings a sacrifice of impurity; could he bring here a sacrifice of impurity? Does he annul? Is he a <i>nazir</i>, did not you decide for him that he should become impure? You say, he annuls; could he here annul<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Argument and meaning are completely parallel to the preceding.</i>?
The following is obvious: If he became impure and then was manumitted, he brings a sacrifice of impurity. When is that, if his master forced him or if his master did not force him<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">What was the cause of the slave’s impurity?</i>? If you say that he forced him, how can be bring a sacrifice of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">It was shown in the preceding paragraph that no sacrifice is due.</i>? If you say he did not force him, should he not count for his master as long as he still is in the latter’s power<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If the slave made a vow of <i>nazir</i> for a specified period, he should be able to count that period as long as he did not violate its terms by his own will.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, we are dealing with the case that his master forced him.
